# Division B Anatomy & Physiology
## Complete State Scope Study Guide — 2026
*Includes: Multiple Choice, Free Response, and Case Studies*
*State/National scope additions: brainstem/cerebellum nuclei & tracts, cranial nerve pathways, spinal cord ascending/descending tracts, general senses & proprioception, visual pathway defects, pupillary reflex pathway, Cushing's Syndrome, Addison's Disease*

---

# PART I: MULTIPLE CHOICE & SHORT ANSWER

## Visual Pathways and Phototransduction

**Q1.** Which of the following is true of vision and phototransduction?
- A. Damage to the optic chiasm will cause bitemporal hemianopia
- B. GMP is converted to cGMP by phosphodiesterase
- C. Transducin is a G-protein
- D. Na+ channels open when rhodopsin's retinal is converted to its all-trans form

**Answer: A**
Damage to the optic chiasm affects crossing nasal fibers, leading to loss of the temporal visual fields (bitemporal hemianopia). Transducin is indeed a G-protein, but A is the primary clinical defect associated with chiasm lesions. Note that Na+ channels actually CLOSE in light.

---

**Q2.** Michelle cuts the fibers of Larry's left nasal optic nerve before the optic chiasm. What visual field defect results?
- A. Loss of vision in left visual field in both eyes
- B. Loss of vision in right visual field in both eyes
- C. Loss of vision in left visual field in left eye
- D. Loss of vision in right visual field in left eye

**Answer: D**
Nasal retinal fibers carry information from the temporal visual field. Cutting the left nasal fibers eliminates the left eye's temporal field, which corresponds to the right visual field of that specific eye.

---

**Q3.** Which best describes the underlying optical defect in astigmatism?
- A. The lens loses its elasticity and cannot accommodate for near vision
- B. The cornea or lens has an irregular curvature, causing light to focus at multiple points
- C. The eyeball is too short, causing light to focus behind the retina
- D. Increased intraocular pressure damages the optic nerve

**Answer: B**
Astigmatism occurs when the cornea or lens is not perfectly spherical, leading to blurred vision at all distances because light is refracted unevenly.

---

**Q4.** Which option correctly describes the pathway of visual information from the retina to the brain?
- A. Retina → Optic chiasm → Optic nerve → LGN → Visual cortex
- B. Retina → Optic nerve → Optic chiasm → Optic tract → LGN → Visual cortex
- C. Retina → LGN → Optic nerve → Optic tract → Visual cortex
- D. Retina → Optic tract → Optic chiasm → Visual cortex → LGN

**Answer: B**
The correct anatomical sequence is Retina → Optic Nerve → Optic Chiasm → Optic Tract → LGN → Visual Cortex.

---

**Q5.** The principal neurotransmitter released by photoreceptors in the dark, and its effect on ON-bipolar cells:
- A. Glutamate; sign-inverting (via mGluR6) — hyperpolarizes ON-bipolar cells in the dark
- B. GABA; directly inhibits bipolar cells
- C. Glycine; depolarizes ON-bipolar cells
- D. Acetylcholine; activates nicotinic receptors on bipolar cells

**Answer: A**
In the dark, photoreceptors are depolarized and continuously release glutamate. For ON-bipolar cells, glutamate is inhibitory (sign-inverting) via mGluR6 receptors.

---

**Q6.** A lesion of Meyer's loop (inferior fibers of the optic radiation in the temporal lobe) causes:
- A. Contralateral superior quadrantanopia
- B. Contralateral inferior quadrantanopia
- C. Ipsilateral superior quadrantanopia
- D. Bitemporal hemianopia

**Answer: A**
Meyer's loop carries information from the superior visual world. A lesion here causes a "pie in the sky" defect, or contralateral superior quadrantanopia.

---

**Q7.** During dark adaptation, photoreceptors exhibit:
- A. Low cGMP; Na+/Ca2+ channels closed; hyperpolarized
- B. High cGMP; Na+/Ca2+ channels open; depolarized
- C. High cAMP; K+ channels open; hyperpolarized
- D. Low Ca2+; glutamate release decreased

**Answer: B**
In the dark, cGMP levels are high, keeping cyclic nucleotide-gated channels open. This allows the "dark current" (influx of Na+ and Ca2+) to keep the cell depolarized.

---

**Q8.** Photon absorption activates transducin → PDE6 → lowers cGMP. How does this alter the photoreceptor's electrical state?
- A. Depolarization due to increased Na+ entry through cGMP-gated channels
- B. Depolarization due to decreased Ca2+ extrusion
- C. Hyperpolarization due to closure of cGMP-gated Na+/Ca2+ channels in the outer segment
- D. Hyperpolarization due to activation of voltage-gated Na+ channels

**Answer: C**

---

**Q9.** Which visual field defect results from damage to the left optic nerve?
**Answer:** Left monocular blindness (complete loss of vision in the left eye only).

---

**Q10.** Which visual field defect results from severing the nerve fibers that decussate at the optic chiasm?
**Answer:** Bitemporal hemianopia (loss of both temporal/peripheral visual fields).

---

**Q11.** Which visual field defect results from lesioning both banks of the calcarine fissure in the right primary visual cortex (V1)?
**Answer:** Left homonymous hemianopia (loss of the left visual field in both eyes), with possible macular sparing.

---

**Q12.** How is visual information from the two eyes kept separate in the LGN?
- A. Both eyes' information is mixed in all six layers
- B. The LGN contains six layers, three receiving input from each eye
- C. Cells receive input from one eye but are not laminar
- D. Right LGN receives only from left eye; left LGN only from right eye

**Answer: B**

---

**Q13.** How does the extrastriate cortex (V2–V5) differ from V1?
- A. V1 responds to all visual input; extrastriate responds only to cones
- B. V1 processes simple features (orientation, direction); extrastriate regions process complex input like objects, color, and motion
- C. V1 sends output to extrastriate but receives none back
- D. V1 separates "where" and "what" pathways; extrastriate does not

**Answer: B**

---

## Olfactory and Gustatory Systems

**Q14.** Which second messenger system is primarily involved in olfactory signal transduction?
- A. IP3 pathway
- B. cAMP pathway involving Golf protein
- C. cGMP pathway
- D. JAK-STAT pathway

**Answer: B**
Olfactory receptors are GPCRs that activate a specific G-protein called Golf, which stimulates adenylyl cyclase to produce cAMP.

---

**Q15.** Which statement about the olfactory pathway is uniquely correct compared to other special senses?
- A. Projects first to thalamus
- B. Regenerating receptor neurons from basal cells
- C. Uses primarily glycine as its neurotransmitter
- D. Travels exclusively in CN V

**Answer: B**
Olfactory neurons are unique because they are regularly replaced by basal stem cells. Additionally, olfaction is the only special sense that bypasses the thalamus on its way to the primary cortex.

---

**Q16.** Which cranial nerve carries taste from the anterior two-thirds of the tongue?
- A. CN IX
- B. CN VII
- C. CN X
- D. CN V3

**Answer: B**
The Facial Nerve (CN VII) carries taste from the anterior 2/3 of the tongue via the chorda tympani branch.

---

**Q17.** The primary function of filiform papillae is:
- A. Detection of bitter taste at highest density
- B. Detection of umami through mGluR4 receptors
- C. Providing texture and friction for mechanical manipulation of food
- D. Releasing lingual lipase to begin fat digestion

**Answer: C**
Filiform papillae are the only type of papillae that do NOT contain taste buds; they are purely mechanical.

---

**Q18.** Which taste sensation is mediated by ion channels rather than GPCRs?
- A. Sweet and Bitter
- B. Salty and Sour
- C. Umami and Sweet
- D. Bitter and Umami

**Answer: B**
Salty (Na+) and Sour (H+) tastes involve ions directly entering or blocking channels, whereas Sweet, Bitter, and Umami utilize G-protein coupled receptors.

---

**Q19.** Olfactory receptor neurons express GPCRs coupled to Golf protein. When an odorant binds, which downstream event initiates depolarization?
- A. Inhibition of adenylate cyclase III → decreased cAMP
- B. Opening of voltage-gated Na+ channels independent of second messengers
- C. Activation of adenylate cyclase III → increased cAMP → CNG channel opening
- D. Activation of phospholipase C → IP3 → Ca2+ store release

**Answer: C**

---

**Q20.** Different odorants activate overlapping receptor sets, and each receptor responds to multiple odorants. Which principle allows discrimination of a vast number of odors?
- A. Each odorant binds a single unique receptor
- B. Each receptor detects only one specific odorant
- C. Discrimination depends solely on concentration
- D. Odor identity is encoded by a combinatorial pattern of receptor activation

**Answer: D**

---

**Q21.** Salty and sour tastes are detected by ion channels rather than GPCRs. Which ion movements characterize transduction of these two modalities?
- A. Sour: Na+ influx; Salty: H+ blocks Ca2+ channels
- B. Salty: H+ influx; Sour: Na+ blocks K+ channels
- C. Salty: Na+ influx; Sour: H+ blocks K+ channels
- D. Salty: Ca2+ influx; Sour: Cl− efflux

**Answer: C**

---

**Q22.** COVID-19 patients reported foods tasting metallic or less sweet/salty after recovery. How would you classify this phenomenon?
- A. Anosmia
- B. Myopia
- C. Dysosmia
- D. Dysgeusia

**Answer: D**

---

## Vestibular and Auditory Systems

**Q23.** The utricle detects ____, the saccule detects ____, and the semicircular canals detect ____.
- A. Angular; Vertical; Horizontal
- B. Angular; Horizontal; Vertical
- C. Vertical; Horizontal; Angular
- D. Horizontal; Vertical; Angular

**Answer: D**
The utricle senses horizontal linear acceleration, the saccule senses vertical linear acceleration, and the semicircular canals sense angular (rotational) acceleration.

---

**Q24.** Organ of Corti hair cell depolarization occurs when stereocilia deflect:
- A. Toward shortest cilium → K+ efflux
- B. Toward tallest cilium → K+ influx from endolymph
- C. Toward tallest cilium → Na+ influx from perilymph
- D. Toward shortest cilium → Ca2+ influx from endolymph

**Answer: B**
Deflection toward the kinocilium (tallest) opens tip-links, allowing K+ from the high-potassium endolymph to enter and depolarize the cell.

---

**Q25.** Which part of the cochlea responds to high-frequency sounds?
- A. Base (near oval window)
- B. Apex
- C. Middle turn
- D. Helicotrema

**Answer: A**
The basilar membrane is stiff and narrow at the base, making it resonate at high frequencies. It is wide and floppy at the apex, which responds to low frequencies.

---

**Q26.** A 32-year-old woman presents with episodic vertigo (5–8 mins), nausea, tinnitus, and fluctuating hearing loss. Diagnosis?
- A. Otosclerosis
- B. Benign Paroxysmal Positional Vertigo
- C. Ménière's disease
- D. Labyrinthitis

**Answer: C**
The triad of vertigo, tinnitus, and fluctuating hearing loss is classic for Ménière's disease, caused by endolymphatic hydrops.

---

**Q27.** Stereocilia are immersed in high-K+ endolymph with a large positive endocochlear potential. Deflection toward the tallest cilium opens tip-link-gated channels. Which ionic event follows?
- A. K+ efflux → hyperpolarization
- B. K+ influx driven by electrical and chemical gradients → graded depolarization
- C. Ca2+ efflux reducing neurotransmitter release
- D. Na+ influx through voltage-gated channels

**Answer: B**

---

**Q28.** A musician develops selective loss of sensitivity above 8 kHz. Which cochlear region is damaged, and why?
- A. Apex — wide, flexible membrane optimized for high frequencies
- B. Base — narrow, stiff basilar membrane tuned for high frequencies
- C. Middle turn — transmits frequencies near the helicotrema
- D. Helicotrema — where pressure waves dissipate

**Answer: B**

---

**Q29.** A patient shows normal auditory nerve transmission at moderate/high intensities but markedly reduced sensitivity to soft sounds. Which structure is most likely impaired?
- A. Inner hair cells
- B. Type II spiral ganglion neurons
- C. Tectorial membrane
- D. Outer hair cells

**Answer: D** — Outer hair cells amplify soft sounds via electromotility; their loss selectively reduces sensitivity at low amplitudes.

---

## Spinal Cord and Proprioception

**Q30.** A patient suffers a complete right-sided hemisection at T4 (Brown-Séquard syndrome). What is expected below T4?
- A. Loss of all sensation bilaterally; preserved motor function
- B. Loss of pain/temp on left; loss of fine touch/proprioception and voluntary motor on right
- C. Loss of pain/temp on right; loss of fine touch/proprioception and voluntary motor on left
- D. Loss of all sensation and motor on the left

**Answer: B**
In Brown-Séquard, motor and DCML (touch) loss is ipsilateral (same side) because they cross in the medulla. Spinothalamic (pain/temp) loss is contralateral (opposite side) because it crosses in the spinal cord.

---

**Q31.** Syringomyelia presents with a "cape-like" loss of pain and temperature. Why?
- A. Dorsal columns are most resistant to compression
- B. Fine touch fibers are myelinated and immune to central cavitation
- C. The crossing spinothalamic fibers at the anterior commissure are disrupted first
- D. The syrinx forms posteriorly, sparing anterior spinothalamic fibers

**Answer: C**
A syrinx (cavity) in the center of the cord hits the fibers crossing the anterior white commissure first. These are the spinothalamic fibers, leading to bilateral loss of pain and temp at those segments.

---

**Q32.** Muscle spindles detect ____, while Golgi tendon organs detect ____.
- A. Tension; Length
- B. Length/Stretch; Tension/Force
- C. Vibration; Pressure
- D. Heat; Pain

**Answer: B**
Spindles are in parallel with muscle fibers and detect stretch. GTOs are in series at the tendon and detect tension/contraction force.

---

**Q33.** Upper motor neuron (UMN) lesions produce:
- A. Flaccid paralysis, muscle atrophy, fasciculations, absent reflexes
- B. Spastic paralysis, hyperreflexia, Babinski sign, minimal atrophy
- C. Flaccid paralysis, hyperreflexia, intact sensation
- D. Spastic paralysis, absent reflexes, sensory loss

**Answer: B**
UMN lesions (like stroke or MS) remove descending inhibition, leading to "overactive" muscles (spasticity and hyperreflexia). LMN lesions lead to "dead" muscles (flaccidity and atrophy).

---

**Q34.** What is the difference between Aδ and C nerve fibers? *(3 pts)*
**Answer:** Aδ fibers — myelinated, medium diameter, fast conduction (~5–30 m/s), carry sharp/acute pain and temperature. C fibers — unmyelinated, small diameter, slow conduction (<2 m/s), carry dull/chronic pain, temperature, itch, and some autonomic signals.

---

**Q35.** During the stretch reflex, inhibition of the antagonist muscle occurs primarily via:
- A. Direct inhibition by the Ia afferent fiber
- B. Recurrent inhibition through Renshaw cells
- C. Reciprocal inhibition via inhibitory interneurons
- D. Golgi tendon organ activation
- E. Descending corticospinal tract input

**Answer: C**

---

**Q36.** Neurons in primary somatosensory cortex (S1) are arranged somatotopically, with regions requiring fine tactile discrimination occupying disproportionately large cortical space. Which body region receives greatest cortical representation?
- A. Upper back
- B. Forearm
- C. Calf
- D. Fingertips

**Answer: D**

---

**Q37.** Spasticity, as seen in cerebral palsy, is defined as:
- A. Lack of balance or muscle coordination
- B. Stiff muscles with regular reflexive activity
- C. Stiff muscles with irregular reflexive activity
- D. Uncontrollable, jerky movements

**Answer: B**

---

**Q38.** Describe the correct pathway for a somatic reflex arc:
- A. Somatic receptor → efferent fibers → integrating center → afferent fibers → effector muscle
- B. Somatic receptor → afferent nerve fibers → integrating center → efferent nerve fibers → effector muscle
- C. Effector muscle → efferent fibers → integrating center → afferent fibers → somatic receptor
- D. Effector muscle → afferent fibers → integrating center → efferent fibers → somatic receptor

**Answer: B**

---

## Cranial Nerves & Brainstem

**Q39.** Which nucleus in the medulla receives visceral sensory input from cranial nerves VII, IX, and X?
- A. Nucleus ambiguus
- B. Dorsal motor nucleus
- C. Inferior olivary nucleus
- D. Solitary nucleus (NTS)
- E. Sensorinucleus

**Answer: D**

---

**Q40.** Select the correct pairings of neurological lesions and their resulting effects: *(Select all that apply)*
- a. Substantia nigra — rigidity
- b. Hippocampus — inability to form new long-term memories
- c. Putamen — chorea
- d. Cerebellum — loss of fine motor coordination and balance
- e. Primary motor cortex — loss of sensation on the contralateral side
- f. Subthalamus — flailing movements of entire limb (hemiballismus)
- g. Wernicke's area — non-fluent, effortful speech
- h. Medulla oblongata — loss of voluntary skeletal muscle movement

**Correct: a, b, c, d, f**
Note: Primary motor cortex → motor loss, not sensory loss (e is wrong). Wernicke's = fluent but nonsensical speech; Broca's = non-fluent (g is wrong). Medulla controls vital autonomic functions, not voluntary movement directly (h is wrong).

---

**Q41.** What are the afferent and efferent nerve fibers for the pupillary light reflex?
**Answer:** Afferent: CN II (optic nerve). Efferent: CN III (oculomotor nerve) — via Edinger-Westphal nucleus → ciliary ganglion → sphincter pupillae.

---

**Q42.** Which cranial nerve aids in digestion, blood pressure regulation, and other autonomic functions?
- A. CN III
- B. CN VIII
- C. CN X (Vagus)
- D. CN XII

**Answer: C**

---

**Q43.** A student touches their eyelid and the eye blinks (corneal reflex). Which cranial nerve carries the sensory signal?
- A. CN VII
- B. CN IX
- C. CN III
- D. CN V

**Answer: D** — Afferent = CN V1; Efferent = CN VII.

---

**Q44.** A cortical region in the superior temporal lobe receives processed auditory information and maps speech sounds onto stored lexical/semantic representations. Damage most likely results in:
- A. Nonfluent, effortful speech with preserved comprehension
- B. Intact comprehension but impaired repetition
- C. Inability to attach semantic meaning to heard words (Wernicke's/receptive aphasia)
- D. Difficulty articulating speech due to impaired motor control

**Answer: C**

---

**Q45.** A person reflexively pulls their foot away after stepping on a nail, then consciously decides to look down and move. Which response occurs faster, and why? *(4 pts)*
**Answer:** The reflex withdrawal is faster. It travels only to the spinal cord (not the brain) via a polysynaptic reflex arc, bypassing cortical processing. The voluntary decision requires sensory information to ascend to the brain, be processed in the cortex, and then a motor command to descend — adding significant conduction and processing time.

---

## Endocrine System

**Q46.** Which hormone acts on nuclear receptors?
- A. Insulin
- B. PTH
- C. Cortisol
- D. TSH

**Answer: C**
Cortisol is a steroid hormone. Steroids are lipid-soluble and travel through the cell membrane to bind receptors inside the nucleus to alter gene expression.

---

**Q47.** Which endocrine organ stores hormone-containing colloid extracellularly?
- A. Adrenal cortex
- B. Thyroid gland
- C. Pancreas
- D. Pituitary gland

**Answer: B**
The thyroid is unique in storing vast amounts of precursor hormone (thyroglobulin) in extracellular follicles called colloid.

---

**Q48.** A patient has low cortisol, high ACTH, salt craving, and hyperpigmentation. Diagnosis?
- A. Cushing's Syndrome
- B. Addison's Disease
- C. Conn's Syndrome
- D. Diabetes Insipidus

**Answer: B**
This is primary adrenal insufficiency (Addison's). Hyperpigmentation occurs because high ACTH levels (due to lack of feedback) stimulate melanocytes.

---

**Q49.** Which cells secrete renin, and what stimulates them?
- A. Macula densa; high blood pressure
- B. Juxtaglomerular (JG) cells; low blood pressure
- C. Podocytes; high protein levels
- D. Mesangial cells; high glucose

**Answer: B**
JG cells in the kidney act as baroreceptors; they release renin when they detect low blood pressure or sympathetic stimulation.

---

**Q50.** What is the effect of ADH on the collecting duct?
- A. It increases Na+ reabsorption via ENaC
- B. It inserts aquaporin-2 channels to increase water reabsorption
- C. It inhibits the Na+/K+ pump
- D. It causes vasoconstriction of the afferent arteriole

**Answer: B**
ADH (Vasopressin) targets V2 receptors, which trigger the translocation of aquaporin-2 water channels to the apical membrane, allowing water to leave the urine and enter the blood.

---

**Q51.** You stain cells with a fluorescent indicator for an active steroid hormone-receptor complex acting as a transcription factor. Where would you see fluorescence?
- A. Cell membrane
- B. Cytoplasm
- C. Nucleus
- D. All of the above

**Answer: C**

---

**Q52.** Fill in the table for the three hormone classes. *(Tiebreaker, 6 pts)*

| Class | Blood Transport | Receptor Location | Speed | Example |
|---|---|---|---|---|
| Steroid | Bound to carrier protein | Intracellular (cytoplasm/nucleus) | Slow (hours) | Cortisol |
| Peptide | Dissolved in plasma | Cell surface (membrane receptor) | Fast (seconds–minutes) | Insulin |
| Amine | Varies: catecholamines dissolved; thyroid hormones protein-bound | Varies: catecholamines → membrane; thyroid → intracellular | Varies | Epinephrine / T3 |

---

**Q53.** Hormone X binds cytoplasmic receptors and changes transcription. Hormone Y activates a GPCR and cAMP signaling. Which classification is correct?
- A. X = Peptide; Y = Steroid
- B. X = Steroid; Y = Peptide
- C. X = Amine; Y = Steroid
- D. X = Steroid; Y = Amine

**Answer: B**

---

**Q54.** Which hormone pair best demonstrates that chemical class does not always predict receptor location?
- A. Cortisol/Aldosterone
- B. Growth Hormone/Prolactin
- C. Thyroxine/Epinephrine
- D. Insulin/Glucagon

**Answer: C** — Both are amines, but T4 acts on nuclear receptors while epinephrine acts on membrane GPCRs.

---

**Q55.** How do T4 and T3 differ?
- A. T3 has a shorter half-life than T4
- B. T4 has stronger metabolic effects than T3
- C. Blood concentration of T4 is far higher than T3
- D. A and C

**Answer: D** — T3 is faster-acting with a shorter half-life; T4 blood concentration is ~50× higher than T3.

---

**Q56.** How does the posterior pituitary compare to the anterior pituitary?
- A. Posterior pituitary produces and secretes GH and prolactin
- B. Posterior pituitary produces oxytocin and ADH; anterior stores and releases them
- C. Anterior pituitary produces and secretes GH, prolactin, and others; posterior stores and releases oxytocin and ADH
- D. Anterior produces oxytocin and ADH; posterior stores and releases GH and prolactin

**Answer: C**

---

**Q57.** In congenital iodine deficiency syndrome, severe neurodevelopmental delay occurs. Which mechanism best explains this?
- A. Decreased thyroid hormone reduces neuronal protein synthesis and myelination
- B. Lack of thyroid hormone increases calcitonin, disrupting neuronal metabolism
- C. Excess TSH overstimulates thyroid → toxic T4 levels
- D. Low iodine damages neuronal mitochondria

**Answer: A**

---

**Q58.** A biochemist detects elevated ACTH and cortisol despite low CRH in a patient with a pituitary tumor. What dysfunction is most likely?
- A. Secondary Hyposecretion
- B. Primary Hypersecretion
- C. Secondary Hypersecretion
- D. Primary Hyposecretion

**Answer: C** — A pituitary tumor autonomously secretes excess ACTH, bypassing hypothalamic CRH.

---

**Q59.** Which physiological abnormality is associated with Cushing's Syndrome regarding glucocorticoid secretion?
- A. Stress decreases plasma cortisol in Cushing's
- B. The negative feedback loop is defective — cortisol cannot suppress CRH and ACTH
- C. Cortisol is higher in the evening than morning in Cushing's (opposite of normal)
- D. Cortisol levels fluctuate greatly throughout the day vs. being stable in healthy patients

**Answer: B**

---

**Q60.** A 42-year-old presents with hyperpigmentation, orthostatic hypotension, weight loss, hyponatremia, elevated ACTH, elevated CRH, low cortisol, and low aldosterone. Which molecular mechanism explains the hyperpigmentation?
- A. Upregulation of tyrosinase by chronic cortisol deficiency
- B. Excessive ACTH from POMC cleavage increases α-MSH activity at melanocortin receptors on melanocytes
- C. CRH directly stimulates melanocyte MC1R receptors
- D. Elevated renin causes secondary increases in melanin synthesis

**Answer: B**

---

**Q61.** A patient with autoimmune destruction limited to the zona glomerulosa presents with hyperkalemia and low serum sodium, but normal cortisol and androgen levels. Which finding best explains the electrolyte abnormalities?
- A. Impaired cortisol-mediated sodium retention
- B. Excess ACTH production
- C. Loss of aldosterone-sensitive Na+/K+ exchange in the distal nephron
- D. Compensatory adrenal androgen overproduction

**Answer: C**

---

**Q62.** Low level of which hormone would most directly cause low blood pressure and high serum potassium?
- A. Cortisol
- B. Epinephrine
- C. Aldosterone
- D. Thyroxine

**Answer: C**

---

**Q63.** A patient with low serum calcium has elevated PTH. Which combination of tissue responses is most consistent with PTH action?
- A. Decreased Ca2+ reabsorption in kidney and decreased calcitriol
- B. Increased Ca2+ reabsorption in kidney and increased osteoblast activity
- C. Increased Ca2+ reabsorption in kidney, increased osteoclast activity, and increased calcitriol production
- D. Increased osteoclast activity but decreased intestinal Ca2+ absorption

**Answer: C**

---

**Q64.** What lab value distinguishes secondary from primary hyperparathyroidism?
- A. In primary, serum PTH is low; in secondary it is high
- B. In primary, serum Ca2+ is elevated; in secondary it is low or normal
- C. In primary, calcitonin is low; in secondary it is high
- D. In primary, serum Ca2+ is low; in secondary it is elevated

**Answer: B**

---

**Q65.** A patient with heat intolerance, tachycardia, and weight loss has low TSH, high T3/T4, and positive TSH-receptor antibodies. Which mechanism explains these findings?
- A. Excess pituitary TSH secretion
- B. Autoantibody activation of the TSH receptor increases thyroid hormone production and suppresses TSH via negative feedback
- C. Release of preformed thyroid hormone due to gland destruction
- D. Peripheral conversion of T4 to T3 increases TSH

**Answer: B**

---

**Q66.** Which symptom distinguishes Hashimoto's thyroiditis from simple iodine deficiency?
- A. Goiter
- B. Cold intolerance
- C. Fatigue
- D. Puffy skin
- E. Circulating autoantibodies (anti-TPO, anti-Tg)

**Answer: E**

---

**Q67.** Which finding best distinguishes central from nephrogenic diabetes insipidus?
- A. High plasma osmolarity
- B. Low urine osmolarity
- C. Low circulating ADH levels
- D. Excessive water loss through sweat glands

**Answer: C** — Central DI = ADH deficiency; nephrogenic DI = ADH present but kidney unresponsive.

---

**Q68.** Which Addison's disease case progression is most accurate and thorough?
- a. Autoimmune destruction slowly depletes adrenal cortex over time; symptoms remain subclinical until a physiological stressor (infection) increases cortisol demand beyond reserve capacity ✓
- b. The flu virus directly destroys adrenal tissue
- c. Addison's is caused by a virus with long latency
- d. The PCP misdiagnosed; the patient was actually experiencing Addison's onset all along

**Answer: A**

---

# PART II: CASE STUDIES

## Nervous System Cases

### Case 1 — ALS *(SOAPS Invitational)*
A 50-year-old man presents to his primary care physician reporting difficulty with fine hand movements, twitching in his arms, weakness in his ankles, a propensity for tripping and falling, and some unexpected changes to his emotional state. He has had no issues with bladder control, sensory degeneration, and does not report pain associated with muscle weakness. He has a history of regular smoking and a career in military service.

**(a)** What disease does this patient have? *(2 pts)*

**(b)** Describe the physiological effects of this disease. *(4 pts)*

**(c)** List two treatment options. *(2 pts)*

**Answer Key:**
- (a) ALS (Amyotrophic Lateral Sclerosis)
- (b) Progressive degeneration of both UMN and LMN: UMN loss → spasticity, hyperreflexia, Babinski sign; LMN loss → fasciculations, muscle atrophy, flaccid weakness; bulbar involvement → dysphagia, dysarthria; sensory and bladder function preserved
- (c) Riluzole (glutamate antagonist, slows progression); Edaravone (antioxidant); supportive/palliative care (ventilatory support, PEG tube)

---

### Case 2 — ALS with UMN/LMN Classification *(UCR Highlander Invitational)*
A 61-year-old male speaks slowly and reports left-sided hand weakness, difficulty walking, and tongue fasciculations. On exam: left forearm atrophy, depressed left arm reflexes, right leg hypertonia. His doctor mentions a positive "Ba-something" sign.

**(a)** What disease does he have? *(1 pt)*

**(b)** Name all relevant symptoms from the passage. Classify each as a UMN or LMN sign. *(4 pts — all or nothing)*

**(c)** What is the "positive Ba-something sign"? *(2 pts)*

**Answer Key:**
- (a) ALS
- (b) Tongue fasciculations (LMN), left forearm atrophy (LMN), depressed left arm reflexes (LMN), slow/dysarthric speech (bulbar LMN), right leg hypertonia (UMN), positive Babinski sign (UMN)
- (c) Babinski sign: plantar stimulation causes dorsiflexion of the big toe and fanning of the other toes; indicates UMN lesion (loss of descending inhibition on the reflex arc)

---

### Case 3 — Multiple Sclerosis *(UCR Highlander Invitational)*
Manasvi, a 21-year-old female, just moved from Norway (8 years of low sunlight) to California. She develops dizziness, vertigo, coordination problems, and blurry vision over several days. She experiences partial or complete vision loss that she attributes to the bright California sun. She eventually sees a doctor.

**(a)** What disease does she have? *(2 pts)*

**(b)** Name three risk factors from the passage that may have contributed to this diagnosis. *(3 pts)*

**(c)** What type of MS is depicted in the passage? *(2 pts)*

**(d)** Why does Manasvi have so many symptoms involving her eyes? *(3 pts)*

**(e)** Name specific signs of MS not necessarily included in the passage. *(4 pts)*

**Answer Key:**
- (a) Multiple Sclerosis
- (b) Low sunlight exposure/vitamin D deficiency (Norway), female sex (implied), significant environmental/immune trigger from relocation
- (c) Relapsing-remitting MS (RRMS)
- (d) The optic nerve is a common early target of demyelination (optic neuritis); the medial longitudinal fasciculus (MLF) is also frequently involved, causing internuclear ophthalmoplegia; both produce visual symptoms
- (e) Lhermitte's sign (electric shock sensation on neck flexion), Uhthoff's phenomenon (symptom worsening with heat), bladder dysfunction, spasticity, cognitive changes, diplopia

---

### Case 4 — Epilepsy *(UMSO Invitational)*
Annalisa, a previously healthy 7-year-old, was brought to the ED following two episodes of sudden loss of consciousness accompanied by generalized body stiffening, twitching, and shaking lasting 1–2 minutes, followed by brief confusion and fatigue. EEG confirmed epilepsy with activity in both hemispheres.

**(a)** Which type of seizure did Annalisa experience? Be specific. *(1 pt)*

**(b)** Describe one symptom common to this seizure type that Annalisa did NOT experience. *(1 pt)*

**(c)** How does a seizure appear on an EEG? What are the general EEG characteristics that define a seizure? *(3 pts)*

**Answer Key:**
- (a) Generalized tonic-clonic seizure (grand mal)
- (b) Urinary incontinence; tongue biting; cyanosis; automatisms (any valid generalized seizure symptom she lacked)
- (c) Seizures on EEG show: (1) high-amplitude, (2) high-frequency, (3) rhythmic/synchronous spike-and-wave discharges across multiple channels, disrupting normal background activity; postictal suppression (flattening) may follow

---

## Special Senses Cases

### Case 5 — Auditory Processing Disorder *(UMSO Invitational)*
A 10-year-old girl struggles to follow instructions in class and did not improve when moved to the front row. She hears environmental sounds better than speech, can identify speech sounds and language but not words, and symptoms worsen in noise. Audiogram shows mild hearing loss. Speech Discrimination: Right Ear 28%, Left Ear 8%. Tympanometry: normal. Acoustic Reflex: absent. Rinne and Weber tests: normal. OAE: normal. ABR: abnormal.

**(a)** What is your diagnosis? Give 2 examples from the passage that support it and explain how they relate. *(3 pts)*

**(b)** Explain why the Rinne, Weber, and OAE tests are normal, but the ABR is abnormal. What does this indicate about the location of dysfunction? *(4 pts)*

**(c)** Given your diagnosis, why can she hear environmental sounds but struggle with speech? *(Tiebreaker, 3 pts)*

**Answer Key:**
- (a) Auditory Neuropathy Spectrum Disorder (ANSD) — outer hair cells are intact but neural synchrony is disrupted; supported by: (1) can identify speech sounds but not words = neural processing failure, not cochlear failure; (2) worse in noise = poor temporal coding that cannot extract signal from background noise
- (b) Normal OAE = outer hair cells functioning normally (cochlear transduction intact); normal Rinne/Weber = no conductive or peripheral cochlear loss; abnormal ABR = dysfunction at the auditory nerve or lower brainstem level (dyssynchronous neural firing). Lesion is post-cochlear, at the auditory nerve
- (c) Environmental sounds are typically louder, lower in frequency, and do not require precise temporal fine structure resolution. Speech relies on rapid temporal coding that demands neural synchrony; ANSD specifically disrupts this temporal processing while leaving basic sound detection relatively intact

---

### Case 6 — Ménière's Disease *(UCR Highlander / State Question Bank)*
A 32-year-old woman presents with severe dizzy spells lasting 5–8 minutes, accompanied by nausea and ear ringing. She also reports fluctuating hearing loss in her left ear.

**(a)** What disease does she have? *(2 pts)*

**(b)** How does this disease occur? Describe the mechanism. *(2 pts)*

**(c)** What dietary modifications should this patient make? *(3 pts)*

**Answer Key:**
- (a) Ménière's disease
- (b) Endolymphatic hydrops: excess accumulation of endolymph distends the membranous labyrinth, mechanically disrupting both cochlear hair cells (→ hearing loss, tinnitus) and vestibular hair cells (→ episodic vertigo)
- (c) Low-sodium diet (reduces endolymph production via osmotic pressure); limit caffeine and alcohol (both can affect endolymph homeostasis); maintain adequate hydration

---

### Case 7 — Otosclerosis *(UCR Highlander / State Question Bank)*
A 22-year-old woman presents with progressive left-ear hearing loss. Her father started wearing a hearing aid at age 25. A tuning fork placed at the center of the forehead shows lateralization to the left ear.

**(a)** What disease does she have? *(2 pts)*

**(b)** What is the inheritance pattern? *(1 pt)*

**(c)** What test was performed, and what does lateralization to the left indicate? *(1 pt)*

**Answer Key:**
- (a) Otosclerosis — abnormal bone remodeling fixes the stapes footplate, reducing sound transmission and causing conductive hearing loss
- (b) Autosomal dominant with incomplete penetrance
- (c) Weber test; lateralization to the left indicates conductive hearing loss in the left ear (or sensorineural loss in the right ear); sound is louder in the ear with conductive loss because ambient noise is excluded

---

### Case 8 — Bacterial Conjunctivitis *(UCR Highlander Invitational)*
Bob, a 21-year-old man, presents with eye discomfort, redness, and an eye stuck shut in the morning. Physical exam reveals thick, globular purulent discharge on the lid margins and corners of the eye.

**(a)** What disease does Bob have? *(3 pts)*

**(b)** How does this disease occur? *(2 pts)*

**(c)** What form of the disease is this? *(1 pt)*

**Answer Key:**
- (a) Conjunctivitis (pink eye)
- (b) Bacterial infection of the conjunctiva, most commonly Staphylococcus aureus or Streptococcus pneumoniae, causing inflammation, increased mucus/pus production, and crusting
- (c) Bacterial conjunctivitis (as opposed to viral or allergic); purulent discharge is the distinguishing feature

---

### Case 9 — Glaucoma *(UCR Highlander Invitational)*
A 50-year-old African American man with hypertension and type II diabetes complains of slight peripheral vision loss. IOP = 45 mmHg. Optic disc shows an enlarged cup-to-disc ratio.

**(a)** What disease does he have? *(2 pts)*

**(b)** What are the two types of this disease? *(2 pts)*

**(c)** Which type does this patient most likely have? *(2 pts)*

**(d)** Name symptoms of the other type. *(3 pts)*

**Answer Key:**
- (a) Glaucoma
- (b) Open-angle glaucoma and angle-closure (closed-angle) glaucoma
- (c) Open-angle glaucoma: gradual, painless, peripheral vision loss with elevated IOP; fits his presentation
- (d) Angle-closure glaucoma: sudden severe eye pain, headache, nausea/vomiting, halos around lights, rapid vision loss, redness — a medical emergency

---

## Endocrine Cases

### Case 10 — Graves' Disease *(Purdue Invitational)*

| Parameter | Normal Range | Patient Result |
|---|---|---|
| T3 | 80–200 ng/dL | 245 ng/dL |
| T4 | 5–12 μg/dL | 15 μg/dL |
| TSH | 0.5–5 μU/mL | 0.1 μU/mL |
| Anti-TSH Receptor Antibodies | (−) | (+) |

**(a)** Identify the most appropriate disorder. *(1 pt)*

**(b)** Which gland is primarily impacted? *(2 pts)*

**(c)** Explain the mechanistic underpinning. *(3 pts)*

**(d)** List at least 2 symptoms the patient is likely experiencing. *(2 pts)*

**Answer Key:**
- (a) Graves' disease
- (b) Thyroid gland
- (c) Autoantibodies against TSH receptors (thyroid-stimulating immunoglobulins) mimic TSH → constitutive activation of thyroid follicular cells → excess T3/T4 production → negative feedback suppresses pituitary TSH release → low TSH despite high thyroid hormone levels
- (d) Exophthalmos (proptosis), tachycardia, weight loss despite normal/increased appetite, heat intolerance, tremor, anxiety, pretibial myxedema

---

### Case 11 — Primary Hyperparathyroidism *(Purdue Invitational)*

| Parameter | Normal Range | Patient Result |
|---|---|---|
| Serum Ca²⁺ | 8.5–10.5 mg/dL | 12.6 mg/dL |
| PTH | 15–65 pg/mL | 150 pg/mL |
| Calcitonin | 0–10 pg/mL | 3 pg/mL |
| Urinary Ca²⁺ Excretion | 100–250 mg/day | 400 mg/day |

**(a)** Identify the most appropriate disorder. *(1 pt)*

**(b)** Which gland is primarily impacted? *(2 pts)*

**(c)** Explain the mechanistic underpinning. *(3 pts)*

**(d)** List at least 2 symptoms the patient is likely experiencing. *(2 pts)*

**Answer Key:**
- (a) Primary hyperparathyroidism
- (b) Parathyroid glands (usually a single adenoma)
- (c) Autonomous PTH oversecretion → increased osteoclast activity (bone resorption → hypercalcemia) + increased renal Ca²⁺ reabsorption + increased calcitriol synthesis (→ increased intestinal Ca²⁺ absorption); urinary Ca²⁺ rises because filtered load overwhelms reabsorption capacity
- (d) "Bones, stones, groans, moans": bone pain/fractures, kidney stones (hypercalciuria), constipation/nausea, fatigue/depression, polyuria

---

### Case 12 — Type 1 Diabetes Mellitus *(Purdue Invitational)*

| Parameter | Normal Range | Patient Result |
|---|---|---|
| Blood Glucose | 70–100 mg/dL | 290 mg/dL |
| Insulin | 5–15 μU/mL | 2 μU/mL |
| Glucagon | 50–100 pg/mL | 160 pg/mL |
| Urine Glucose | (−) | (+) |
| Serum Ketones | (−) | (+) |

**(a)** Identify the most appropriate disorder. *(1 pt)*

**(b)** Which gland is primarily impacted? *(2 pts)*

**(c)** Explain the mechanistic underpinning. *(3 pts)*

**(d)** List at least 2 symptoms the patient is likely experiencing. *(2 pts)*

**Answer Key:**
- (a) Type 1 Diabetes Mellitus
- (b) Pancreas (islets of Langerhans, specifically β-cells)
- (c) Autoimmune destruction of β-cells → absent insulin → cells cannot take up glucose (hyperglycemia) → glucose exceeds renal threshold → glucosuria + osmotic diuresis; unopposed glucagon → gluconeogenesis + lipolysis → ketone body production → ketonemia/ketonuria; risk of diabetic ketoacidosis (DKA)
- (d) Polyuria, polydipsia, polyphagia, weight loss, fatigue, fruity breath (ketones)

---

### Case 13 — Conn's Syndrome / Primary Hyperaldosteronism *(Purdue Invitational)*

| Parameter | Normal Range | Patient Result |
|---|---|---|
| Serum Na⁺ | 135–145 mEq/L | 149 mEq/L |
| Serum K⁺ | 3.5–5.0 mEq/L | 2.8 mEq/L |
| Blood Pressure | 110/70 mmHg | 162/98 mmHg |
| Plasma Renin Activity | 1.0–3.0 ng/mL/hr | 0.2 ng/mL/hr |
| Plasma Aldosterone | 4–15 ng/dL | 44 ng/dL |

**(a)** Identify the most appropriate disorder. *(1 pt)*

**(b)** Which gland is primarily impacted? *(2 pts)*

**(c)** Explain the mechanistic underpinning. *(3 pts)*

**(d)** List at least 2 symptoms the patient is likely experiencing. *(2 pts)*

**Answer Key:**
- (a) Primary hyperaldosteronism (Conn's syndrome)
- (b) Adrenal cortex, zona glomerulosa
- (c) Autonomous aldosterone hypersecretion → ENaC upregulation in collecting duct → Na⁺ and water retention (hypertension, hypernatremia) → K⁺ wasting (hypokalemia) → volume expansion suppresses renin via negative feedback (low PRA distinguishes primary from secondary hyperaldosteronism)
- (d) Hypertension (often resistant), muscle weakness/cramps from hypokalemia, polyuria, headache, metabolic alkalosis

---

### Case 14 — Hashimoto's Thyroiditis with Hashitoxicosis *(UMSO Invitational)*
A 40-year-old woman presents with weight loss, palpitations, tremors, nervousness, and heat intolerance over 2 months. Diffuse firm thyroid enlargement, HR 112 bpm. Labs: TSH <0.01 μIU/mL, free T4 2.1 ng/dL (elevated), free T3 5.8 pg/mL (elevated). No orbitopathy.

Six months later she returns with fatigue, cold intolerance, constipation, weight gain (5–7 kg), dry skin, hair thinning, HR 58 bpm, thyroid still enlarged. Labs: TSH 12 μIU/mL (elevated), free T4 0.6 ng/dL (low), positive anti-TPO and anti-Tg antibodies. Biopsy and ultrasound performed.

**(a)** List key signs/symptoms from the initial visit suggesting a thyroid disorder. How do these relate to thyroid hormone activity? *(4 pts)*

**(b)** Based on the initial presentation, hyperthyroidism or hypothyroidism? Give 2 reasons from lab results and 2 from signs/symptoms. *(5 pts)*

**(c)** Given the follow-up symptoms, labs, and biopsy results, what is the most likely diagnosis? Explain how all findings relate. *(6 pts)*

**(d)** Explain a physiological mechanism relevant to the diagnosis that accounts for the earlier hyperthyroid presentation. *(4 pts)*

**Answer Key:**
- (a) Tachycardia, tremors, heat intolerance, weight loss — thyroid hormones increase basal metabolic rate, upregulate β-adrenergic receptors (→ tachycardia, tremor), increase thermogenesis (→ heat intolerance), and stimulate catabolism (→ weight loss)
- (b) Hyperthyroidism: Lab — (1) TSH suppressed below detectable levels (high T3/T4 inhibits pituitary); (2) free T4 and T3 both elevated; Clinical — (1) tachycardia; (2) heat intolerance and weight loss
- (c) Hashimoto's thyroiditis: autoimmune destruction of thyroid tissue (anti-TPO and anti-Tg antibodies confirm); lymphocytic infiltration on biopsy; hypoechoic heterogeneous appearance on ultrasound; progressive loss of functional tissue → hypothyroidism (high TSH, low T4)
- (d) Hashitoxicosis: early inflammatory destruction of thyroid follicles releases preformed stored thyroid hormone into circulation → transient hyperthyroid phase. As destruction continues and hormone stores are depleted, hypothyroidism develops. This is distinct from Graves' disease (no TSH receptor antibodies, no orbitopathy)

---

### Case 15 — Addison's Disease (Acute Crisis Precipitated by Infection) *(UMSO Invitational)*
A 30-year-old man is seen for flu-like symptoms and sent home. Days later he returns to the ED with severe fatigue, lightheadedness, muscle weakness, nausea, and hyperpigmentation in certain areas. Addison's disease is suspected.

**(a)** Why is this case progression (subclinical until an acute stressor) common for Addison's disease patients? *(Select the most accurate and thorough explanation)* *(2 pts)*

**(b)** Explain the mechanism behind the hyperpigmentation. *(3 pts)*

**(c)** What electrolyte abnormalities would you expect and why? *(3 pts)*

**Answer Key:**
- (a) Autoimmune destruction slowly depletes adrenal cortex over time; symptoms remain subclinical until a physiological stressor (infection) increases cortisol demand beyond reserve capacity
- (b) Low cortisol removes negative feedback on the pituitary/hypothalamus → markedly elevated ACTH; ACTH is cleaved from POMC (proopiomelanocortin), the same precursor as α-MSH; elevated ACTH cross-reacts with melanocortin-1 receptors (MC1R) on melanocytes → increased melanin production → hyperpigmentation (especially in skin creases, buccal mucosa, scars)
- (c) Hyponatremia (aldosterone deficiency → Na⁺ wasting); hyperkalemia (aldosterone deficiency → K⁺ retention); hypoglycemia (cortisol deficiency → impaired gluconeogenesis)

---

### Case 16 — Cushing's Syndrome *(UCR Highlander Invitational)*
A 59-year-old obese man presents with facial flushing, purple abdominal striae, and a buffalo hump. He frequently runs out of breath and has poor appetite. He is a current smoker with a 30 pack-year history.

**(a)** What disease does he have? *(1 pt)*

**(b)** Identify 6 symptoms from the passage that support your diagnosis and explain why each occurs. *(8 pts)*

**(c)** List 3 additional symptoms he may have that were not observed. *(2 pts)*

**Answer Key:**
- (a) Cushing's syndrome (likely ectopic ACTH from small cell lung cancer given smoking history)
- (b) (1) Obesity/weight gain — cortisol promotes lipogenesis and fat redistribution; (2) Buffalo hump — fat deposits between shoulders from cortisol-driven central adiposity; (3) Purple abdominal striae — thin skin from cortisol-driven protein catabolism, skin tears as adipose expands; (4) Facial flushing — increased vascular sensitivity and polycythemia; (5) Shortness of breath — lung mass (SCLC) compressing airway/mediastinum; (6) Poor appetite — possible from metabolic disturbances or primary lung pathology
- (c) Hypertension (cortisol promotes Na⁺ retention); hyperglycemia/insulin resistance (cortisol opposes insulin); osteoporosis (cortisol inhibits osteoblasts); muscle wasting/proximal myopathy; mood changes/depression; easy bruising; immunosuppression/frequent infections

---

### Case 17 — PCOS *(Purdue Invitational — Tiebreaker)*
A 17-year-old presents with irregular periods (skipping 3–6 months), heavy periods, new acne, coarse hair growth along the jawline and abdomen, and 12 lb weight gain over one year. Mother had fertility problems in her twenties.

| Parameter | Normal | Patient |
|---|---|---|
| LH | 2–12 mIU/mL | 20 mIU/mL |
| FSH | 3–10 mIU/mL | 5 mIU/mL |
| LH:FSH Ratio | ~1:1 | ~4:1 |
| Estradiol | 50–250 pg/mL | 60 pg/mL |
| Progesterone | 3–20 ng/mL | 1 ng/mL |
| Total Testosterone | 15–70 ng/dL | 110 ng/dL |
| SHBG | 30–100 nmol/L | 25 nmol/L |
| Fasting Insulin | 2–20 μU/mL | 30 μU/mL |
| Fasting Glucose | 70–100 mg/dL | 128 mg/dL |
| Triglycerides | <150 mg/dL | 175 mg/dL |
| HDL | >50 mg/dL | 42 mg/dL |

**(a)** What is the most appropriate endocrine disorder? *(2 pts)*

**(b)** Which gland is primarily impacted? *(2 pts)*

**(c)** Explain the mechanistic underpinning connecting the hormonal and metabolic findings. *(5 pts)*

**(d)** List at least 4 key symptoms of this disorder. *(4 pts)*

**(e)** What would ultrasound of the most impacted organ likely reveal? *(3 pts)*

**(f)** List two long-term conditions whose risk is greatly increased by this disorder. *(2 pts)*

**Answer Key:**
- (a) Polycystic Ovary Syndrome (PCOS)
- (b) Ovaries (with significant contribution from the pituitary and adrenal glands)
- (c) Elevated LH relative to FSH (LH:FSH ~4:1) → stimulates theca cells to overproduce androgens (↑ testosterone) → excess androgens cannot be fully aromatized to estrogen → anovulation and irregular cycles; low SHBG (driven by hyperinsulinemia) → more free testosterone → worsens androgenic symptoms; insulin resistance → hyperinsulinemia → further stimulates ovarian androgen production and suppresses hepatic SHBG synthesis; low progesterone confirms anovulation; dyslipidemia and pre-diabetes are metabolic manifestations of insulin resistance
- (d) Oligomenorrhea/amenorrhea, hirsutism (coarse facial/body hair), acne, weight gain, infertility, acanthosis nigricans
- (e) Bilateral enlarged ovaries with ≥12 follicles per ovary measuring 2–9 mm in diameter (polycystic appearance), often with increased ovarian stromal volume
- (f) Type 2 diabetes mellitus; cardiovascular disease; endometrial cancer (from unopposed estrogen due to anovulation); metabolic syndrome

# Division B State-Level Case Studies
## Extracted from 2026 Invitational Tests
*All cases require state-scope mechanistic reasoning. Answer keys included.*

---

## NERVOUS SYSTEM CASES

### Case 1 — ALS *(SOAPS Invitational)*
A 50-year-old man presents to his primary care physician reporting difficulty with fine hand movements, twitching in his arms, weakness in his ankles, a propensity for tripping and falling, and some unexpected changes to his emotional state. He has had no issues with bladder control, sensory degeneration, and does not report pain associated with muscle weakness. He has a history of regular smoking and a career in military service.

**(a)** What disease does this patient have? *(2 pts)*

**(b)** Describe the physiological effects of this disease. *(4 pts)*

**(c)** List two treatment options. *(2 pts)*

> **Answer key:** (a) ALS (Amyotrophic Lateral Sclerosis) — (b) Progressive degeneration of both UMN and LMN: UMN loss → spasticity, hyperreflexia, Babinski sign; LMN loss → fasciculations, muscle atrophy, flaccid weakness; bulbar involvement → dysphagia, dysarthria; sensory and bladder function preserved — (c) Riluzole (glutamate antagonist, slows progression); Edaravone (antioxidant); supportive/palliative care (ventilatory support, PEG tube)

---

### Case 2 — ALS with UMN/LMN Classification *(UCR Highlander Invitational)*
A 61-year-old male speaks slowly and reports left-sided hand weakness, difficulty walking, and tongue fasciculations. On exam: left forearm atrophy, depressed left arm reflexes, right leg hypertonia. His doctor mentions a positive "Ba-something" sign.

**(a)** What disease does he have? *(1 pt)*

**(b)** Name all relevant symptoms from the passage. Classify each as a UMN or LMN sign. *(4 pts — all or nothing)*

**(c)** What is the "positive Ba-something sign"? *(2 pts)*

> **Answer key:** (a) ALS — (b) Tongue fasciculations (LMN), left forearm atrophy (LMN), depressed left arm reflexes (LMN), slow/dysarthric speech (bulbar LMN), right leg hypertonia (UMN), positive Babinski sign (UMN) — (c) Babinski sign: plantar stimulation causes dorsiflexion of the big toe and fanning of the other toes; indicates UMN lesion (loss of descending inhibition on the reflex arc)

---

### Case 3 — Multiple Sclerosis *(UCR Highlander Invitational)*
Manasvi, a 21-year-old female, just moved from Norway (8 years of low sunlight) to California. She develops dizziness, vertigo, coordination problems, and blurry vision over several days. She experiences partial or complete vision loss that she attributes to the bright California sun. She eventually sees a doctor.

**(a)** What disease does she have? *(2 pts)*

**(b)** Name three risk factors from the passage that may have contributed to this diagnosis. *(3 pts)*

**(c)** What type of MS is depicted in the passage? *(2 pts)*

**(d)** Why does Manasvi have so many symptoms involving her eyes? *(3 pts)*

**(e)** Name specific signs of MS not necessarily included in the passage. *(4 pts)*

> **Answer key:** (a) Multiple Sclerosis — (b) Low sunlight exposure/vitamin D deficiency (Norway), female sex (implied), significant environmental/immune trigger from relocation — (c) Relapsing-remitting MS (RRMS) — (d) The optic nerve is a common early target of demyelination (optic neuritis); the medial longitudinal fasciculus (MLF) is also frequently involved, causing internuclear ophthalmoplegia; both produce visual symptoms — (e) Lhermitte's sign (electric shock sensation on neck flexion), Uhthoff's phenomenon (symptom worsening with heat), bladder dysfunction, spasticity, cognitive changes, diplopia

---

EEG confirmed epilepsy with activity in both hemispheres.

**(a)** Which type of seizure did Annalisa experience? Be specific. *(1 pt)*

**(b)** Describe one symptom common to this seizure type that Annalisa did NOT experience. *(1 pt)*

**(c)** How does a seizure appear on an EEG? What are the general EEG characteristics that define a seizure? *(3 pts)*
