## ENDOCRINE CASES

### Case 10 — Graves' Disease *(Purdue Invitational)*

| Parameter | Normal Range | Patient Result |
|---|---|---|
| T3 | 80–200 ng/dL | 245 ng/dL |
| T4 | 5–12 μg/dL | 15 μg/dL |
| TSH | 0.5–5 μU/mL | 0.1 μU/mL |
| Anti-TSH Receptor Antibodies | (−) | (+) |

**(a)** Identify the most appropriate disorder. *(1 pt)*

**(b)** Which gland is primarily impacted? *(2 pts)*

**(c)** Explain the mechanistic underpinning. *(3 pts)*

**(d)** List at least 2 symptoms the patient is likely experiencing. *(2 pts)*

> **Answer key:** (a) Graves' disease — (b) Thyroid gland — (c) Autoantibodies against TSH receptors (thyroid-stimulating immunoglobulins) mimic TSH → constitutive activation of thyroid follicular cells → excess T3/T4 production → negative feedback suppresses pituitary TSH release → low TSH despite high thyroid hormone levels — (d) Exophthalmos (proptosis), tachycardia, weight loss despite normal/increased appetite, heat intolerance, tremor, anxiety, pretibial myxedema

---

### Case 11 — Primary Hyperparathyroidism *(Purdue Invitational)*

| Parameter | Normal Range | Patient Result |
|---|---|---|
| Serum Ca²⁺ | 8.5–10.5 mg/dL | 12.6 mg/dL |
| PTH | 15–65 pg/mL | 150 pg/mL |
| Calcitonin | 0–10 pg/mL | 3 pg/mL |
| Urinary Ca²⁺ Excretion | 100–250 mg/day | 400 mg/day |

**(a)** Identify the most appropriate disorder. *(1 pt)*

**(b)** Which gland is primarily impacted? *(2 pts)*

**(c)** Explain the mechanistic underpinning. *(3 pts)*

**(d)** List at least 2 symptoms the patient is likely experiencing. *(2 pts)*

> **Answer key:** (a) Primary hyperparathyroidism — (b) Parathyroid glands (usually a single adenoma) — (c) Autonomous PTH oversecretion → increased osteoclast activity (bone resorption → hypercalcemia) + increased renal Ca²⁺ reabsorption + increased calcitriol synthesis (→ increased intestinal Ca²⁺ absorption); urinary Ca²⁺ rises because filtered load overwhelms reabsorption capacity — (d) "Bones, stones, groans, moans": bone pain/fractures, kidney stones (hypercalciuria), constipation/nausea, fatigue/depression, polyuria

---

### Case 12 — Type 1 Diabetes Mellitus *(Purdue Invitational)*

| Parameter | Normal Range | Patient Result |
|---|---|---|
| Blood Glucose | 70–100 mg/dL | 290 mg/dL |
| Insulin | 5–15 μU/mL | 2 μU/mL |
| Glucagon | 50–100 pg/mL | 160 pg/mL |
| Urine Glucose | (−) | (+) |
| Serum Ketones | (−) | (+) |

**(a)** Identify the most appropriate disorder. *(1 pt)*

**(b)** Which gland is primarily impacted? *(2 pts)*

**(c)** Explain the mechanistic underpinning. *(3 pts)*

**(d)** List at least 2 symptoms the patient is likely experiencing. *(2 pts)*

> **Answer key:** (a) Type 1 Diabetes Mellitus — (b) Pancreas (islets of Langerhans, specifically β-cells) — (c) Autoimmune destruction of β-cells → absent insulin → cells cannot take up glucose (hyperglycemia) → glucose exceeds renal threshold → glucosuria + osmotic diuresis; unopposed glucagon → gluconeogenesis + lipolysis → ketone body production → ketonemia/ketonuria; risk of diabetic ketoacidosis (DKA) — (d) Polyuria, polydipsia, polyphagia, weight loss, fatigue, fruity breath (ketones)

---

### Case 13 — Conn's Syndrome / Primary Hyperaldosteronism *(Purdue Invitational)*

| Parameter | Normal Range | Patient Result |
|---|---|---|
| Serum Na⁺ | 135–145 mEq/L | 149 mEq/L |
| Serum K⁺ | 3.5–5.0 mEq/L | 2.8 mEq/L |
| Blood Pressure | 110/70 mmHg | 162/98 mmHg |
| Plasma Renin Activity | 1.0–3.0 ng/mL/hr | 0.2 ng/mL/hr |
| Plasma Aldosterone | 4–15 ng/dL | 44 ng/dL |

**(a)** Identify the most appropriate disorder. *(1 pt)*

**(b)** Which gland is primarily impacted? *(2 pts)*

**(c)** Explain the mechanistic underpinning. *(3 pts)*

**(d)** List at least 2 symptoms the patient is likely experiencing. *(2 pts)*

> **Answer key:** (a) Primary hyperaldosteronism (Conn's syndrome) — (b) Adrenal cortex, zona glomerulosa — (c) Autonomous aldosterone hypersecretion → ENaC upregulation in collecting duct → Na⁺ and water retention (hypertension, hypernatremia) → K⁺ wasting (hypokalemia) → volume expansion suppresses renin via negative feedback (low PRA distinguishes primary from secondary hyperaldosteronism) — (d) Hypertension (often resistant), muscle weakness/cramps from hypokalemia, polyuria, headache, metabolic alkalosis

---

### Case 14 — Hashimoto's Thyroiditis with Hashitoxicosis *(UMSO Invitational)*
A 40-year-old woman presents with weight loss, palpitations, tremors, nervousness, and heat intolerance over 2 months. Diffuse firm thyroid enlargement, HR 112 bpm. Labs: TSH <0.01 μIU/mL, free T4 2.1 ng/dL (elevated), free T3 5.8 pg/mL (elevated). No orbitopathy.

Six months later she returns with fatigue, cold intolerance, constipation, weight gain (5–7 kg), dry skin, hair thinning, HR 58 bpm, thyroid still enlarged. Labs: TSH 12 μIU/mL (elevated), free T4 0.6 ng/dL (low), positive anti-TPO and anti-Tg antibodies. Biopsy and ultrasound performed.

**(a)** List key signs/symptoms from the initial visit suggesting a thyroid disorder. How do these relate to thyroid hormone activity? *(4 pts)*

**(b)** Based on the initial presentation, hyperthyroidism or hypothyroidism? Give 2 reasons from lab results and 2 from signs/symptoms. *(5 pts)*

**(c)** Given the follow-up symptoms, labs, and biopsy results, what is the most likely diagnosis? Explain how all findings relate. *(6 pts)*

**(d)** Explain a physiological mechanism relevant to the diagnosis that accounts for the earlier hyperthyroid presentation. *(4 pts)*

> **Answer key:** (a) Tachycardia, tremors, heat intolerance, weight loss — thyroid hormones increase basal metabolic rate, upregulate β-adrenergic receptors (→ tachycardia, tremor), increase thermogenesis (→ heat intolerance), and stimulate catabolism (→ weight loss) — (b) Hyperthyroidism: Lab — (1) TSH suppressed below detectable levels (high T3/T4 inhibits pituitary); (2) free T4 and T3 both elevated; Clinical — (1) tachycardia; (2) heat intolerance and weight loss — (c) Hashimoto's thyroiditis: autoimmune destruction of thyroid tissue (anti-TPO and anti-Tg antibodies confirm); lymphocytic infiltration on biopsy; hypoechoic heterogeneous appearance on ultrasound; progressive loss of functional tissue → hypothyroidism (high TSH, low T4) — (d) Hashitoxicosis: early inflammatory destruction of thyroid follicles releases preformed stored thyroid hormone into circulation → transient hyperthyroid phase. As destruction continues and hormone stores are depleted, hypothyroidism develops. This is distinct from Graves' disease (no TSH receptor antibodies, no orbitopathy)

---

### Case 15 — Addison's Disease (Acute Crisis Precipitated by Infection) *(UMSO Invitational)*
A 30-year-old man is seen for flu-like symptoms and sent home. Days later he returns to the ED with severe fatigue, lightheadedness, muscle weakness, nausea, and hyperpigmentation in certain areas. Addison's disease is suspected.

**(a)** Why is this case progression (subclinical until an acute stressor) common for Addison's disease patients? *(Select the most accurate and thorough explanation)*
- a. **Autoimmune destruction slowly depletes adrenal cortex over time; symptoms remain subclinical until a physiological stressor (infection) increases cortisol demand beyond reserve capacity** ✓
- b. The flu virus directly destroys adrenal tissue
- c. Addison's is caused by a virus with long latency
- d. The PCP misdiagnosed; the patient was actually experiencing Addison's onset all along

**(b)** Explain the mechanism behind the hyperpigmentation. *(3 pts)*

**(c)** What electrolyte abnormalities would you expect and why? *(3 pts)*

> **Answer key:** (b) Low cortisol removes negative feedback on the pituitary/hypothalamus → markedly elevated ACTH; ACTH is cleaved from POMC (proopiomelanocortin), the same precursor as α-MSH; elevated ACTH cross-reacts with melanocortin-1 receptors (MC1R) on melanocytes → increased melanin production → hyperpigmentation (especially in skin creases, buccal mucosa, scars) — (c) Hyponatremia (aldosterone deficiency → Na⁺ wasting); hyperkalemia (aldosterone deficiency → K⁺ retention); hypoglycemia (cortisol deficiency → impaired gluconeogenesis)

---

### Case 16 — Cushing's Syndrome *(UCR Highlander Invitational)*
A 59-year-old obese man presents with facial flushing, purple abdominal striae, and a buffalo hump. He frequently runs out of breath and has poor appetite. He is a current smoker with a 30 pack-year history.

**(a)** What disease does he have? *(1 pt)*

**(b)** Identify 6 symptoms from the passage that support your diagnosis and explain why each occurs. *(8 pts)*

**(c)** List 3 additional symptoms he may have that were not observed. *(2 pts)*

> **Answer key:** (a) Cushing's syndrome (likely ectopic ACTH from small cell lung cancer given smoking history) — (b) (1) Obesity/weight gain — cortisol promotes lipogenesis and fat redistribution; (2) Buffalo hump — fat deposits between shoulders from cortisol-driven central adiposity; (3) Purple abdominal striae — thin skin from cortisol-driven protein catabolism, skin tears as adipose expands; (4) Facial flushing — increased vascular sensitivity and polycythemia; (5) Shortness of breath — lung mass (SCLC) compressing airway/mediastinum; (6) Poor appetite — less consistent but possible from metabolic disturbances or primary lung pathology — (c) Hypertension (cortisol promotes Na⁺ retention); hyperglycemia/insulin resistance (cortisol opposes insulin); osteoporosis (cortisol inhibits osteoblasts); muscle wasting/proximal myopathy; mood changes/depression; easy bruising; immunosuppression/frequent infections

---

### Case 17 — PCOS *(Purdue Invitational — Tiebreaker)*
A 17-year-old presents with irregular periods (skipping 3–6 months), heavy periods, new acne, coarse hair growth along the jawline and abdomen, and 12 lb weight gain over one year. Mother had fertility problems in her twenties.

| Parameter | Normal | Patient |
|---|---|---|
| LH | 2–12 mIU/mL | 20 mIU/mL |
| FSH | 3–10 mIU/mL | 5 mIU/mL |
| LH:FSH Ratio | ~1:1 | ~4:1 |
| Estradiol | 50–250 pg/mL | 60 pg/mL |
| Progesterone | 3–20 ng/mL | 1 ng/mL |
| Total Testosterone | 15–70 ng/dL | 110 ng/dL |
| SHBG | 30–100 nmol/L | 25 nmol/L |
| Fasting Insulin | 2–20 μU/mL | 30 μU/mL |
| Fasting Glucose | 70–100 mg/dL | 128 mg/dL |
| Triglycerides | <150 mg/dL | 175 mg/dL |
| HDL | >50 mg/dL | 42 mg/dL |

**(a)** What is the most appropriate endocrine disorder? *(2 pts)*

**(b)** Which gland is primarily impacted? *(2 pts)*

**(c)** Explain the mechanistic underpinning connecting the hormonal and metabolic findings. *(5 pts)*

**(d)** List at least 4 key symptoms of this disorder. *(4 pts)*

**(e)** What would ultrasound of the most impacted organ likely reveal? *(3 pts)*

**(f)** List two long-term conditions whose risk is greatly increased by this disorder. *(2 pts)*

> **Answer key:** (a) Polycystic Ovary Syndrome (PCOS) — (b) Ovaries (with significant contribution from the pituitary and adrenal glands) — (c) Elevated LH relative to FSH (LH:FSH ~4:1) → stimulates theca cells to overproduce androgens (↑ testosterone) → excess androgens cannot be fully aromatized to estrogen → anovulation and irregular cycles; low SHBG (driven by hyperinsulinemia) → more free testosterone → worsens androgenic symptoms; insulin resistance → hyperinsulinemia → further stimulates ovarian androgen production and suppresses hepatic SHBG synthesis; low progesterone confirms anovulation; dyslipidemia and pre-diabetes are metabolic manifestations of insulin resistance — (d) Oligomenorrhea/amenorrhea, hirsutism (coarse facial/body hair), acne, weight gain, infertility, acanthosis nigricans — (e) Bilateral enlarged ovaries with ≥12 follicles per ovary measuring 2–9 mm in diameter (polycystic appearance), often with increased ovarian stromal volume — (f) Type 2 diabetes mellitus; cardiovascular disease; endometrial cancer (from unopposed estrogen due to anovulation); metabolic syndrome

# Division B State-Level Exam Questions
## Extracted from 2026 Invitational Tests
*Filtered against the official 2026 Science Olympiad **Division B** syllabus — State & National scope only*
*State/National additions for Div B: brainstem/cerebellum nuclei & tracts, cranial nerve pathways, spinal cord ascending/descending tracts, general senses & proprioception, visual pathway defects, pupillary reflex pathway, Cushing's Syndrome, Addison's Disease*

---

## NERVOUS SYSTEM — STATE SCOPE

### Cranial Nerves & Brainstem Nuclei

**[UCR Q1]** Which nucleus in the medulla receives visceral sensory input from cranial nerves VII, IX, and X?
- a. Nucleus ambiguus
- b. Dorsal motor nucleus
- c. Inferior olivary nucleus
- d. **Solitary nucleus (NTS)** ✓
- e. Sensorinucleus

---

**[UCR Q7 — Select all]** Select the correct pairings of neurological lesions and their resulting effects:
- a. Substantia nigra — rigidity ✓
- b. Hippocampus — inability to form new long-term memories ✓
- c. Putamen — chorea ✓
- d. Cerebellum — loss of fine motor coordination and balance ✓
- e. Primary motor cortex — loss of sensation on the contralateral side ✗ *(motor cortex → motor loss, not sensory)*
- f. Subthalamus — flailing movements of entire limb (hemiballismus) ✓
- g. Wernicke's area — non-fluent, effortful speech ✗ *(Broca's = non-fluent; Wernicke's = fluent but nonsensical)*
- h. Medulla oblongata — loss of voluntary skeletal muscle movement ✗ *(medulla controls vital autonomic functions, not voluntary movement directly)*

**Correct: a, b, c, d, f**

---

**[UCR Q18]** What are the afferent and efferent nerve fibers for the pupillary light reflex?
- **Afferent:** CN II (optic nerve)
- **Efferent:** CN III (oculomotor nerve) — via Edinger-Westphal nucleus → ciliary ganglion → sphincter pupillae

---

**[SOAPS Q]** Which cranial nerve aids in digestion, blood pressure regulation, and other autonomic functions?
- a. CN III
- b. CN VIII
- c. **CN X (Vagus)** ✓
- d. CN XII

---

**[SOAPS Q]** A student touches their eyelid and the eye blinks (corneal reflex). Which cranial nerve carries the sensory signal?
- a. CN VII
- b. CN IX
- c. CN III
- d. **CN V** ✓ *(afferent = CN V1; efferent = CN VII)*

---

### Spinal Cord Tracts & General Senses

**[SOAPS Q]** Describe the correct pathway for a somatic reflex arc:
- a. Somatic receptor → efferent fibers → integrating center → afferent fibers → effector muscle
- b. **Somatic receptor → afferent nerve fibers → integrating center → efferent nerve fibers → effector muscle** ✓
- c. Effector muscle → efferent fibers → integrating center → afferent fibers → somatic receptor
- d. Effector muscle → afferent fibers → integrating center → efferent fibers → somatic receptor

---

**[UCR Q16]** What is the difference between Aδ and C nerve fibers? *(3 pts)*

> **Answer key:** Aδ fibers — myelinated, medium diameter, fast conduction (~5–30 m/s), carry sharp/acute pain and temperature; C fibers — unmyelinated, small diameter, slow conduction (<2 m/s), carry dull/chronic pain, temperature, itch, and some autonomic signals

---

**[UCR Q13]** During the stretch reflex, inhibition of the antagonist muscle occurs primarily via:
- a. Direct inhibition by the Ia afferent fiber
- b. Recurrent inhibition through Renshaw cells
- c. **Reciprocal inhibition via inhibitory interneurons** ✓
- d. Golgi tendon organ activation
- e. Descending corticospinal tract input

---

**[UMSO Q17]** Neurons in primary somatosensory cortex (S1) are arranged somatotopically, with regions requiring fine tactile discrimination occupying disproportionately large cortical space. Which body region receives greatest cortical representation?
- a. Upper back
- b. Forearm
- c. Calf
- d. **Fingertips** ✓

---

**[UMSO Q16]** A cortical region in the superior temporal lobe receives processed auditory information and maps speech sounds onto stored lexical/semantic representations. Damage most likely results in:
- a. Nonfluent, effortful speech with preserved comprehension
- b. Intact comprehension but impaired repetition
- c. **Inability to attach semantic meaning to heard words (Wernicke's/receptive aphasia)** ✓
- d. Difficulty articulating speech due to impaired motor control

---

**[SOAPS Q]** Spasticity, as seen in cerebral palsy, is defined as:
- a. Lack of balance or muscle coordination
- b. **Stiff muscles with regular reflexive activity** ✓
- c. Stiff muscles with irregular reflexive activity
- d. Uncontrollable, jerky movements

---

**[Purdue Q10]** A person reflexively pulls their foot away after stepping on a nail, then consciously decides to look down and move. Which response occurs faster, and why? *(4 pts)*

> **Answer key:** The reflex withdrawal is faster. It travels only to the spinal cord (not the brain) via a polysynaptic reflex arc, bypassing cortical processing. The voluntary decision requires sensory information to ascend to the brain, be processed in the cortex, and then a motor command to descend — adding significant conduction and processing time.

---

### Disorders — State Scope

---

## SPECIAL SENSES — STATE SCOPE

### Visual Pathway & Field Defects

**[UMSO Q14]** Which visual field defect results from damage to the left optic nerve?
> **Answer:** Left monocular blindness (complete loss of vision in the left eye only)

---

**[UMSO Q15]** Which visual field defect results from severing the nerve fibers that decussate at the optic chiasm?
> **Answer:** Bitemporal hemianopia (loss of both temporal/peripheral visual fields)

---

**[UMSO Q16]** Which visual field defect results from lesioning both banks of the calcarine fissure in the right primary visual cortex (V1)?
> **Answer:** Left homonymous hemianopia (loss of the left visual field in both eyes), with possible macular sparing

---

**[SOAPS Q]** Which option correctly describes the pathway of visual information?
- a. Retina → Optic chiasm → Optic nerve → LGN → Visual cortex
- b. **Retina → Optic nerve → Optic chiasm → Optic tract → LGN → Visual cortex** ✓
- c. Retina → LGN → Optic nerve → Optic tract → Visual cortex
- d. Retina → Optic tract → Optic chiasm → Visual cortex → LGN

---

**[UMSO Q4 — Special Senses]** How is visual information from the two eyes kept separate in the LGN?
- a. Both eyes' information is mixed in all six layers
- b. **The LGN contains six layers, three receiving input from each eye** ✓
- c. Cells receive input from one eye but are not laminar
- d. Right LGN receives only from left eye; left LGN only from right eye

---

**[UMSO Q15 — Special Senses]** How does the extrastriate cortex (V2–V5) differ from V1?
- a. V1 responds to all visual input; extrastriate responds only to cones
- b. **V1 processes simple features (orientation, direction); extrastriate regions process complex input like objects, color, and motion** ✓
- c. V1 sends output to extrastriate but receives none back
- d. V1 separates "where" and "what" pathways; extrastriate does not

---

**[UMSO Q16 — Special Senses]** Photon absorption activates transducin → PDE6 → lowers cGMP. How does this alter the photoreceptor's electrical state?
- a. Depolarization due to increased Na⁺ entry through cGMP-gated channels
- b. Depolarization due to decreased Ca²⁺ extrusion
- c. **Hyperpolarization due to closure of cGMP-gated Na⁺/Ca²⁺ channels in the outer segment** ✓
- d. Hyperpolarization due to activation of voltage-gated Na⁺ channels

---

### Auditory & Vestibular

**[UMSO Q11 — Special Senses]** Stereocilia are immersed in high-K⁺ endolymph with a large positive endocochlear potential. Deflection toward the tallest cilium opens tip-link-gated channels. Which ionic event follows?
- a. K⁺ efflux → hyperpolarization
- b. **K⁺ influx driven by electrical and chemical gradients → graded depolarization** ✓
- c. Ca²⁺ efflux reducing neurotransmitter release
- d. Na⁺ influx through voltage-gated channels

---

**[UMSO Q13 — Special Senses]** A musician develops selective loss of sensitivity above 8 kHz. Which cochlear region is damaged, and why?
- a. Apex — wide, flexible membrane optimized for high frequencies
- b. **Base — narrow, stiff basilar membrane tuned for high frequencies** ✓
- c. Middle turn — transmits frequencies near the helicotrema
- d. Helicotrema — where pressure waves dissipate

---

**[UMSO Q14 — Special Senses]** A patient shows normal auditory nerve transmission at moderate/high intensities but markedly reduced sensitivity to soft sounds. Which structure is most likely impaired?
- a. Inner hair cells
- b. Type II spiral ganglion neurons
- c. Tectorial membrane
- d. **Outer hair cells** ✓ *(outer hair cells amplify soft sounds via electromotility; their loss selectively reduces sensitivity at low amplitudes)*

---

**[UMSO Q17 — Case: Auditory Processing]** A 10-year-old girl struggles with speech discrimination (especially in noise) but hears environmental sounds. Speech Awareness Threshold is mildly elevated, Speech Discrimination is 8% in the left ear, tympanometry is normal, OAE is normal, ABR is abnormal.

**(a)** What is your diagnosis? Provide 2 supporting examples from the passage. *(3 pts)*

**(b)** Why are Rinne, Weber, and OAE normal, but ABR abnormal? What does this indicate about the lesion location? *(4 pts)*

**(c)** Why can she hear environmental sounds but not speech? *(Tiebreaker, 3 pts)*

> **Answer key:** (a) Auditory Neuropathy Spectrum Disorder (ANSD) — outer hair cells intact (normal OAE) but neural synchrony disrupted; supported by: poor speech discrimination despite mild hearing loss, worse in noise, can identify sounds but not words — (b) Normal OAE = outer hair cells functioning; normal Rinne/Weber = no conductive or peripheral cochlear loss; abnormal ABR = dysfunction at the level of the auditory nerve or lower brainstem (neural dyssynchrony) — (c) Environmental sounds are often louder, lower frequency, and do not require precise temporal resolution; speech relies on rapid temporal fine structure that requires neural synchrony, which is disrupted in ANSD

---

**[SOAPS Q — Case: Ménière's]** A 32-year-old woman presents with episodic vertigo (5–8 min), nausea, tinnitus, and fluctuating left-ear hearing loss.

**(a)** What is the diagnosis?
**(b)** Describe the mechanism.
**(c)** What dietary modifications are recommended?

> **Answer key:** (a) Ménière's disease — (b) Endolymphatic hydrops: excess endolymph accumulation distorts the membranous labyrinth, disrupting cochlear and vestibular hair cell function — (c) Low-sodium diet to reduce endolymph production; limit caffeine and alcohol; maintain hydration

---

**[SOAPS Q — Case: Otosclerosis]** A 22-year-old has progressive left-ear hearing loss; father wore hearing aids at 25. Weber test lateralizes to the left.

**(a)** What is the diagnosis?
**(b)** What is the inheritance pattern?
**(c)** What test was performed and what does lateralization indicate?

> **Answer key:** (a) Otosclerosis (stapes fixation → conductive hearing loss) — (b) Autosomal dominant with incomplete penetrance — (c) Weber test; lateralization to the left = conductive loss on that side (or sensorineural loss on the opposite side)

---

### Olfaction & Gustation

**[UMSO Q17 — Special Senses]** Olfactory receptor neurons express GPCRs coupled to Golf protein. When an odorant binds, which downstream event initiates depolarization?
- a. Inhibition of adenylate cyclase III → decreased cAMP
- b. Opening of voltage-gated Na⁺ channels independent of second messengers
- c. **Activation of adenylate cyclase III → increased cAMP → CNG channel opening** ✓
- d. Activation of phospholipase C → IP3 → Ca²⁺ store release

---

**[UMSO Q18 — Special Senses]** Different odorants activate overlapping receptor sets, and each receptor responds to multiple odorants. Which principle allows discrimination of a vast number of odors?
- a. Each odorant binds a single unique receptor
- b. Each receptor detects only one specific odorant
- c. Discrimination depends solely on concentration
- d. **Odor identity is encoded by a combinatorial pattern of receptor activation** ✓

---

**[UMSO Q20 — Special Senses]** Salty and sour tastes are detected by ion channels rather than GPCRs. Which ion movements characterize transduction of these two modalities?
- a. Sour: Na⁺ influx; Salty: H⁺ blocks Ca²⁺ channels
- b. Salty: H⁺ influx; Sour: Na⁺ blocks K⁺ channels
- c. **Salty: Na⁺ influx; Sour: H⁺ blocks K⁺ channels** ✓
- d. Salty: Ca²⁺ influx; Sour: Cl⁻ efflux

---

**[UMSO Q3 — Special Senses]** COVID-19 patients reported foods tasting metallic or less sweet/salty after recovery. How would you classify this phenomenon?
- a. Anosmia
- b. Myopia
- c. Dysosmia
- d. **Dysgeusia** ✓

---

## ENDOCRINE — STATE SCOPE

### Hormone Signaling & Mechanisms

**[UMSO — Endocrine Q1]** You stain cells with a fluorescent indicator for an active steroid hormone-receptor complex acting as a transcription factor. Where would you see fluorescence?
- a. Cell membrane
- b. Cytoplasm
- c. **Nucleus** ✓
- d. All of the above

---

**[UMSO — Endocrine Q21]** Fill in the table for the three hormone classes. *(Tiebreaker, 6 pts)*

| Class | Blood transport | Receptor location | Speed | Example |
|---|---|---|---|---|
| Steroid | Bound to carrier protein | Intracellular (cytoplasm/nucleus) | Slow (hours) | Cortisol |
| Peptide | Dissolved in plasma | Cell surface (membrane receptor) | Fast (seconds–minutes) | Insulin |
| Amine | Varies: catecholamines dissolved; thyroid hormones protein-bound | Varies: catecholamines → membrane; thyroid → intracellular | Varies | Epinephrine / T3 |

---

**[Purdue — MCQ Q1]** Hormone X binds cytoplasmic receptors and changes transcription. Hormone Y activates a GPCR and cAMP signaling. Which classification is correct?
- a. X = Peptide; Y = Steroid
- b. **X = Steroid; Y = Peptide** ✓
- c. X = Amine; Y = Steroid
- d. X = Steroid; Y = Amine

---

**[Purdue — MCQ Q2]** Which hormone pair best demonstrates that chemical class does not always predict receptor location?
- a. Cortisol/Aldosterone
- b. Growth Hormone/Prolactin
- c. **Thyroxine/Epinephrine** ✓ *(both amines, but T4 acts on nuclear receptors while epinephrine acts on membrane GPCRs)*
- d. Insulin/Glucagon

---

**[UMSO — Endocrine Q7]** How do T4 and T3 differ?
- a. T3 has a shorter half-life than T4
- b. T4 has stronger metabolic effects than T3
- c. Blood concentration of T4 is far higher than T3
- d. **A and C** ✓ *(T3 is faster-acting/shorter half-life; T4 concentration in blood is ~50× higher than T3)*

---

**[UMSO — Endocrine Q8]** How does the posterior pituitary compare to the anterior pituitary?
- a. Posterior pituitary produces and secretes GH and prolactin
- b. Posterior pituitary produces oxytocin and ADH; anterior stores and releases them
- c. **Anterior pituitary produces and secretes GH, prolactin, and others; posterior stores and releases oxytocin and ADH** ✓
- d. Anterior produces oxytocin and ADH; posterior stores and releases GH and prolactin

---

**[Purdue — MCQ Q9]** In congenital iodine deficiency syndrome, severe neurodevelopmental delay occurs. Which mechanism best explains this?
- a. **Decreased thyroid hormone reduces neuronal protein synthesis and myelination** ✓
- b. Lack of thyroid hormone increases calcitonin, disrupting neuronal metabolism
- c. Excess TSH overstimulates thyroid → toxic T4 levels
- d. Low iodine damages neuronal mitochondria

---

### Endocrine Cycles & Negative Feedback

**[Purdue — MCQ Q7]** A biochemist detects elevated ACTH and cortisol despite low CRH in a patient with a pituitary tumor. What dysfunction is most likely?
- a. Secondary Hyposecretion
- b. Primary Hypersecretion
- c. **Secondary Hypersecretion** ✓ *(pituitary tumor autonomously secretes excess ACTH, bypassing hypothalamic CRH)*
- d. Primary Hyposecretion

---

**[UMSO — Endocrine Q3]** Which physiological abnormality is associated with Cushing's Syndrome regarding glucocorticoid secretion?
- a. Stress decreases plasma cortisol in Cushing's
- b. **The negative feedback loop is defective — cortisol cannot suppress CRH and ACTH** ✓
- c. Cortisol is higher in the evening than morning in Cushing's (opposite of normal)
- d. Cortisol levels fluctuate greatly throughout the day vs. being stable in healthy patients

---

**[UMSO — Endocrine Q11]** A 42-year-old presents with hyperpigmentation, orthostatic hypotension, weight loss, hyponatremia, elevated ACTH, elevated CRH, low cortisol, and low aldosterone. Which molecular mechanism explains the hyperpigmentation?
- a. Upregulation of tyrosinase by chronic cortisol deficiency
- b. **Excessive ACTH from POMC cleavage increases α-MSH activity at melanocortin receptors on melanocytes** ✓
- c. CRH directly stimulates melanocyte MC1R receptors
- d. Elevated renin causes secondary increases in melanin synthesis

---

**[UMSO — Endocrine Q15 — Case]** A 30-year-old man with confirmed influenza develops severe fatigue, lightheadedness, muscle weakness, nausea, and hyperpigmentation. Addison's disease is suspected. Why is this case progression common?
- a. **The most common cause (autoimmune destruction) slowly destroys adrenal cortex; symptoms remain subclinical until the body faces a stressor like infection that increases cortisol demand** ✓
- b. The flu virus directly destroys adrenal tissue
- c. Addison's is caused by a virus with long latency
- d. The PCP misdiagnosed the patient; he was already experiencing Addison's onset

---

### Endocrine Case Studies

**[UMSO — Case Q22/23: Thyroid]** A 40-year-old woman presents with weight loss, palpitations, tremors, heat intolerance, diffuse thyroid enlargement, heart rate 112 bpm, TSH <0.01 μIU/mL, free T4 2.1 ng/dL (elevated), free T3 5.8 pg/mL (elevated).

**(a)** List key signs/symptoms suggesting thyroid disorder and relate to thyroid hormone activity. *(4 pts)*

**(b)** Hyperthyroidism or hypothyroidism? Cite 2 lab and 2 clinical reasons. *(5 pts)*

Six months later she returns with fatigue, cold intolerance, constipation, weight gain, dry skin, hair thinning, HR 58 bpm, TSH 12 μIU/mL (elevated), T4 0.6 ng/dL (low), positive anti-TPO and anti-Tg antibodies.

**(c)** Most likely diagnosis, explaining how symptoms, labs, and biopsy relate. *(6 pts)*

**(d)** Explain a physiological mechanism relevant to the diagnosis that accounts for her earlier hyperthyroid presentation. *(4 pts)*

> **Answer key:** (a) Tachycardia, tremors, heat intolerance, weight loss — all caused by thyroid hormone's role in increasing basal metabolic rate and upregulating β-adrenergic receptors — (b) Hyperthyroidism: low TSH (gland autonomous, suppresses pituitary), high T3/T4; clinically: tachycardia, heat intolerance, weight loss, tremors — (c) Hashimoto's thyroiditis — autoimmune destruction of thyroid (anti-TPO/Tg antibodies), lymphocytic infiltration on biopsy → progressive hypothyroidism — (d) Hashitoxicosis: early inflammatory destruction of follicles releases preformed thyroid hormone → transient hyperthyroid phase before hypothyroidism develops

---

**[Purdue — Lab Q1: Graves' Disease]**

| Parameter | Normal | Patient |
|---|---|---|
| T3 | 80–200 ng/dL | 245 ng/dL |
| T4 | 5–12 μg/dL | 15 μg/dL |
| TSH | 0.5–5 μU/mL | 0.1 μU/mL |
| Anti-TSH Receptor Ab | (−) | (+) |

**(a)** Identify the disorder.
**(b)** Which gland is primarily impacted?
**(c)** Explain the mechanistic underpinning.
**(d)** List at least 2 symptoms the patient is likely experiencing.

> **Answer key:** (a) Graves' disease — (b) Thyroid gland — (c) Autoantibodies against TSH receptors mimic TSH → constitutive stimulation of thyroid → excess T3/T4 production → negative feedback suppresses TSH — (d) Exophthalmos, tachycardia, weight loss, heat intolerance, tremor, anxiety

---

**[Purdue — Lab Q4: Conn's Syndrome]**

| Parameter | Normal | Patient |
|---|---|---|
| Serum Na⁺ | 135–145 mEq/L | 149 mEq/L |
| Serum K⁺ | 3.5–5.0 mEq/L | 2.8 mEq/L |
| Blood Pressure | 110/70 | 162/98 |
| Plasma Renin | 1.0–3.0 ng/mL/hr | 0.2 ng/mL/hr |
| Plasma Aldosterone | 4–15 ng/dL | 44 ng/dL |

**(a)** Identify the disorder.
**(b)** Which gland is primarily impacted?
**(c)** Explain the mechanistic underpinning.
**(d)** List at least 2 symptoms.

> **Answer key:** (a) Primary hyperaldosteronism (Conn's syndrome) — (b) Adrenal cortex (zona glomerulosa) — (c) Autonomous aldosterone excess → Na⁺/water retention (hypertension, hypernatremia) → K⁺ wasting (hypokalemia) → suppresses renin via negative feedback (volume expansion) — (d) Hypertension, muscle weakness/cramps (hypokalemia), polyuria, headache

---

**[UMSO — Endocrine Q19]** A patient with autoimmune destruction limited to the zona glomerulosa presents with hyperkalemia and low serum sodium, but normal cortisol and androgen levels. Which finding best explains the electrolyte abnormalities?
- a. Impaired cortisol-mediated sodium retention
- b. Excess ACTH production
- c. **Loss of aldosterone-sensitive Na⁺/K⁺ exchange in the distal nephron** ✓
- d. Compensatory adrenal androgen overproduction

---

**[Purdue — MCQ Q16]** Low level of which hormone would most directly cause low blood pressure and high serum potassium?
- a. Cortisol
- b. Epinephrine
- c. **Aldosterone** ✓
- d. Thyroxine

---

**[UMSO — Endocrine Q12]** A patient with low serum calcium has elevated PTH. Which combination of tissue responses is most consistent with PTH action?
- a. Decreased Ca²⁺ reabsorption in kidney and decreased calcitriol
- b. Increased Ca²⁺ reabsorption in kidney and increased osteoblast activity
- c. **Increased Ca²⁺ reabsorption in kidney, increased osteoclast activity, and increased calcitriol production** ✓
- d. Increased osteoclast activity but decreased intestinal Ca²⁺ absorption

---

**[Purdue — MCQ Q10]** What lab value distinguishes secondary from primary hyperparathyroidism?
- a. In primary, serum PTH is low; in secondary it is high
- b. **In primary, serum Ca²⁺ is elevated; in secondary it is low or normal** ✓
- c. In primary, calcitonin is low; in secondary it is high
- d. In primary, serum Ca²⁺ is low; in secondary it is elevated

---

**[UMSO — Endocrine Q16]** A patient with heat intolerance, tachycardia, and weight loss has low TSH, high T3/T4, and positive TSH-receptor antibodies. Which mechanism explains these findings?
- a. Excess pituitary TSH secretion
- b. **Autoantibody activation of the TSH receptor increases thyroid hormone production and suppresses TSH via negative feedback** ✓
- c. Release of preformed thyroid hormone due to gland destruction
- d. Peripheral conversion of T4 to T3 increases TSH

---

**[Purdue — MCQ Q15]** Which symptom distinguishes Hashimoto's thyroiditis from simple iodine deficiency?
- a. Goiter
- b. Cold intolerance
- c. Fatigue
- d. Puffy skin
- e. **Circulating autoantibodies (anti-TPO, anti-Tg)** ✓

---

**[UMSO — Endocrine Q17]** Which finding best distinguishes central from nephrogenic diabetes insipidus?
- a. High plasma osmolarity
- b. Low urine osmolarity
- c. **Low circulating ADH levels** ✓ *(central DI = ADH deficiency; nephrogenic DI = ADH present but kidney unresponsive)*
- d. Excessive water loss through sweat glands

# Science Olympiad 2026 Division B — Anatomy & Physiology
## State-Level Question Bank
*Nervous System · Special Senses · Endocrine*
*Compiled from: 2026 Invitationals + Practice Exams + State-Level Supplement*
*Tier tags: [Regional] [State] [State+] (hardest within state scope)*

---

## VISUAL PATHWAYS & PHOTOTRANSDUCTION

**[Regional]** List the following structures in the order light passes through them: lens, cornea, retina, pupil, optic nerve

**[Regional]** Which of the following is true of vision and phototransduction? Select all that apply.
- a. Damage to the optic chiasm will cause bitemporal hemianopia ✓
- b. GMP is converted to cGMP by phosphodiesterase ✗ *(phosphodiesterase hydrolyzes cGMP → GMP; guanylyl cyclase synthesizes cGMP)*
- c. Transducin is a G-protein ✓
- d. Na⁺ channels open when rhodopsin's retinal is converted to its all-trans form ✗ *(channels CLOSE)*
- e. Vitamin A is converted into retinal for use in rhodopsins ✓
**Correct answers: a, c, e**

**[State]** Michelle cuts the fibers of Larry's left nasal optic nerve before the optic chiasm. What visual field defect results?
- a. Loss of vision in left visual field in both eyes
- b. Loss of vision in right visual field in both eyes
- c. Loss of vision in left visual field in left eye
- d. Loss of vision in right visual field in left eye ✓
- e. Loss of temporal vision in both eyes
*(Nasal retinal fibers carry temporal visual field; cutting left nasal fibers eliminates left eye's temporal field = right visual field of left eye)*

**[Regional]** Which best describes the underlying optical defect in astigmatism?
- A. The lens loses its elasticity and cannot accommodate for near vision
- B. The cornea or lens has an irregular curvature, causing light to focus at multiple points ✓
- C. The eyeball is too short, causing light to focus behind the retina
- D. Increased intraocular pressure damages the optic nerve

**[Regional]** Which cranial nerve carries visual information from the retina to the brain?
- a. Trochlear nerve (CN IV) / b. Optic nerve (CN II) ✓ / c. Oculomotor nerve (CN III) / d. Abducens nerve (CN VI)

**[State]** Which option correctly describes the pathway of visual information from the retina to the brain?
- A. Retina → Optic chiasm → Optic nerve → LGN → Visual cortex
- B. Retina → Optic nerve → Optic chiasm → Optic tract → LGN → Visual cortex ✓
- C. Retina → LGN → Optic nerve → Optic tract → Visual cortex
- D. Retina → Optic tract → Optic chiasm → Visual cortex → LGN

**[State]** Damage to the optic chiasm typically results in which visual field defect?
- A. Monocular blindness / B. Homonymous hemianopia / C. Bitemporal hemianopia ✓ / D. Central scotoma

**[State]** A lesion of the right optic tract causes:
- A. Right monocular vision loss / B. Left homonymous hemianopia ✓ / C. Right homonymous hemianopia / D. Bitemporal hemianopia
*(Right optic tract carries right visual field info from both eyes → lesion = left homonymous hemianopia)*

**[State]** The principal neurotransmitter released by photoreceptors in the dark, and its effect on ON-bipolar cells:
- A. Glutamate; sign-inverting (via mGluR6) — hyperpolarizes ON-bipolar cells in the dark ✓
- B. GABA; directly inhibits bipolar cells
- C. Glycine; depolarizes ON-bipolar cells
- D. Acetylcholine; activates nicotinic receptors on bipolar cells

**[State]** Aqueous humor outflow resistance increases mainly at the:
- A. Uveoscleral pathway / B. Trabecular meshwork ✓ / C. Ciliary body epithelium / D. Canal of Schlemm endothelium
*(Increased resistance at trabecular meshwork → elevated intraocular pressure → glaucoma)*

**[State]** A craniopharyngioma compresses the optic chiasm from above. Which visual defect is most classic?
- A. Bitemporal hemianopia ✓ / B. Right homonymous hemianopia / C. Left superior quadrantanopia / D. Monocular scotoma

**[State+]** A lesion of Meyer's loop (inferior fibers of the optic radiation in the temporal lobe) causes:
- A. Contralateral superior quadrantanopia ✓ *("pie in the sky")*
- B. Contralateral inferior quadrantanopia
- C. Ipsilateral superior quadrantanopia
- D. Bitemporal hemianopia
*(Meyer's loop carries superior visual field fibers; a left Meyer's loop lesion → right superior quadrantanopia)*

**[State]** During dark adaptation, photoreceptors exhibit:
- A. Low cGMP; Na⁺/Ca²⁺ channels closed; hyperpolarized
- B. High cGMP; Na⁺/Ca²⁺ channels open; depolarized ✓ *(dark = high cGMP = open channels = depolarized = continuous glutamate release)*
- C. High cAMP; K⁺ channels open; hyperpolarized
- D. Low Ca²⁺; glutamate release decreased

**[Regional]** True/False: The fovea contains more rods than cones. **FALSE** *(fovea is all cones; rods are absent at the fovea)*

**[Regional]** True/False: The "blind spot" of the eye is caused by the optic disk. **TRUE**

**[Regional]** True/False: Bipolar neurons are found in the retina. **TRUE**

**[Regional]** Which structure adjusts the amount of light entering the eye?
- A. Cornea / B. Lens / C. Retina / D. Iris ✓

**[Regional]** Which region of the retina is responsible for high-resolution central vision?
- A. Optic disc / B. Peripheral retina / C. Macula (containing the fovea) ✓ / D. Choroid

**[Regional]** Which photoreceptors function in dim light?
- A. Cones / B. Bipolar cells / C. Ganglion cells / D. Rods ✓

**[Regional]** Which photoreceptors are more numerous in the peripheral retina?
- A. Cones / B. Rods ✓ / C. Bipolar cells / D. Ganglion cells

**[Regional]** A person looks directly at a dim star and cannot see it, but sees it when looking slightly to the side. Why?
- A. The fovea contains mostly cones, which are ineffective in low light ✓
- B. Rods are concentrated in the fovea
- C. The optic disc is at the center of gaze
- D. The iris blocks central vision in dark conditions

**[Regional]** Overlapping visual fields of both eyes produce:
- A. Peripheral vision / B. Tunnel vision / C. Monochromatic vision / D. Binocular vision ✓

**[State]** What cutting causes contralateral hemianopia?
*(A. Nasal Hemiretina / B. Temporal Hemiretina / C. Geniculocalcarine Tract / D. Optic Chiasm / E. Optic Nerve / AB. Optic Tract)*
**Answer: AB (Optic Tract)** *(also C if fibers are unilaterally cut post-chiasm; optic tract is the cleaner answer for contralateral homonymous hemianopia)*

**[State]** A patient cannot see the periphery of their right eye, but their macula is spared. What was the lesion?
**Answer: A (Nasal Hemiretina of the right eye)** *(nasal retina = temporal/peripheral visual field)*

**[State]** Looking straight at a large painting, unable to see the outer regions bilaterally. What was the lesion?
**Answer: D (Optic Chiasm)** *(bitemporal hemianopia)*

**[Regional]** Phineas Gage lost his left eye. What did the iron rod sever?
**Answer: E (Optic Nerve)** *(monocular blindness = optic nerve lesion ipsilateral to the lost eye)*

**[State]** Damage to the left lateral geniculate nucleus leads to:
- a. Inability to perceive information from the left half of the retina
- b. Inability to move the right half of the body
- c. Inability to perceive the right half of your visual field ✓
- d. Inability to perceive information from your left ear
*(Left LGN receives right visual field input from both eyes)*

**[Regional]** Which cranial nerve carries visual information to the brain?
- a. CN I / b. CN II ✓ / c. CN III / d. CN V / e. CN VI

**[Regional]** The bending of light as it passes through different media is called **refraction**.

**[Regional]** The structure where optic nerve fibers partially cross is the **optic chiasm**.

**[Regional]** A buildup of fluid in the interior of the eyeball is known as:
- a. Astigmatism / b. Cataract / c. Myopia / d. Glaucoma ✓

**[Regional]** Which cranial nerve is primarily responsible for vision?
- a. CN II ✓ / b. CN III / c. CN V / d. CN VIII

**[Regional]** What neuron is used to form the optic nerve?
- a. Retinal ganglion cell ✓ / b. Retinal Schwann cell / c. Retinal Satellite cell / d. Retinal Astrocyte cell

**[State]** Identify molecules E (cis-retinal) and F (trans-retinal) in the phototransduction diagram. Explain their interaction.
- E: 11-cis-retinal (dark-adapted; bound to opsin, keeps rhodopsin inactive)
- F: all-trans-retinal (light-activated isomer; photon absorption causes isomerization from 11-cis → all-trans, activating opsin → transducin cascade → cGMP hydrolysis → channel closure → hyperpolarization)

**[State]** TRUE or FALSE: All information from the right eye is processed in the left side of the brain.
**FALSE** — Only the nasal retinal fibers of the right eye cross at the chiasm. The temporal retinal fibers of the right eye remain ipsilateral (processed in the right hemisphere). The entire right *visual field* (from both eyes) is processed in the left hemisphere, but not all information from the right *eye*.

**[State]** Order the structures of the visual pathway from first to last:
**Retina → Optic Nerve → Optic Chiasma → Optic Tract → Lateral Geniculate Nucleus → Optic Radiations → Visual Cortex → Visual Associative Cortex**

**[Regional]** Which vision disorder is associated with Vitamin A deficiency?
- a. Myopia / b. Hyperopia / c. Presbyopia / d. Nyctalopia ✓ *(night blindness)*

**[Regional]** Which retinal cells perform lateral inhibition to enhance contrast detection? **Horizontal cells**

**[Regional]** What neurotransmitter is released by photoreceptors in the dark? **Glutamate**

**[Regional]** Color blindness is caused primarily by: **Defective or missing cone opsins**

**[Regional]** Myopia results from: **Too long an eyeball or excessively curved cornea**

**[Regional]** Glaucoma is caused by: **Increased intraocular pressure damaging the optic nerve**

**[State]** Which best explains why damage to the optic chiasm produces bitemporal hemianopia?
- A. The chiasm carries only temporal retinal fibers, which represent the nasal visual field
- B. Both optic nerves are compressed symmetrically, causing monocular blindness bilaterally
- C. Nasal retinal fibers from both eyes cross at the chiasm; damaging these eliminates the temporal visual fields of both eyes ✓
- D. Temporal retinal fibers from both eyes cross at the chiasm; damaging these eliminates the nasal visual fields

**[State]** Bobby fell asleep in a dark movie theater. When he walked into bright sunlight, he was momentarily blinded. Which best explains this?
- A. His cones had bleached all available photopigment in the dark and needed time to regenerate
- B. His rods, which had accumulated rhodopsin in the dark, were overwhelmed by the sudden bright light and could not rapidly inactivate ✓
- C. His pupils were maximally dilated and could not constrict fast enough
- D. Rod and cone photopigments simultaneously isomerized, flooding the visual cortex

## PUPILLARY REFLEX & OCULOMOTOR *(State scope — autonomic pathway only; reflex circuit labeling only)*

**[Regional]** True/False: The oculomotor nerve (CN III) is responsible for pupil constriction. **TRUE** *(via parasympathetic fibers to sphincter pupillae)*

**[State]** Which statement correctly describes autonomic control of the ciliary muscle?
- A. Parasympathetic activation relaxes the ciliary muscle, flattening the lens for near vision
- B. Sympathetic activation contracts the ciliary muscle to increase lens curvature
- C. Parasympathetic activation contracts the ciliary muscle, allowing the lens to thicken for near vision ✓
- D. Sympathetic activation has no effect on the ciliary muscle

**[Regional]** Which pairing correctly matches the autonomic pathway with its effect on the pupil?
- A. Parasympathetic activation causes constriction by stimulating the sphincter pupillae ✓
- B. Parasympathetic activation causes dilation by stimulating the radial muscles
- C. Sympathetic activation causes constriction by stimulating the sphincter pupillae
- D. Sympathetic activation has no effect on pupil size

**[State]** The Edinger-Westphal nucleus is a visceral nucleus of CN III. Which correctly identifies its role?
- A. The ciliary ganglion lies caudal to the EW nucleus and is stimulated for accommodation
- B. Fibers from the EW nucleus synapse in the ciliary ganglion and control the ciliary muscle and sphincter of the iris ✓
- C. The ciliary ganglion contains no true synapses
- D. CN II is responsible for efferent stimulation of the ciliary muscles more than the EW nucleus

**[State]** Jack has a negative direct and positive indirect pupillary response when light is shone into his left eye. Which is the most likely site of damage?
- a. Right Optic Tract / b. Left Optic Tract / c. Right Oculomotor Nerve / d. Left Oculomotor Nerve ✓ / e. Optic Chiasm
*(Negative direct = efferent failure of left eye. Positive consensual = afferent CN II intact. Left CN III efferent limb damaged.)*

**[Regional]** Accommodation — which structure adjusts the shape of the lens for near vs. far vision?
- A. Iris / B. Ciliary muscles ✓ / C. Cornea / D. Sclera

**[Regional]** Pupil dilation is primarily controlled by which system?
- A. Parasympathetic / B. Sympathetic ✓ / C. Somatic / D. Enteric

**[Regional]** Which structure controls the amount of light entering the eye?
- a. Lens / b. Cornea / c. Iris ✓ / d. Ciliary bodies

## EXTRAOCULAR MUSCLES & EYE MOVEMENT

**[State]** What muscles does CN III (oculomotor nerve) innervate? List all five:
1. Superior rectus (elevation)
2. Inferior rectus (depression)
3. Medial rectus (adduction)
4. Inferior oblique (elevation + extorsion)
5. Levator palpebrae superioris (eyelid elevation)
*Also carries parasympathetic fibers to sphincter pupillae and ciliary muscle.*

**[State]** There are two extraocular muscles not innervated by CN III. What are they and what nerve innervates each?
- Superior oblique → CN IV (Trochlear) — depresses and intorts the eye
- Lateral rectus → CN VI (Abducens) — abducts the eye

**[State]** An inward-turning eye (esotropia) may be caused by damage to:
- A. CN III / B. CN IV / C. CN V / D. CN VI ✓
*(CN VI palsy → lateral rectus paralysis → unopposed medial rectus → eye turns in)*

**[State]** A lesion of the right medial longitudinal fasciculus (MLF) in internuclear ophthalmoplegia leads to:
- A. Impaired right eye abduction on right gaze
- B. Impaired right eye adduction on left gaze, with nystagmus of left eye ✓
- C. Impaired left eye adduction on right gaze, with nystagmus of right eye
- D. Bilateral abduction deficits
*(Right MLF connects right CN VI nucleus to left CN III nucleus; a right MLF lesion impairs left-gaze adduction of the right eye)*

**[Regional]** Identify the cranial nerve that controls the majority of the extrinsic muscles of the eye.
- A. Optic / B. Oculomotor ✓ / C. Trochlear / D. Abducens

**[State]** Which structure is composed of ascending fibers from the vestibular nuclei to the motor nuclei of CN III, IV, and VI?
- A. Medial lemniscus / B. Fasciculus gracilis / C. Lateral lemniscus / D. Fasciculus cuneatus / E. Medial longitudinal fasciculus ✓

**[Regional]** Which cranial nerve allows for eye movement? (select one)
- 1. Abducens ✓ / 2. Optic / 3. Facial / 4. Trochlear
*(All three motor nerves control eye movement; abducens is the correct answer here as it is exclusively motor to an extraocular muscle)*

**[State+]** A patient cannot look to the right with either eye. The lesion is most likely in the:
- A. Right oculomotor nucleus
- B. Left oculomotor nucleus
- C. Right abducens nucleus (or right paramedian pontine reticular formation — PPRF) ✓
- D. Left abducens nucleus
*(The abducens nucleus drives ipsilateral horizontal conjugate gaze; right PPRF/abducens nucleus damage = inability to look right)*

## OLFACTORY SYSTEM

**[State]** What does it mean for an odorant to be volatile, and why does this matter for smell?
- A. Volatile molecules are large and water-soluble, allowing them to bind mucous glycoproteins
- B. Volatile molecules have low vapor pressure and remain in solution
- C. Volatile molecules readily enter the gas phase at room temperature, enabling them to reach and dissolve in olfactory mucus to bind GPCRs ✓
- D. Volatile molecules are lipid-soluble and cross the olfactory epithelium directly without dissolving in mucus

**[Regional]** Which is NOT a function of mucus in the nasal cavity?
- a. Contains discarded cell bodies of dead olfactory receptors
- b. Traps and dissolves odorants to allow receptor binding
- c. Contains odorant-binding proteins (OBPs)
- d. Travels to the lacrimal gland to produce tears ✓ *(tears are produced by lacrimal glands, not nasal mucus)*
- e. Lubricates the nasal cavity

**[Regional]** The olfactory epithelium is located in which structure?
- A. Oral cavity / B. Pharynx / C. Superior portion of the nasal cavity ✓ / D. Larynx

**[Regional]** A patient tastes sweet, sour, salty, and bitter but reports food "has no flavor." Most likely explanation?
- A. Damage to gustatory cortex / B. Damage to CN VII / C. Loss of olfactory function (anosmia) ✓ / D. Destruction of taste receptor cells

**[State]** Which second messenger system is primarily involved in olfactory signal transduction?
- A. IP3 pathway / B. cAMP pathway involving Golf protein ✓ / C. cGMP pathway / D. JAK-STAT pathway

**[Regional]** What type of epithelium is the olfactory epithelium composed of?
- a. Pseudostratified columnar ✓ / b. Stratified cuboidal / c. Stratified squamous / d. Simple squamous

**[State]** Which correctly describes the pathway of an odor molecule from air to olfactory cortex?
- A. Nasal cavity → binds GPCR → Golf → adenylyl cyclase → cAMP → CNG channels open → depolarization → CN I → olfactory bulb → olfactory tract → piriform cortex (bypassing thalamus) ✓
- B. Nasal cavity → binds ion channel receptor → Na⁺ influx → CN I → thalamus → olfactory cortex
- C. Nasal cavity → dissolves in mucus → binds GPCR → cGMP → CNG channel closure → hyperpolarization
- D. Nasal cavity → binds GPCR → Golf → phospholipase C → IP3 → Ca²⁺ release → amygdala directly

**[State]** Which statement about the olfactory pathway is uniquely correct compared to other special senses?
- A. Projects first to thalamus
- B. Regenerating receptor neurons from basal cells ✓
- C. Uses primarily glycine as its neurotransmitter
- D. Travels exclusively in CN V

**[Regional]** True/False: Olfactory inputs are sent to the cerebrum through the thalamus. **FALSE** *(olfaction uniquely bypasses the thalamus, projecting directly to the piriform cortex and limbic system)*

**[Regional]** True/False: A fracture in the cribriform plate can lead to a loss of smell. **TRUE** *(olfactory nerve fibers pass through the cribriform plate of the ethmoid bone)*

**[Regional]** Smell signals are unique because they travel:
- A. Through the cerebellum first
- B. Directly to the cortex without a mandatory thalamic relay ✓
- C. Via the vagus nerve only
- D. Through the optic nerve

**[Regional]** A child blocks one nostril and food tastes much weaker. Why?
- A. Bitter receptors stop functioning
- B. Taste buds shut down
- C. The tongue becomes dry
- D. Much of "flavor" is actually volatile molecules detected by olfaction (retronasal olfaction) ✓

**[Regional]** These olfactory epithelial cells are in contact with the trigeminal nerve and have microvilli:
- A. Brush cells ✓ / B. Basal cells / C. Supporting cells / D. Olfactory sensory neurons

**[State]** Select all true statements about the olfactory adaptation mechanism:
- A. Olfactory fibers only go from receptor to brain ✗
- B. Olfactory cells coming from the olfactory regions of the brain terminate on inhibitory granule cells ✓
- C. Feedback inhibition is at play to suppress smell signals in the olfactory bulbs ✓
- D. Feedback inhibition happens mainly outside of the olfactory bulb ✗
**Correct: B, C**

**[State]** Which sense would be lost if the lateral olfactory areas of both sides were removed, keeping medial areas?
- A. Production of saliva / B. Secretion of gastric acid / C. Instinctually avoiding foul odors / D. Conscious analysis of odor ✓ / E. Vomiting from a rotten odor

**[State]** Which special sense doesn't relay through the thalamus?
- a. Vision / b. Olfaction ✓ / c. Auditory / d. Gustatory / e. Vestibular

**[State]** The olfactory nerve is the only cranial nerve that can regenerate. Which best describes why?
- 1. High amounts of Dysferlin and MG53
- 2. The olfactory epithelium contains horizontal basal cells and globose basal cells that differentiate into olfactory receptor neurons ✓
- 3. Neurogenesis at damaged nerve ends
- 4. Wallerian degeneration and regeneration
- 5. Actually the optic nerve regenerates

**[Regional]** The first relay station for olfactory information is the: **Olfactory bulb**

**[Regional]** Olfactory receptor neurons are unusual because they: **Are replaced every ~30–60 days**

**[State]** Why is smell so strongly linked to emotion and memory compared to other senses?
- A. Olfactory signals are processed in the occipital lobe, which has dense limbic connections
- B. Olfactory signals first relay through the thalamus, where they integrate with emotional memories
- C. The olfactory bulb projects directly to the amygdala and hippocampus (limbic structures) without a mandatory thalamic relay ✓
- D. Olfactory signals activate the locus coeruleus, which activates limbic circuits via norepinephrine

## GUSTATORY SYSTEM

**[State]** Which cranial nerve carries taste from the anterior two-thirds of the tongue?
- a. CN IX / b. CN VII ✓ / c. CN X / d. CN V3
*(CN VII via chorda tympani branch)*

**[State]** Which cranial nerve carries GENERAL SENSATION from the posterior one-third of the tongue?
- a. CN IX / b. CN V3 ✓ / c. CN VI / d. CN VII
*(CN IX carries taste from posterior 1/3; CN V3 carries general somatic sensation from posterior 1/3)*

**[Regional]** Which are basic tastes of the gustatory system? Select all that apply.
- A. Sweet ✓ / B. Sour ✓ / C. Spicy ✗ / D. Salty ✓ / E. Bitter ✓ / F. Umami ✓

**[Regional]** Which taste papillae does NOT carry sensory information?
- A. Fungiform / B. Foliate / C. Circumvallate / D. Filiform ✓

**[Regional]** What is the primary function of filiform papillae?
- A. Detection of bitter taste at highest density
- B. Detection of umami through mGluR4 receptors
- C. Providing texture and friction for mechanical manipulation of food — they contain NO taste buds ✓
- D. Releasing lingual lipase to begin fat digestion

**[Regional]** Which papillae are primarily responsible for detecting bitter taste?
- A. Fungiform / B. Filiform / C. Vallate (circumvallate) ✓ / D. Foliate

**[State]** The taste pathway (CN VII, IX, X → NTS → VPM thalamus → lower post-central gyrus) terminates next in the:
- A. Calcarine sulcus (occipital lobe)
- B. Heschl's gyrus (primary auditory cortex)
- C. Piriform cortex
- D. Posterior insula
- E. Anterior insular/opercular area ✓
*(Primary gustatory cortex = anterior insula and frontal operculum)*

**[State]** From the nucleus tractus solitarius (NTS) in the brainstem, where are taste signals projected to trigger salivation?
- A. Raphe nuclei / B. Substantia nigra and VTA / C. Superior and inferior salivatory nuclei ✓ / D. Nucleus ambiguus / E. Solitary nucleus

**[Regional]** Which taste sensation is triggered by hydrogen ions?
- a. Salty / b. Bitter / c. Sour ✓ / d. Umami / e. Sweet

**[Regional]** Which taste sensation is triggered by glutamate?
- a. Sweet / b. Salty / c. Bitter / d. Umami ✓ / e. Sour

**[State]** Which cranial nerves carry taste sensations? Select all.
- a. Facial Nerve (CN VII) ✓ *(anterior 2/3 tongue, soft palate)*
- b. Trigeminal Nerve (CN V) ✗ *(general sensation only, no taste)*
- c. Glossopharyngeal Nerve (CN IX) ✓ *(posterior 1/3 tongue)*
- d. Vagus Nerve (CN X) ✓ *(epiglottis, laryngopharynx)*

**[State]** Damage to the chorda tympani could lead to:
- a. Anosmia / b. Dysgeusia ✓ / c. Hyperopia / d. Vertigo
*(Chorda tympani = branch of CN VII carrying taste from anterior 2/3 tongue)*

**[Regional]** Which of the following is NOT a basic taste? **Spicy** *(a trigeminal/TRPV1 chemical irritation, not a gustatory quality)*

**[Regional]** Which taste sensation is mediated by ion channels rather than GPCRs? **Salty and sour** *(Na⁺ and H⁺ ions directly permeate channels)*

## VESTIBULAR SYSTEM & AUDITORY

**[Regional]** The utricle detects _____, the saccule detects _____, and the semicircular canals detect _____.
- A. Angular; Vertical; Horizontal
- B. Angular; Horizontal; Vertical
- C. Vertical; Horizontal; Angular
- D. Horizontal; Vertical; Angular ✓ *(utricle = horizontal linear; saccule = vertical linear; semicircular canals = angular/rotational)*
- E. Horizontal; Angular; Vertical

**[Regional]** What is the primary function of the otoliths in the vestibular system?
- A. Detect rotational movements of the head
- B. Detect linear acceleration and head position relative to gravity ✓
- C. Amplify sound waves entering the cochlea
- D. Maintain pressure in the middle ear

**[State]** Organ of Corti hair cell depolarization occurs when stereocilia deflect:
- A. Toward shortest cilium → K⁺ efflux
- B. Toward tallest cilium → K⁺ influx from endolymph ✓
- C. Toward tallest cilium → Na⁺ influx from perilymph
- D. Toward shortest cilium → Ca²⁺ influx from endolymph

**[Regional]** True/False: The utricle senses horizontal motion, while the saccule senses vertical motion. **TRUE**

**[Regional]** True/False: The cochlear duct (scala media) contains a high concentration of potassium ions. **TRUE** *(endolymph is uniquely high in K⁺; K⁺ influx depolarizes hair cells)*

**[Regional]** The stapes transmits vibration to which structure?
- A. Round window / B. Tympanic membrane / C. Cochlear duct / D. Oval window ✓

**[Regional]** Motion sickness occurs due to conflict between which two sensory systems?
- A. Olfactory and gustatory / B. Somatosensory and olfactory / C. Visual and vestibular ✓ / D. Auditory and somatosensory

**[Regional]** Which structures detect head tilt and linear acceleration?
- A. Semicircular canals / B. Cochlea / C. Utricle and saccule ✓ / D. Organ of Corti

**[Regional]** Which part of the cochlea responds to high-frequency sounds?
- A. Base (near oval window) ✓ / B. Apex / C. Middle turn / D. Helicotrema

**[Regional]** Which cells transduce sound waves into electrical signals in the cochlea?
- A. Schwann cells / B. Supporting cells / C. Spiral ganglion neurons / D. Hair cells in the organ of Corti ✓

**[Regional]** Which structure detects angular acceleration and spinning?
- A. Otoliths / B. Cochlear hair cells / C. Saccule / D. Cristae ampullaris ✓ *(hair cell structures within semicircular canals)*

**[Regional]** Which type of hearing loss results from damage to hair cells or the auditory nerve?
- A. Conductive / B. Sensorineural ✓ / C. Mixed / D. Central

**[Regional]** Which structure detects linear acceleration such as moving in an elevator?
- a. Saccule and utricle ✓ / b. Cochlea / c. Semicircular canals / d. Tympanic membrane / e. Oval window

**[Regional]** A person has trouble detecting high-pitched sounds. Damage is likely in the:
- a. Apex / b. Base ✓ / c. Tympanic membrane / d. Auditory nerve / e. Semicircular canals

**[Regional]** True/False: Endolymph and perilymph have identical ion compositions. **FALSE** *(endolymph = high K⁺, low Na⁺; perilymph = high Na⁺, low K⁺, similar to CSF)*

**[Regional]** A common childhood infection of the middle ear:
- a. Otitis media ✓ / b. Tinnitus / c. Labyrinthitis / d. Otitis externa

**[State]** Which intervention/ailment pairings are INCORRECT? Select all.
- a. Intratympanic gentamicin; Ménière's Disease ✓ *(correct pairing)*
- b. Tympanic membrane perforation; Otitis Media ✓ *(correct pairing — myringotomy)*
- c. Cochlear implants → directly signal **CN VII**; Sensorineural Hearing Loss ✗ *(incorrect — cochlear implants signal CN VIII, not CN VII)* ✓ **This one is WRONG — select it**
- d. Stapedotomy; Otosclerosis ✓ *(correct pairing)*
- e. Hearing aids; Presbycusis ✓ *(correct pairing)*
**Answer: c is incorrect**

**[State — Case]** A 32-year-old woman presents with episodic vertigo lasting 5–8 minutes, nausea, tinnitus, and fluctuating left-ear hearing loss.
- a. Diagnosis: **Ménière's disease**
- b. Mechanism: **Endolymphatic hydrops — excess endolymph accumulation distorts the membraneous labyrinth, disrupting both vestibular and cochlear hair cell function**
- c. Dietary modifications: **Low-sodium diet (reduce endolymph production); limit caffeine and alcohol; adequate hydration**

**[State — Case]** A 22-year-old presents with progressive left-ear hearing loss. Father wore hearing aids at 25. Weber test lateralizes to the left.
- a. Disease: **Otosclerosis** *(stapes fixation causing conductive hearing loss)*
- b. Inheritance pattern: **Autosomal dominant** *(with incomplete penetrance)*
- c. Test performed: **Weber test** *(tuning fork placed on midline forehead; lateralizes to affected/conductive loss side)*

**[Regional]** Which ion causes depolarization of hair cells when stereocilia are deflected? **Potassium (K⁺)** *(enters from endolymph through tip-link-gated channels)*

**[State]** In the auditory pathway, where does the first binaural processing occur? **Superior olivary complex**

**[State]** Which best explains how the basilar membrane achieves tonotopic organization?
- A. Different regions contain different types of hair cells that respond to specific neurotransmitters
- B. Stiff and narrow at the apex; wide and floppy at the base → apex = high frequency
- C. Stiff and narrow at the base (near oval window) → resonates to high frequencies; wide and floppy at the apex → resonates to low frequencies ✓
- D. Hair cell density varies along the membrane, with highest density at the base for high frequencies

**[State]** What is the primary function of the round window?
- A. To amplify sound pressure by acting as a second oval window
- B. To transmit vibrations from the stapes into the scala vestibuli
- C. To serve as a pressure-relief membrane, allowing perilymph displacement to dissipate ✓
- D. To separate the endolymph from the perilymph

**[Regional]** Distinguish between conductive hearing loss and sensorineural hearing loss.
- **Conductive**: problem with the outer or middle ear (e.g., cerumen impaction, tympanic membrane perforation, otosclerosis) — sound waves fail to reach the cochlea properly
- **Sensorineural**: damage to cochlear hair cells, CN VIII, or central auditory pathways (e.g., presbycusis, noise exposure, ototoxic drugs)

## BRAINSTEM & CRANIAL NERVE NUCLEI *(State scope)*

**[State]** Which nucleus in the medulla receives visceral sensory input from CN VII, IX, and X?
- a. Nucleus ambiguus / b. Dorsal motor nucleus / c. Inferior olivary nucleus / d. Nucleus tractus solitarius (NTS/solitary nucleus) ✓ / e. "Sensorinucleus" (not a real structure)

**[State]** Möbius Syndrome (underdevelopment of CN VI + VII). Which would likely NOT be affected?
- a. Facial paralysis *(CN VII — affected)*
- b. Movement of eyes *(CN VI — affected)*
- c. Sensation of the eyelid ✓ *(CN V — NOT affected; eyelid sensation = CN V1)*
- d. All of the above

**[State]** Which cranial nerve innervates the dura mater and mediates the pain of meningitis?
- A. III / B. V ✓ / C. VI / D. IX / E. XI
*(CN V branches innervate the dura; meningeal irritation activates these fibers, producing the headache of meningitis)*

**[Regional]** A student touches their eyelid and their eye blinks (corneal reflex). Which cranial nerve carries the sensory signal to the brainstem?
- A. CN VII / B. CN IX / C. CN III / D. CN V ✓ *(afferent = CN V1; efferent = CN VII)*

**[State]** Which brainstem nucleus serves as the primary relay station for visceral sensory information — including taste, baroreceptor input, and gut mechanoreceptors?
- A. Nucleus ambiguus / B. Dorsal motor nucleus of vagus / C. Nucleus tractus solitarius (NTS) ✓ / D. Hypoglossal nucleus

**[State]** A patient suffers damage to the nucleus ambiguus. Which deficit is expected?
- A. Loss of taste from the anterior 2/3 of the tongue
- B. Dysphagia and hoarseness due to paralysis of pharyngeal and laryngeal muscles ✓ *(nucleus ambiguus provides motor output to pharynx/larynx via CN IX, X)*
- C. Loss of voluntary tongue movements
- D. Loss of parasympathetic input to the heart

**[State]** The dorsal motor nucleus of vagus (DMNV) provides parasympathetic preganglionic output primarily to:
- A. Salivary glands and lacrimal glands
- B. Heart, lungs, and abdominal viscera (up to the splenic flexure) ✓
- C. Bladder and reproductive organs
- D. Pupillary constriction and lens accommodation

**[State+]** Which midbrain nucleus is degenerated in Parkinson's disease, and what neurotransmitter does it produce?
- A. Red nucleus; GABA / B. Substantia nigra pars compacta; dopamine ✓ / C. Locus coeruleus; norepinephrine / D. Raphe nuclei; serotonin

**[State]** The inferior olivary nucleus sends climbing fibers to the cerebellum. Its primary role is:
- A. Relaying auditory information to the cortex
- B. Transmitting error signals to the cerebellum for motor learning and adaptation ✓
- C. Coordinating eye movements
- D. Processing vestibular signals for balance

**[State]** Which nucleus in the pons is the FIRST site of binaural processing in the auditory pathway?
- A. Cochlear nucleus / B. Superior olivary complex ✓ / C. Inferior colliculus / D. Medial geniculate nucleus

**[State]** True/False: The nucleus ambiguus provides motor output to pharyngeal and laryngeal muscles via CN IX and X. **TRUE**

**[State]** True/False: The Edinger-Westphal nucleus controls pupillary constriction via parasympathetic fibers in CN III. **TRUE**

**[State]** True/False: The inferior colliculus is a relay station in the auditory pathway, while the superior colliculus mediates visual reflexes. **TRUE**

**[Practice — Cranial Nerve Reference Table]**

| Clinical Test / Function | Cranial Nerve |
|---|---|
| Tongue symmetry | CN XII (Hypoglossal) |
| Shoulder shrug strength | CN XI (Spinal Accessory) |
| Bite, skin sensation of face, corneal reflex afferent | CN V (Trigeminal) |
| Smell aromatic oils | CN I (Olfactory) |
| Rinne/Weber tuning fork, balance | CN VIII (Vestibulocochlear) |
| "Say Ahh," uvula position, gag reflex | CN IX/X (Glossopharyngeal/Vagus) |
| Taste posterior 1/3 tongue | CN IX (Glossopharyngeal) |
| Facial muscle motor (expression) | CN VII (Facial) |
| Eye abduction (lateral rectus) | CN VI (Abducens) |
| Eye movement (most muscles), pupillary constriction | CN III (Oculomotor) |
| Parasympathetic to thoracic/abdominal viscera | CN X (Vagus) |
| Eye depression and intorsion (superior oblique) | CN IV (Trochlear) |

How many pairs of cranial nerves? **12** | How many pairs of spinal nerves? **31**

## SPINAL CORD TRACTS *(State scope)*

### Quick Reference Table

| Tract | Direction | Modality | Decussation | Side at Cortex |
|---|---|---|---|---|
| Dorsal Column–Medial Lemniscal (DCML) | Ascending | Fine touch, vibration, 2-point discrimination, conscious proprioception | Medulla (nucleus gracilis/cuneatus) | Contralateral |
| Spinothalamic (Anterolateral) | Ascending | Pain, temperature, crude touch | Spinal cord (1–2 segments of entry) | Contralateral |
| Dorsal Spinocerebellar | Ascending | Unconscious proprioception (lower limb) | Does NOT decussate | Ipsilateral |
| Ventral Spinocerebellar | Ascending | Unconscious proprioception (lower limb) | Decussates twice (net ipsilateral) | Ipsilateral |
| Lateral Corticospinal | Descending | Voluntary motor (distal limbs) | Pyramidal decussation in medulla | Contralateral |
| Anterior Corticospinal | Descending | Voluntary motor (axial/trunk) | At spinal level | Bilateral |
| Rubrospinal | Descending | Motor coordination (upper limb) | Midbrain | Contralateral |
| Vestibulospinal | Descending | Balance, posture, extensor tone | Does NOT decussate | Ipsilateral |

**[State]** Which ascending spinal tract carries fine touch, vibration sense, and conscious proprioception?
- A. Spinothalamic tract / B. Dorsal column–medial lemniscal pathway ✓ / C. Ventral spinocerebellar tract / D. Rubrospinal tract

**[State]** A patient can feel pain and temperature in their right arm but cannot feel light touch or vibration. Where is the lesion?
- A. Right dorsal column at cervical level ✓ / B. Right spinothalamic tract / C. Left dorsal column at cervical level / D. Left spinothalamic tract

**[State]** The DCML pathway decussates at which location?
- A. Spinal cord at level of entry / B. Midbrain / C. Medulla (nucleus gracilis and cuneatus) ✓ / D. Thalamus

**[State]** The spinothalamic tract decussates at which location?
- A. Spinal cord, within 1–2 segments of the level of entry ✓ / B. Medulla / C. Pons / D. Midbrain

**[State+]** A patient suffers a complete right-sided hemisection at T4 (Brown-Séquard syndrome). Which set of deficits is expected below T4?
- A. Loss of all sensation bilaterally; preserved motor function
- B. Loss of pain/temp on left; loss of fine touch/proprioception and voluntary motor on right ✓
- C. Loss of pain/temp on right; loss of fine touch/proprioception and voluntary motor on left
- D. Loss of all sensation and motor on the left

**[State+]** In Brown-Séquard syndrome, why is motor loss ipsilateral while pain/temp loss is contralateral?
- A. Corticospinal tract decussates in the spinal cord; spinothalamic decussates in the medulla
- B. Corticospinal tract decussates in the medulla (pyramidal decussation) then descends; spinothalamic fibers cross at the spinal cord level of entry ✓
- C. Both tracts decussate at the same level; difference is due to fiber diameter
- D. Motor fibers travel in the dorsal column; sensory fibers travel in the lateral column

**[State+]** Syringomyelia presents with loss of pain and temperature in a "cape-like" distribution across the shoulders and arms, with preserved fine touch. Why?
- A. Dorsal columns are most resistant to compression
- B. Fine touch fibers are myelinated and immune to central cavitation
- C. The crossing spinothalamic fibers at the anterior commissure are disrupted first, while dorsal column fibers traveling ipsilaterally remain intact ✓
- D. The syrinx forms posteriorly, sparing anterior spinothalamic fibers

**[State]** Which descending tract crosses in the pyramidal decussation and controls voluntary movement of distal limb muscles?
- A. Anterior corticospinal tract / B. Lateral corticospinal tract ✓ / C. Rubrospinal tract / D. Reticulospinal tract

**[State]** A stroke affects the right motor cortex. Which limb(s) will show weakness?
- A. Right arm and right leg / B. Left arm and left leg ✓ / C. Right arm only / D. Left leg only

**[State]** After synapsing in the medulla, DCML fibers ascend as the medial lemniscus to which thalamic nucleus?
- A. Lateral geniculate nucleus / B. Medial geniculate nucleus / C. Ventral posterior lateral (VPL) nucleus ✓ / D. Pulvinar nucleus

**[State]** Which spinal cord tract carries unconscious proprioceptive information from the lower limbs to the cerebellum WITHOUT decussating?
- A. Ventral spinocerebellar tract / B. Dorsal spinocerebellar tract ✓ / C. Spinothalamic tract / D. Dorsal column

**[State]** Upper motor neuron (UMN) lesions produce:
- A. Flaccid paralysis, muscle atrophy, fasciculations, absent reflexes
- B. Spastic paralysis, hyperreflexia, Babinski sign, minimal atrophy ✓
- C. Flaccid paralysis, hyperreflexia, intact sensation
- D. Spastic paralysis, absent reflexes, sensory loss

**[State]** Lower motor neuron (LMN) lesions produce:
- A. Flaccid paralysis, muscle atrophy, fasciculations, absent/reduced reflexes ✓
- B. Spastic paralysis, hyperreflexia, Babinski sign
- C. Spastic paralysis, minimal atrophy, preserved reflexes
- D. Loss of pain only, preserved motor function

**[State]** ALS affects both UMN and LMN. Which combination would you expect?
- A. Pure spastic paralysis with hyperreflexia only
- B. Pure flaccid paralysis with atrophy only
- C. Mixed spastic and flaccid paralysis with both hyperreflexia and atrophy/fasciculations ✓
- D. Sensory loss with preserved motor function

**[State]** A patient with tabes dorsalis (syphilitic dorsal column degeneration) would have difficulty with:
- A. Feeling pain from a pin prick
- B. Detecting vibration from a tuning fork and knowing limb position with eyes closed ✓
- C. Moving their limbs voluntarily
- D. Feeling heat from a warm object

**[State — True/False]**
- The DCML pathway decussates at the spinal cord level of entry. **FALSE** *(decussates in the medulla)*
- In Brown-Séquard syndrome, motor loss is ipsilateral to the lesion. **TRUE**
- Syringomyelia first disrupts dorsal column function. **FALSE** *(syrinx disrupts crossing spinothalamic fibers first)*
- The dorsal spinocerebellar tract conveys unconscious proprioception and does not decussate. **TRUE**
- UMN lesions produce flaccid paralysis and muscle atrophy. **FALSE** *(LMN = flaccid; UMN = spastic)*
- The lateral corticospinal tract decussates in the pyramidal decussation of the medulla. **TRUE**

**[State+]** A patient loses pain/temp on the left below T8, and loses fine touch/voluntary motor on the right below T8. Which best describes this?
- A. Complete spinal cord transection at T8
- B. Syringomyelia at T8
- C. Brown-Séquard syndrome — right-sided hemisection at T8 ✓ *(ipsilateral motor/DCML loss right; contralateral spinothalamic loss left)*
- D. Anterior spinal artery infarction at T8

**[State+]** Compare the DCML and spinothalamic tracts. Which statement is CORRECT?
- A. Both decussate in the medulla; DCML carries pain, spinothalamic carries fine touch
- B. DCML decussates in the medulla and carries fine touch/proprioception; spinothalamic decussates in the spinal cord and carries pain/temperature ✓
- C. DCML decussates in the spinal cord; spinothalamic decussates in the medulla
- D. Both carry pain and temperature but differ in fiber diameter only
