## PROPRIOCEPTION MECHANICS *(State scope)*

### Quick Reference Table

| Receptor | Location | Stimulus | Afferent Fiber | Response |
|---|---|---|---|---|
| Muscle spindle | Within muscle belly, parallel to extrafusal fibers | Muscle length/stretch | Ia (primary) & II (secondary) | Stretch reflex; inhibition of antagonist |
| Golgi tendon organ (GTO) | Musculotendinous junction, in series | Muscle tension/force | Ib | Autogenic inhibition; activation of antagonist |
| Pacinian corpuscle | Deep dermis, joints, periosteum | Vibration, rapid pressure | Aβ | Vibration/deep pressure |
| Ruffini ending | Dermis, joint capsule | Sustained pressure, skin stretch, joint position | Aβ | Joint angle, skin stretch |
| Free nerve endings | Throughout tissue | Pain, temperature, crude touch | Aδ (fast/sharp pain) & C (slow/dull pain) | Nociception |

**[State]** Muscle spindles detect which stimulus, and what is their primary functional role?
- A. Muscle tension; they inhibit the contracting muscle to prevent injury
- B. Muscle length/stretch; they trigger a reflexive contraction of the stretched muscle via the stretch reflex ✓
- C. Joint angle; they send conscious position sense to the somatosensory cortex
- D. Tendon force; they coordinate antagonist muscle activation

**[State]** Golgi tendon organs (GTOs) are in series with extrafusal fibers. Their primary role is to:
- A. Detect muscle length change and trigger stretch reflexes
- B. Detect excessive muscle tension and reflexively inhibit the contracting muscle (autogenic inhibition) ✓
- C. Provide conscious proprioception to the cerebellum
- D. Regulate muscle tone during sleep

**[State]** The patellar tendon reflex is monosynaptic. Which afferent fiber carries the signal from the muscle spindle?
- A. Aδ fibers / B. C fibers / C. Ib fibers / D. Ia fibers ✓

**[State]** In the patellar reflex, the hamstrings simultaneously relax when the quadriceps contracts. This is accomplished by:
- A. Direct inhibition via a Ib fiber from the GTO of the hamstring
- B. Reciprocal inhibition via an inhibitory interneuron, activated by the Ia afferent from the quadriceps spindle ✓
- C. Direct co-activation by the same Ia fiber
- D. Descending inhibition from the motor cortex

**[State]** Which afferent fiber type carries signals from the Golgi tendon organ?
- A. Ia / B. Ib ✓ / C. II / D. Aδ

**[State]** Gamma motor neurons (γ-MNs) innervate intrafusal fibers of the muscle spindle. Their role is to:
- A. Directly contract the muscle to produce movement
- B. Adjust the sensitivity of the muscle spindle by maintaining intrafusal fiber tension during muscle contraction ✓
- C. Inhibit the Golgi tendon organ signal
- D. Relay proprioceptive information to the cerebellum

**[State]** A patient with hyperactive deep tendon reflexes (hyperreflexia). This is most consistent with:
- A. Lower motor neuron lesion — loss of the reflex arc
- B. Upper motor neuron lesion — loss of descending inhibition on the reflex arc ✓
- C. Golgi tendon organ lesion — loss of autogenic inhibition
- D. Muscle spindle degeneration — loss of stretch sensing

**[State]** Conscious proprioception from muscle spindles travels to the cortex via which pathway?
- A. Spinothalamic tract → VPL thalamus → somatosensory cortex
- B. Dorsal column–medial lemniscal pathway → VPL thalamus → somatosensory cortex ✓
- C. Dorsal spinocerebellar tract → cerebellum
- D. Rubrospinal tract → motor cortex

**[State]** Unconscious proprioception reaches the cerebellum via which tracts?
- A. Corticospinal and rubrospinal tracts
- B. Spinothalamic tract
- C. Dorsal and ventral spinocerebellar tracts ✓
- D. Dorsal column–medial lemniscal pathway

**[State]** Which receptor detects vibration and rapid pressure changes in deep skin and periosteum?
- A. Pacinian corpuscles ✓ / B. Meissner's corpuscles / C. Merkel discs / D. Ruffini endings

**[State — True/False]**
- Muscle spindles are in series with extrafusal fibers and detect tension. **FALSE** *(in parallel; GTOs are in series)*
- Ia afferent fibers from muscle spindles produce monosynaptic excitation of alpha motor neurons in the stretch reflex. **TRUE**
- Golgi tendon organs produce autogenic inhibition — they inhibit the muscle in which they reside. **TRUE**
- Gamma motor neurons directly produce skeletal muscle contraction. **FALSE** *(they regulate spindle sensitivity by contracting intrafusal fibers)*
- The dorsal spinocerebellar tract decussates. **FALSE** *(ipsilateral, does NOT decussate)*
- Loss of conscious proprioception with preserved pain sensation indicates a dorsal column lesion. **TRUE**

**[State+]** A patient cannot detect vibration in their feet or identify toe position without looking, but can feel a pin prick. Which correctly identifies the affected pathway?
- A. Spinothalamic tract; pain and vibration fibers decussate differently
- B. Dorsal column–medial lemniscal pathway; it carries fine touch, vibration, and proprioception but NOT pain, which travels in the spinothalamic tract ✓
- C. Dorsal spinocerebellar tract; cerebellar damage spares pain
- D. Lateral corticospinal tract; motor loss misinterpreted as loss of proprioception

**[State+]** Which best explains what happens if gamma motor neurons are selectively blocked during a voluntary contraction?
- A. The muscle cannot contract at all
- B. The muscle contracts but the spindle goes slack, losing its ability to detect further stretch or changes in muscle length ✓
- C. The GTO increases firing to compensate
- D. Reciprocal inhibition is eliminated, causing co-contraction

**[State+]** Which correctly orders every component of the patellar tendon reflex arc?
- A. Tap → patellar tendon stretches → Ia fiber → dorsal horn synapse → alpha MN → quadriceps contracts; Ib fiber → inhibitory interneuron → hamstring relaxes
- B. Tap → quadriceps stretches → Ia fiber → direct monosynaptic synapse on alpha MN (quadriceps) → quadriceps contracts; Ia fiber → inhibitory interneuron → hamstring alpha MN inhibited → hamstring relaxes ✓
- C. Tap → quadriceps stretches → Ib fiber → inhibitory interneuron → alpha MN → quadriceps contracts
- D. Tap → GTO stretches → Ia fiber → monosynaptic synapse → quadriceps contracts; Ia fiber → excitatory interneuron → hamstring contracts

## NEUROPHYSIOLOGY — ACTION POTENTIALS & MEMBRANE POTENTIALS

**[Regional]** What primarily causes the neuron's resting membrane potential to be negative?
- a. The Na⁺/K⁺ pump pumps out 3 Na⁺ for every 2 K⁺
- b. Open K⁺ leak channels allow more K⁺ to leave than Na⁺ can enter ✓
- c. Intracellular proteins carry negative charge
- d. Voltage-gated Na⁺ channels are closed at rest

**[Regional]** Approximate resting membrane potential of a neuron: **−70 mV**

**[Regional]** Initial depolarization of an action potential is caused by: **Na⁺ entering** through voltage-gated Na⁺ channels

**[Regional]** Which correctly describes the function of the sodium-potassium pump?
- c. Actively exports 3 Na⁺ and imports 2 K⁺ using ATP ✓

**[Regional]** Which statement about the refractory period is TRUE?
- c. During the relative refractory period, K⁺ channels are open ✓

**[Regional]** True/False: Action potentials happen in an all-or-nothing fashion. **TRUE**

**[State]** Which best describes what happens to a neuron's membrane during each phase of an action potential?
- B. Depolarization: voltage-gated Na⁺ channels open → Na⁺ enters → membrane rises to ~+30 mV; Repolarization: Na⁺ channels inactivate and K⁺ channels open → K⁺ exits → membrane returns toward resting; Hyperpolarization: K⁺ channels remain briefly open → membrane overshoots below resting potential ✓

**[State]** Which best describes the all-or-nothing law?
- B. A neuron either fires a full action potential at a fixed amplitude regardless of stimulus strength, or does not fire at all, depending on whether the membrane potential reaches threshold ✓

---

## SALTATORY CONDUCTION & MYELINATION

**[Regional]** In saltatory conduction, action potentials propagate along which part of myelinated axons?
- b. Nodes of Ranvier ✓

**[Regional]** Which part of the neuron integrates incoming signals and initiates an action potential?
- b. Axon hillock ✓

**[Regional]** Which glial cell type myelinates axons in the CNS?
- c. Oligodendrocytes ✓

**[Regional]** True/False: The myelin sheath in the CNS is produced by Schwann cells. **FALSE** *(Schwann cells = PNS; oligodendrocytes = CNS)*

**[State]** A patient has relapsing-remitting neurological symptoms, optic neuritis, limb weakness worsened by heat, demyelinating MRI lesions, and CSF oligoclonal bands. Which correctly explains the pathophysiology?
- B. Autoimmune attack on oligodendrocytes in the CNS destroys the myelin sheath; without myelin, action potentials cannot jump between nodes of Ranvier and propagation slows or fails; heat worsens symptoms because elevated temperature further impairs ion channel function in already-compromised axons ✓
*(Multiple sclerosis)*

---

## SYNAPTIC TRANSMISSION & NEUROTRANSMITTERS

**[Regional]** Neurotransmitters are released from axon terminals via: **Exocytosis**

**[Regional]** An IPSP occurs when the membrane becomes more permeable to: **Potassium ions** *(K⁺ exits, hyperpolarizing the membrane)*

**[Regional]** Neurotransmitter at the neuromuscular junction: **Acetylcholine**

**[Regional]** Which is NOT a catecholamine? **Serotonin** *(serotonin is an indolamine)*

**[Regional]** All catecholamines are synthesized from: **Tyrosine**

**[Regional]** True/False: Voltage-gated calcium channels at the axon terminal trigger neurotransmitter release. **TRUE**

**[State]** Which neurotransmitter category does acetylcholine belong to, and what most distinguishes it?
- C. Quaternary amine; derived from choline rather than an aromatic amino acid, making it highly polar and unable to cross the blood-brain barrier ✓

**[State]** If inhibitory neurotransmitters (GABA) were completely dysfunctional in the CNS, what would result?
- B. Uncontrolled, excessive neuronal firing potentially manifesting as seizures ✓

**[State]** A patient is exposed to an organophosphate nerve agent. Which sequence correctly describes the mechanism?
- B. Inhibits acetylcholinesterase → ACh accumulates at synapses → overstimulation of muscarinic and nicotinic receptors → SLUDGE symptoms and muscle paralysis ✓

---

## GLIAL CELLS

**[Regional]** Which cells line the ventricles and spinal cord? **Ependymal cells**

**[Regional]** Most abundant glial cell in the CNS: **Astrocytes**

**[Regional]** Which glial cell acts as the immune cell of the CNS? **Microglia**

**[Regional]** True/False: Neurons outnumber neuroglia 3 to 1. **FALSE** *(neuroglia outnumber neurons)*

**[State]** Primary role of microglial cells:
- C. Immune surveillance — resident macrophages of the CNS, phagocytosing cellular debris, pathogens, and apoptotic neurons ✓

---

## NEUROANATOMY — BRAIN REGIONS, LOBES & TRACTS

**[Regional]** Which brain region regulates vital functions like breathing and heartbeat? **Medulla oblongata**

**[State]** Patients with damage to Wernicke's area have difficulty: **Understanding language** (receptive aphasia)

**[State]** Which best explains the roles of Broca's and Wernicke's areas?
- B. Broca's area (frontal lobe): formulates motor plans for speech production; Wernicke's area (temporal lobe): comprehends language — damage to Broca's = expressive aphasia; damage to Wernicke's = receptive aphasia with fluent but nonsensical speech ✓

**[Regional]** A stroke in the MCA most likely affects: **Face and arm movement** *(MCA supplies the lateral cortex — face and arm sensorimotor areas)*

**[Regional]** Which region is most affected when a patient cannot form new memories but recalls old ones? **Hippocampus**

**[Regional]** Correct path of CSF: **Lateral ventricles → third ventricle → fourth ventricle → subarachnoid space**

**[State]** True/False: The spinothalamic tract carries body position sense to the cerebellum. **FALSE** *(spinothalamic = pain/temperature; proprioception = dorsal columns and spinocerebellar tracts)*

**[Regional]** True/False: The dorsal root contains motor fibers. **FALSE** *(dorsal root = sensory/afferent; ventral root = motor/efferent — Bell-Magendie Law)*

---

## AUTONOMIC NERVOUS SYSTEM

**[State]** The prevertebral ganglia are innervated by which nerve?
- a. Greater splanchnic nerve ✓ / b. Vagus nerve / c. Sympathetic chain / d. Phrenic nerve

**[Regional]** Primary division involved in fight-or-flight: **Sympathetic**

**[Regional]** True/False: The enteric nervous system cannot function without input from the CNS. **FALSE** *(it has intrinsic neural circuits capable of functioning independently)*

**[Regional]** Adrenal medulla catecholamine ratio during fight-or-flight: **~80% epinephrine, ~20% norepinephrine**

**[State]** Which correctly contrasts sympathetic and parasympathetic divisions?
- C. Sympathetic: short preganglionic, long postganglionic; ganglia in sympathetic chain; ACh preganglionic / NE postganglionic. Parasympathetic: long preganglionic, short postganglionic; ganglia near/in target organs; ACh at both synapses ✓

---

## SLEEP & EEG

**[Regional]** EEG waveform during deep (slow-wave) sleep: **Delta waves**

**[Regional]** Sleep spindles and K-complexes are characteristic of: **N2 (Stage 2 NREM)**

**[Regional]** Which is NOT associated with REM sleep? **Body repair** *(occurs in N3 slow-wave sleep)*

**[State]** Which correctly describes sleep stages and brainwave patterns?
- B. N1: Theta (light sleep, hypnic jerks possible); N2: Sleep spindles and K-complexes; N3: Delta waves (deep, restorative slow-wave sleep); REM: Beta-like desynchronized waves, vivid dreaming, muscle atonia ✓

---

## SOMATOSENSORY SYSTEM

**[Regional]** Which touch receptor senses deep pressure and vibration changes? **Pacinian corpuscles**

**[State]** Why do heart attack patients feel pain in both the skin of the left arm and chest AND in the heart?
- B. Referred pain — cardiac pain fibers (C and Aδ) converge on the same spinal cord segments (T1–T4) as somatic fibers from the skin of the left arm and chest; the brain misattributes the signal to the skin ✓

**[State]** Why is it sometimes difficult to distinguish very cold from very warm objects touching the skin?
- B. At temperatures outside the normal range of cold receptors (10–35°C) and warm receptors (30–45°C), both stimuli activate nociceptors (free nerve endings), which do not discriminate between extreme cold and extreme heat ✓

---

## NEUROLOGICAL DISORDERS

**[Regional]** In which disease do sclerotic sheaths result in poor action potential conduction? **Multiple Sclerosis**

**[Regional]** Loss of dopamine causing tremors and rigidity: **Parkinson's Disease**

**[State]** In ALS, which neurons are lost? **Both upper and lower motor neurons**

**[State — Disorder Matching]**

| Disorder | Key Features |
|---|---|
| Epilepsy | Sudden excessive neuronal discharge; seizures |
| Multiple Sclerosis | Autoimmune CNS demyelination (oligodendrocytes); optic neuritis, heat sensitivity |
| Alzheimer's Disease | APOE4 risk; cholinergic neuron loss; amyloid plaques; neurofibrillary tangles |
| ALS | UMN + LMN loss; mixed spastic/flaccid paralysis; progressive |
| Tay-Sachs | Hexosaminidase A deficiency; lipid accumulation in neurons |
| Parkinson's Disease | Substantia nigra dopamine loss; resting tremor, bradykinesia, cogwheel rigidity |
| Cerebral Palsy | Brain damage before/during/shortly after birth; non-progressive motor disorder |
| Myasthenia Gravis | Autoimmune blockade of ACh receptors at NMJ; fatigable muscle weakness |
| Brown-Séquard Syndrome | Spinal hemisection: ipsilateral motor + DCML loss; contralateral spinothalamic loss |
| Syringomyelia | Central spinal cord cavitation; cape-like pain/temp loss, preserved fine touch |
| Tabes Dorsalis | Syphilitic dorsal column degeneration; loss of vibration/proprioception, preserved pain |

---

## ENDOCRINE SYSTEM — HORMONE MECHANISMS & SIGNALING

**[State]** Which most accurately describes the difference between peptide and steroid hormones?
- C. Peptide hormones typically activate second messenger systems; steroid hormones directly influence gene expression ✓

**[State]** Which hormone acts on nuclear receptors?
- A. Insulin / B. PTH / C. Cortisol ✓ / D. TSH

**[Regional]** Which hormone cannot be taken orally (denatured by digestive enzymes)? **Insulin**

**[State]** What limits the duration of peptide hormone signaling most?
- B. Conversion of cAMP to AMP by phosphodiesterase ✓

**[State]** Which hormone is both an amine and lipid-soluble, enabling nuclear receptor action? **Thyroxine (T4)**

**[State]** The enzyme inhibited by Viagra that breaks down cAMP and cGMP: **Phosphodiesterase**

**[State]** Iodine uptake into thyroid cells is powered by:
- B. Secondary active transport via the Na⁺ gradient by the basolateral NIS symporter ✓

**[State]** Why can steroids diffuse through the plasma membrane, unlike peptide hormones?
- B. Steroids are derived from cholesterol and are lipid-soluble, allowing them to dissolve in the phospholipid bilayer and diffuse directly into the cell ✓

**[State]** Which best describes the cyclic AMP second messenger cascade?
- B. Hormone binds GPCR → Gs activated → adenylyl cyclase → ATP → cAMP → PKA activated → phosphorylates target enzymes/transcription factors → cellular response; cAMP degraded by phosphodiesterase ✓

---

## ENDOCRINE SYSTEM — GLANDS, HORMONES & FEEDBACK

**[Regional]** Most hormones are regulated by: **Negative feedback**

**[Regional]** Which primarily regulates serum potassium? **Aldosterone**

**[State]** Which correctly traces how PTH increases intestinal calcium uptake?
- C. PTH → kidney → activation of vitamin D → calcitriol → increased intestinal Ca²⁺ absorption ✓

**[Regional]** Beta cells of the pancreas secrete: **Insulin and Amylin**

**[Regional]** The "satiety hormone": **Leptin**

**[Regional]** Which endocrine organ stores hormone-containing colloid extracellularly? **Thyroid gland**

**[State — Endocrine Gland Matching]**

| Gland | Key Secretions |
|---|---|
| Anterior Pituitary | GH, TSH, ACTH, FSH, LH, Prolactin, MSH |
| Posterior Pituitary | Oxytocin and ADH (synthesized by hypothalamus, stored/released here) |
| Thyroid | T3, T4 (follicular cells); calcitonin (parafollicular cells) |
| Parathyroid | PTH; increases blood calcium |
| Adrenal Medulla | Epinephrine (~80%), Norepinephrine (~20%) |
| Adrenal Cortex — Zona Glomerulosa | Mineralocorticoids (aldosterone) |
| Adrenal Cortex — Zona Fasciculata | Glucocorticoids (cortisol) |
| Adrenal Cortex — Zona Reticularis | Androgens (DHEA) |
| Thymus | Thymosin; T-cell maturation |
| Pineal Gland | Melatonin; circadian rhythm |
| Pancreas — Beta cells | Insulin, amylin |
| Pancreas — Alpha cells | Glucagon |
| Ovaries — Granulosa cells | Estrogen |
| Testes — Leydig cells | Testosterone |
| Testes — Sertoli cells | Inhibin B; estrogen in prepubertal males |

**[State]** Which best describes the relationship between prolactin and oxytocin in lactation?
- B. Prolactin stimulates milk synthesis in alveolar cells; oxytocin triggers milk ejection by contracting myoepithelial cells — both released in response to infant suckling ✓

**[State]** What does ADH do and where is it released from?
- B. ADH is synthesized by the hypothalamus (supraoptic nucleus) and released from the posterior pituitary; it inserts aquaporin-2 channels into the collecting duct, increasing water reabsorption and concentrating urine ✓

---

## ENDOCRINE — HPA AXIS & STRESS RESPONSE *(State scope)*

### Quick Reference: HPA Axis
```
STRESS / LOW CORTISOL
        ↓
Hypothalamus → CRH
        ↓ (hypophyseal portal)
Anterior Pituitary → ACTH
        ↓ (systemic)
Adrenal Cortex (zona fasciculata) → CORTISOL
        ↓ NEGATIVE FEEDBACK → ↓CRH, ↓ACTH
```

**[State]** Correct sequence of the HPA axis stress response:
- A. ACTH → CRH → cortisol
- B. CRH → ACTH → cortisol ✓
- C. Cortisol → CRH → ACTH
- D. ACTH → cortisol → CRH

**[State]** Cortisol exerts negative feedback primarily on:
- B. Hypothalamus (↓CRH) and anterior pituitary (↓ACTH) ✓

**[State]** A patient on long-term corticosteroid therapy. What happens to their HPA axis?
- B. CRH and ACTH decrease due to negative feedback; the adrenal glands atrophy ✓

**[State]** Primary danger of abruptly stopping long-term corticosteroid therapy:
- B. Adrenal insufficiency crisis — atrophied adrenal glands cannot produce sufficient cortisol ✓

**[State]** Which best describes how cortisol mobilizes energy during stress?
- B. Promotes gluconeogenesis in the liver, lipolysis in adipose, and protein catabolism in muscle, raising blood glucose ✓

**[State]** Cortisol suppresses the immune system. Which mechanism best explains this?
- B. Inhibits pro-inflammatory cytokines (IL-1, IL-6, TNF-α) and reduces prostaglandin synthesis by inhibiting phospholipase A2 ✓

**[State]** Which is NOT a consequence of chronically elevated cortisol?
- D. Increased hippocampal neurogenesis ✓ *(Chronic cortisol actually damages/atrophies the hippocampus)*

**[State]** During acute stress, which receptor mediates increased heart rate from epinephrine?
- B. β1 receptors on the SA node ✓

**[State]** Which correctly distinguishes acute from chronic stress response?
- A. Acute: epinephrine/norepinephrine from adrenal medulla (seconds–minutes); Chronic: cortisol from adrenal cortex (minutes–hours, sustained) ✓

**[State]** A patient has low cortisol, high ACTH, hyponatremia, hyperkalemia, hypoglycemia, and hypotension. Most likely diagnosis:
- B. Primary adrenal insufficiency (Addison's disease) ✓

**[State]** Why do Addison's disease patients develop hyperpigmentation?
- B. Low cortisol removes negative feedback → very high ACTH → ACTH shares precursor POMC with MSH, which stimulates melanocytes ✓

**[State — True/False]**
- In the HPA axis, cortisol exerts positive feedback on CRH and ACTH. **FALSE** *(negative feedback)*
- Long-term corticosteroid therapy causes adrenal atrophy due to ACTH suppression. **TRUE**
- Cortisol promotes insulin sensitivity in peripheral tissues. **FALSE** *(cortisol causes insulin resistance)*
- ACTH and MSH are both derived from the same precursor molecule, POMC. **TRUE**

**[State+]** Which correctly compares primary vs. secondary adrenal insufficiency?
- B. Primary: low cortisol, high ACTH, hyperpigmentation, no cortisol response to cosyntropin (adrenal glands damaged); Secondary: low cortisol, low ACTH, no hyperpigmentation, normal cortisol response to cosyntropin (pituitary/hypothalamus damaged, adrenal glands intact) ✓

---

## ENDOCRINE — RAAS & ALDOSTERONE *(State scope)*

### Quick Reference: RAAS Cascade
```
Low BP / Low Na⁺ / Sympathetic activation
        ↓
JG cells → RENIN
        ↓
Angiotensinogen (liver) → Angiotensin I → ACE (lung) → Angiotensin II
        ↓
① Adrenal zona glomerulosa → ALDOSTERONE → Na⁺/H₂O retention → ↑BP
② Vasoconstriction → ↑BP
③ Hypothalamus → ↑ADH → water retention
④ Thirst
```

**[State]** Which cells secrete renin, and what stimulates them?
- A. Juxtaglomerular (JG) cells; stimulated by low blood pressure, low Na⁺ delivery to the macula densa, and sympathetic (β1) activation ✓

**[State]** ACE is found predominantly in the: **Lung** *(pulmonary endothelium)*

**[State]** Aldosterone's mechanism of action in the collecting duct:
- B. Binds intracellular mineralocorticoid receptors → increases transcription of ENaC and Na⁺/K⁺-ATPase → increased Na⁺ reabsorption and K⁺ excretion ✓

**[State]** A patient with primary hyperaldosteronism (Conn's syndrome). Expected lab findings:
- B. Low renin, high aldosterone, hypokalemia, metabolic alkalosis ✓

**[State]** Why does primary hyperaldosteronism cause low renin?
- B. Elevated blood pressure and Na⁺/volume expansion from excess aldosterone suppresses renin via negative feedback ✓

**[State]** ADH's mechanism in the collecting duct:
- B. Inserts aquaporin-2 (AQP2) channels into the apical membrane, increasing water reabsorption ✓

**[State]** In SIADH, expected combination:
- C. Low serum Na⁺, high urine osmolality ✓

**[State — True/False]**
- Renin is produced by juxtaglomerular cells of the afferent arteriole. **TRUE**
- ACE is found primarily in the liver. **FALSE** *(primarily in the lung)*
- Aldosterone acts on nuclear receptors to increase transcription of ENaC. **TRUE**
- Primary hyperaldosteronism is associated with high renin and high aldosterone. **FALSE** *(low renin, high aldosterone)*

**[State+]** Trace the complete RAAS response triggered by hemorrhagic shock. Which sequence is correct?
- B. Low BP → JG cells → renin → angiotensinogen (liver) → angiotensin I → ACE (lung) → angiotensin II → vasoconstriction + aldosterone (adrenal zona glomerulosa) + ADH (hypothalamus/posterior pituitary) → Na⁺/H₂O retention + vasoconstriction → restored BP and volume ✓

---

## ENDOCRINE SYSTEM — DISORDERS

**[State]** Labs: elevated TSH, low T3. Best explanation:
- A. Primary hypothyroidism from thyroid gland failure ✓

**[State]** Disorder caused by TSH receptor autoantibodies: **Graves' disease**

**[State]** NOT an effect of Graves' Disease:
- C. Decreased bone reabsorption ✓ *(Graves' actually INCREASES bone reabsorption → osteoporosis risk)*

**[Regional]** Pituitary dwarfism is caused by: **GH hyposecretion**

**[State]** GH hypersecretion in adulthood: **Acromegaly** *(enlarged hands, feet, facial features)*

**[State]** Pheochromocytoma is a tumor of the: **Adrenal Medulla** *(excess catecholamines; episodic hypertension)*

**[Regional]** Type 1 Diabetes Mellitus results from: **Autoimmune destruction of insulin-producing beta cells**

**[State]** Diabetes insipidus is due to deficiency of: **ADH** *(excessive dilute urine)*

**[State — Case: Addison's Disease]** Harry: weakness, fatigue, dizziness, salt craving, persistent tan, weight loss.
1. Disease: **Addison's disease (primary adrenal insufficiency)**
2. Cause: **Hyposecretion of cortisol AND aldosterone** (usually autoimmune destruction of adrenal cortex)
3. Treatment: **Lifelong glucocorticoid replacement (hydrocortisone) + mineralocorticoid (fludrocortisone)**
4. Hyperpigmentation: **Yes — high ACTH (loss of negative feedback) → POMC-derived MSH → melanocyte stimulation**

---

## MASTER DISORDER REFERENCE

| Disorder | System | Key Features |
|---|---|---|
| Epilepsy | Nervous | Excessive neuronal discharge; seizures |
| Multiple Sclerosis | Nervous | Autoimmune CNS demyelination; optic neuritis; heat sensitivity |
| Alzheimer's Disease | Nervous | APOE4; cholinergic loss; amyloid plaques; memory deterioration |
| ALS | Nervous | UMN + LMN loss; mixed spastic/flaccid; progressive |
| Tay-Sachs | Nervous | HexA enzyme deficiency; lipid accumulation in neurons |
| Parkinson's Disease | Nervous | Substantia nigra dopamine loss; resting tremor, bradykinesia, rigidity |
| Cerebral Palsy | Nervous | Non-progressive brain damage at/near birth; motor disorder |
| Myasthenia Gravis | Nervous | Autoimmune ACh receptor blockade at NMJ; fatigable weakness |
| Brown-Séquard Syndrome | Nervous | Spinal hemisection: ipsilateral motor + DCML loss; contralateral spinothalamic loss |
| Syringomyelia | Nervous | Central spinal cavitation; cape-like pain/temp loss; fine touch preserved |
| Tabes Dorsalis | Nervous | Syphilitic dorsal column degeneration; vibration/proprioception lost; pain preserved |
| Graves' Disease | Endocrine | TSH receptor autoantibodies; hyperthyroidism; exophthalmos |
| Hashimoto's Disease | Endocrine | Autoimmune thyroid destruction; hypothyroidism |
| Addison's Disease | Endocrine | Low cortisol, high ACTH; hyperpigmentation; hyponatremia; hyperkalemia; hypotension |
| Cushing's Syndrome | Endocrine | High cortisol; moon face; central obesity; hyperglycemia; osteoporosis |
| Conn's Syndrome | Endocrine | Primary hyperaldosteronism; low renin; high aldosterone; hypokalemia; hypertension |
| Acromegaly | Endocrine | GH hypersecretion in adulthood; enlarged extremities and facial features |
| Pheochromocytoma | Endocrine | Adrenal medulla tumor; excess catecholamines; episodic hypertension |
| Diabetes Insipidus | Endocrine | ADH deficiency; excessive dilute urine; hypernatremia |
| SIADH | Endocrine | Excess ADH; low serum Na⁺; high urine osmolality |
| Glaucoma | Sensory | ↑Intraocular pressure; optic nerve degeneration |
| Cataracts | Sensory | Lens opacity; cloudy vision; night glare |
| Macular Degeneration | Sensory | Central retinal cell breakdown; central vision loss |
| Otitis Media | Sensory | Middle ear infection; common in children |
| Presbycusis | Sensory | Age-related cochlear hair cell loss; high-frequency hearing first |
| Otosclerosis | Sensory | Stapes fixation; autosomal dominant; conductive hearing loss |
| Ménière's Disease | Sensory | Endolymphatic hydrops; episodic vertigo; tinnitus; fluctuating hearing loss |
| Myopia | Sensory | Nearsightedness; eyeball too long; concave lens correction |
| Presbyopia | Sensory | Age-related near vision loss; decreased lens flexibility |
| Nyctalopia | Sensory | Night blindness; Vitamin A deficiency → rhodopsin deficiency |
| Anosmia | Sensory | Loss of smell; often post-viral or post-traumatic |
| Dysgeusia | Sensory | Distorted or altered sense of taste |

# Anatomy & Physiology – Fully State-Level Filtered Question Bank (Div B)

## Classification Key
🟢 Regionals  
🔵 State  
🔷 Upper-State (hard but appropriate)

---

### Q1 🔵
Which of the following most accurately describes the difference in mechanism of action between peptide and steroid hormones?

A. Peptide hormones bind to nuclear receptors; steroid hormones bind to membrane-bound receptors.  
B. Peptide hormones require intracellular transport proteins; steroid hormones are freely soluble in plasma.  
C. Peptide hormones typically activate second messenger systems; steroid hormones directly influence gene expression.  
D. Peptide hormones alter transcription directly; steroid hormones act only through enzymatic phosphorylation.  

---

### Q2 🔷
What is a major structural difference between catecholamines and thyroid hormones?

A. Catecholamines are lipid-soluble  
B. Thyroid hormones require plasma transport proteins  
C. Catecholamines bind nuclear receptors  
D. Thyroid hormones act within the cytoplasm  

---

### Q3 🟢
Why do peptide hormones act quickly but briefly?

A. They are stored in vesicles and cleared quickly  
B. They bind intracellular receptors  
C. They are derived from cholesterol  
D. They require transport proteins  

---

### Q4 🔷
Which of the following best explains how peptide hormone signaling is terminated inside the cell?

A. Hormones are degraded in the nucleus  
B. Second messengers are broken down, reducing the signal  
C. Hormones leave the bloodstream  
D. DNA transcription stops immediately  

---

### Q5 🔵
An individual presents with fatigue, weight gain, and bradycardia. Labs reveal elevated TSH and low T3. Which best explains this?

A. Primary hypothyroidism  
B. Secondary hypothyroidism  
C. Hypersecretion of TRH  
D. Hormone resistance

### Q6 🔵
A patient has low ACTH and low cortisol levels. Which gland is most likely malfunctioning?

A. Adrenal cortex  
B. Hypothalamus  
C. Pituitary  
D. Liver

A. Increase  
B. Decrease  
C. Stay the same  
D. No predictable change

D. Cortisol

B. Estrogen and FSH  
C. FSH only  
D. Progesterone

### Q10 🔵

D. Epinephrine

### Q11 🔵

A. Binds nuclear receptors  
B. Blocks intracellular signaling  
C. Activates membrane receptors to increase glucose uptake  
D. Directly alters DNA transcription

### Q12 🔵

A. PTH → thyroid → calcitonin  
B. PTH → liver → vitamin D

### Q13 🔵

A. Increases bone storage  
B. Decreases kidney reabsorption

### Q14 🔷
Why can a small amount of epinephrine produce a large cellular response?

A. It remains in the bloodstream longer  
B. It activates intracellular signaling that amplifies the effect

### Q15 🔵

A. Stored in vesicles

A. Blocking all hormones

# Science Olympiad Master Question Bank
*Compiled from: 2026 Invitationals (Troy, UCR, HPDI, UMSO, Solon, Archimedean, MVSO, Wolfpack, HG, HDSO, Seven Lakes, TAMU, Purdue, Carmel Valley, USC, UCR Div C, WashU) + Practice Exams (2010–2026) + State-Level Advanced Supplement*

---

## VISUAL PATHWAYS & PHOTOTRANSDUCTION

**[Troy]** List the following structures in the order light passes through them: lens, cornea, retina, pupil, optic nerve

**[Troy]** Which of the following is true of vision and phototransduction? Select all that apply.
- a. Damage to the optic chiasm will cause bitemporal hemianopia
- b. GMP is converted to cGMP by phosphodiesterase
- c. Transducin is a G-protein
- d. Na⁺ channels open when rhodopsin's retinal is converted to its all-trans form
- e. Vitamin A is converted into retinal for use in rhodopsins

**[Troy]** Michelle cuts the fibers of Larry's left nasal optic nerve before the optic chiasm. What visual field defect results?
- a. Loss of vision in left visual field in both eyes
- b. Loss of vision in right visual field in both eyes
- c. Loss of vision in left visual field in left eye
- d. Loss of vision in right visual field in left eye
- e. Loss of temporal vision in both eyes

**[HPDI]** Which best describes the underlying optical defect in astigmatism?
- A. The lens loses its elasticity and cannot accommodate for near vision
- B. The cornea or lens has an irregular curvature, causing light to focus at multiple points
- C. The eyeball is too short, causing light to focus behind the retina
- D. Increased intraocular pressure damages the optic nerve

**[UMSO]** Which cranial nerve carries visual information from the retina to the brain?
- a. Trochlear nerve (CN IV) / b. Optic nerve (CN II) / c. Oculomotor nerve (CN III) / d. Abducens nerve (CN VI)

**[Archimedean]** Which option correctly describes the pathway of visual information from the retina to the brain?
- A. Retina → Optic chiasm → Optic nerve → LGN → Visual cortex
- B. Retina → Optic nerve → Optic chiasm → Optic tract → LGN → Visual cortex
- C. Retina → LGN → Optic nerve → Optic tract → Visual cortex
- D. Retina → Optic tract → Optic chiasm → Visual cortex → LGN

**[Archimedean]** Damage to the optic chiasm typically results in which visual field defect?
- A. Monocular blindness / B. Homonymous hemianopia / C. Bitemporal hemianopia / D. Central scotoma

**[MVSO]** A lesion of the right optic tract causes:
- A) Right monocular vision loss / B) Left homonymous hemianopia / C) Right homonymous hemianopia / D) Bitemporal hemianopia

**[MVSO]** The principal excitatory neurotransmitter from photoreceptors to ON-bipolar cells is:
- A) Glutamate (sign-inverting via mGluR6) / B) GABA / C) Glycine / D) Acetylcholine

**[MVSO]** Aqueous humor outflow resistance increases mainly at the:
- A) Uveoscleral pathway / B) Trabecular meshwork / C) Ciliary body epithelium / D) Canal of Schlemm endothelium

**[MVSO]** A craniopharyngioma most classically causes which visual defect?
- A) Bitemporal hemianopia / B) Right homonymous hemianopia / C) Left superior quadrantanopia / D) Monocular scotoma

**[MVSO]** A lesion of Meyer's loop causes:
- A) Right superior quadrantanopia / B) Left superior quadrantanopia / C) Right inferior quadrantanopia / D) Left inferior quadrantanopia

**[MVSO]** During dark adaptation, photoreceptors exhibit:
- A) Low cGMP; Na⁺/Ca²⁺ channels closed; hyperpolarized
- B) High cGMP; Na⁺/Ca²⁺ channels open; depolarized
- C) High cAMP; K⁺ channels open; hyperpolarized
- D) Low Ca²⁺; glutamate release decreased

**[MVSO]** Which statements about intrinsically photosensitive retinal ganglion cells (ipRGCs) are accurate? Select all that apply.
- A) Express melanopsin, ~480 nm peak, sustained photoresponses
- B) Depolarize via G-protein cascade distinct from rods/cones; contribute to circadian rhythm and pupil light reflex
- C) Project first to LGN then to non-image-forming areas
- D) Convey irradiance signals for mood/sleep regulation
- E) Rely on canonical rod cascade (transducin → PDE6)

**[HG]** True/False: The fovea contains more rods than cones.

**[HG]** True/False: The "blind spot" of the eye is caused by the optic disk.

**[Practice — MCQ converted from FRQ]** Which of the following best explains why damage to the optic chiasm produces bitemporal hemianopia?
- A. The chiasm carries only temporal retinal fibers, which represent the nasal visual field
- B. Both optic nerves are compressed symmetrically, causing monocular blindness bilaterally
- C. Nasal retinal fibers from both eyes cross at the chiasm; damaging these eliminates the temporal visual fields of both eyes
- D. Temporal retinal fibers from both eyes cross at the chiasm; damaging these eliminates the nasal visual fields
**Answer: C**

**[Practice — MCQ converted from FRQ]** A patient has no image-forming vision but their pupillary light reflex is preserved. Which cells are most likely responsible for maintaining the reflex?
- A. Rods, which bypass the LGN and project directly to the pretectal nucleus
- B. Cones, which project via the optic tract to the Edinger-Westphal nucleus
- C. Intrinsically photosensitive retinal ganglion cells (ipRGCs), which project to the pretectal nucleus independently of image-forming pathways
- D. Horizontal cells, which sustain tonic signaling when photoreceptors are non-functional
**Answer: C**

**[Practice — MCQ converted from FRQ]** Bobby fell asleep in a dark movie theater. When he walked into bright sunlight, he was momentarily blinded. Which best explains this?
- A. His cones had bleached all available photopigment in the dark and needed time to regenerate
- B. His rods, which had accumulated rhodopsin in the dark, were overwhelmed by the sudden bright light and could not rapidly inactivate
- C. His pupils were maximally dilated and could not constrict fast enough to reduce incoming light
- D. The sudden shift caused both rod and cone photopigments to simultaneously isomerize, flooding the visual cortex
**Answer: B**

**[Practice — MCQ converted from FRQ]** A woman used eye drops and could smell and "taste" them afterward. Which pathway best explains this?
- A. The drops crossed the blood-brain barrier and directly stimulated the olfactory bulb
- B. The drops diffused through the conjunctiva into the bloodstream, reaching taste receptors
- C. The drops drained from the lacrimal system through the nasolacrimal duct into the nasal cavity, where volatile molecules reached the olfactory epithelium
- D. The drops activated trigeminal chemoreceptors, which share a pathway with taste fibers to the cortex
**Answer: C**

- D. Sympathetic activation has no effect on pupil size

**[MVSO — FRQ converted to MCQ]** A patient receives phenylephrine (α1 agonist) eye drops. Their pupil dilates more than expected. Which condition does this suggest?
- A. CN III palsy — the efferent limb is damaged, causing supersensitivity of the sphincter pupillae
- B. Adie pupil — postganglionic parasympathetic denervation causes denervation supersensitivity of the dilator muscle
- C. Horner syndrome — loss of sympathetic input causes supersensitivity of α1 receptors in the radial dilator muscle
- D. Optic neuritis — loss of afferent signal removes the inhibitory drive on the dilator muscle
**Answer: C**

**[MVSO — FRQ converted to MCQ]** Pilocarpine (muscarinic agonist) is used to distinguish Adie pupil from CN III palsy. In Adie pupil, a dilute solution of pilocarpine (0.1%) constricts the pupil, but not in CN III palsy. Why?
- A. In CN III palsy, the iris sphincter muscle itself is damaged and cannot respond to any agonist
- B. In Adie pupil, postganglionic denervation causes upregulation (supersensitivity) of muscarinic receptors on the sphincter pupillae, making it hypersensitive to dilute pilocarpine
- C. CN III palsy involves damage to the pretectal nucleus, eliminating central muscarinic signaling
- D. Adie pupil involves damage to the ciliary ganglion's preganglionic input, making dilute pilocarpine ineffective
**Answer: B**

## OLFACTORY SYSTEM

**[Troy]** What does it mean for an odorant to be volatile, and why does this matter for smell?
- A. Volatile molecules are large and water-soluble, allowing them to bind mucous glycoproteins
- B. Volatile molecules have low vapor pressure and remain in solution, enabling sustained receptor binding
- C. Volatile molecules readily enter the gas phase at room temperature, enabling them to reach and dissolve in olfactory mucus to bind GPCRs
- D. Volatile molecules are lipid-soluble and cross the olfactory epithelium directly without dissolving in mucus
**Answer: C**

- B. Nasal cavity → binds ion channel receptor → Na⁺ influx → depolarization → CN I → thalamus → olfactory cortex
- C. Nasal cavity → dissolves in mucus → binds GPCR → cGMP → CNG channel closure → hyperpolarization → CN I → olfactory bulb
- D. Nasal cavity → binds GPCR → Golf → phospholipase C → IP3 → Ca²⁺ release → CN I → amygdala directly
**Answer: A**

- B. Olfactory signals first relay through the thalamus, where they are integrated with emotional memories before reaching the cortex
- C. The olfactory bulb projects directly to the amygdala and hippocampus (limbic structures) without a mandatory thalamic relay

**Answer: C**

**Answer: C**

- D. The medial geniculate nucleus, eliminating thalamic relay of auditory information
**Answer: B**

## BRAINSTEM & CRANIAL NERVE NUCLEI

**[Troy / UCR Div B / UCR Div C]** Which nucleus in the medulla receives visceral sensory input from CN VII, IX, and X?

- a. Contralateral facial paralysis / b. Bilateral weakness / c. Only limb ataxia / d. Vertigo and ipsilateral hearing loss / e. Ipsilateral facial paralysis and loss of taste

**[Troy]** Möbius Syndrome (CN VI + VII underdevelopment) — which would NOT be affected?
- a. Facial paralysis / b. Movement of eyes / c. Sensation of the eyelid / d. All of the above

**[Seven Lakes]** Which cranial nerve innervates the dura mater and mediates the pain of meningitis?
- A) III / B) V / C) VI / D) IX / E) XI

**[HG]** A student touches their eyelid and their eye blinks (corneal reflex). Which cranial nerve carries the sensory signal?
- A) facial nerve (VII) / B) glossopharyngeal (IX) / C) oculomotor (III) / D) trigeminal (V)

**[Advanced]** Which brainstem nucleus serves as the primary relay station for visceral sensory information, including taste, baroreceptor input, and gut mechanoreceptors?
- A. Nucleus ambiguus / B. Dorsal motor nucleus of vagus / C. Nucleus tractus solitarius (NTS) / D. Hypoglossal nucleus

**[Advanced]** A patient suffers a lateral medullary infarction (Wallenberg syndrome) damaging the nucleus ambiguus. Which deficit is expected?
- A. Loss of taste from the anterior 2/3 of the tongue
- B. Dysphagia and hoarseness due to paralysis of pharyngeal and laryngeal muscles
- C. Loss of voluntary tongue movements
- D. Loss of parasympathetic input to the heart

**[Advanced]** The dorsal motor nucleus of vagus provides parasympathetic preganglionic output primarily to:
- A. Salivary glands and lacrimal glands
- B. Heart, lungs, and abdominal viscera (up to the splenic flexure)
- C. Bladder and reproductive organs
- D. Pupillary constriction and lens accommodation

**[Advanced]** A lesion of the dorsal motor nucleus of vagus would most likely cause:
- A. Loss of taste / B. Ipsilateral facial droop / C. Reduced parasympathetic tone to heart and gut (tachycardia, decreased GI motility) / D. Inability to move the tongue

**[Advanced]** The Edinger-Westphal nucleus provides parasympathetic output via CN III. A lesion here would cause:
- A. Ipsilateral ptosis and downward gaze
- B. Fixed, dilated (mydriatic) pupil due to loss of pupillary constriction
- C. Loss of lateral gaze
- D. Facial muscle paralysis

**[Advanced]** Which midbrain nucleus is degenerated in Parkinson's disease, and what neurotransmitter does it produce?
- A. Red nucleus; GABA / B. Substantia nigra pars compacta; dopamine / C. Locus coeruleus; norepinephrine / D. Raphe nuclei; serotonin

**[Advanced]** The periaqueductal gray (PAG) suppresses pain via:
- A. Glutamate and GABA released into the dorsal horn
- B. Enkephalins and endorphins acting via raphe nuclei and locus coeruleus
- C. Dopamine and serotonin released directly onto nociceptors
- D. Acetylcholine and substance P inhibiting C-fiber transmission

**[Advanced]** The inferior olivary nucleus sends climbing fibers to the cerebellum. Its primary role is:
- A. Relaying auditory information to the cortex
- B. Transmitting error signals to the cerebellum for motor learning and adaptation
- C. Coordinating eye movements
- D. Processing vestibular signals for balance

**[Advanced]** Which nucleus in the pons is the FIRST site of binaural processing in the auditory pathway?
- A. Cochlear nucleus / B. Superior olivary complex / C. Inferior colliculus / D. Medial geniculate nucleus

**[Advanced]** Lateral medullary (Wallenberg) syndrome results from occlusion of the PICA. Which combination of deficits is characteristic?
- A. Ipsilateral facial pain/temperature loss, contralateral body pain/temperature loss, ipsilateral ataxia, dysphagia, Horner syndrome
- B. Ipsilateral hemiplegia, contralateral sensory loss, aphasia
- C. Bilateral sensory loss with preserved motor function
- D. Contralateral facial droop with ipsilateral body weakness

**[Advanced]** Which brainstem structure is the primary source of norepinephrine in the brain?
- A. Raphe nuclei / B. Substantia nigra / C. Locus coeruleus / D. Periaqueductal gray

**[Advanced]** Raphe nuclei are distributed throughout the brainstem. Their primary neurotransmitter is:
- A. Dopamine / B. Norepinephrine / C. Serotonin / D. Acetylcholine

**[Advanced]** True/False: The nucleus ambiguus provides motor output to pharyngeal and laryngeal muscles via CN IX and X. *(True)*

**[Advanced]** True/False: The locus coeruleus is the primary source of serotonin in the brain. *(False — raphe nuclei)*

**[Advanced]** True/False: The Edinger-Westphal nucleus controls pupillary constriction via parasympathetic fibers in CN III. *(True)*

**[Advanced]** True/False: The inferior colliculus is a relay station in the auditory pathway, while the superior colliculus mediates visual reflexes. *(True)*

**[Advanced]** True/False: The periaqueductal gray activates pain by releasing substance P. *(False — PAG suppresses pain via endogenous opioids)*

**[Advanced — MCQ converted from FRQ]** A patient presents with hoarseness, dysphagia, loss of gag reflex, ipsilateral facial numbness, contralateral body pain/temperature loss, and ipsilateral Horner syndrome. Which artery is most likely occluded?
- A. Anterior inferior cerebellar artery (AICA)
- B. Posterior inferior cerebellar artery (PICA)
- C. Basilar artery
- D. Posterior cerebral artery (PCA)
**Answer: B** *(Wallenberg syndrome = PICA occlusion)*

**[Advanced — MCQ converted from FRQ]** A patient with damage to both the locus coeruleus and raphe nuclei would most likely experience which combination of symptoms?
- A. Spastic paralysis and hyperreflexia
- B. Inability to form new declarative memories and visual hallucinations
- C. Profound sleepiness, depression, impaired pain modulation, and blunted stress response
- D. Anosmia and bitemporal hemianopia
**Answer: C**

**[Practice — Cranial Nerve Reference]**

| Test / Function | Cranial Nerve |
|---|---|
| Tongue symmetry | CN XII (Hypoglossal) |
| Strength/evenness of shrug | CN XI (Accessory) |
| Biting pencil, skin sensation, corneal reflex | CN V (Trigeminal) |
| Smelling aromatic oils | CN I (Olfactory) |
| Rinne/Weber tuning fork, balance | CN VIII (Vestibulocochlear) |
| "Ah!", uvula position, gag reflex | CN IX/X (Glossopharyngeal/Vagus) |
| Taste posterior 1/3 tongue | CN IX (Glossopharyngeal) |
| Motor to facial muscles | CN VII (Facial) |
| Eye abduction | CN VI (Abducens) |
| Eye movement (most), pupillary constriction | CN III (Oculomotor) |
| Parasympathetic to thoracic/abdominal viscera | CN X (Vagus) |

How many pairs of cranial nerves? **12 pairs** | How many pairs of spinal nerves? **31 pairs**

## SPINAL CORD TRACTS *(Advanced)*

### Quick Reference Table

| Tract | Direction | Modality | Decussation Site | Side at Cortex |
|---|---|---|---|---|
| Dorsal Column–Medial Lemniscal (DCML) | Ascending | Fine touch, vibration, 2-point discrimination, conscious proprioception | Medulla (nucleus gracilis/cuneatus) | Contralateral |
| Spinothalamic (Anterolateral) | Ascending | Pain, temperature, crude touch, pressure | Spinal cord (within 1–2 segments of entry) | Contralateral |
| Dorsal Spinocerebellar | Ascending | Unconscious proprioception — lower limb | Does NOT decussate | Ipsilateral |
| Ventral Spinocerebellar | Ascending | Unconscious proprioception — lower limb | Decussates twice (net ipsilateral) | Ipsilateral |
| Lateral Corticospinal | Descending | Voluntary motor — limb muscles | Pyramidal decussation in medulla | Contralateral |
| Anterior Corticospinal | Descending | Voluntary motor — axial/trunk muscles | Decussates at spinal level | Bilateral |
| Rubrospinal | Descending | Motor coordination — limb movements | Midbrain | Contralateral |
| Reticulospinal | Descending | Muscle tone, posture, autonomic | Variable (bilateral) | Bilateral |
| Vestibulospinal | Descending | Balance, posture, extensor tone | Does NOT decussate | Ipsilateral |
| Tectospinal | Descending | Head/neck reflexes to visual/auditory stimuli | Midbrain | Contralateral |

**[Advanced]** Which ascending spinal tract carries fine touch, vibration sense, and conscious proprioception?
- A. Spinothalamic tract / B. Dorsal column–medial lemniscal pathway / C. Ventral spinocerebellar tract / D. Rubrospinal tract

**[Advanced]** A patient can feel pain and temperature in their right arm but cannot feel light touch or vibration. Where is the lesion most likely?
- A. Right dorsal column at cervical level / B. Right spinothalamic tract / C. Left dorsal column at cervical level / D. Left spinothalamic tract

**[Advanced]** The dorsal column–medial lemniscal pathway decussates at which location?
- A. Spinal cord at level of entry / B. Midbrain / C. Medulla (nucleus gracilis and cuneatus) / D. Thalamus

**[Advanced]** The spinothalamic tract decussates at which location?
- A. Spinal cord, within 1–2 segments of the level of entry / B. Medulla / C. Pons / D. Midbrain

**[Advanced]** A patient suffers a complete right-sided hemisection of the spinal cord at T4 (Brown-Séquard syndrome). Which set of deficits is expected?
- A. Loss of all sensation bilaterally below T4; preserved motor function
- B. Loss of pain/temp on the right; loss of fine touch/proprioception on left below T4; ipsilateral motor loss right
- C. Loss of pain/temp on the left; loss of fine touch/proprioception and voluntary motor on the right, below T4
- D. Loss of all sensation and motor on the left below T4

**[Advanced]** In Brown-Séquard syndrome, why is motor loss ipsilateral to the lesion while pain/temp loss is contralateral?
- A. Corticospinal tract decussates in the spinal cord; spinothalamic decussates in the medulla
- B. Corticospinal tract decussates in the medulla before descending; spinothalamic decussates at the spinal cord level of entry
- C. Both tracts decussate at the same level; the difference is due to fiber diameter
- D. Motor fibers travel in the dorsal column; sensory fibers travel in the lateral column

**[Advanced]** Syringomyelia typically presents with loss of pain and temperature in a "cape-like" distribution across both shoulders and arms, with preserved fine touch. Why is fine touch preserved?
- A. The dorsal columns are most resistant to compression by the syrinx
- B. Fine touch fibers are myelinated and therefore immune to central cavitation
- C. The crossing spinothalamic fibers at the anterior commissure are disrupted first, while dorsal column fibers traveling ipsilaterally remain intact
- D. The syrinx forms posteriorly, sparing anterior spinothalamic fibers

**[Advanced]** Which descending tract crosses in the pyramidal decussation of the medulla and primarily controls voluntary movement of the distal limb muscles?
- A. Anterior corticospinal tract / B. Lateral corticospinal tract / C. Rubrospinal tract / D. Reticulospinal tract

**[Advanced]** A stroke affects the right motor cortex. Which limb(s) will show weakness?
- A. Right arm and right leg / B. Left arm and left leg / C. Right arm only / D. Left leg only

**[Advanced]** Which descending tract primarily regulates posture, balance, and extensor muscle tone and does NOT decussate?
- A. Lateral corticospinal tract / B. Rubrospinal tract / C. Vestibulospinal tract / D. Tectospinal tract

**[Advanced]** After synapsing in the medulla, DCML fibers cross as the medial lemniscus and ascend to which thalamic nucleus?
- A. Lateral geniculate nucleus / B. Medial geniculate nucleus / C. Ventral posterior lateral (VPL) nucleus / D. Pulvinar nucleus

**[Advanced]** Which spinal cord tract carries unconscious proprioceptive information from the lower limbs to the cerebellum WITHOUT decussating?
- A. Ventral spinocerebellar tract / B. Dorsal spinocerebellar tract / C. Spinothalamic tract / D. Dorsal column

**[Advanced]** Upper motor neuron (UMN) lesions produce which set of signs?
- A. Flaccid paralysis, muscle atrophy, fasciculations, absent reflexes
- B. Spastic paralysis, hyperreflexia, Babinski sign, minimal atrophy
- C. Flaccid paralysis, hyperreflexia, intact sensation
- D. Spastic paralysis, absent reflexes, sensory loss

**[Advanced]** Lower motor neuron (LMN) lesions produce which set of signs?
- A. Flaccid paralysis, muscle atrophy, fasciculations, absent/reduced reflexes
- B. Spastic paralysis, hyperreflexia, Babinski sign
- C. Spastic paralysis, minimal atrophy, preserved reflexes
- D. Loss of pain only, preserved motor function

**[Advanced]** ALS affects both UMN and LMN. Which combination of signs would you expect?
- A. Pure spastic paralysis with hyperreflexia only
- B. Pure flaccid paralysis with atrophy only
- C. Mixed spastic and flaccid paralysis with both hyperreflexia and atrophy/fasciculations
- D. Sensory loss with preserved motor function

**[Advanced]** The anterior spinal artery supplies primarily which region of the spinal cord?
- A. Dorsal columns
- B. Anterior two-thirds, including anterior horn, lateral corticospinal tract, and spinothalamic tract
- C. Posterior columns only
- D. The entire cord equally

**[Advanced]** A patient with tabes dorsalis (syphilitic degeneration of dorsal columns) would have difficulty with which of the following?
- A. Feeling pain from a pin prick
- B. Detecting vibration from a tuning fork and knowing the position of their limbs with eyes closed
- C. Moving their limbs voluntarily
- D. Feeling heat from a warm object

**[Advanced — True/False]**
- The dorsal column pathway decussates at the spinal cord level of entry. *(False — decussates in the medulla)*
- In Brown-Séquard syndrome, motor loss is ipsilateral to the lesion. *(True)*
- Syringomyelia first disrupts dorsal column function. *(False — syrinx forms centrally, disrupting crossing spinothalamic fibers first)*
- The dorsal spinocerebellar tract conveys unconscious proprioception and does not decussate. *(True)*
- UMN lesions produce flaccid paralysis and muscle atrophy. *(False — LMN lesions do; UMN = spastic paralysis)*
- The lateral corticospinal tract decussates in the pyramidal decussation of the medulla. *(True)*
- The vestibulospinal tract enhances extensor muscle tone to maintain upright posture. *(True)*

**[Advanced — MCQ converted from FRQ]** A patient loses pain and temperature sensation on the left side of their body below T8, and loses fine touch and voluntary motor function on the right side below T8. Which best describes this presentation?
- A. Complete spinal cord transection at T8
- B. Syringomyelia at T8, disrupting central crossing fibers bilaterally
- C. Brown-Séquard syndrome — right-sided hemisection at T8, with ipsilateral motor/DCML loss and contralateral spinothalamic loss
- D. Anterior spinal artery infarction at T8, sparing the dorsal columns
**Answer: C**

**[Advanced — MCQ converted from FRQ]** Compare the DCML pathway and the spinothalamic tract. Which statement is CORRECT?
- A. Both decussate in the medulla; DCML carries pain and temperature while spinothalamic carries fine touch
- B. DCML decussates in the medulla and carries fine touch/proprioception; spinothalamic decussates in the spinal cord and carries pain/temperature
- C. DCML decussates in the spinal cord; spinothalamic decussates in the medulla; both reach the VPL thalamus
- D. Both carry pain and temperature but differ in fiber diameter and conduction velocity
**Answer: B**

## PROPRIOCEPTION MECHANICS *(Advanced)*

### Quick Reference Table

| Receptor | Location | Stimulus | Afferent Fiber | Response |
|---|---|---|---|---|
| Muscle spindle | Within muscle belly, parallel to extrafusal fibers | Muscle stretch (length change) | Ia (primary — nuclear bag) & II (secondary — nuclear chain) | Stretch reflex; inhibition of antagonist |
| Golgi tendon organ (GTO) | Musculotendinous junction, in series | Muscle tension / force | Ib | Autogenic inhibition; activation of antagonist |
| Pacinian corpuscle | Deep dermis, joints, periosteum | Vibration, rapid pressure change | Aβ | Vibration / deep pressure |
| Ruffini ending | Dermis, joint capsule | Sustained pressure, skin stretch, joint position | Aβ | Joint angle, skin stretch |
| Free nerve endings | Throughout tissue | Pain, temperature, crude touch | Aδ (fast/sharp pain) & C (slow/dull pain) | Nociception |

**[Advanced]** Muscle spindles detect which stimulus, and what is their primary functional role?
- A. Muscle tension; they inhibit the contracting muscle to prevent injury
- B. Muscle length/stretch; they trigger a reflexive contraction of the stretched muscle via the stretch reflex
- C. Joint angle; they send conscious position sense to the somatosensory cortex
- D. Tendon force; they coordinate antagonist muscle activation

**[Advanced]** Golgi tendon organs (GTOs) are in series with extrafusal muscle fibers. Their primary role is to:
- A. Detect muscle length change and trigger stretch reflexes
- B. Detect excessive muscle tension and reflexively inhibit the contracting muscle (autogenic inhibition)
- C. Provide conscious proprioception to the cerebellum
- D. Regulate muscle tone during sleep

**[Advanced]** The patellar tendon reflex is a monosynaptic stretch reflex. Which afferent fiber type carries the signal from the muscle spindle to the spinal cord?
- A. Aδ fibers / B. C fibers / C. Ib fibers / D. Ia fibers

**[Advanced]** In the patellar reflex, when the quadriceps contracts, the hamstrings must simultaneously relax. This is accomplished by:
- A. Direct inhibition via a Ib fiber from the GTO of the hamstring
- B. Reciprocal inhibition via an inhibitory interneuron, activated by the Ia afferent from the quadriceps spindle
- C. Direct co-activation by the same Ia fiber
- D. Descending inhibition from the motor cortex

**[Advanced]** Which afferent fiber type carries signals from the Golgi tendon organ?
- A. Ia / B. Ib / C. II / D. Aδ

**[Advanced]** A muscle spindle contains two types of intrafusal fibers. Which pairing is correct?
- A. Nuclear bag → static; nuclear chain → dynamic
- B. Nuclear bag → dynamic; nuclear chain → static
- C. Both nuclear bag and nuclear chain detect static stretch equally
- D. Nuclear chain → dynamic; nuclear bag is only for proprioception to the cerebellum

**[Advanced]** Gamma motor neurons (γ-MNs) innervate intrafusal fibers of the muscle spindle. Their role is to:
- A. Directly contract the muscle to produce movement
- B. Adjust the sensitivity of the muscle spindle by maintaining intrafusal fiber tension during muscle contraction
- C. Inhibit the Golgi tendon organ signal
- D. Relay proprioceptive information to the cerebellum

**[Advanced]** During a sustained muscle contraction, without gamma motor neuron co-activation, what would happen to spindle sensitivity?
- A. The spindle would go slack and stop firing, losing the ability to detect further stretch
- B. The spindle would increase firing due to the tension on the tendon
- C. The GTO would directly activate the spindle to compensate
- D. Nothing — spindle sensitivity is independent of muscle length

**[Advanced]** A patient's deep tendon reflexes are hyperactive (hyperreflexia). This is most consistent with:
- A. Lower motor neuron lesion — loss of the reflex arc
- B. Upper motor neuron lesion — loss of descending inhibition on the reflex arc
- C. Golgi tendon organ lesion — loss of autogenic inhibition
- D. Muscle spindle degeneration — loss of stretch sensing

**[Advanced]** Conscious proprioception from muscle spindles travels to the cortex via which pathway?
- A. Spinothalamic tract → VPL thalamus → somatosensory cortex
- B. Dorsal column–medial lemniscal pathway → VPL thalamus → somatosensory cortex
- C. Dorsal spinocerebellar tract → cerebellum
- D. Rubrospinal tract → motor cortex

**[Advanced]** Unconscious proprioception reaches the cerebellum via which tracts?
- A. Corticospinal and rubrospinal tracts
- B. Spinothalamic tract
- C. Dorsal and ventral spinocerebellar tracts
- D. Dorsal column–medial lemniscal pathway

**[Advanced]** A patient has difficulty with fine coordination of movement but has intact conscious sensation of body position. Which pathway is most likely disrupted?
- A. Dorsal column–medial lemniscal pathway / B. Spinocerebellar tracts / C. Spinothalamic tract / D. Lateral corticospinal tract

**[Advanced]** Which receptor detects vibration and rapid pressure changes in deep skin and periosteum?
- A. Pacinian corpuscles (Aβ fibers) / B. Meissner's corpuscles / C. Merkel discs / D. Ruffini endings

**[Advanced — True/False]**
- Muscle spindles are in series with extrafusal muscle fibers and detect tension. *(False — in parallel; GTOs are in series and detect tension)*
- Ia afferent fibers from muscle spindles produce monosynaptic excitation of alpha motor neurons in the stretch reflex. *(True)*
- Golgi tendon organs produce autogenic inhibition — they inhibit the muscle in which they reside. *(True)*
- Gamma motor neurons directly produce skeletal muscle contraction. *(False — they regulate spindle sensitivity by contracting intrafusal fibers)*
- The dorsal spinocerebellar tract decussates and reaches the contralateral cerebellum. *(False — ipsilateral, does NOT decussate)*
- Reciprocal inhibition is mediated by Ib fibers. *(False — mediated by Ia fibers acting on an inhibitory interneuron)*
- Loss of conscious proprioception with preserved pain sensation indicates a dorsal column lesion. *(True)*

**[Advanced — MCQ converted from FRQ]** A patient cannot feel vibration in their feet or detect the direction their big toe is pointing without looking, but can still feel a pin prick. Which correctly identifies the affected pathway and the reason for this dissociation?
- A. Spinothalamic tract; pain fibers decussate ipsilaterally while vibration fibers remain contralateral
- B. Dorsal column–medial lemniscal pathway; it carries fine touch, vibration, and proprioception but NOT pain, which travels in the spinothalamic tract
- C. Dorsal spinocerebellar tract; cerebellar damage spares pain but eliminates proprioception
- D. Lateral corticospinal tract; motor loss is misinterpreted as loss of proprioception
**Answer: B**

**[Advanced — MCQ converted from FRQ]** Which best explains what would happen if gamma motor neurons were selectively blocked during a voluntary contraction?
- A. The muscle would be unable to contract at all, as gamma neurons are required for alpha motor neuron activation
- B. The muscle would contract but the spindle would go slack and lose its ability to detect further stretch or changes in muscle length, impairing reflex control
- C. The GTO would increase its firing rate to compensate for the loss of spindle feedback
- D. Reciprocal inhibition would be eliminated, causing co-contraction of agonist and antagonist
**Answer: B**

**[Advanced — MCQ converted from FRQ]** Explain the full patellar tendon reflex arc. Which option correctly orders every component?
- A. Tap → patellar tendon stretches → Ia fiber → dorsal horn synapse → alpha MN → quadriceps contracts; Ib fiber → inhibitory interneuron → hamstring relaxes
- B. Tap → quadriceps stretches → Ia fiber → direct monosynaptic synapse on alpha MN (quadriceps) → quadriceps contracts; Ia fiber → inhibitory interneuron → hamstring alpha MN inhibited → hamstring relaxes
- C. Tap → quadriceps stretches → Ib fiber → inhibitory interneuron → alpha MN → quadriceps contracts
- D. Tap → GTO stretches → Ia fiber → monosynaptic synapse → quadriceps contracts; Ia fiber → excitatory interneuron → hamstring contracts
**Answer: B**

## NEUROPHYSIOLOGY — ACTION POTENTIALS & MEMBRANE POTENTIALS

**[Practice]** What primarily causes the neuron's resting membrane potential to be negative?
- a. The Na⁺/K⁺ pump pumps out 3 Na⁺ for every 2 K⁺
- b. Open K⁺ leak channels allow more K⁺ to leave than Na⁺ can enter
- c. Intracellular proteins carry negative charge
- d. Voltage-gated Na⁺ channels are closed at rest

**[Practice]** What is the approximate resting potential of a neuron?
- a. −90 mV / b. −82 mV / c. −70 mV / d. +20 mV

**[Practice]** Initial depolarization of an action potential is caused by:
- a. K⁺ entering / b. K⁺ exiting / c. Na⁺ entering / d. Na⁺ exiting

**[Practice]** Which correctly describes the function of the sodium-potassium pump?
- a. Allows passive movement of ions / b. Hyperpolarizes by allowing Cl⁻ in
- c. Actively exports 3 Na⁺ and imports 2 K⁺ using ATP
- d. Actively transports 3 K⁺ in and 2 Na⁺ out

**[Practice]** Which statement about the refractory period is TRUE?
- a. During absolute refractory, a strong stimulus can trigger another AP
- b. Relative refractory period occurs when Na⁺ channels are open and inactivated
- c. During the relative refractory period, K⁺ channels are open
- d. The refractory period affects the entire neuron equally

**[Practice]** True/False: Action potentials happen in an all-or-nothing fashion. *(True)*

**[Practice — MCQ converted from FRQ]** Which best describes what happens to a neuron's membrane during each phase of an action potential?
- A. Depolarization: K⁺ channels open → K⁺ exits → membrane goes positive; Repolarization: Na⁺ channels inactivate, K⁺ channels close; Hyperpolarization: Na⁺ channels reopen
- B. Depolarization: voltage-gated Na⁺ channels open → Na⁺ enters → membrane potential rises to ~+30 mV; Repolarization: Na⁺ channels inactivate and K⁺ channels open → K⁺ exits → membrane returns toward resting; Hyperpolarization: K⁺ channels remain open briefly → membrane overshoots below resting potential
- C. Depolarization: Ca²⁺ enters through voltage-gated channels; Repolarization: Ca²⁺ is pumped back; Hyperpolarization: Cl⁻ enters briefly
- D. Depolarization: Na⁺/K⁺ pump reverses direction; Repolarization: pump restores normal ion distribution; Hyperpolarization: pump overshoots
**Answer: B**

**[Practice — MCQ converted from FRQ]** Which best describes the all-or-nothing law?
- A. A neuron either fires at maximum possible amplitude or produces a graded potential, depending on stimulus duration
- B. A neuron either fires a full action potential (at a fixed amplitude regardless of stimulus strength) or does not fire at all, depending on whether the membrane potential reaches threshold
- C. An action potential either propagates along the entire axon or is degraded before reaching the terminal, depending on myelination
- D. A neuron either releases all of its neurotransmitter vesicles upon stimulation or releases none
**Answer: B**

---

## SALTATORY CONDUCTION & MYELINATION

**[Practice]** In saltatory conduction, action potentials propagate along what part of myelinated axons?
- a. Along the entire axon / b. Nodes of Ranvier / c. Only through the myelin sheath / d. Dendrites only

**[Practice]** Which part of the neuron integrates incoming signals and initiates an action potential?
- a. Axon terminal / b. Axon hillock / c. Dendrite / d. Myelin sheath

**[Practice]** Which glial cell type is primarily responsible for myelinating axons in the CNS?
- a. Schwann cells / b. Astrocytes / c. Oligodendrocytes / d. Ependymal cells

**[Practice]** True/False: The myelin sheath in the CNS is produced by Schwann cells. *(False — oligodendrocytes)*

**[Practice — MCQ converted from FRQ]** A patient has relapsing-remitting neurological symptoms including optic neuritis and limb weakness that worsen with heat. MRI shows demyelinating lesions disseminated in time and space. CSF shows oligoclonal bands. Which correctly explains the pathophysiology?
- A. Autoimmune destruction of Schwann cells in the PNS impairs saltatory conduction in peripheral nerves
- B. Autoimmune attack on oligodendrocytes in the CNS destroys the myelin sheath; without myelin, action potentials cannot jump between nodes of Ranvier and propagation slows or fails; heat worsens symptoms because elevated temperature further impairs ion channel function in already-compromised axons
- C. Degeneration of alpha motor neurons eliminates efferent signaling to muscles, causing the observed motor symptoms
- D. Viral infection of astrocytes disrupts the blood-brain barrier, causing inflammatory infiltrates that compress axons
**Answer: B** *(Multiple sclerosis)*

## SYNAPTIC TRANSMISSION & NEUROTRANSMITTERS

**[Practice]** Neurotransmitters are released from axon terminals via:
- a. Osmosis / b. Active transport / c. Diffusion / d. Transcytosis / e. Exocytosis

**[Practice]** An IPSP occurs when the membrane becomes more permeable to:
- a. Potassium ions / b. Sodium ions / c. Calcium ions

**[Practice]** What neurotransmitter is released at the neuromuscular junction? *(Acetylcholine)*

**[Practice]** Which of the following is NOT a catecholamine? **a. Serotonin**

**[Practice]** All catecholamines are synthesized from: **b. Tyrosine**

**[Practice]** True/False: Voltage-gated calcium channels at the axon terminal trigger neurotransmitter release. *(True)*

**[Practice — MCQ converted from FRQ]** Which neurotransmitter category does acetylcholine belong to, and what most distinguishes it from classical biogenic amines?
- A. Catecholamine; it is derived from tyrosine rather than tryptophan
- B. Indolamine; it contains an indole ring unlike other cholinergic compounds
- C. Quaternary amine; it is derived from choline rather than an aromatic amino acid, making it highly polar and unable to cross the blood-brain barrier
- D. Neuropeptide; it is synthesized in the cell body and transported to the terminal in secretory vesicles
**Answer: C**

**[Practice — MCQ converted from FRQ]** If inhibitory neurotransmitters (e.g., GABA) were completely dysfunctional in the CNS, what would be the expected consequence?
- A. Widespread neuronal silence, as neurons require inhibitory input to initiate action potentials
- B. Uncontrolled, excessive neuronal firing potentially manifesting as seizures, since excitatory inputs would no longer be counterbalanced
- C. Flaccid paralysis, as inhibitory neurons are required to activate alpha motor neurons
- D. Loss of all sensory perception, since sensory relay neurons in the thalamus are inhibitory
**Answer: B**

## PHARMACOLOGY OF THE NERVOUS SYSTEM

**[Practice]** How does caffeine enhance alertness? **d. Blocking adenosine receptors**

**[Practice]** The primary effect of cocaine is that it blocks re-uptake of: **a. Monoamines**

**[Practice]** Botulinum toxin impairs neurotransmission by: **c. Preventing vesicular release of ACh**

**[Practice — MCQ converted from FRQ]** A patient is exposed to an organophosphate nerve agent. Which sequence correctly describes the mechanism of toxicity?
- A. Nerve agent → blocks nicotinic ACh receptors → prevents muscle depolarization → flaccid paralysis
- B. Nerve agent → inhibits acetylcholinesterase → ACh accumulates at synapses → overstimulation of muscarinic and nicotinic receptors → SLUDGE symptoms (salivation, lacrimation, urination, defecation, GI distress, emesis) and muscle paralysis
- C. Nerve agent → blocks voltage-gated Na⁺ channels → prevents action potential propagation → sensory and motor loss
- D. Nerve agent → inhibits GABA release → uncontrolled excitation → seizures without peripheral effects
**Answer: B**

## GLIAL CELLS

**[Practice]** Which type of cells line the ventricles and spinal cord? **c. Ependymal cells**

**[Practice]** Which type of glial cell is most abundant in the CNS? **a. Astrocytes**

**[Practice]** Which glial cell type acts as an immune cell in the CNS? **a. Microglia**

**[Practice]** True/False: Neurons outnumber neuroglia 3 to 1. *(False — neuroglia outnumber neurons)*

**[Practice — MCQ converted from FRQ]** What is the primary role of microglial cells in the CNS?
- A. Myelination of CNS axons and maintenance of the nodes of Ranvier
- B. Production and circulation of cerebrospinal fluid through the ventricles
- C. Immune surveillance — they are the resident macrophages of the CNS, phagocytosing cellular debris, pathogens, and apoptotic neurons
- D. Metabolic support of neurons via the astrocyte-neuron lactate shuttle
**Answer: C**

---

## NEUROANATOMY — BRAIN REGIONS, LOBES & TRACTS

**[Practice]** Which brain region regulates vital functions like breathing and heartbeat? *(Medulla oblongata)*

**[Practice]** Patients with damage to Wernicke's area have difficulty:
- a. Coordinating limb movement / b. Generating speech / c. Recognizing faces / d. Understanding language

**[Practice]** A stroke in the MCA would most likely affect: **c. Face and arm movement**

**[Practice]** A patient demonstrates difficulty forming new memories but recalls old ones. Which region is most likely affected? **c. Hippocampus**

**[Practice]** Which lists the correct path of CSF? **b. Lateral ventricles → third ventricle → fourth ventricle → subarachnoid space**

**[Practice]** True/False: The spinothalamic tract transmits information about body position to the cerebellum. *(False — carries pain and temperature; proprioception via dorsal columns)*

**[Practice]** True/False: The dorsal root contains motor fibers. *(False — sensory/afferent; motor in ventral root)*

**[Practice — MCQ converted from FRQ]** Which best explains the roles of Broca's and Wernicke's areas?
- A. Broca's area (temporal lobe): interprets incoming language; Wernicke's area (frontal lobe): formulates motor speech commands
- B. Broca's area (frontal lobe): formulates motor plans for speech production; Wernicke's area (temporal lobe): comprehends language — damage to Broca's = expressive aphasia; damage to Wernicke's = receptive aphasia with fluent but nonsensical speech
- C. Both areas are in the parietal lobe and share the function of integrating sensory and motor language signals
- D. Broca's area processes written language; Wernicke's area processes spoken language
**Answer: B**

---

## AUTONOMIC NERVOUS SYSTEM

**[Solon]** The prevertebral ganglia are innervated by which nerve?
- a. Greater splanchnic nerve / b. Vagus nerve / c. Sympathetic chain / d. Phrenic nerve

**[Practice]** Which part of the vertebrate nervous system is most involved in the fight-or-flight response? **a. Sympathetic**

**[Practice]** Which is NOT true of the enteric nervous system?
- a. Subdivision of PNS / b. Contains sensory and motor neurons / c. Cannot function without input from CNS / d. Located within the digestive tract

**[Practice]** During a fight-or-flight response, the adrenal medulla releases epinephrine and norepinephrine in which ratio? **c. 80% epinephrine, 20% norepinephrine**

**[Practice — MCQ converted from FRQ]** Which correctly contrasts the sympathetic and parasympathetic divisions?
- A. Sympathetic: long preganglionic, short postganglionic fibers; ganglia near target organs; uses ACh at all synapses
- B. Parasympathetic: short preganglionic, long postganglionic fibers; ganglia in the sympathetic chain; uses norepinephrine at postganglionic synapses
- C. Sympathetic: short preganglionic, long postganglionic fibers; ganglia in the sympathetic chain; uses ACh preganglionic and norepinephrine postganglionic (mostly); Parasympathetic: long preganglionic, short postganglionic fibers; ganglia near/in target organs; uses ACh at both synapses
- D. Both divisions use norepinephrine at postganglionic synapses; they differ only in fiber length
**Answer: C**

## SLEEP & EEG

**[Practice]** Which EEG waveform is most prominent during deep sleep? **d. Delta**

**[Practice]** Sleep spindles and K-complexes are most characteristic of which sleep stage? **b. N2**

**[Practice]** Which is NOT associated with REM sleep? **e. Body repair** *(occurs in N3 deep sleep)*

**[Practice — MCQ converted from FRQ]** Which correctly describes the 5 stages of sleep and their associated brainwave patterns?
- A. N1: Beta; N2: Alpha; N3: Theta; N4: Delta; REM: Alpha
- B. N1: Theta (light sleep, hypnic jerks); N2: Sleep spindles and K-complexes; N3: Delta waves (slow-wave, deep, restorative sleep); REM: Beta-like desynchronized waves, vivid dreaming, muscle atonia
- C. N1: Delta; N2: Theta; N3: Alpha; REM: Gamma; NREM: Beta throughout
- D. All NREM stages share uniform alpha activity; REM is distinguished by theta waves and muscle atonia
**Answer: B**
