## SOMATOSENSORY SYSTEM & PROPRIOCEPTION

**[Practice]** Which touch receptor senses pressure and vibration changes deep in the skin? **a. Pacinian corpuscles**

**[Practice]** What are proprioceptors? **c. Receptors that detect stretch and stimulate a reflex contraction**

**[Practice — MCQ converted from FRQ]** Why do heart attack patients feel pain in both the skin and the heart?
- A. The heart's sensory fibers directly synapse on skin nociceptors via gap junctions
- B. Referred pain occurs because cardiac pain fibers (C and Aδ) converge on the same spinal cord segments (T1–T4) as somatic fibers from the skin of the left arm and chest — the brain misattributes the signal to the skin
- C. The vagus nerve carries pain from the heart directly to the somatosensory cortex, which maps it onto the body surface
- D. Inflammatory cytokines from the ischemic heart travel through the bloodstream and sensitize skin nociceptors in the left arm
**Answer: B**

**[Practice — MCQ converted from FRQ]** Why is it sometimes difficult to distinguish very cold from very warm objects touching the skin?
- A. Both cold and warm stimuli activate the same A-beta mechanoreceptors at extreme intensities
- B. At temperatures outside the normal range of cold receptors (10–35°C) and warm receptors (30–45°C), both stimuli activate nociceptors (free nerve endings), which do not discriminate between extreme cold and extreme heat
- C. The thalamus processes extreme temperatures in the same VPM nucleus used for taste, causing cross-modal confusion
- D. Pacinian corpuscles detect both temperature extremes via the same vibration-sensitive channel
**Answer: B**

## NEUROLOGICAL DISORDERS

**[Practice]** In which disease do sclerotic sheaths result in poor conduction of action potentials? **a. Multiple Sclerosis**

**[Practice]** What disease is caused by the loss of dopamine? **a. Parkinson's Disease**

**[Practice]** In ALS, which neurons are lost? **b. Upper and lower motor neurons**

**[Practice — Disorder Matching]**

| Disorder | Key Features |
|---|---|
| Epilepsy | Sudden bursts of electrochemical activity; seizures |
| Multiple Sclerosis | Autoimmune demyelination; optic neuritis, limb weakness, worsens with heat |
| Alzheimer's Disease | APOE gene risk; cholinergic neuron loss; memory deterioration |
| ALS | Upper and lower motor neuron loss; progressive paralysis |
| Tay-Sachs | Enzyme deficiency; cannot break down lipids in nerve cells |
| Parkinson's Disease | Dopamine loss in substantia nigra; tremors, rigidity |
| Cerebral Palsy | Brain damage before/during/shortly after birth; movement disorders |
| Myasthenia Gravis | ACh receptor blockade by antibodies; muscle weakness |

## ENDOCRINE SYSTEM — HORMONE MECHANISMS & SIGNALING

**[Practice]** Which most accurately describes the difference in mechanism between peptide and steroid hormones?
- A. Peptide hormones bind to nuclear receptors; steroid hormones bind to membrane-bound receptors
- B. Peptide hormones require intracellular transport proteins; steroid hormones are freely soluble
- C. Peptide hormones typically activate second messenger systems; steroid hormones directly influence gene expression
- D. Peptide hormones alter transcription directly; steroid hormones act only through enzymatic phosphorylation

**[Practice]** Which hormone has a mechanism that acts on nuclear receptors?
- A. Insulin / B. PTH / C. Cortisol / D. TSH

**[Practice]** Which hormone cannot be taken orally due to being denatured by digestive enzymes? **A. Insulin**

**[Practice]** Which limits the duration of peptide hormone signaling most?
- A. Receptor regulation of lysosomal digestion
- B. Conversion of cAMP to AMP by phosphodiesterase
- C. Desensitization of GPCRs
- D. Competitive inhibition at receptor site

**[Practice]** Which hormone is both an amine and lipid-soluble, enabling nuclear receptor action? **B. Thyroxine (T4)**

**[Practice]** What enzyme, inhibited by Viagra, breaks down cAMP and cGMP? **B. Phosphodiesterase**

**[Practice]** Uptake of iodine into thyroid cells is powered by:
- A. Primary active transport via ATP hydrolysis by apical NIS symporter
- B. Secondary active transport via the Na⁺ gradient by the basolateral NIS symporter
- C. Facilitated diffusion through iodide channels
- D. Secondary active transport via proton-coupled transport at the apical NIS symporter

**[Practice — MCQ converted from FRQ]** Why can steroids diffuse through the plasma membrane, unlike peptide hormones?
- A. Steroids are very small molecules (<100 Da) that pass through aquaporin channels
- B. Steroids are derived from cholesterol and are lipid-soluble, allowing them to dissolve in the phospholipid bilayer and diffuse directly into the cell without a membrane receptor
- C. Steroids are positively charged and are drawn through the membrane by the negative resting potential of the cell
- D. Steroids are transported into the cell by specific active transport proteins embedded in the membrane
**Answer: B**

**[Practice — MCQ converted from FRQ]** Which best describes the cyclic AMP second messenger cascade from hormone binding to cellular response?
- A. Hormone binds membrane receptor → receptor directly phosphorylates target proteins → gene expression changes
- B. Hormone binds GPCR → Gs protein activated → adenylyl cyclase activated → ATP → cAMP → protein kinase A (PKA) activated → phosphorylates target enzymes/transcription factors → cellular response; cAMP degraded by phosphodiesterase
- C. Hormone binds GPCR → Gq protein activated → phospholipase C → IP3 + DAG → Ca²⁺ release → calmodulin activation → cAMP
- D. Hormone binds nuclear receptor → hormone-receptor complex enters nucleus → directly activates adenylyl cyclase → cAMP → PKA
**Answer: B**

## ENDOCRINE SYSTEM — GLANDS, HORMONES & FEEDBACK

**[Practice]** The release of most hormones is regulated by the: **B. Negative feedback loop**

**[Practice]** Which represents negative feedback? **D. X stimulates Y, then Y inhibits X**

**[Practice]** Which hormone primarily regulates serum potassium levels? **B. Aldosterone**

**[Practice]** Which hormone increases renal calcium reabsorption and decreases phosphate reabsorption? **C. PTH**

**[Practice]** Which correctly traces how PTH increases intestinal calcium uptake?
- A. PTH → thyroid → calcitonin → increased Ca absorption
- B. PTH → liver → vitamin D3 → increased Ca uptake
- C. PTH → kidney → activation of vitamin D → calcitriol → increased Ca absorption
- D. PTH → adrenal cortex → aldosterone → sodium retention

**[Practice]** Which hormones are secreted from the beta cells of the pancreas? **A. Insulin and Amylin**

**[Practice]** Which hormone is known as the "satiety hormone"? **D. Leptin**

**[Practice]** Which endocrine organ stores hormone-containing colloid extracellularly before secretion? *(Thyroid gland)*

**[Practice — Endocrine Gland Matching]**

| Gland | Key Secretions / Notes |
|---|---|
| Anterior Pituitary | GH, TSH, ACTH, FSH, LH, Prolactin, MSH |
| Posterior Pituitary | Oxytocin and ADH (synthesized by hypothalamus) |
| Thyroid | T3, T4 (follicular cells); calcitonin (parafollicular cells) |
| Parathyroid | PTH; increases blood calcium |
| Adrenal Medulla | Epinephrine (~80%), Norepinephrine (~20%) |
| Adrenal Cortex — Zona Glomerulosa | Mineralocorticoids (aldosterone) |
| Adrenal Cortex — Zona Fasciculata | Glucocorticoids (cortisol) |
| Adrenal Cortex — Zona Reticularis | Androgens (DHEA) |
| Thymus | Thymosin; promotes T-cell maturation |
| Pineal Gland | Melatonin; circadian rhythm regulation |
| Pancreas — Beta cells | Insulin, amylin |
| Pancreas — Alpha cells | Glucagon |
| Ovaries — Granulosa cells | Estrogen |
| Testes — Leydig cells | Testosterone |
| Testes — Sertoli cells | Inhibin B; estrogen in prepubertal males |

**[Practice — MCQ converted from FRQ]** Which best describes the relationship between oxytocin and prolactin in lactation?
- A. Oxytocin stimulates milk production in alveolar cells; prolactin triggers milk ejection via myoepithelial cells
- B. Prolactin stimulates milk synthesis in alveolar cells; oxytocin triggers milk ejection by contracting myoepithelial cells — both are released in response to infant suckling
- C. Prolactin and oxytocin are released by the anterior pituitary simultaneously and have identical targets in breast tissue
- D. Oxytocin inhibits prolactin via dopamine, so the two hormones never function simultaneously
**Answer: B**

**[Practice — MCQ converted from FRQ]** What does ADH do and where is it released from?
- A. ADH (antidiuretic hormone / vasopressin) is synthesized in the anterior pituitary and released from the neurohypophysis; it promotes glycogenolysis in the liver
- B. ADH is synthesized by the hypothalamus (supraoptic nucleus) and released from the posterior pituitary; it inserts aquaporin-2 channels into the collecting duct, increasing water reabsorption and concentrating urine
- C. ADH is synthesized in the adrenal cortex and acts on the loop of Henle to increase Na⁺ reabsorption
- D. ADH is synthesized by the juxtaglomerular cells and released in response to high plasma osmolality; it triggers aldosterone release
**Answer: B**

## ENDOCRINE SYSTEM — HPA AXIS & STRESS RESPONSE *(Advanced)*

### Quick Reference: HPA Axis

```
STRESS / LOW CORTISOL
        ↓
Hypothalamus → CRH
        ↓ (hypophyseal portal system)
Anterior Pituitary → ACTH
        ↓ (systemic circulation)
Adrenal Cortex (zona fasciculata) → CORTISOL
        ↓
Cortisol → NEGATIVE FEEDBACK to hypothalamus (↓CRH) and anterior pituitary (↓ACTH)
```

**[Advanced]** Which correctly describes the sequence of the HPA axis stress response?
- A. ACTH → CRH → cortisol / B. CRH → ACTH → cortisol / C. Cortisol → CRH → ACTH / D. ACTH → cortisol → CRH

**[Advanced]** Cortisol exerts negative feedback primarily on which two structures?
- A. Adrenal cortex and kidney
- B. Hypothalamus (↓CRH) and anterior pituitary (↓ACTH)
- C. Thyroid and parathyroid
- D. Liver and adrenal medulla

**[Advanced]** A patient is on long-term exogenous corticosteroid therapy. What happens to their endogenous HPA axis?
- A. CRH and ACTH increase to compensate
- B. CRH and ACTH decrease due to negative feedback; the adrenal glands atrophy
- C. Cortisol production in the adrenal gland increases
- D. The adrenal medulla produces more epinephrine to compensate

**[Advanced]** If a patient abruptly stops long-term corticosteroid therapy, what is the primary danger?
- A. Cushing's syndrome from rebound cortisol hypersecretion
- B. Adrenal insufficiency crisis — the atrophied adrenal glands cannot produce sufficient cortisol
- C. Graves' disease from thyroid overstimulation
- D. Hypoaldosteronism only, with preserved cortisol

**[Advanced]** In Cushing's disease (pituitary adenoma), what result is expected from a dexamethasone suppression test?
- A. Cortisol is fully suppressed because the pituitary responds normally to feedback
- B. Cortisol is NOT suppressed because the autonomous pituitary adenoma continues to overproduce ACTH despite negative feedback
- C. ACTH increases further due to the adenoma being stimulated by dexamethasone
- D. Cortisol decreases but ACTH increases

**[Advanced]** Which best describes the mechanism by which cortisol mobilizes energy during stress?
- A. It directly phosphorylates glucose transporters to increase cellular uptake
- B. It promotes gluconeogenesis in the liver, lipolysis in adipose, and protein catabolism in muscle, raising blood glucose
- C. It stimulates insulin release to drive glucose into cells
- D. It activates glycogenesis to store extra energy for later use

**[Advanced]** Cortisol suppresses the immune system. Which mechanism best explains this?
- A. Cortisol directly kills T cells
- B. Cortisol inhibits pro-inflammatory cytokines (IL-1, IL-6, TNF-α) and reduces prostaglandin synthesis by inhibiting phospholipase A2
- C. Cortisol activates natural killer cells to destroy immune cells
- D. Cortisol upregulates MHC expression, causing immune tolerance

**[Advanced]** Chronic stress leads to persistently elevated cortisol. Which is NOT a consequence?
- A. Insulin resistance and increased risk of Type 2 diabetes
- B. Immunosuppression and increased susceptibility to infection
- C. Bone loss (osteoporosis) due to inhibition of osteoblasts
- D. Increased hippocampal neurogenesis, improving memory *(Chronic cortisol actually damages the hippocampus)*

**[Advanced]** During the acute stress response, which receptor mediates increased heart rate from epinephrine?
- A. α1 receptors on the SA node / B. β1 receptors on the SA node / C. M2 muscarinic receptors / D. β2 receptors in the heart

**[Advanced]** Which correctly distinguishes the acute (fast) stress response from the chronic (slow) stress response?
- A. Acute: epinephrine/norepinephrine from adrenal medulla (seconds); Chronic: cortisol from adrenal cortex (minutes to hours)
- B. Acute: cortisol (minutes); Chronic: epinephrine (hours)
- C. Both responses involve cortisol at different concentrations
- D. The acute response is parasympathetic; the chronic response is sympathetic

**[Advanced]** A patient is found unconscious with low cortisol, high ACTH, hyponatremia, hyperkalemia, hypoglycemia, and hypotension. What is the most likely diagnosis?
- A. Cushing's disease / B. Primary adrenal insufficiency (Addison's disease) / C. SIADH / D. Pheochromocytoma

**[Advanced]** The same patient is given exogenous ACTH (cosyntropin stimulation test), and cortisol levels do NOT rise. What does this confirm?
- A. The adrenal glands themselves are damaged and cannot respond to ACTH — confirming primary (adrenal) insufficiency
- B. The pituitary is not producing ACTH
- C. The hypothalamus is not producing CRH
- D. The patient has secondary adrenal insufficiency

**[Advanced]** Why do patients with Addison's disease develop hyperpigmentation?
- A. Low cortisol directly stimulates melanocytes
- B. Low cortisol removes negative feedback → very high ACTH → ACTH shares a precursor (POMC) with MSH, which stimulates melanocytes
- C. Aldosterone deficiency causes skin pigment deposition
- D. Adrenal androgens cause hyperpigmentation when they are absent

**[Advanced — True/False]**
- In the HPA axis, cortisol exerts positive feedback on CRH and ACTH to amplify the stress response. *(False — negative feedback)*
- Long-term corticosteroid therapy causes adrenal atrophy due to suppression of ACTH. *(True)*
- Cortisol promotes insulin sensitivity in peripheral tissues. *(False — cortisol causes insulin resistance)*
- In the cosyntropin stimulation test, a failure of cortisol to rise confirms primary adrenal insufficiency. *(True)*
- ACTH and MSH are both derived from the same precursor molecule, POMC. *(True)*

**[Advanced — MCQ converted from FRQ]** A patient with small-cell lung cancer has high cortisol and high ACTH, and cortisol does NOT suppress with a HIGH-dose dexamethasone suppression test. Which best explains this?
- A. The pituitary adenoma is too large to respond to dexamethasone's negative feedback
- B. The ectopic ACTH-secreting tumor is autonomous and does not express glucocorticoid receptors, so it is unresponsive to dexamethasone negative feedback — cortisol cannot suppress ACTH, and cortisol levels remain high
- C. The adrenal gland has hypertrophied and is now secreting cortisol autonomously, independent of ACTH
- D. Dexamethasone upregulates cortisol synthesis in the zona fasciculata at high doses
**Answer: B** *(Ectopic ACTH syndrome)*

**[Advanced — MCQ converted from FRQ]** Which correctly compares primary vs. secondary adrenal insufficiency?
- A. Primary: low cortisol, low ACTH, no hyperpigmentation, normal cosyntropin response; Secondary: low cortisol, high ACTH, hyperpigmentation, abnormal cosyntropin response
- B. Primary: low cortisol, high ACTH, hyperpigmentation, no response to cosyntropin; Secondary: low cortisol, low ACTH, no hyperpigmentation, normal response to cosyntropin
- C. Primary: high cortisol, high ACTH, no hyperpigmentation; Secondary: low cortisol, low ACTH, hyperpigmentation
- D. Primary and secondary are distinguished only by ACTH levels; cortisol is equally low in both
**Answer: B**

---

## ENDOCRINE SYSTEM — RAAS & ALDOSTERONE *(Advanced)*

### Quick Reference: RAAS Cascade

```
LOW BP / LOW Na⁺ / Sympathetic activation
        ↓
Juxtaglomerular (JG) cells → RENIN
        ↓
Angiotensinogen (liver) → Angiotensin I
        ↓ ACE (lung)
Angiotensin II
        ↓
1. Adrenal cortex (zona glomerulosa) → ALDOSTERONE → Na⁺/H₂O retention → ↑BP
2. Vasoconstriction → ↑BP directly
3. Hypothalamus → ↑ADH → water retention
4. Hypothalamus → thirst
5. Sympathetic NS → ↑HR, ↑contractility
```

**[Advanced]** Which cells in the kidney secrete renin, and what stimulates their activation?
- A. Juxtaglomerular (JG) cells; stimulated by low blood pressure, low Na⁺ delivery to the macula densa, and sympathetic (β1) activation
- B. Mesangial cells; stimulated by angiotensin II
- C. Collecting duct principal cells; stimulated by ADH
- D. Podocytes; stimulated by aldosterone

**[Advanced]** ACE is found predominantly in the:
- A. Liver / B. Lung / C. Kidney / D. Adrenal cortex

**[Advanced]** ACE inhibitors (e.g., lisinopril) treat hypertension. Which correctly describes their mechanism?
- A. They block aldosterone receptors in the collecting duct
- B. They prevent conversion of angiotensin I to angiotensin II, reducing vasoconstriction and aldosterone release
- C. They directly block renin secretion from the juxtaglomerular cells
- D. They block β1 receptors, reducing renin release

**[Advanced]** Aldosterone acts on principal cells of the collecting duct. What is its mechanism of action?
- A. Binds membrane receptors → cAMP cascade → opens Na⁺ channels
- B. Binds intracellular mineralocorticoid receptors → increases transcription of ENaC (Na⁺ channels) and Na⁺/K⁺-ATPase → increased Na⁺ reabsorption and K⁺ excretion
- C. Directly phosphorylates aquaporin-2, increasing water permeability
- D. Activates the thiazide-sensitive NaCl cotransporter in the distal tubule

**[Advanced]** A patient with primary hyperaldosteronism (Conn's syndrome) presents with hypertension. What lab abnormalities would you expect?
- A. High renin, high aldosterone, hyperkalemia
- B. Low renin, high aldosterone, hypokalemia, metabolic alkalosis
- C. High renin, low aldosterone, hyperkalemia
- D. Low renin, low aldosterone, hyponatremia

**[Advanced]** Why does primary hyperaldosteronism cause low renin?
- A. Aldosterone directly destroys JG cells
- B. The elevated blood pressure and sodium/volume expansion from excess aldosterone suppresses renin via negative feedback
- C. Aldosterone directly inhibits renin gene transcription
- D. The adrenal adenoma produces a renin-inhibiting factor

**[Advanced]** ADH's mechanism in the collecting duct is to:
- A. Increase Na⁺ reabsorption via ENaC insertion
- B. Insert aquaporin-2 (AQP2) channels into the apical membrane, increasing water reabsorption
- C. Stimulate active Cl⁻ reabsorption
- D. Activate the Na⁺/K⁺/2Cl⁻ cotransporter in the thick ascending limb

**[Advanced]** In SIADH, what is the most likely combination of serum sodium and urine osmolality?
- A. Low serum Na⁺, low urine osmolality / B. High serum Na⁺, high urine osmolality / C. Low serum Na⁺, high urine osmolality / D. High serum Na⁺, low urine osmolality

**[Advanced — True/False]**
- Renin is produced by the juxtaglomerular cells of the afferent arteriole. *(True)*
- ACE is found primarily in the liver, where it converts angiotensin I to angiotensin II. *(False — primarily in the lung)*
- Aldosterone acts on nuclear receptors to increase transcription of sodium channel subunits (ENaC). *(True)*
- Primary hyperaldosteronism (Conn's syndrome) is associated with high renin and high aldosterone. *(False — low renin, high aldosterone)*
- During acute stress, epinephrine is released within seconds from the adrenal medulla via direct sympathetic innervation. *(True)*

**[Advanced — MCQ converted from FRQ]** Trace the complete RAAS response triggered by hemorrhagic shock. Which sequence is correct?
- A. Low BP → adrenal cortex directly secretes aldosterone → kidney retains Na⁺ → BP restored
- B. Low BP → JG cells → renin → angiotensinogen (liver) → angiotensin I → ACE (lung) → angiotensin II → [vasoconstriction + aldosterone (adrenal zona glomerulosa) + ADH (hypothalamus/posterior pituitary)] → Na⁺/H₂O retention + vasoconstriction → restored BP and volume
- C. Low BP → ADH released → angiotensin II formed in the kidney → renin released → aldosterone stimulated
- D. Low BP → sympathetic activation → direct aldosterone release → renin stimulated as a secondary response
**Answer: B**

**[Advanced — MCQ converted from FRQ]** A patient has a cortisol-secreting adrenal adenoma. Which lab profile would you predict?
- A. High cortisol, high ACTH, high renin, hypokalemia, hypertension
- B. High cortisol, low ACTH (suppressed by negative feedback), normal aldosterone, hyperglycemia, hypertension, insulin resistance, central obesity
- C. High cortisol, high ACTH, low aldosterone, hyperkalemia, hypotension
- D. High cortisol, normal ACTH, high aldosterone, hypokalemia, metabolic alkalosis
**Answer: B** *(Cushing's syndrome from adrenal adenoma: autonomous cortisol suppresses ACTH)*

## ENDOCRINE SYSTEM — DISORDERS

**[Practice]** An individual presents with fatigue, weight gain, and bradycardia. Labs: elevated TSH, low T3. Which best explains this?
- A. Primary hypothyroidism from thyroid gland failure / B. Secondary hypothyroidism / C. Hypersecretion of TRH / D. Hyposensitivity to thyroid hormone

**[Practice]** Which disorder is caused by TSH receptor autoantibodies? **A. Graves' disease**

**[Practice]** Which is NOT an effect of Graves' Disease?
- A. Enlargement of extrinsic eye muscles / B. Louder heart sounds / C. Decreased bone reabsorption / D. Muscle atrophy and weakness

**[Practice]** Pituitary dwarfism is caused by: **C. GH hyposecretion**

**[Practice]** A condition caused by hypersecretion of somatotrophin in adulthood: **B. Acromegaly**

**[Practice]** Pheochromocytomas are a tumor of the: **D. Adrenal Medulla**

**[Practice]** Type 1 Diabetes Mellitus results from: **B. Immune system destruction of insulin-producing beta cells**

**[Practice]** Diabetes insipidus is due to a deficiency of: **C. ADH**

**[Practice — Case: Lisa (Type 1 Diabetes)]** Blood glucose 545 mg/dL; pH 7.23; urine + glucose and ketones. 1) Diagnosis? 2) Why elevated glucose? 3) Why fruity breath? 4) Describe mechanism of glucagon action.

**[Practice — Case: Harry Sullivan (Addison's Disease)]** Weakness, fatigue, dizziness, salt craving, persistent tan, weight loss. 1) Disease? 2) Due to hyposecretion of cortisol? 3) Best treatment? 4) Hyperpigmentation due to hypersecretion of ACTH?

## MASTER DISORDER REFERENCE

# Science Olympiad Master Question Bank
*Compiled from: 2026 Invitationals (Troy, UCR, HPDI, UMSO, Solon, Archimedean, MVSO, Wolfpack, HG, HDSO, Seven Lakes, TAMU, Purdue, Carmel Valley, USC, UCR Div C, WashU) + Practice Exams (2010–2013, 2016–2017, 2021, Mesa-Wilson 2017, Eastside 2013, Holly Springs 2025–26, Dulles HS 2025, FCS Innovation 2025, Bergen County Academies, Columbia Jan 2026)*

---

## VISUAL PATHWAYS & PHOTOTRANSDUCTION

**[Troy]** List the following structures in the order light passes through them: lens, cornea, retina, pupil, optic nerve

**[Troy]** Which of the following is true of vision and phototransduction? Select all that apply.
- a. Damage to the optic chiasm will cause bitemporal hemianopia
- b. GMP is converted to cGMP by phosphodiesterase
- c. Transducin is a G-protein
- d. Na⁺ channels open when rhodopsin's retinal is converted to its all-trans form
- e. Vitamin A is converted into retinal for use in rhodopsins

**[Troy]** Michelle cuts the fibers of Larry's left nasal optic nerve before the optic chiasm. What visual field defect results?
- a. Loss of vision in left visual field in both eyes
- b. Loss of vision in right visual field in both eyes
- c. Loss of vision in left visual field in left eye
- d. Loss of vision in right visual field in left eye
- e. Loss of temporal vision in both eyes

**[HPDI]** Which best describes the underlying optical defect in astigmatism?
- A. The lens loses its elasticity and cannot accommodate for near vision
- B. The cornea or lens has an irregular curvature, causing light to focus at multiple points
- C. The eyeball is too short, causing light to focus behind the retina
- D. Increased intraocular pressure damages the optic nerve

**[UMSO]** Which cranial nerve carries visual information from the retina to the brain?
- a. Trochlear nerve (CN IV) / b. Optic nerve (CN II) / c. Oculomotor nerve (CN III) / d. Abducens nerve (CN VI)

**[Archimedean]** Which option correctly describes the pathway of visual information from the retina to the brain?
- A. Retina → Optic chiasm → Optic nerve → LGN → Visual cortex
- B. Retina → Optic nerve → Optic chiasm → Optic tract → LGN → Visual cortex
- C. Retina → LGN → Optic nerve → Optic tract → Visual cortex
- D. Retina → Optic tract → Optic chiasm → Visual cortex → LGN

**[Archimedean]** Damage to the optic chiasm typically results in which visual field defect?
- A. Monocular blindness / B. Homonymous hemianopia / C. Bitemporal hemianopia / D. Central scotoma

**[MVSO]** A lesion of the right optic tract causes:
- A) Right monocular vision loss / B) Left homonymous hemianopia / C) Right homonymous hemianopia / D) Bitemporal hemianopia

**[MVSO]** The principal excitatory neurotransmitter from photoreceptors to ON-bipolar cells is:
- A) Glutamate (sign-inverting via mGluR6) / B) GABA / C) Glycine / D) Acetylcholine

**[MVSO]** Aqueous humor outflow resistance increases mainly at the:

- a. Hearing pitch differences / b. Detecting rotational movement / c. Detecting linear acceleration / d. Equalizing pressure / e. Transmitting vibrations to the cochlea

- a. Saccule and utricle / b. Cochlea / c. Semicircular canals / d. Tympanic membrane / e. Oval window

- a. Smell / b. Balance only / c. Taste / d. Hearing / e. None

- a. Apex / b. Base / c. Tympanic membrane / d. Auditory nerve / e. Semicircular canals

- a. Cochlea / b. Semicircular canals / c. Ossicles / d. Olfactory bulb / e. Auditory cortex

- a. Labrynthitis / b. Vertigo / c. Vestibular migraine / d. Vestibular neuronitis

- a) Cochlea / b) Saccule and utricle / c) Semicircular canals / d) Round window

- 1. Eyes / 2. Ears / 3. Nose / 4. Skin / 5. Tongue

- 1. Frontal Lobe / 2. Parietal Lobe / 3. Temporal Lobe / 4. Occipital Lobe

**[Practice — Sense Organs]** Name the three bones that make up the middle ear. *(Malleus, Incus, Stapes)*

**[Practice — Sense Organs]** The cochlea is part of the _____ ear. *(Inner)*

**[Practice — Sense Organs]** Motion sickness is due to disturbances within the _____ ear. *(Inner)*

**[Practice — Sense Organs]** What fills the cochlea to transmit sound? *(Perilymph / endolymph)*

**[Practice — Sense Organs]** What type of cells are the receptors for sound in the cochlea? *(Hair cells)*

**[Practice — Sense Organs]** What is the function of the vestibule in the inner ear?

**[Practice — Sense Organs]** What is the function of the pinna? **c. Collects sound waves and funnels them into the ear canal**

**[Practice — Sense Organs]** What are the tiny bones in the middle ear responsible for? **c. Conducting sound waves**

**[Practice — Sense Organs]** Which structure in the vestibular apparatus detects angular acceleration in the horizontal plane? **c. Ampulla of the lateral semicircular canal**

**[Practice — Sense Organs]** Which structure detects "side-to-side" movements? **b. Utricle**

**[Practice — Sense Organs]** What fluid is located within the membranous labyrinth? *(Endolymph)*

**[Practice — Sense Organs]** What fluid is in the scala vestibuli and scala tympani? *(Perilymph)*

**[Practice — Sense Organs]** Which ion causes depolarization of hair cells when stereocilia are deflected? **b. Potassium (K⁺)**

**[Practice — Sense Organs]** The tympanic reflex protects the inner ear from loud sounds by: **b. Contracting the tensor tympani and stapedius**

**[Practice — Sense Organs]** Which structure equalizes pressure between the middle ear and throat? **a. Eustachian tube**

**[Practice — Sense Organs]** Children are more likely to get ear infections because their Eustachian tubes are: **a. Shorter and more horizontal**

**[Practice — Sense Organs]** The cochlea coils around what screwlike bony structure? **a. Modiolus**

**[Practice — Sense Organs]** Which cranial nerve is formed via convergence of the vestibular and cochlear nerves? **c. CN VIII (Vestibulocochlear)**

**[Practice — Sense Organs]** The function of inner hair cells is to: **b. Provide afferent auditory input**

**[Practice — Sense Organs]** In the auditory pathway, where does the first binaural processing occur? **b. Superior olivary complex**

**[Practice — Sense Organs]** Which type of deafness occurs when there is damage to the cochlea or auditory nerve? **c. Sensorineural deafness**

**[Practice — Sense Organs]** Which is a cause of conductive hearing loss? **c. Blockage from earwax**

**[Practice — Sense Organs]** Presbycusis primarily results from degeneration of: **c. Hair cells in the cochlea**

**[Practice — Sense Organs]** What structure vibrates first when sound waves enter the ear? **b. Eardrum (tympanic membrane)**

## BRAINSTEM & CRANIAL NERVE NUCLEI

**[Troy / UCR Div B / UCR Div C]** Which nucleus in the medulla receives visceral sensory input from CN VII, IX, and X?
- a. Nucleus ambiguus / b. Dorsal motor nucleus / c. Inferior olivary nucleus / d. Solitary nucleus / e. Sensorinucleus

**[Archimedean]** A stroke of the AICA — what neurological deficits are expected?
- a. Contralateral facial paralysis / b. Bilateral weakness / c. Only limb ataxia / d. Vertigo and ipsilateral hearing loss / e. Ipsilateral facial paralysis and loss of taste

**[Troy]** Möbius Syndrome (CN VI + VII underdevelopment) — which would NOT be affected?
- a. Facial paralysis / b. Movement of eyes / c. Sensation of the eyelid / d. All of the above

**[Wolfpack]** Which cranial nerve sends signals from the brain to the heart? Give number and name.

**[Seven Lakes]** Which cranial nerve innervates the dura mater and mediates the pain of meningitis?
- A) III / B) V / C) VI / D) IX / E) XI

**[Seven Lakes]** Which statements about the Trigeminal System are true? Select all.
- A) Innervation of CN V is in the mucosal lining
- B) Pathway connects trigeminal nucleus, VPM, and thalamus
- C) Uses TRPV1 for chemical sensation
- D) Powerful stimulation induces bronchospasms but not inflammation
- E) Cerebral cortex is not involved

**[HDSO]** The cranial accessory nerve:
- A) Lies on the scalenus medius in the posterior triangle / B) Supplies the levator scapulae / C) Passes through the sternomastoid muscle / D) Crosses anterior to the styloid process

**[Carmel Valley — FRQ]** Describe how sympathetic and parasympathetic divisions maintain homeostasis in the cardiovascular system. Include the role of the vagus nerve.

**[HG]** A student touches their eyelid and their eye blinks (corneal reflex). Which cranial nerve carries the sensory signal?
- A) facial nerve (VII) / B) glossopharyngeal (IX) / C) oculomotor (III) / D) trigeminal (V)

**[Practice — Nervous System]** Which cranial nerve carries parasympathetic fibers to thoracic and abdominal viscera? **d. CN X (Vagus nerve)**

**[Practice — Cranial Nerve Reference]**

| Test / Function | Cranial Nerve |
|---|---|
| Tongue symmetry | CN XII (Hypoglossal) |
| Strength/evenness of shrug | CN XI (Accessory) |
| Biting pencil, skin sensation, corneal reflex | CN V (Trigeminal) |
| Smelling aromatic oils | CN I (Olfactory) |
| Rinne/Weber tuning fork, balance | CN VIII (Vestibulocochlear) |
| "Ah!", uvula position, gag reflex | CN IX/X (Glossopharyngeal/Vagus) |
| Taste posterior 1/3 tongue | CN IX (Glossopharyngeal) |
| Motor to facial muscles | CN VII (Facial) |
| Eye abduction | CN VI (Abducens) |
| Eye movement (most), pupillary constriction | CN III (Oculomotor) |

How many pairs of cranial nerves? **12 pairs**
How many pairs of spinal nerves? **31 pairs**

**[Practice — Nervous System]** Which cranial nerve controls eye movement (abducens)? **a. CN VI**

**[Practice — Nervous System]** Cranial nerve VII is called _____ and has _____ function: **c. Facial nerve — motor to the facial muscles**

## NEUROPHYSIOLOGY — ACTION POTENTIALS & MEMBRANE POTENTIALS

**[Practice — Nervous System]** What primarily causes the neuron's resting membrane potential to be negative?
- a. The Na⁺/K⁺ pump pumps out 3 Na⁺ for every 2 K⁺
- **b. Open K⁺ leak channels allow more K⁺ to leave than Na⁺ can enter**
- c. Intracellular proteins carry negative charge
- d. Voltage-gated Na⁺ channels are closed at rest

**[Practice — Nervous System]** What is the approximate resting potential of a neuron?
- a. −90 mV / b. −82 mV / **c. −70 mV** / d. +20 mV

**[Practice — Nervous System]** At resting membrane potential, potassium wants to move ___ the cell, and sodium wants to move ___ the cell? *(K⁺: out; Na⁺: in)*

**[Practice — Nervous System]** Initial depolarization of an action potential is caused by:
- a. K⁺ entering / b. K⁺ exiting / **c. Na⁺ entering** / d. Na⁺ exiting

**[Practice — Nervous System]** Which is a factor in generating the resting membrane potential?
- a. Lower concentration of K⁺ immediately inside the cell membrane
- **b. Higher concentration of Na⁺ immediately outside the cell membrane**
- c. Greater permeability to Na⁺ than K⁺
- d. All of the above

**[Practice — Nervous System]** Which correctly describes the function of the sodium-potassium pump?
- a. Allows passive movement of ions / b. Hyperpolarizes by allowing Cl⁻ in
- **c. Actively exports 3 Na⁺ and imports 2 K⁺ using ATP**
- d. Actively transports 3 K⁺ in and 2 Na⁺ out

**[Practice — Nervous System]** Which ion's relative permeability influences membrane potential the most (GHK equation)?
- a. Na⁺ / b. Cl⁻ / **c. K⁺** / d. Ca²⁺

**[Practice — Nervous System]** For an increase in K⁺ movement out of the neuron, this causes:
- a. Depolarization / **b. Hyperpolarization** / c. K⁺ replaced by Na⁺ / d. K⁺ replaced by Ca²⁺

**[Practice — Nervous System]** Which statement about the refractory period is TRUE?
- a. During absolute refractory period, a strong stimulus can trigger another AP
- b. Relative refractory period occurs when Na⁺ channels are open and inactivated
- **c. During the relative refractory period, K⁺ channels are open**
- d. The refractory period affects the entire neuron equally

**[Practice — Nervous System]** A neuron's threshold is −55 mV and it is in its absolute refractory period. How strong a stimulus is needed for a second AP?
- **d. None — no stimulus can trigger an AP during absolute refractory period**

**[Practice — Nervous System]** What is the "threshold" potential of a membrane?
- **d. The minimum depolarization needed to operate voltage-gated sodium and potassium channels**

**[Practice — Nervous System]** True/False: Action potentials happen in an all-or-nothing fashion. *(True)*

**[Practice — Nervous System]** True/False: In a neuron that depolarizes, Na⁺ flows into the cell. *(True)*

**[Practice — Nervous System]** The Goldman-Hodgkin-Katz equation is used to calculate what? *(Resting membrane potential)*

**[Practice — Nervous System]** What ion primarily drives the depolarization phase? *(Na⁺)*

**[Practice — Nervous System — FRQ]** Describe what happens to a neuron's membrane during each phase of an action potential (depolarization, repolarization, hyperpolarization).

**[Practice — Nervous System — FRQ]** Describe the all-or-nothing law.

**[Practice — Nervous System — FRQ]** Which class of neurotransmitters is acetylcholine most similar to structurally? What differentiates it? Why are its distinctive properties crucial to its usability in the brain?

**[Practice — Nervous System — FRQ]** If inhibitory neurotransmitters were dysfunctional, what would happen to neurons? Give an example using a specific neurotransmitter.

## SALTATORY CONDUCTION & MYELINATION

**[Practice — Nervous System]** In saltatory conduction, action potentials propagate along what part of myelinated axons?
- a. Along the entire axon / **b. Nodes of Ranvier** / c. Only through the myelin sheath / d. Dendrites only

**[Practice — Nervous System]** What process describes an action potential jumping from one node of Ranvier to the next? *(Saltatory conduction)*

**[Practice — Nervous System]** Which part of the neuron integrates incoming signals and initiates an action potential?
- a. Axon terminal / **b. Axon hillock** / c. Dendrite / d. Myelin sheath

**[Practice — Nervous System]** Action potentials move along axons:
- a. More slowly in large-diameter axons
- b. By direct action of acetylcholine on the axonal membrane
- c. By activating the sodium-potassium pump at each point
- **d. More rapidly in myelinated than in non-myelinated axons**

**[Practice — Nervous System]** Saltatory conduction refers to:
- a. Conduction across electrical synapses / b. AP skipping the axon hillock / c. Rapid reverberating movement / d. Jumping from one neuron to another / **e. Jumping from one node of Ranvier to the next**

**[Practice — Nervous System]** The progression of a nerve impulse along nodes of Ranvier is called:
- **a. Saltatory Conduction** / b. Relative Conduction / c. Action Potential / d. Resting Potential

**[Practice — Nervous System]** What are the gaps between myelinated axons called? *(Nodes of Ranvier)*

**[Practice — Nervous System]** True/False: The midbrain is the largest region of the brainstem. *(False — the pons is the largest)*

**[Practice — Nervous System]** True/False: Slightly decreasing axon diameter increases conduction velocity by reducing internal resistance. *(False — larger diameter increases velocity)*

**[Practice — Nervous System]** Which is characteristic of myelination exclusively in the CNS?
- **d. Newer layers of myelin are formed under preexisting layers**

**[Practice — Nervous System]** Which best describes Wallerian degeneration?
- **b. Degeneration of the axon distal to the site of injury, while the proximal segment remains connected to the neurosoma**

**[Practice — Nervous System — FRQ]** Explain the function of myelination, which cells perform it, and give an example of a related disorder.

**[Practice — Nervous System — FRQ]** Describe the regeneration of a damaged nerve.

## SYNAPTIC TRANSMISSION & NEUROTRANSMITTERS

**[Practice — Nervous System]** Neurotransmitters are released from axon terminals via:
- a. Osmosis / b. Active transport / c. Diffusion / d. Transcytosis / **e. Exocytosis**

**[Practice — Nervous System]** An IPSP occurs when the membrane becomes more permeable to:
- **a. Potassium ions** / b. Sodium ions / c. Calcium ions

**[Practice — Nervous System]** What property distinguishes temporal summation from spatial summation?
- **a. It involves inputs at the same synapse over time**

**[Practice — Nervous System]** When several EPSPs arrive at the axon hillock from different dendritic locations, this is an example of:
- **b. Spatial summation**

**[Practice — Nervous System]** What best distinguishes monosynaptic from polysynaptic reflexes?
- **a. Polysynaptic reflexes require interneurons while monosynaptic reflexes do not**

**[Practice — Nervous System]** Which structure detects muscle stretch to initiate the stretch reflex?
- **c. Muscle spindle**

**[Practice — Nervous System]** Where is acetylcholine synthesized and enclosed?
- **c. Presynaptic terminal and synaptic vesicles**

**[Practice — Nervous System]** True/False: Voltage-gated calcium channels at the axon terminal trigger neurotransmitter release. *(True)*

**[Practice — Nervous System]** What neurotransmitter is released at the neuromuscular junction? *(Acetylcholine)*

**[Practice — Nervous System]** What neurotransmitter do muscarinic receptors bind to? *(Acetylcholine)*

**[Practice — Nervous System]** Which of the following is NOT a catecholamine? **a. Serotonin**

**[Practice — Nervous System]** All catecholamines are synthesized from: **b. Tyrosine**

**[Practice — Nervous System]** Which neuromodulator is created via arginine metabolism in endothelial cells? **d. Nitric oxide**

**[Practice — Nervous System]** What is the role of an enkephalin? **b. To act as a natural painkiller by inhibiting pain signals**

**[Practice — Nervous System]** Which receptor decreases heart rate when activated? **b. M2**

**[Practice — Nervous System]** True/False: All biogenic amines are derived from modifications to threonine. *(False — from aromatic amino acids)*

**[Practice — Nervous System]** True/False: The effects of serotonin tend to have a slower onset than other neurotransmitters. *(True)*

**[Practice — Nervous System]** True/False: Stimulation of 5-HT2A receptors can cause hallucinogenic effects. *(True)*

**[Practice — Nervous System — Neurotransmitter Categorization]**

| Neurotransmitter | Category |
|---|---|
| Epinephrine | Catecholamine |
| Norepinephrine | Catecholamine |
| Dopamine | Catecholamine |
| Serotonin | Indolamine |
| Histamine | Imidazolamine |

**[Practice — Nervous System]** What is the neurotransmitter used in the sympathetic nervous system? **a. Epinephrine**

**[Practice — Nervous System]** What is the brain's main excitatory neurotransmitter? **b. Glutamate**

**[Practice — Nervous System]** What do muscarinic receptors bind to? **c. Acetylcholine**

**[Practice — Nervous System — FRQ]** Describe the 5 neurotransmitter categories and give one example of each.

## PHARMACOLOGY OF THE NERVOUS SYSTEM

**[Practice — Nervous System]** How does caffeine enhance alertness? **d. Blocking adenosine receptors**

**[Practice — Nervous System]** How does caffeine affect coffee drinkers?
- a. Prevents reuptake of dopamine / b. Causes dopamine to bind to receptors / **c. Binds adenosine receptors causing epinephrine release** / d. Acts on hypothalamus to increase heart rate

**[Practice — Nervous System]** The primary effect of cocaine is that it blocks re-uptake of: **a. Monoamines**

**[Practice — Nervous System]** Botulinum toxin impairs neurotransmission by: **c. Preventing vesicular release of ACh**

**[Practice — Nervous System]** In a short sentence, how is botulinum toxin used in cosmetic medicine? *(Injected in small amounts to temporarily paralyze facial muscles, reducing wrinkles)*

**[Practice — Nervous System]** Which medication is commonly used to treat spasticity in multiple sclerosis? **b. Baclofen**

**[Practice — Nervous System]** True/False: Botulism destroys SNARES that activate skeletal muscle in excitatory neurons. *(True)*

**[Practice — Nervous System]** True/False: Monoamine oxidase inhibitors act by inhibiting the degradation of glutamate. *(False — they inhibit degradation of monoamines)*

**[Practice — Nervous System — FRQ]** Explain the effects of the following drugs on the nervous system: a) Alcohol, b) Caffeine, c) Nicotine.

**[Practice — Nervous System — FRQ]** Describe the paralytic effects of nerve gas. Mention the enzyme affected and what is inhibited.

**[Practice — Nervous System — FRQ]** When *Clostridium tetani* produces tetanospasmin, what disorder results and how does it cause muscle stiffness? *(Tetanus; disinhibition of motor neurons by a GABA blockade)*

**[Practice — Nervous System — FRQ]** Outline the behavioral effects of cannabis and alcohol.

## NEUROANATOMY — BRAIN REGIONS, LOBES & TRACTS

**[Practice — Nervous System]** What is the brain region best known as the sensory routing system?
- a. Hypothalamus / b. Post central gyrus / c. Cerebrum / **d. Thalamus**

**[Practice — Nervous System]** A rising ice skater suffers a blood clot robbing her of most of her eyesight, leaving only light and blurry shapes. Which brain lobe could the clot be in?

**[Practice — Nervous System]** In severe epilepsy, an operation severs a brain part to prevent communication between hemispheres. What is that part?
- a. Cerebrum / b. Brainstem / **c. Corpus callosum** / d. Thalamus

**[Practice — Nervous System]** Which brain region regulates vital functions like breathing and heartbeat? *(Medulla oblongata)*

**[Practice — Nervous System]** The motor cortex is part of which region? **a. Cerebrum**

**[Practice — Nervous System]** Patients with damage to Wernicke's area have difficulty:
- a. Coordinating limb movement / b. Generating speech / c. Recognizing faces / **d. Understanding language**

**[Practice — Nervous System]** Cerebrospinal fluid is found between:
- a. Arachnoid Mater and Dura Mater / b. Pia Mater and Dura Mater / **c. Arachnoid Mater and Pia Mater** / d. Pia Mater and CSF layer

**[Practice — Nervous System]** What controls heart rate? **b. Medulla**

**[Practice — Nervous System]** Auditory information is processed in which lobe of the cerebrum? *(Temporal lobe)*

**[Practice — Nervous System]** Which brain region is responsible for proper balance and posture? **c. Cerebellum**

**[Practice — Nervous System]** Which tract carries fine touch and proprioceptive information to the brain? **b. Dorsal column (DCML pathway)**

**[Practice — Nervous System]** True/False: The spinothalamic tract transmits information about body position to the cerebellum. *(False — spinothalamic carries pain and temperature; proprioception via dorsal columns)*

**[Practice — Nervous System]** True/False: The dorsal root contains motor fibers. *(False — sensory/afferent; motor in ventral root)*

**[Practice — Nervous System]** True/False: Sensory information about fine touch and proprioception ascends via the DCML pathway. *(True)*

**[Practice — Nervous System]** True/False: The forebrain develops into the cerebrum and diencephalon. *(True)*

**[Practice — Nervous System]** Which lobe of the brain houses the visual areas? **d. Occipital Lobe**

**[Practice — Nervous System]** Which pathway is related to movement coordination? **a. Rubrospinal**

**[Practice — Nervous System]** A stroke in the MCA would most likely affect: **c. Face and arm movement**

**[Practice — Nervous System]** A patient demonstrates difficulty forming new memories but recalls old ones. Which region is most likely affected? **c. Hippocampus**

**[Practice — Nervous System]** Damage to the right posterior parietal cortex most likely causes: **c. Lack of spatial awareness**

**[Practice — Nervous System]** Which lists the correct path of CSF? **b. Lateral ventricles → third ventricle → fourth ventricle → subarachnoid space**

**[Practice — Nervous System]** A split-brain patient is shown a key flashed in the left visual field. Most likely response? **b. Unable to say what it is but can select the key with the left hand**

**[Practice — Nervous System]** A patient suffers a stroke affecting the internal capsule. Most likely outcome? **a. Loss of signal transmission from cortex to spinal cord**

**[Practice — Nervous System]** Which best describes the function of the Circle of Willis? **c. Provides collateral blood flow to the brain**

**[Practice — Nervous System]** Which statement about CSF is true? **a. Produced primarily by ependymal cells lining the ventricles**

**[Practice — Nervous System]** Which region of the vertebral column contains more spinal nerves than vertebrae? **d. Sacral**

**[Practice — Nervous System]** Intercostal nerves are found in which region? **b. Thoracic**

**[Practice — Nervous System]** The pia mater continues beyond the medullary cone to form what structure? **b. Terminal filum**

**[Practice — Nervous System]** What part of the spinal cord primarily carries motor signals? **b. Ventral horn**

**[Practice — Nervous System]** Which brain region converts short-term memory into long-term memory? *(Hippocampus)*

**[Practice — Nervous System]** What structure allows communication between the two cerebral hemispheres? *(Corpus callosum)*

**[Practice — Nervous System]** Which specific part of the brain is affected in Parkinson's disease? *(Substantia nigra)*

**[Practice — Nervous System]** What are the parts of the brainstem? *(Medulla, Pons, Midbrain)*

**[Practice — Nervous System]** What is the function of the cerebellum? *(Muscle coordination, balance, muscle tone)*

**[Practice — Nervous System]** What is the function of the thalamus? *(Brain's switchboard / sensory relay station)*

**[Practice — Nervous System]** True/False: Pyramidal neurons tend to give outputs to other parts of the CNS and cortex. *(True)*

## NEUROLOGICAL DISORDERS

**[Practice — Nervous System]** In which disease do sclerotic sheaths result in poor conduction of action potentials? **a. Multiple Sclerosis**

**[Practice — Nervous System]** What disease is caused by the loss of dopamine? **a. Parkinson's Disease**

**[Practice — Nervous System]** Which neurotransmitter system is primarily affected in Parkinson's disease? **b. Dopamine**

**[Practice — Nervous System]** A patient presents with optic neuritis and intermittent limb weakness. Which diagnosis is most likely? **d. Multiple sclerosis**

**[Practice — Nervous System]** In ALS, which neurons are lost? **b. Upper and lower motor neurons**

**[Practice — Nervous System]** Which best explains why hyperkalemia can lead to muscle weakness?
- **a. It increases the resting membrane potential, inactivating voltage-gated sodium channels**

**[Practice — Nervous System]** What is an early symptom of Alzheimer's disease? *(Memory loss / forgetting recent events)*

**[Practice — Nervous System]** True/False: Overproduction of cholinergic receptors is linked to Alzheimer's disease. *(False)*

**[Practice — Nervous System — Disorder Matching]**

| Disorder | Key Features |
|---|---|
| Epilepsy | Sudden bursts of electrochemical activity; seizures |
| Multiple Sclerosis | Autoimmune demyelination; worsens with heat |
| Alzheimer's Disease | APOE gene risk; cholinergic neuron loss |
| ALS | Upper and lower motor neuron loss; progressive paralysis |
| Tay-Sachs | Enzyme deficiency; cannot break down lipids in nerve cells |
| Parkinson's Disease | Dopamine loss in substantia nigra; tremors, rigidity |
| Cerebral Palsy | Brain damage before/during/shortly after birth |
| Myasthenia Gravis | ACh receptor blockade by antibodies; muscle weakness |
| Shingles (Herpes Zoster) | Painful stripe of blisters on one side |
| Stroke | Blood flow to brain interrupted |

Explain the diagnosis and pathophysiology. Address: role of myelin, how demyelination affects action potentials, and why heat worsens symptoms.

**[Practice — Nervous System — FRQ]** FRQ (case): Phillip, 67, misplaces items, repeats words, struggles to finish sentences, loses interest in crossword puzzles. a) Disease? b) Brain structures, cell type, and neurotransmitter primarily involved? c) Stage? d) Treatments and mechanism?

**[Practice — Nervous System — FRQ]** FRQ (case): Neural tube defects — fetus with craniorachischisis. Predict three specific brain regions and one spinal cord structure that would fail to develop properly and explain why.

**[Practice — Nervous System — FRQ]** FRQ: Compare anencephaly with meningoencephalocele. What key difference in tissue organization explains why one condition may retain partial brain structures?

**[Practice — Nervous System — FRQ]** FRQ (case): A 17-year-old with focal epilepsy. fMRI shows Wernicke's area overlapping the tumor, with developing activation in the contralateral hemisphere. Why was Wernicke's area targeted for inhibition, and how did this cause language activation on the contralateral hemisphere?

## ENDOCRINE SYSTEM — HORMONE MECHANISMS & SIGNALING

**[Practice — Endocrine]** Which most accurately describes the difference in mechanism between peptide and steroid hormones?
- A. Peptide hormones bind to nuclear receptors; steroid hormones bind to membrane-bound receptors
- B. Peptide hormones require intracellular transport proteins; steroid hormones are freely soluble
- **C. Peptide hormones typically activate second messenger systems; steroid hormones directly influence gene expression**
- D. Peptide hormones alter transcription directly; steroid hormones act only through enzymatic phosphorylation

**[Practice — Endocrine]** What is a major structural difference between catecholamines and thyroid hormones?
- A. Catecholamines are lipid-soluble / **B. Thyroid hormones require plasma transport proteins** / C. Catecholamines bind nuclear receptors / D. Thyroid hormones act within the cytoplasm

**[Practice — Endocrine]** Why do peptide hormones act quickly but briefly? **A. They're stored in vesicles and cleared fast**

**[Practice — Endocrine]** Which limits the duration of peptide hormone signaling most?
- A. Receptor regulation of lysosomal digestion / **B. Conversion of cAMP to AMP by phosphodiesterase** / C. Desensitization of GPCRs / D. Competitive inhibition at receptor site

**[Practice — Endocrine]** Which hormone is both an amine and lipid-soluble, enabling nuclear receptor action? **B. Thyroxine (T4)**

**[Practice — Endocrine]** Which mechanism best describes the action of insulin?
- A. Binds to nuclear receptors / B. Inhibits secondary messengers / **C. Activates membrane receptors leading to glucose uptake** / D. Crosses membrane to alter transcription

**[Practice — Endocrine]** Which hormone has a mechanism that acts on nuclear receptors?
- A. Insulin / B. PTH / **C. Cortisol** / D. TSH

**[Practice — Endocrine]** Which hormone cannot be taken orally due to being denatured by digestive enzymes? **A. Insulin**

**[Practice — Endocrine]** Which hormone is most likely able to easily pass through a semi-permeable cell membrane? **D. Estrogen**

**[Practice — Endocrine]** Which best explains how steroid hormones induce gene transcription?
- A. Activation of membrane receptors / B. Secondary messenger cascades / **C. Direct DNA binding in the nucleus** / D. mRNA translation control

**[Practice — Endocrine]** What enzyme, inhibited by Viagra, breaks down cAMP and cGMP? **B. Phosphodiesterase**

**[Practice — Endocrine]** The eicosanoid family of paracrine regulators have backbones derived from: **B. Arachidonic acid**

**[Practice — Endocrine]** Uptake of iodine into thyroid cells is powered by:
- A. Primary active transport via ATP hydrolysis by apical NIS symporter
- **B. Secondary active transport via the Na⁺ gradient by the basolateral NIS symporter**
- C. Facilitated diffusion through iodide channels
- D. Secondary active transport via proton-coupled transport at the apical NIS symporter

## ENDOCRINE SYSTEM — GLANDS, HORMONES & FEEDBACK

**[Practice — Endocrine]** What gland controls other glands? **B. Pituitary**

**[Practice — Endocrine]** What is another name for the posterior pituitary gland? **A. Neurohypophysis**

**[Practice — Endocrine]** What is another name for the anterior pituitary gland? **B. Adenohypophysis**

**[Practice — Endocrine]** What type of hormones are produced by the anterior pituitary and target other endocrine glands? **A. Tropic**

**[Practice — Endocrine]** Which portion of the pituitary gland is composed of nervous tissue? **C. Posterior pituitary**

**[Practice — Endocrine]** A researcher blocks all hypothalamic releasing hormones. Which hormone would still be secreted normally? **D. Oxytocin**

**[Practice — Endocrine]** Which is a unique feature of the supraoptic nucleus compared to other hypothalamic nuclei?
- A. Projects to the anterior pituitary via releasing factors
- **B. Contains osmoreceptors and sends long axons directly into the posterior pituitary**
- C. Regulates circadian rhythm by responding to retinal light signals
- D. Secretes dopamine to inhibit prolactin

**[Practice — Endocrine]** Which feature most distinguishes magnocellular hypothalamic neurons from parvocellular ones?
- A. They synapse on anterior pituitary cells / B. Use axonal transport to reach median eminence capillaries / **C. They release hormones into systemic circulation** / D. They release both releasing and inhibiting factors

**[Practice — Endocrine]** Which cell type plays a bigger role in estrogen production in prepubertal males? **B. Sertoli cells**

**[Practice — Endocrine]** The release of most hormones is regulated by the: **B. Negative feedback loop**

**[Practice — Endocrine]** Which represents negative feedback? **D. X stimulates Y, then Y inhibits X**

**[Practice — Endocrine]** Which represents positive feedback? **E. X stimulates Y, then Y stimulates X**

**[Practice — Endocrine]** How does prolactin inhibit the reproductive axis? **A. Suppresses GnRH release**

**[Practice — Endocrine]** Which hormone is secreted in a pulsatile manner to prevent receptor desensitization? **C. GnRH**

**[Practice — Endocrine]** What cells secrete thyroid hormone (TH)? **A. Follicular cells**

**[Practice — Endocrine]** Which layer of the suprarenal gland secretes mineralocorticoids? **A. Zona glomerulosa**

**[Practice — Endocrine]** Which adrenal cortex zone produces glucocorticoids? **B. Zona fasciculata**

**[Practice — Endocrine]** What are the main hormones released from the adrenal medulla? *(Epinephrine ~80% and norepinephrine ~20%)*

**[Practice — Endocrine]** Which cells in the ovaries secrete estrogen? **A. Granulosa cells**

**[Practice — Endocrine]** Which enzyme is critical in the conversion of testosterone to estrogen in adipose tissue? **A. Aromatase**

**[Practice — Endocrine]** Which enzyme catalyzes the rate-limiting step of steroid hormone synthesis from cholesterol? **C. Cholesterol desmolase**

**[Practice — Endocrine]** Which hormone primarily regulates serum potassium levels? **B. Aldosterone**

**[Practice — Endocrine]** Which hormone increases renal calcium reabsorption and decreases phosphate reabsorption? **C. PTH**

**[Practice — Endocrine]** Which correctly traces how PTH increases intestinal calcium uptake?
- A. PTH → thyroid → calcitonin / B. PTH → liver → vitamin D3
- **C. PTH → kidney → activation of vitamin D → calcitriol → increased Ca absorption**
- D. PTH → adrenal cortex → aldosterone → sodium retention

**[Practice — Endocrine]** Which best describes how PTH stimulates calcium release from bone at the cellular level?
- A. PTH binds to osteoclast receptors, directly stimulating bone resorption
- B. PTH promotes osteoblast activity, which inhibits osteoclasts
- **C. PTH stimulates osteoblasts to express RANKL, which activates osteoclast precursors**
- D. PTH activates vitamin D in bone tissue

**[Practice — Endocrine]** Calcitonin is produced in what part of the thyroid gland? **B. Parafollicular cells**

**[Practice — Endocrine]** Which hormone decreases the rate of bone resorption? **A. Calcitonin**

**[Practice — Endocrine]** Which is promoted by the direct secretory product of alpha cells of the pancreas? **A. Gluconeogenesis / Cori cycle** *(glucagon)*

**[Practice — Endocrine]** Insulin is secreted by which specific cell? **A. Beta cells of the Islet of Langerhans**

**[Practice — Endocrine]** Which hormones are secreted from the beta cells of the pancreas? **A. Insulin and Amylin**

**[Practice — Endocrine]** Which hormone is known as the "satiety hormone"? **D. Leptin**

**[Practice — Endocrine]** Which hormone causes blood sugar to rise? **B. Glucagon**

**[Practice — Endocrine]** What hormone is measured during a dexamethasone suppression test? **A. Cortisol** *(screening for Cushing's syndrome)*

**[Practice — Endocrine]** Which hormone is primarily involved in regulating the body's response to stress? **B. Cortisol**

**[Practice — Endocrine]** In evolution, what is the primary survival benefit of secreting cortisol? **C. Mobilizes energy during a threat**

**[Practice — Endocrine]** Which hormone is photosensitive? *(Melatonin)*

**[Practice — Endocrine]** The thymus secretes hormones that help mature which type of immune cell? *(T cells)*

**[Practice — Endocrine]** Which hormone increases Na reabsorption in the kidneys, affecting blood pressure? *(Aldosterone)*

**[Practice — Endocrine]** What is the primary function of prolactin? *(Milk production/lactation)*

**[Practice — Endocrine]** Which gland is connected to both the nervous and endocrine systems? *(Hypothalamus)*

**[Practice — Endocrine]** What is it called when a hormone acts on another endocrine gland, stimulating it to release its own hormones? *(Tropic effect)*

**[Practice — Endocrine]** Which endocrine organ stores hormone-containing colloid extracellularly before secretion? *(Thyroid gland)*

**[Practice — Endocrine]** Which hormone is responsible for upregulating aquaporin-2 in the collecting ducts? *(ADH/Vasopressin)*

**[Practice — Endocrine]** What is the primary site of peripheral T4-to-T3 conversion? *(Liver)*

**[Practice — Endocrine]** Which hormone is secreted in pulses primarily during sleep? *(Growth hormone)*

**[Practice — Endocrine]** What hormone is secreted by Leydig cells? *(Testosterone)*

**[Practice — Endocrine]** High levels of what hormone directly induces ovulation? *(LH)*

**[Practice — Endocrine]** Which hormone has both endocrine and neurohormonal functions and is involved in circadian rhythm regulation? **B. Melatonin**

**[Practice — Endocrine]** Which hormones will be secreted in response to vasodilatory shock? **E. All of the above** *(Epinephrine, Cortisol, ADH/Vasopressin, Aldosterone)*

**[Practice — Endocrine]** Normal fasting glucose in mg/dL is closest to: **B. 60–100**

**[Practice — Endocrine]** Which endocrine disruptor binds to both ERα and ERβ? **C. BPA**

**[Practice — Endocrine — True/False]**
- Aldosterone is a glucocorticoid. *(False — mineralocorticoid)*
- In the Zona Fasciculata you'd find enzymes that synthesize aldosterone. *(False — zona glomerulosa)*
- Phenylalanine N-Methyltransferase catalyzes conversion of Vitamin D to calcitriol. *(False)*
- Hyperalgesia is linked to an increase in β-endorphins. *(False — β-endorphins decrease pain)*
- Administering epinephrine to adipocytes leads to significant fatty acid release. *(True)*
- A mutation rendering chondrocytes insensitive to peptide GHs would make an individual taller. *(False — shorter)*
- EPO release is normally triggered by high oxygen in the blood. *(False — low oxygen triggers EPO)*
- An increase in PTHrp and decrease in PTH is likely due to a cytotoxic tumor. *(True)*
- Treatment with 1,25-dihydroxyvitamin D is a common solution to hypocalcemia. *(True)*
- Steroids are water-soluble. *(False — lipid-soluble)*
- Amine hormones are synthesized from tryptophan or threonine. *(False — tryptophan or tyrosine)*
- Type 2 diabetes mellitus occurs when too little insulin is secreted by the pancreas. *(False — primarily insulin resistance)*

**[Practice — Endocrine — Fill in the Blank — HPT Axis]**
To maintain stable metabolism: (1) hypothalamus detects low TH → releases TRH → travels through (2) hypophyseal portal system → anterior pituitary releases TSH → stimulates (3) thyroid gland to produce (4) T3 (triiodothyronine) and (5) T4 (thyroxine) → sufficient T3+T4 inhibits TRH and TSH through (6) negative feedback.

**[Practice — Endocrine — Gland Matching]**

| Gland | Key Secretions / Notes |
|---|---|
| Anterior Pituitary | GH, TSH, ACTH, FSH, LH, Prolactin, MSH; tropic hormones targeting other endocrine glands |
| Posterior Pituitary | Oxytocin and ADH (synthesized by hypothalamus, stored/released here) |
| Thyroid | T3, T4 from follicular cells; calcitonin from parafollicular cells |
| Parathyroid | PTH; increases blood calcium |
| Adrenal Medulla | Epinephrine (~80%), Norepinephrine (~20%) |
| Adrenal Cortex — Zona Glomerulosa | Mineralocorticoids (aldosterone) |
| Adrenal Cortex — Zona Fasciculata | Glucocorticoids (cortisol) |
| Adrenal Cortex — Zona Reticularis | Androgens (DHEA) |
| Thymus | Thymosin; promotes T-cell maturation |
| Pineal Gland | Melatonin; circadian rhythm regulation |
| Pancreas — Beta cells | Insulin, amylin |
| Pancreas — Alpha cells | Glucagon |
| Ovaries — Granulosa cells | Estrogen |
| Testes — Leydig cells | Testosterone |
| Testes — Sertoli cells | Inhibin B; estrogen in prepubertal males |

## ENDOCRINE SYSTEM — DISORDERS

**[Practice — Endocrine]** An individual presents with fatigue, weight gain, and bradycardia. Labs: elevated TSH, low T3. Which best explains this?
- **A. Primary hypothyroidism from thyroid gland failure** / B. Secondary hypothyroidism / C. Hypersecretion of TRH / D. Hyposensitivity to thyroid hormone

**[Practice — Endocrine]** What would be the expected hormonal profile in secondary hypothyroidism?
- A. High TSH, Low T3/T4 / **B. Low TSH, Low T3/T4** / C. High TSH, High T3/T4 / D. Normal TSH, High T3/T4

**[Practice — Endocrine]** Which disorder is caused by TSH receptor autoantibodies? **A. Graves' disease**

**[Practice — Endocrine]** Which is NOT an effect of Graves' Disease?
- A. Enlargement of extrinsic eye muscles / B. Louder heart sounds / **C. Decreased bone reabsorption** / D. Muscle atrophy and weakness

**[Practice — Endocrine]** Which symptom is most specific to myxedema?
- A. Hypertension and weight loss / **B. Puffy face with dry skin** / C. Sudden drop in blood calcium / D. Flushed skin with tremors

**[Practice — Endocrine]** Melissa: thickened skin on shins and bulging eyes. What does her doctor suspect? *(Graves' disease / hyperthyroidism)*

**[Practice — Endocrine]** Billy: intense swelling in neck, cough, hoarseness. What condition and treatments? *(Goiter; iodine supplementation, thyroid hormone therapy, or surgery)*

**[Practice — Endocrine]** Caitlyn: severe mental deficiency due to lack of iodine, causing motor spasticity, deaf mutism, and squint. What condition? *(Congenital Iodine Deficiency Syndrome / Cretinism)*

**[Practice — Endocrine]** Which lab results hint at Addison's disease?
- A. High cortisol, high ACTH / B. Low cortisol, low ACTH / **C. Low cortisol, high ACTH** / D. High aldosterone, low renin

**[Practice — Endocrine]** Which condition presents with high ACTH but low cortisol? **B. Addison's disease**

**[Practice — Endocrine]** In Cushing's disease, what is the most common underlying pathophysiology?
- A. Adrenal tumor / B. Ectopic ACTH secretion / **C. Pituitary adenoma overproducing ACTH** / D. Iatrogenic steroid use

**[Practice — Endocrine]** Pituitary dwarfism is caused by: **C. GH hyposecretion**

**[Practice — Endocrine]** A condition caused by hypersecretion of somatotrophin in adulthood, leading to enlargement of hands, feet, and facial features: **B. Acromegaly**

**[Practice — Endocrine]** What hormone causes gigantism? *(Growth hormone; anterior pituitary)*

**[Practice — Endocrine]** In primary hyperaldosteronism (Conn's syndrome), which laboratory finding is most characteristic?
- A. Hyperkalemia and metabolic acidosis / **B. Hypokalemia and metabolic alkalosis** / C. Hypernatremia and hyperkalemia / D. Hyponatremia and metabolic acidosis

**[Practice — Endocrine]** A tumor in the adrenal medulla causes excessive catecholamine release. What symptoms?
- A. Bradycardia, hypotension, fatigue / **B. Tachycardia, hypertension, anxiety** / C. Weight gain, heat intolerance / D. Hypoglycemia, sweating, coma

**[Practice — Endocrine]** Pheochromocytomas are a tumor of the: **D. Adrenal Medulla**

**[Practice — Endocrine]** Type 1 Diabetes Mellitus results from: **B. Immune system destruction of insulin-producing beta cells**

**[Practice — Endocrine]** Type 2 Diabetes accounts for about ___ of all diagnosed cases, while Type 1 accounts for ___: **C. 90%, 10%**

**[Practice — Endocrine]** A patient with low serum calcium and high PTH likely has: **B. Secondary hyperparathyroidism**

**[Practice — Endocrine]** Which is the primary symptom of Congenital Iodine Deficiency Syndrome? **C. Cognitive developmental delays**

**[Practice — Endocrine]** Tumors in which gland can cause changes in vision due to proximity to the optic nerve? **A. Pituitary gland**

**[Practice — Endocrine]** The hypothalamus-pituitary-adrenal axis is suppressed in: **B. Long-term corticosteroid therapy**

**[Practice — Endocrine]** In SIADH, what is the most likely combination of serum sodium and urine osmolality?
- A. Low serum sodium, low urine osmolality / B. High serum sodium, high urine osmolality / **C. Low serum sodium, high urine osmolality** / D. High serum sodium, low urine osmolality

**[Practice — Endocrine]** A patient presents with hypernatremia and low ADH. What condition is most likely?
- A. SIADH / B. Diabetes mellitus / **C. Diabetes insipidus** / D. Addison's disease

**[Practice — Endocrine]** Diabetes insipidus is due to a deficiency of: **C. ADH**

**[Practice — Endocrine]** Which patient is most likely to experience a temporary form of diabetes insipidus?
- A. Patient with tumor directly damaging hypothalamus / B. Patient whose kidneys don't respond to ADH / **C. Patient who recently had pituitary stalk surgery** / D. Patient with lung cancer producing excess ADH

**[Practice — Endocrine]** What is the name of the autoimmune disorder that targets the thyroid and causes hypothyroidism? *(Hashimoto's thyroiditis)*

**[Practice — Endocrine]** Endemic goiter results from a lack of _______ in the diet: **B. Iodine**

**[Practice — Endocrine]** Giantism is abnormal growth in young people because of the __________ of growth hormone by the pituitary gland.

**[Practice — Endocrine — FRQ]** Name a disorder caused by hyposecretion that results in pancreatic cell destruction.

**[Practice — Endocrine — Case: Lisa (Type 1 Diabetes)]**
Lisa (7 yr): thirsty, polyuria, polyphagia, weight loss; glucose 545 mg/dL; pH 7.23; urine + glucose and ketones.
1. Diagnosis? 2. Why elevated glucose? 3. Why fruity breath? 4. Describe mechanism of glucagon action.

**[Practice — Endocrine — Case: Harry Sullivan (Addison's Disease)]**
Harry (21 yr): weakness, fatigue, dizziness, salt craving, persistent tan, weight loss.
1. Disease? 2. Due to hyposecretion of cortisol? 3. Best treatment? 4. Hyperpigmentation due to hypersecretion of ACTH?
