## MASTER DISORDER REFERENCE

| Disorder | System | Key Features |
|---|---|---|
| Epilepsy | Nervous | Sudden bursts of electrochemical activity; seizures |
| Multiple Sclerosis | Nervous | Autoimmune demyelination; optic neuritis, limb weakness, worsens with heat |
| Alzheimer's Disease | Nervous | APOE gene risk; cholinergic neuron loss; memory deterioration |
| ALS | Nervous | Upper and lower motor neuron loss; progressive paralysis |
| Tay-Sachs | Nervous | Enzyme deficiency; cannot break down lipids in nerve cells |
| Parkinson's Disease | Nervous | Dopamine loss in substantia nigra; tremors, rigidity |
| Cerebral Palsy | Nervous | Brain damage before/during/shortly after birth; movement disorders |
| Myasthenia Gravis | Nervous | ACh receptor blockade by antibodies; muscle weakness |
| Graves' Disease | Endocrine | TSH receptor autoantibodies; hyperthyroidism, exophthalmos |
| Hashimoto's Disease | Endocrine | Autoimmune thyroid destruction; hypothyroidism |
| Addison's Disease | Endocrine | Low cortisol, high ACTH; hyperpigmentation, fatigue |
| Cushing's Syndrome | Endocrine | High cortisol; moon face, central obesity |
| Acromegaly | Endocrine | GH hypersecretion in adulthood; enlarged extremities |
| Pheochromocytoma | Endocrine | Adrenal medulla tumor; excess catecholamines |
| Diabetes Insipidus | Endocrine | ADH deficiency; excessive dilute urine |
| Glaucoma | Sensory | Increased intraocular pressure; optic nerve degeneration |
| Cataracts | Sensory | Lens opacity; cloudy vision |
| Macular Degeneration | Sensory | Breakdown of cells near center of retina; central vision loss |
| Conjunctivitis | Sensory | Inflammation of conjunctiva; pink eye |
| Otitis Media | Sensory | Middle ear infection; common in children |
| Presbycusis | Sensory | Age-related cochlear hair cell loss |
| Otosclerosis | Sensory | Stapes fixation; inherited conductive hearing loss |
| Ménière's Disease | Sensory | Endolymphatic hydrops; episodic vertigo, tinnitus, hearing loss |
| Myopia | Sensory | Nearsightedness; eyeball too long |
| Hyperopia | Sensory | Farsightedness; eyeball too short |
| Presbyopia | Sensory | Age-related loss of near vision; decreased lens flexibility |
| Nyctalopia | Sensory | Night blindness; vitamin A deficiency / rhodopsin deficiency |
| Astigmatism | Sensory | Uneven focus; irregular curvature of cornea or lens |
| Anosmia | Sensory | Loss of the ability to smell |
| Dysgeusia | Sensory | Distorted or altered sense of taste |

# A&P State-Level Supplementary Question Bank
## Spinal Cord Tracts · Brainstem Nuclei · Proprioception Mechanics · Deep Endocrine
*Original questions written to fill gaps identified across all uploaded exam sources. Targeted at state-level Science Olympiad Anatomy & Physiology scope.*

---

## SECTION 1: SPINAL CORD TRACTS

### Quick Reference Table

| Tract | Direction | Modality | Decussation Site | Ipsi or Contra at cortex |
|---|---|---|---|---|
| Dorsal Column–Medial Lemniscal (DCML) | Ascending | Fine touch, vibration, 2-point discrimination, conscious proprioception | Medulla (nucleus gracilis / cuneatus) | Contralateral |
| Spinothalamic (Anterolateral) | Ascending | Pain, temperature, crude touch, pressure | Spinal cord (at level of entry, within 1–2 segments) | Contralateral |
| Dorsal Spinocerebellar | Ascending | Unconscious proprioception — lower limb | Does NOT decussate | Ipsilateral |
| Ventral Spinocerebellar | Ascending | Unconscious proprioception — lower limb | Decussates twice (net ipsilateral) | Ipsilateral |
| Lateral Corticospinal | Descending | Voluntary motor — limb muscles | Pyramidal decussation in medulla | Contralateral |
| Anterior Corticospinal | Descending | Voluntary motor — axial/trunk muscles | Decussates at spinal level | Bilateral |
| Rubrospinal | Descending | Motor coordination — limb movements | Midbrain | Contralateral |
| Reticulospinal | Descending | Muscle tone, posture, autonomic | Variable (bilateral) | Bilateral |
| Vestibulospinal | Descending | Balance, posture, extensor tone | Does NOT decussate | Ipsilateral |
| Tectospinal | Descending | Head/neck reflexes to visual & auditory stimuli | Midbrain | Contralateral |

---

### Multiple Choice

1. Which ascending spinal tract carries fine touch, vibration sense, and conscious proprioception?
   - A. Spinothalamic tract
   - **B. Dorsal column–medial lemniscal pathway**
   - C. Ventral spinocerebellar tract
   - D. Rubrospinal tract

2. A patient can feel pain and temperature in their right arm but cannot feel light touch or vibration. Where is the lesion most likely located?
   - A. Right dorsal column at cervical level
   - **B. Right spinothalamic tract**
   - C. Left dorsal column at cervical level
   - D. Left spinothalamic tract

3. The dorsal column–medial lemniscal pathway decussates at which location?
   - A. Spinal cord at the level of entry
   - B. Midbrain
   - **C. Medulla (at the nucleus gracilis and cuneatus)**
   - D. Thalamus

4. The spinothalamic tract decussates at which location?
   - **A. Spinal cord, within 1–2 segments of the level of entry**
   - B. Medulla
   - C. Pons
   - D. Midbrain

5. A patient suffers a complete right-sided hemisection of the spinal cord at T4 (Brown-Séquard syndrome). Which set of deficits is expected?
   - A. Loss of all sensation bilaterally below T4; preserved motor function
   - B. Loss of pain/temperature on the right; loss of fine touch/proprioception on the left, below T4; ipsilateral motor loss on the right
   - **C. Loss of pain/temperature on the left; loss of fine touch/proprioception and voluntary motor on the right, below T4**
   - D. Loss of all sensation and motor on the left below T4

6. In Brown-Séquard syndrome, why is motor loss ipsilateral to the lesion while pain/temperature loss is contralateral?
   - A. The corticospinal tract decussates in the spinal cord; the spinothalamic tract decussates in the medulla
   - **B. The corticospinal tract decussates in the medulla before descending; the spinothalamic tract decussates at the spinal cord level of entry**
   - C. Both tracts decussate at the same level; the difference is due to fiber diameter
   - D. Motor fibers travel in the dorsal column; sensory fibers travel in the lateral column

7. Syringomyelia is a condition in which a fluid-filled cavity (syrinx) forms within the central spinal cord, damaging crossing fibers. Which modality is typically lost FIRST and why?
   - A. Fine touch, because the dorsal columns are compressed first
   - **B. Pain and temperature, because the crossing spinothalamic fibers at the anterior commissure are disrupted first**
   - C. Proprioception, because the dorsal root ganglia are directly compressed
   - D. Voluntary motor, because the anterior horn cells are the most vulnerable

8. A patient presents with loss of pain and temperature in a "cape-like" distribution across both shoulders and arms, with preserved fine touch. This pattern is most consistent with:
   - A. Brown-Séquard syndrome
   - B. Complete transection of the spinal cord
   - **C. Syringomyelia**
   - D. Posterior spinal artery infarction

9. Which descending tract crosses in the pyramidal decussation of the medulla and primarily controls voluntary movement of the distal limb muscles?
   - A. Anterior corticospinal tract
   - **B. Lateral corticospinal tract**
   - C. Rubrospinal tract
   - D. Reticulospinal tract

10. A stroke affects the right motor cortex. Which limb(s) will show weakness?
    - A. Right arm and right leg
    - **B. Left arm and left leg**
    - C. Right arm only
    - D. Left leg only

11. Which descending tract primarily regulates posture, balance, and extensor muscle tone and does NOT decussate?
    - A. Lateral corticospinal tract
    - B. Rubrospinal tract
    - **C. Vestibulospinal tract**
    - D. Tectospinal tract

12. The nucleus gracilis and nucleus cuneatus, where DCML fibers synapse, are located in the:
    - A. Thalamus
    - B. Pons
    - **C. Medulla**
    - D. Midbrain

13. After synapsing in the medulla, DCML fibers cross as the medial lemniscus and ascend to which thalamic nucleus before reaching the somatosensory cortex?
    - A. Lateral geniculate nucleus
    - B. Medial geniculate nucleus
    - **C. Ventral posterior lateral (VPL) nucleus**
    - D. Pulvinar nucleus

14. The spinothalamic tract carries pain and temperature from the body. Which thalamic nucleus does it synapse in?
    - A. Lateral geniculate nucleus
    - **B. Ventral posterior lateral (VPL) nucleus**
    - C. Dorsomedial nucleus
    - D. Anterior nucleus

15. Which spinal cord tract carries unconscious proprioceptive information from the lower limbs to the cerebellum WITHOUT decussating?
    - A. Ventral spinocerebellar tract
    - **B. Dorsal spinocerebellar tract**
    - C. Spinothalamic tract
    - D. Dorsal column

16. A patient with a posterior spinal artery infarction at C5 would most likely lose which function bilaterally below the lesion?
    - **A. Fine touch, vibration, and proprioception** *(dorsal columns are supplied by posterior spinal arteries)*
    - B. Pain and temperature
    - C. Voluntary motor control
    - D. All sensation equally

17. Upper motor neuron (UMN) lesions produce which set of signs?
    - A. Flaccid paralysis, muscle atrophy, fasciculations, absent reflexes
    - **B. Spastic paralysis, hyperreflexia, Babinski sign, minimal atrophy**
    - C. Flaccid paralysis, hyperreflexia, intact sensation
    - D. Spastic paralysis, absent reflexes, sensory loss

18. Lower motor neuron (LMN) lesions produce which set of signs?
    - **A. Flaccid paralysis, muscle atrophy, fasciculations, absent/reduced reflexes**
    - B. Spastic paralysis, hyperreflexia, Babinski sign
    - C. Spastic paralysis, minimal atrophy, preserved reflexes
    - D. Loss of pain only, preserved motor function

19. ALS affects both UMN and LMN. Which combination of signs would you expect?
    - A. Pure spastic paralysis with hyperreflexia only
    - B. Pure flaccid paralysis with atrophy only
    - **C. Mixed spastic and flaccid paralysis with both hyperreflexia and atrophy/fasciculations**
    - D. Sensory loss with preserved motor function

20. The anterior spinal artery supplies primarily which region of the spinal cord?
    - A. Dorsal columns
    - **B. Anterior two-thirds, including the anterior horn, lateral corticospinal tract, and spinothalamic tract**
    - C. Posterior columns only
    - D. The entire cord equally

---

### True / False — Spinal Tracts

1. The dorsal column pathway decussates at the spinal cord level of entry. **(False — decussates in the medulla)**
2. In Brown-Séquard syndrome, motor loss is ipsilateral to the lesion. **(True)**
3. Syringomyelia first disrupts dorsal column function because the syrinx forms in the posterior cord. **(False — syrinx forms centrally, first disrupting crossing spinothalamic fibers at the anterior commissure)**
4. The dorsal spinocerebellar tract conveys unconscious proprioception and does not decussate. **(True)**
5. UMN lesions produce flaccid paralysis and muscle atrophy. **(False — that describes LMN lesions; UMN lesions produce spastic paralysis)**
6. The lateral corticospinal tract decussates in the pyramidal decussation of the medulla. **(True)**
7. The vestibulospinal tract enhances extensor muscle tone to maintain upright posture. **(True)**

---

### FRQs — Spinal Cord Tracts

1. A patient is in a motorcycle accident and is found to have lost pain and temperature sensation on the left side of their body below T8, and lost fine touch and voluntary motor function on the right side below T8. Name this syndrome, identify the location and side of the lesion, and explain why each deficit appears on its respective side.

2. Compare and contrast the dorsal column–medial lemniscal pathway and the spinothalamic tract in terms of: modality carried, site of decussation, and the clinical deficit resulting from a unilateral lesion of each.

3. A patient develops a syrinx at spinal level C5–T1. Describe the expected pattern of sensory loss, which tracts are affected first, and why fine touch is preserved in the early stages.

4. Distinguish upper motor neuron (UMN) from lower motor neuron (LMN) lesions using four clinical signs each, and explain why each sign occurs mechanistically.

---

## SECTION 2: BRAINSTEM NUCLEI

### Quick Reference Table

| Nucleus | Location | Primary Function |
|---|---|---|
| Nucleus tractus solitarius (NTS) | Medulla | Receives visceral afferents: taste (VII, IX, X), baroreceptors, chemoreceptors, gut signals |
| Nucleus ambiguus | Medulla | Motor output for CN IX, X, XI — pharynx, larynx, soft palate (swallowing, speech) |
| Dorsal motor nucleus of vagus (DMV) | Medulla | Parasympathetic preganglionic output via CN X to thoracic and abdominal viscera |
| Hypoglossal nucleus | Medulla | Motor output for CN XII — tongue movements |
| Inferior olivary nucleus | Medulla | Relays error signals to cerebellum for motor learning |
| Raphe nuclei | Throughout brainstem | Serotonin synthesis; modulate mood, sleep, pain gating |
| Locus coeruleus | Pons | Norepinephrine synthesis; arousal, attention, stress response |
| Pontine nuclei | Pons | Relay voluntary motor signals from cortex to cerebellum |
| Superior olivary complex | Pons | First binaural processing; sound localization |
| Facial nucleus | Pons | Motor output for CN VII — facial muscles |
| Abducens nucleus | Pons | Motor output for CN VI — lateral eye movement |
| Edinger-Westphal nucleus | Midbrain | Parasympathetic output for pupillary constriction and lens accommodation (CN III) |
| Red nucleus | Midbrain | Origin of rubrospinal tract; motor coordination |
| Substantia nigra (pars compacta) | Midbrain | Dopamine synthesis; modulates basal ganglia for voluntary movement |
| Superior colliculus | Midbrain | Visual reflexes; coordinates eye and head movements to visual stimuli |
| Inferior colliculus | Midbrain | Auditory relay; sound localization |
| Periaqueductal gray (PAG) | Midbrain | Endogenous pain suppression via descending inhibitory pathways |

---

### Multiple Choice

1. Which brainstem nucleus serves as the primary relay station for visceral sensory information, including taste, baroreceptor input, and gut mechanoreceptors?
   - A. Nucleus ambiguus
   - B. Dorsal motor nucleus of vagus
   - **C. Nucleus tractus solitarius (NTS)**
   - D. Hypoglossal nucleus

2. A patient suffers a lateral medullary infarction (Wallenberg syndrome) damaging the nucleus ambiguus. Which of the following deficits would be expected?
   - A. Loss of taste from the anterior 2/3 of the tongue
   - **B. Dysphagia (difficulty swallowing) and hoarseness due to paralysis of pharyngeal and laryngeal muscles**
   - C. Loss of voluntary tongue movements
   - D. Loss of parasympathetic input to the heart

3. The dorsal motor nucleus of vagus (DMV) provides parasympathetic preganglionic output. Which of the following best describes its primary targets?
   - A. Salivary glands and lacrimal glands
   - **B. Heart, lungs, and abdominal viscera (up to the splenic flexure)**
   - C. Bladder and reproductive organs
   - D. Pupillary constriction and lens accommodation

4. A patient with a lesion of the dorsal motor nucleus of vagus would most likely experience:
   - A. Loss of taste
   - B. Ipsilateral facial droop
   - **C. Reduced parasympathetic tone to heart and gut (tachycardia, decreased GI motility)**
   - D. Inability to move the tongue

5. Raphe nuclei are distributed throughout the brainstem. Their primary neurotransmitter is:
   - A. Dopamine
   - B. Norepinephrine
   - **C. Serotonin**
   - D. Acetylcholine

6. Which brainstem structure is the primary source of norepinephrine in the brain and plays a central role in arousal, attention, and the stress response?
   - A. Raphe nuclei
   - B. Substantia nigra
   - **C. Locus coeruleus**
   - D. Periaqueductal gray

7. Damage to the raphe nuclei would most directly impair which of the following?
   - A. Voluntary motor control
   - B. Pupillary reflexes
   - **C. Mood regulation, sleep-wake cycles, and descending pain modulation**
   - D. Cardiovascular autonomic reflexes

8. The Edinger-Westphal nucleus provides parasympathetic output via CN III. A lesion here would cause:
   - A. Ipsilateral ptosis and downward gaze
   - **B. Fixed, dilated (mydriatic) pupil due to loss of pupillary constriction**
   - C. Loss of lateral gaze
   - D. Facial muscle paralysis

9. A patient cannot look to the right with either eye. The lesion is most likely in the:
   - A. Right oculomotor nucleus
   - B. Left oculomotor nucleus
   - **C. Right abducens nucleus** *(or right paramedian pontine reticular formation — controls conjugate gaze to ipsilateral side)*
   - D. Left abducens nucleus

10. Which midbrain nucleus is degenerated in Parkinson's disease, and what neurotransmitter does it produce?
    - A. Red nucleus; GABA
    - **B. Substantia nigra pars compacta; dopamine**
    - C. Locus coeruleus; norepinephrine
    - D. Raphe nuclei; serotonin

11. The periaqueductal gray (PAG) is involved in endogenous pain suppression. Which neurotransmitters mediate this descending inhibition?
    - A. Glutamate and GABA
    - **B. Enkephalins and endorphins (endogenous opioids), acting via raphe nuclei and locus coeruleus**
    - C. Dopamine and serotonin
    - D. Acetylcholine and substance P

12. The inferior olivary nucleus sends climbing fibers to the cerebellum. Its primary role is:
    - A. Relaying auditory information to the cortex
    - **B. Transmitting error signals to the cerebellum for motor learning and adaptation**
    - C. Coordinating eye movements
    - D. Processing vestibular signals for balance

13. A patient has difficulty learning a new motor skill after a lesion. Which brainstem-cerebellar circuit is most likely disrupted?
    - A. Superior olivary complex → auditory cortex
    - **B. Inferior olivary nucleus → cerebellar climbing fibers (Purkinje cells)**
    - C. Raphe nuclei → spinal cord
    - D. Locus coeruleus → prefrontal cortex

14. The superior colliculus coordinates reflexive eye and head movements in response to:
    - A. Auditory stimuli only
    - B. Vestibular stimuli only
    - **C. Both visual and auditory stimuli**
    - D. Proprioceptive stimuli from the neck

15. Which nucleus in the pons is the FIRST site of binaural (both-ear) processing in the auditory pathway?
    - A. Cochlear nucleus
    - **B. Superior olivary complex**
    - C. Inferior colliculus
    - D. Medial geniculate nucleus

16. Lateral medullary (Wallenberg) syndrome results from occlusion of the posterior inferior cerebellar artery (PICA). Which combination of deficits is characteristic?
    - **A. Ipsilateral facial pain/temperature loss, contralateral body pain/temperature loss, ipsilateral ataxia, dysphagia, Horner syndrome**
    - B. Ipsilateral hemiplegia, contralateral sensory loss, aphasia
    - C. Bilateral sensory loss with preserved motor function
    - D. Contralateral facial droop with ipsilateral body weakness

---

### True / False — Brainstem Nuclei

1. The nucleus ambiguus provides motor output to pharyngeal and laryngeal muscles via CN IX and X. **(True)**
2. The dorsal motor nucleus of vagus is responsible for voluntary tongue movements. **(False — that is the hypoglossal nucleus/CN XII)**
3. The locus coeruleus is the primary source of serotonin in the brain. **(False — raphe nuclei are the primary source of serotonin; locus coeruleus is the primary source of norepinephrine)**
4. The Edinger-Westphal nucleus controls pupillary constriction via parasympathetic fibers in CN III. **(True)**
5. The inferior colliculus is a relay station in the auditory pathway, while the superior colliculus mediates visual reflexes. **(True)**
6. Raphe nuclei play a role in descending pain modulation by releasing serotonin into the dorsal horn. **(True)**
7. The periaqueductal gray activates pain by releasing substance P. **(False — PAG suppresses pain via endogenous opioids)**

---

### FRQs — Brainstem Nuclei

1. Distinguish the nucleus tractus solitarius, nucleus ambiguus, and dorsal motor nucleus of vagus in terms of function, neurotransmitter/output type, and the clinical deficit expected from a lesion of each.

2. A patient presents with hoarseness, difficulty swallowing, loss of the gag reflex, ipsilateral facial numbness, contralateral body pain/temperature loss, and an ipsilateral Horner syndrome. Name this syndrome, identify the artery most likely occluded, and explain which structures are damaged.

3. Describe the role of the locus coeruleus and raphe nuclei in modulating arousal and pain. What would happen to a patient if both were selectively damaged?

4. Explain how the periaqueductal gray (PAG) suppresses pain. Name the descending pathway and the neurotransmitters involved at each synapse.

---

## SECTION 3: PROPRIOCEPTION MECHANICS

### Quick Reference Table

| Receptor | Location | Stimulus | Afferent Fiber | Response |
|---|---|---|---|---|
| Muscle spindle (intrafusal fibers) | Within muscle belly, parallel to extrafusal fibers | Muscle stretch (length change) | Ia (primary — nuclear bag) & II (secondary — nuclear chain) | Reflexive contraction of same muscle (stretch reflex); inhibition of antagonist |
| Golgi tendon organ (GTO) | Musculotendinous junction, in series with extrafusal fibers | Muscle tension / force | Ib | Inhibition of same muscle (autogenic inhibition); activation of antagonist |
| Pacinian corpuscle | Deep dermis, joints, periosteum | Vibration, rapid pressure change | Aβ | Sensation of vibration / deep pressure |
| Ruffini ending | Dermis, joint capsule | Sustained pressure, skin stretch, joint position | Aβ | Joint angle, skin stretch |
| Free nerve endings | Throughout tissue | Pain, temperature, crude touch | Aδ (fast/sharp pain) & C (slow/dull pain) | Nociception |

---

### Multiple Choice

1. Muscle spindles detect which stimulus, and what is their primary functional role?
   - A. Muscle tension; they inhibit the contracting muscle to prevent injury
   - **B. Muscle length/stretch; they trigger a reflexive contraction of the stretched muscle via the stretch reflex**
   - C. Joint angle; they send conscious position sense to the somatosensory cortex
   - D. Tendon force; they coordinate antagonist muscle activation

2. Golgi tendon organs (GTOs) are located in series with extrafusal muscle fibers, at the musculotendinous junction. Their primary role is to:
   - A. Detect muscle length change and trigger stretch reflexes
   - **B. Detect excessive muscle tension and reflexively inhibit the contracting muscle (autogenic inhibition)**
   - C. Provide conscious proprioception to the cerebellum
   - D. Regulate muscle tone during sleep

3. The patellar tendon reflex (knee-jerk) is a classic monosynaptic stretch reflex. Which afferent fiber type carries the signal from the muscle spindle to the spinal cord?
   - A. Aδ fibers
   - B. C fibers
   - C. Ib fibers
   - **D. Ia fibers**

4. In the patellar reflex, when the quadriceps contracts, the hamstrings must simultaneously relax. This is accomplished by:
   - A. Direct inhibition via a Ib fiber from the GTO of the hamstring
   - **B. Reciprocal inhibition via an inhibitory interneuron, activated by the Ia afferent from the quadriceps spindle**
   - C. Direct co-activation by the same Ia fiber
   - D. Descending inhibition from the motor cortex

5. Which afferent fiber type carries signals from the Golgi tendon organ?
   - A. Ia
   - **B. Ib**
   - C. II
   - D. Aδ

6. A muscle spindle contains two types of intrafusal fibers. Which type is primarily associated with static (tonic) stretch detection, and which is associated with dynamic (phasic) stretch?
   - A. Nuclear bag → static; nuclear chain → dynamic
   - **B. Nuclear bag → dynamic; nuclear chain → static**
   - C. Both nuclear bag and nuclear chain detect static stretch equally
   - D. Nuclear chain → dynamic; nuclear bag is only for proprioception to the cerebellum

7. Gamma motor neurons (γ-MNs) innervate the intrafusal fibers of the muscle spindle. Their role is to:
   - A. Directly contract the muscle to produce movement
   - **B. Adjust the sensitivity of the muscle spindle by maintaining intrafusal fiber tension during muscle contraction**
   - C. Inhibit the Golgi tendon organ signal
   - D. Relay proprioceptive information to the cerebellum

8. During a sustained muscle contraction, alpha motor neurons (α-MNs) fire and shorten the extrafusal muscle fibers. Without gamma motor neuron co-activation, what would happen to spindle sensitivity?
   - **A. The spindle would go slack and stop firing, losing the ability to detect further stretch**
   - B. The spindle would increase firing due to the tension on the tendon
   - C. The GTO would directly activate the spindle to compensate
   - D. Nothing — spindle sensitivity is independent of muscle length

9. A patient's deep tendon reflexes (DTRs) are hyperactive (hyperreflexia). This is most consistent with:
   - A. Lower motor neuron lesion — loss of the reflex arc
   - **B. Upper motor neuron lesion — loss of descending inhibition on the reflex arc**
   - C. Golgi tendon organ lesion — loss of autogenic inhibition
   - D. Muscle spindle degeneration — loss of stretch sensing

10. Proprioceptive signals from muscle spindles (conscious proprioception) travel to the cortex via which pathway?
    - A. Spinothalamic tract → VPL thalamus → somatosensory cortex
    - **B. Dorsal column–medial lemniscal pathway → VPL thalamus → somatosensory cortex**
    - C. Dorsal spinocerebellar tract → cerebellum
    - D. Rubrospinal tract → motor cortex

11. Unconscious proprioception from muscle spindles and GTOs reaches the cerebellum via which tracts?
    - A. Corticospinal and rubrospinal tracts
    - B. Spinothalamic tract
    - **C. Dorsal and ventral spinocerebellar tracts**
    - D. Dorsal column–medial lemniscal pathway

12. A patient has difficulty with fine coordination of movement but has intact conscious sensation of body position. Which pathway is most likely disrupted?
    - A. Dorsal column–medial lemniscal pathway
    - **B. Spinocerebellar tracts**
    - C. Spinothalamic tract
    - D. Lateral corticospinal tract

13. Ruffini endings in joint capsules detect:
    - A. Vibration and rapid changes in pressure
    - **B. Joint angle and sustained skin stretch**
    - C. Muscle length change
    - D. Muscle tension at the tendon

14. Which receptor detects vibration and rapid pressure changes in deep skin and periosteum?
    - **A. Pacinian corpuscles (Aβ fibers)**
    - B. Meissner's corpuscles
    - C. Merkel discs
    - D. Ruffini endings

15. A patient with tabes dorsalis (syphilitic degeneration of dorsal columns) would have difficulty with which of the following?
    - A. Feeling pain from a pin prick
    - **B. Detecting vibration from a tuning fork and knowing the position of their limbs with eyes closed**
    - C. Moving their limbs voluntarily
    - D. Feeling heat from a warm object

---

### True / False — Proprioception

1. Muscle spindles are in series with extrafusal muscle fibers and detect tension. **(False — in parallel; GTOs are in series and detect tension)**
2. Ia afferent fibers from muscle spindles produce monosynaptic excitation of alpha motor neurons in the stretch reflex. **(True)**
3. Golgi tendon organs produce autogenic inhibition — they inhibit the muscle in which they reside. **(True)**
4. Gamma motor neurons directly produce skeletal muscle contraction. **(False — they regulate spindle sensitivity by contracting intrafusal fibers)**
5. The dorsal spinocerebellar tract decussates and reaches the contralateral cerebellum. **(False — it is ipsilateral and does NOT decussate)**
6. Reciprocal inhibition is mediated by Ib fibers. **(False — reciprocal inhibition is mediated by Ia fibers acting on an inhibitory interneuron)**
7. Loss of conscious proprioception with preserved pain sensation indicates a dorsal column lesion. **(True)**

---

### FRQs — Proprioception

1. Compare muscle spindles and Golgi tendon organs using the following criteria for each: anatomical location, stimulus detected, afferent fiber type, and reflex response produced. Include a diagram description if helpful.

2. Explain the patellar tendon reflex arc in full detail, naming every component in order from stimulus to response, including the interneuron mechanism responsible for simultaneous hamstring relaxation.

3. A patient loses the ability to feel vibration in their feet and cannot tell which direction their big toe is pointing without looking, but can still feel a pin prick. Identify the pathway affected, name the condition, and explain the spinal cord anatomy responsible for this dissociation.

4. Explain the role of gamma motor neurons in maintaining spindle sensitivity during voluntary contraction (alpha-gamma coactivation). What would the consequence be if gamma neurons were selectively blocked?

---

## SECTION 4: DEEP ENDOCRINE — HPA AXIS, RAAS & STRESS RESPONSE

### Quick Reference: HPA Axis Feedback Loop

```
STRESS / LOW CORTISOL
        ↓
Hypothalamus → CRH (corticotropin-releasing hormone)
        ↓  [via hypophyseal portal system]
Anterior Pituitary → ACTH (adrenocorticotropic hormone)
        ↓  [via systemic circulation]
Adrenal Cortex (zona fasciculata) → CORTISOL
        ↓
Cortisol feeds back NEGATIVELY to:
  • Hypothalamus (↓ CRH)
  • Anterior pituitary (↓ ACTH)
```

### Quick Reference: RAAS Cascade

```
LOW BP / LOW Na⁺ / Sympathetic activation
        ↓
Juxtaglomerular cells of kidney → RENIN
        ↓
Renin cleaves Angiotensinogen (liver) → Angiotensin I
        ↓
ACE (angiotensin-converting enzyme, primarily in lung) → Angiotensin II
        ↓
Angiotensin II effects:
  1. Adrenal cortex (zona glomerulosa) → ALDOSTERONE → Na⁺/H₂O retention → ↑BP
  2. Vasoconstriction → ↑BP directly
  3. Hypothalamus → ↑ADH → water retention
  4. Hypothalamus → thirst
  5. Sympathetic nervous system → ↑HR, ↑contractility
```

---

### Multiple Choice — HPA Axis

1. Which of the following correctly describes the sequence of the HPA axis stress response?
   - A. ACTH → CRH → cortisol
   - **B. CRH → ACTH → cortisol**
   - C. Cortisol → CRH → ACTH
   - D. ACTH → cortisol → CRH

2. Cortisol exerts negative feedback primarily on which two structures?
   - A. Adrenal cortex and kidney
   - **B. Hypothalamus (↓CRH) and anterior pituitary (↓ACTH)**
   - C. Thyroid and parathyroid
   - D. Liver and adrenal medulla

3. A patient is on long-term exogenous corticosteroid therapy (e.g., prednisone). What happens to their endogenous HPA axis?
   - A. CRH and ACTH increase to compensate
   - **B. CRH and ACTH decrease due to negative feedback; the adrenal glands atrophy**
   - C. Cortisol production in the adrenal gland increases
   - D. The adrenal medulla produces more epinephrine to compensate

4. If a patient abruptly stops long-term corticosteroid therapy, what is the primary danger?
   - A. Cushing's syndrome from rebound cortisol hypersecretion
   - **B. Adrenal insufficiency crisis — the atrophied adrenal glands cannot produce sufficient cortisol**
   - C. Graves' disease from thyroid overstimulation
   - D. Hypoaldosteronism only, with preserved cortisol

5. The dexamethasone suppression test works because dexamethasone mimics cortisol's negative feedback. In a healthy person, dexamethasone administration suppresses cortisol. In Cushing's disease (pituitary adenoma), what result is expected?
   - A. Cortisol is fully suppressed because the pituitary responds normally to feedback
   - **B. Cortisol is NOT suppressed because the autonomous pituitary adenoma continues to overproduce ACTH despite negative feedback**
   - C. ACTH increases further due to the adenoma being stimulated by dexamethasone
   - D. Cortisol decreases but ACTH increases

6. Which of the following best describes the mechanism by which cortisol mobilizes energy during stress?
   - A. It directly phosphorylates glucose transporters to increase cellular uptake
   - **B. It promotes gluconeogenesis in the liver, lipolysis in adipose, and protein catabolism in muscle, raising blood glucose**
   - C. It stimulates insulin release to drive glucose into cells
   - D. It activates glycogenesis to store extra energy for later use

7. Cortisol also suppresses the immune system. Which mechanism best explains this?
   - A. Cortisol directly kills T cells
   - **B. Cortisol inhibits the production of pro-inflammatory cytokines (e.g., IL-1, IL-6, TNF-α) and reduces prostaglandin synthesis by inhibiting phospholipase A2**
   - C. Cortisol activates natural killer cells to destroy immune cells
   - D. Cortisol upregulates MHC expression, causing immune tolerance

8. Chronic stress leads to persistently elevated cortisol. Which of the following is NOT a consequence of chronic cortisol elevation?
   - A. Insulin resistance and increased risk of Type 2 diabetes
   - B. Immunosuppression and increased susceptibility to infection
   - C. Bone loss (osteoporosis) due to inhibition of osteoblasts
   - **D. Increased hippocampal neurogenesis, improving memory**
   *(Chronic cortisol actually damages the hippocampus and impairs memory)*

---

### Multiple Choice — RAAS & Aldosterone

9. Which cells in the kidney secrete renin, and what stimulates their activation?
   - **A. Juxtaglomerular (JG) cells; stimulated by low blood pressure, low Na⁺ delivery to the macula densa, and sympathetic (β1) activation**
   - B. Mesangial cells; stimulated by angiotensin II
   - C. Collecting duct principal cells; stimulated by ADH
   - D. Podocytes; stimulated by aldosterone

10. Angiotensin-converting enzyme (ACE) converts angiotensin I to angiotensin II. ACE is found predominantly in the:
    - A. Liver
    - **B. Lung**
    - C. Kidney
    - D. Adrenal cortex

11. ACE inhibitors (e.g., lisinopril) treat hypertension. Which of the following correctly describes their mechanism?
    - A. They block aldosterone receptors in the collecting duct
    - **B. They prevent conversion of angiotensin I to angiotensin II, reducing vasoconstriction and aldosterone release**
    - C. They directly block renin secretion from the juxtaglomerular cells
    - D. They block β1 receptors, reducing renin release

12. Angiotensin II has multiple effects. Which of the following is NOT a direct action of angiotensin II?
    - A. Vasoconstriction
    - B. Aldosterone release from the adrenal cortex
    - C. Increased ADH release from the posterior pituitary
    - **D. Direct inhibition of renin release from the kidney** *(this is indirect — via restored BP)*

13. Aldosterone acts on principal cells of the collecting duct. What is its mechanism of action?
    - A. It binds to membrane receptors and activates a cAMP cascade to open Na⁺ channels
    - **B. It binds to intracellular (nuclear) mineralocorticoid receptors, increasing transcription of ENaC (Na⁺ channels) and Na⁺/K⁺-ATPase, increasing Na⁺ reabsorption and K⁺ excretion**
    - C. It directly phosphorylates aquaporin-2, increasing water permeability
    - D. It activates the thiazide-sensitive NaCl cotransporter in the distal tubule

14. A patient with primary hyperaldosteronism (Conn's syndrome) presents with hypertension. What lab abnormalities would you expect?
    - A. High renin, high aldosterone, hyperkalemia
    - **B. Low renin, high aldosterone, hypokalemia, metabolic alkalosis**
    - C. High renin, low aldosterone, hyperkalemia
    - D. Low renin, low aldosterone, hyponatremia

15. Why does primary hyperaldosteronism cause low renin?
    - A. Aldosterone directly destroys JG cells
    - **B. The elevated blood pressure and sodium/volume expansion from excess aldosterone suppresses renin via negative feedback**
    - C. Aldosterone directly inhibits renin gene transcription
    - D. The adrenal adenoma produces a renin-inhibiting factor

16. A patient is in hemorrhagic shock with low blood pressure. Trace the complete RAAS response in the correct order:
    - **Low BP → JG cells → Renin → Angiotensinogen → Angiotensin I → ACE (lung) → Angiotensin II → [vasoconstriction + aldosterone (adrenal) + ADH (hypothalamus)] → restored BP and volume**

17. ADH (vasopressin) is released in response to angiotensin II and high plasma osmolality. Its mechanism in the collecting duct is to:
    - A. Increase Na⁺ reabsorption via ENaC insertion
    - **B. Insert aquaporin-2 (AQP2) channels into the apical membrane of collecting duct cells, increasing water reabsorption**
    - C. Stimulate active Cl⁻ reabsorption
    - D. Activate the Na⁺/K⁺/2Cl⁻ cotransporter in the thick ascending limb

---

### Multiple Choice — Integrated Stress Response

18. During the acute stress response, the adrenal medulla releases epinephrine. Which receptor mediates increased heart rate?
    - A. α1 receptors on the SA node
    - **B. β1 receptors on the SA node**
    - C. M2 muscarinic receptors
    - D. β2 receptors in the heart

19. Which of the following correctly distinguishes the acute (fast) stress response from the chronic (slow) stress response?
    - **A. Acute: epinephrine/norepinephrine from adrenal medulla (seconds); Chronic: cortisol from adrenal cortex (minutes to hours)**
    - B. Acute: cortisol (minutes); Chronic: epinephrine (hours)
    - C. Both responses involve cortisol at different concentrations
    - D. The acute response is parasympathetic; the chronic response is sympathetic

20. Epinephrine acts on β2 receptors in skeletal muscle vasculature. What is the result?
    - A. Vasoconstriction to redirect blood to the gut
    - **B. Vasodilation to increase blood flow to muscles during fight-or-flight**
    - C. Increased muscle contraction directly
    - D. Decreased oxygen delivery to muscles

21. Which of the following effects of epinephrine on the liver helps provide immediate energy during stress?
    - A. Promotes glycogenesis (glucose storage)
    - **B. Promotes glycogenolysis (breaking glycogen to glucose) and gluconeogenesis**
    - C. Inhibits glucagon release
    - D. Stimulates fatty acid synthesis

22. A patient is found unconscious in the ICU. Lab results show: low cortisol, high ACTH, hyponatremia, hyperkalemia, hypoglycemia, and hypotension. What is the most likely diagnosis?
    - A. Cushing's disease
    - **B. Primary adrenal insufficiency (Addison's disease)**
    - C. SIADH
    - D. Pheochromocytoma

23. The same patient (from question 22) is given exogenous ACTH (cosyntropin stimulation test), and cortisol levels do NOT rise. What does this confirm?
    - **A. The adrenal glands themselves are damaged and cannot respond to ACTH — confirming primary (adrenal) insufficiency**
    - B. The pituitary is not producing ACTH
    - C. The hypothalamus is not producing CRH
    - D. The patient has secondary adrenal insufficiency

24. Which of the following best explains why patients with Addison's disease develop hyperpigmentation?
    - A. Low cortisol directly stimulates melanocytes
    - **B. Low cortisol removes negative feedback → very high ACTH → ACTH shares a precursor (POMC) with MSH, which stimulates melanocytes**
    - C. Aldosterone deficiency causes skin pigment deposition
    - D. Adrenal androgens cause hyperpigmentation when they are absent

---

### True / False — Deep Endocrine

1. In the HPA axis, cortisol exerts positive feedback on CRH and ACTH to amplify the stress response. **(False — negative feedback)**
2. Long-term corticosteroid therapy causes adrenal atrophy due to suppression of ACTH. **(True)**
3. Renin is produced by the juxtaglomerular cells of the afferent arteriole. **(True)**
4. ACE is found primarily in the liver, where it converts angiotensin I to angiotensin II. **(False — primarily in the lung)**
5. Aldosterone acts on nuclear receptors to increase transcription of sodium channel subunits (ENaC). **(True)**
6. Primary hyperaldosteronism (Conn's syndrome) is associated with high renin and high aldosterone. **(False — low renin, high aldosterone)**
7. During acute stress, epinephrine is released within seconds from the adrenal medulla via direct sympathetic innervation. **(True)**
8. Cortisol promotes insulin sensitivity in peripheral tissues, helping clear the glucose it mobilizes. **(False — cortisol causes insulin resistance)**
9. In the cosyntropin stimulation test, a failure of cortisol to rise confirms primary adrenal insufficiency. **(True)**
10. ACTH and MSH are both derived from the same precursor molecule, POMC. **(True)**

---

### FRQs — Deep Endocrine

1. Trace the full HPA axis from initial stress stimulus to cortisol release. Then explain what happens to this axis in a patient who takes high-dose prednisone daily for 6 months and then abruptly stops. What is the clinical risk?

2. Explain the complete RAAS cascade triggered by hemorrhagic shock. Name every enzyme, hormone, and organ involved, and describe each step's effect on blood pressure.

3. Compare primary and secondary adrenal insufficiency in terms of: cause, expected ACTH levels, expected cortisol levels, response to cosyntropin stimulation test, and the presence or absence of hyperpigmentation.

4. A patient with a small-cell lung cancer is found to have high cortisol and high ACTH, and cortisol does NOT suppress with a high-dose dexamethasone suppression test. Explain why. What syndrome does this patient have?

5. Explain the mechanism of aldosterone's action at the molecular level in the collecting duct. Then predict the exact lab abnormalities (Na⁺, K⁺, pH, renin, aldosterone, blood pressure) for a patient with a cortisol-secreting adrenal adenoma vs. an aldosterone-secreting adrenal adenoma.

6. Describe the acute vs. chronic stress response: which gland and hormone is involved in each, the time course, and the downstream metabolic effects. Why is chronic cortisol elevation harmful to the hippocampus?

---

# Science Olympiad Master Question Bank
*Compiled from: 2026 Invitationals (Troy, UCR, HPDI, UMSO, Solon, Archimedean, MVSO, Wolfpack, HG, HDSO, Seven Lakes, TAMU, Purdue, Carmel Valley, USC, UCR Div C, WashU) + Practice Exams (2010–2013, 2016–2017, 2021, Mesa-Wilson 2017, Eastside 2013, Holly Springs 2025–26, Dulles HS 2025, FCS Innovation 2025, Bergen County Academies, Columbia Jan 2026)*

## VISUAL PATHWAYS & PHOTOTRANSDUCTION

**[Troy]** List the following structures in the order light passes through them: lens, cornea, retina, pupil, optic nerve

**[Troy]** Which of the following is true of vision and phototransduction? Select all that apply.
- a. Damage to the optic chiasm will cause bitemporal hemianopia
- b. GMP is converted to cGMP by phosphodiesterase
- c. Transducin is a G-protein
- d. Na⁺ channels open when rhodopsin's retinal is converted to its all-trans form
- e. Vitamin A is converted into retinal for use in rhodopsins

**[Troy]** Michelle cuts the fibers of Larry's left nasal optic nerve before the optic chiasm. What visual field defect results?
- a. Loss of vision in left visual field in both eyes
- b. Loss of vision in right visual field in both eyes
- c. Loss of vision in left visual field in left eye
- d. Loss of vision in right visual field in left eye
- e. Loss of temporal vision in both eyes

**[HPDI]** Which best describes the underlying optical defect in astigmatism?
- A. The lens loses its elasticity and cannot accommodate for near vision
- B. The cornea or lens has an irregular curvature, causing light to focus at multiple points
- C. The eyeball is too short, causing light to focus behind the retina
- D. Increased intraocular pressure damages the optic nerve

**[UMSO]** Which cranial nerve carries visual information from the retina to the brain?
- a. Trochlear nerve (CN IV) / b. Optic nerve (CN II) / c. Oculomotor nerve (CN III) / d. Abducens nerve (CN VI)

**[Archimedean]** Which option correctly describes the pathway of visual information from the retina to the brain?
- A. Retina → Optic chiasm → Optic nerve → LGN → Visual cortex
- B. Retina → Optic nerve → Optic chiasm → Optic tract → LGN → Visual cortex
- C. Retina → LGN → Optic nerve → Optic tract → Visual cortex
- D. Retina → Optic tract → Optic chiasm → Visual cortex → LGN

**[Archimedean]** Damage to the optic chiasm typically results in which visual field defect?
- A. Monocular blindness / B. Homonymous hemianopia / C. Bitemporal hemianopia / D. Central scotoma

**[MVSO]** A lesion of the right optic tract causes:
- A) Right monocular vision loss / B) Left homonymous hemianopia / C) Right homonymous hemianopia / D) Bitemporal hemianopia

**[MVSO]** The principal excitatory neurotransmitter from photoreceptors to ON-bipolar cells is:
- A) Glutamate (sign-inverting via mGluR6) / B) GABA / C) Glycine / D) Acetylcholine

**[MVSO]** Aqueous humor outflow resistance increases mainly at the:
- A) Uveoscleral pathway / B) Trabecular meshwork / C) Ciliary body epithelium / D) Canal of Schlemm endothelium

**[MVSO]** A craniopharyngioma most classically causes which visual defect?
- A) Bitemporal hemianopia / B) Right homonymous hemianopia / C) Left superior quadrantanopia / D) Monocular scotoma

**[MVSO]** A lesion of Meyer's loop causes:
- A) Right superior quadrantanopia / B) Left superior quadrantanopia / C) Right inferior quadrantanopia / D) Left inferior quadrantanopia

**[MVSO]** During dark adaptation, photoreceptors exhibit:
- A) Low cGMP; Na⁺/Ca²⁺ channels closed; hyperpolarized
- B) High cGMP; Na⁺/Ca²⁺ channels open; depolarized
- C) High cAMP; K⁺ channels open; hyperpolarized
- D) Low Ca²⁺; glutamate release decreased

**[MVSO]** Which statements about intrinsically photosensitive retinal ganglion cells (ipRGCs) are accurate? Select all that apply.
- A) Express melanopsin, ~480 nm peak, sustained photoresponses
- B) Depolarize via G-protein cascade distinct from rods/cones; contribute to circadian rhythm and pupil light reflex
- C) Project first to LGN then to non-image-forming areas
- D) Convey irradiance signals for mood/sleep regulation
- E) Rely on canonical rod cascade (transducin → PDE6)

**[HG]** True/False: The fovea contains more rods than cones.

**[HG]** True/False: The "blind spot" of the eye is caused by the optic disk.

**[HG]** True/False: Bipolar neurons are found in the retina.

**[HG]** Which structure adjusts the amount of light entering the eye?
- A) cornea / B) lens / C) retina / D) iris

**[HG]** Which region of the retina is responsible for high-resolution central vision?
- A) optic disc / B) peripheral retina / C) macula (containing the fovea) / D) choroid

**[HG]** If the cornea is scratched, what is the most likely result?
- A) complete blindness / B) color blindness / C) tunnel vision / D) blurred vision

**[HG]** Which photopigment is bleached by a bright flash of light?
- A) melanopsin / B) iodopsin / C) photopsin / D) rhodopsin

**[HG]** Which photoreceptors function in dim light?
- A) cones / B) bipolar cells / C) ganglion cells / D) rods

**[HG]** Which photoreceptors are more numerous in the peripheral retina?
- A) cones / B) rods / C) bipolar cells / D) ganglion cells

**[HG]** A person looks directly at a dim star and cannot see it, but sees it when looking slightly to the side. Why?
- A) The fovea contains mostly cones, which are ineffective in low light
- B) Rods are concentrated in the fovea
- C) The optic disc is at the center of gaze
- D) The iris blocks central vision in dark conditions

**[HG]** Overlapping visual fields of both eyes produce:
- A) peripheral vision / B) tunnel vision / C) monochromatic vision / D) binocular vision

**[Seven Lakes]** What cutting causes contralateral hemianopia? (A. Nasal Hemiretina / B. Temporal Hemiretina / C. Geniculocalcarine Tract / D. Optic Chiasm / E. Optic Nerve / AB. Optic Tract)

**[Seven Lakes]** A patient cannot see the periphery of their right eye, but their macula is spared. What was the lesion? (Use same terms)

**[Seven Lakes]** Looking straight at a large painting, unable to see the outer regions. What was the lesion?

**[Seven Lakes]** Phineas Gage lost his left eye. What did the iron rod sever?

**[TAMU]** Damage to the left lateral geniculate nucleus leads to:
- a. Inability to perceive the left half of the retina / b. Inability to move the right half of the body / c. Inability to perceive the left half of your visual field / d. Inability to perceive info from your left ear

**[TAMU]** Damage to the optic chiasm would MOST likely affect:
- a. Depth perception / b. Hearing volume / c. Taste processing / d. Inner ear pressure / e. Pupil constriction

**[TAMU]** Which cranial nerve carries visual information to the brain?
- a. CN I / b. CN II / c. CN III / d. CN V / e. CN VI

**[TAMU]** The bending of light as it passes through different media is called ______________.

**[TAMU]** The structure where optic nerve fibers partially cross is the __________ __________.

**[Purdue]** A buildup of fluid in the interior of the eyeball is known as:
- a. Astigmatism / b. Cataract / c. Myopia / d. Glaucoma

**[Carmel Valley]** Which cranial nerve is primarily responsible for vision?
- a) CN II / b) CN III / c) CN V / d) CN VIII

**[Carmel Valley]** What neuron is used to form the optic nerve?
- a) Retinal ganglion cell / b) Retinal Schwann cell / c) Retinal Satellite cell / d) Retinal Astrocyte cell

**[UCR Div C]** Identify molecules E (cis-retinal) and F (trans-retinal) in the phototransduction diagram. Explain their interaction.

**[WashU]** TRUE or FALSE: All information from the right eye is processed in the left side of the brain.

**[WashU]** Order the structures of the visual pathway from first to last: Retina, Optic Nerve, Optic Chiasma, Optic Tract, Lateral Geniculate Nucleus, Optic Radiations, Visual Cortex, Visual Associative Cortex

**[Practice — Sense Organs]** Which vision disorder is associated with Vitamin A deficiency?
- a. Myopia / b. Hyperopia / c. Presbyopia / d. Nyctalopia

**[Practice — Sense Organs]** What type of eye disorder is caused by the eyeball being too long? *(Myopia)*

**[Practice — Sense Organs]** What type of eye disorder is nearsightedness? a. Myopia / b. Hyperopia / c. Cataracts

**[Practice — Sense Organs]** The function of the choroid is to:
- a. Make color vision possible / b. Refract light rays / c. Absorb stray light / d. Regulate light entrance

**[Practice — Sense Organs]** The fovea is specialized for: a. Rod concentration / b. Peripheral vision / **c. Highest cone density and sharpest vision**

**[Practice — Sense Organs]** Damage to the macula lutea affects which function most directly? **b. Central sharp vision**

**[Practice — Sense Organs]** Rods differ from cones in that they: **c. Have higher sensitivity to low light**

**[Practice — Sense Organs]** Which cells in the retina contain melanopsin? **d. Ganglion cells (ipRGCs)**

**[Practice — Sense Organs]** Which cells in the retina contain photopsin? **b. Cones**

**[Practice — Sense Organs]** Night blindness is due to inability to form the vitamin A-derived component of what visual pigment? **b. Rhodopsin**

**[Practice — Sense Organs]** Which retinal cells perform lateral inhibition to enhance contrast detection? **d. Horizontal cells**

**[Practice — Sense Organs]** What neurotransmitter is released by photoreceptors in the dark? **a. Glutamate**

**[Practice — Sense Organs]** The ganglionic cells have axons that become the _____ nerve. **d. Optic**

**[Practice — Sense Organs]** Which photoreceptor cells contribute to circadian rhythm regulation rather than image formation? **c. Intrinsically photosensitive retinal ganglion cells (ipRGCs)**

**[Practice — Sense Organs]** Damage to the right optic tract results in: **b. Left homonymous hemianopia**

**[Practice — Sense Organs]** Color blindness is caused primarily by: **c. Defective or missing cone opsins**

**[Practice — Sense Organs]** Which condition is caused by an uneven curvature of the cornea or lens? **b. Astigmatism**

**[Practice — Sense Organs]** Which disorder stems from declining flexibility of the lens correlated to age? **d. Presbyopia**

**[Practice — Sense Organs]** Myopia results from: **b. Too long an eyeball or excessively curved cornea**

**[Practice — Sense Organs]** Diabetic retinopathy initially results from: **e. Microaneurysms from damaged retinal vessels**

**[Practice — Sense Organs]** Glaucoma is caused by: **c. Increased intraocular pressure damaging the optic nerve**

**[Practice — Sense Organs]** Mark: cloudy spots, difficulty seeing at night, light sensitivity. Likely diagnosis? **b. Cataracts; lens**

**[Practice — Sense Organs]** When following a car moving left to right, the muscles and cranial nerves used are: **b. Left medial rectus (CN III) and right lateral rectus (CN VI)**

**[Practice — Sense Organs — FRQ]** Explain the process of phototransduction from photon absorption to action potential in the optic nerve.

**[Practice — Sense Organs — FRQ]** Explain why damage to the optic chiasm produces bitemporal hemianopia.

**[Practice — Sense Organs — FRQ]** Compare and contrast rods and cones in structure, distribution, and specific functions.

**[Practice — Sense Organs — FRQ]** Analyze how a lesion in the right optic tract would affect a patient's vision.

**[Practice — Sense Organs — FRQ]** Bobby fell asleep in a dark movie theater. When he walked into bright sunlight, he was momentarily blinded. Explain why, using: sensitivity, photopigment, rods, cones.

**[Practice — Sense Organs — FRQ]** What causes the irises of albino people to appear red or pink?

**[Practice — Sense Organs — FRQ]** Describe how glaucoma leads to blindness.

**[Practice — Sense Organs — FRQ]** Rohan cannot see the colors of the sky. Which type of cone is he lacking and at what chromosome does he likely have a mutation?

**[Practice — Sense Organs — FRQ]** Vascular tissue near Rohan's ciliary muscle has a thrombus. Which disease could result and how would it be treated?

**[Practice — Sense Organs — Case: Tina (Myopia)]** Tina notices blurry street signs and difficulty reading. 1) What is this condition? 2) What type of lenses? 3) What will glasses do?

## PUPILLARY REFLEX & OCULOMOTOR

**[UCR Div B]** What are the afferent and efferent nerve fibers for the pupillary reflex?
- Afferent: _______________ / Efferent: _______________

**[Archimedean]** True/False: The optomotor nerve is responsible for pupil constriction.

**[Archimedean]** Which statement correctly describes autonomic control of the ciliary muscle?
- A) Parasympathetic activation relaxes the ciliary muscle, flattening the lens for near vision
- B) Sympathetic activation contracts the ciliary muscle to increase lens curvature
- C) Parasympathetic activation contracts the ciliary muscle, allowing the lens to thicken for near vision
- D) Sympathetic activation has no effect on the ciliary muscle

**[Archimedean]** Which structure directly sends signals to the Edinger-Westphal nucleus during the pupillary light reflex?
- A. LGN / B. Optic tract / C. Oculomotor nucleus / D. Pretectal nucleus

**[Archimedean]** Which pairing correctly matches the autonomic pathway with its effect on the pupil?
- A. Parasympathetic activation causes constriction by stimulating the sphincter pupillae
- B. Parasympathetic activation causes dilation by stimulating the radial muscles
- C. Sympathetic activation causes constriction by stimulating the sphincter pupillae
- D. Sympathetic activation has no effect on pupil size

**[MVSO — FRQ]** Contrast sympathetic and parasympathetic control of the pupil and accommodation. Include neurotransmitters and receptors. Predict pharmacologic pupil responses to phenylephrine vs. pilocarpine in Adie pupil vs. CN III palsy.

**[HG]** Accommodation — which structure adjusts the shape of the lens for near vs. far vision?
- A) iris / B) ciliary muscles / C) cornea / D) sclera

**[HG]** Pupil dilation is primarily controlled by which system?
- A) parasympathetic / B) sympathetic / C) somatic / D) enteric

**[Seven Lakes]** Which statement correctly identifies the innervation of the eye related to the Edinger-Westphal nucleus?
- A) Ciliary ganglion lies caudal to EW and stimulated for accommodation
- B) Fibers from EW to ciliary ganglion control ciliary muscle and sphincter of iris
- C) The ciliary ganglion has no true synapses
- D) CN II is responsible for efferent stimulation of ciliary muscles more than EW/CN III

**[TAMU]** Jack has a negative direct and positive indirect pupillary response when light is shone into his left eye. Damage is to:
- a. Right Optic Tract / b. Left Optic Tract / c. Right Oculomotor Nerve / d. Left Oculomotor Nerve / e. Optic Chiasm

**[Practice — Cranial Nerve Reference]**

| Test / Function | Cranial Nerve |
|---|---|
| Tongue symmetry | CN XII (Hypoglossal) |
| Strength/evenness of shrug | CN XI (Accessory) |
| Biting pencil, skin sensation, corneal reflex | CN V (Trigeminal) |
| Smelling aromatic oils | CN I (Olfactory) |
| Rinne/Weber tuning fork, balance | CN VIII (Vestibulocochlear) |
| "Ah!", uvula position, gag reflex | CN IX/X (Glossopharyngeal/Vagus) |
| Taste posterior 1/3 tongue | CN IX (Glossopharyngeal) |
| Motor to facial muscles | CN VII (Facial) |
| Eye abduction | CN VI (Abducens) |
| Eye movement (most), pupillary constriction | CN III (Oculomotor) |

How many pairs of cranial nerves? **12 pairs**
How many pairs of spinal nerves? **31 pairs**

**[Practice — Nervous System]** Which cranial nerve controls eye movement (abducens)? **a. CN VI**

**[Practice — Nervous System]** Cranial nerve VII is called _____ and has _____ function: **c. Facial nerve — motor to the facial muscles**

## NEUROPHYSIOLOGY — ACTION POTENTIALS & MEMBRANE POTENTIALS

**[Practice — Nervous System]** What primarily causes the neuron's resting membrane potential to be negative?
- a. The Na⁺/K⁺ pump pumps out 3 Na⁺ for every 2 K⁺
- **b. Open K⁺ leak channels allow more K⁺ to leave than Na⁺ can enter**
- c. Intracellular proteins carry negative charge
- d. Voltage-gated Na⁺ channels are closed at rest

**[Practice — Nervous System]** What is the approximate resting potential of a neuron?
- a. −90 mV / b. −82 mV / **c. −70 mV** / d. +20 mV

**[Practice — Nervous System]** At resting membrane potential, potassium wants to move ___ the cell, and sodium wants to move ___ the cell? *(K⁺: out; Na⁺: in)*

**[Practice — Nervous System]** Initial depolarization of an action potential is caused by:
- a. K⁺ entering / b. K⁺ exiting / **c. Na⁺ entering** / d. Na⁺ exiting

**[Practice — Nervous System]** Which is a factor in generating the resting membrane potential?
- a. Lower concentration of K⁺ immediately inside the cell membrane
- **b. Higher concentration of Na⁺ immediately outside the cell membrane**
- c. Greater permeability to Na⁺ than K⁺
- d. All of the above

**[Practice — Nervous System]** Which correctly describes the function of the sodium-potassium pump?
- a. Allows passive movement of ions / b. Hyperpolarizes by allowing Cl⁻ in
- **c. Actively exports 3 Na⁺ and imports 2 K⁺ using ATP**
- d. Actively transports 3 K⁺ in and 2 Na⁺ out

**[Practice — Nervous System]** Which ion's relative permeability influences membrane potential the most (GHK equation)?
- a. Na⁺ / b. Cl⁻ / **c. K⁺** / d. Ca²⁺

**[Practice — Nervous System]** For an increase in K⁺ movement out of the neuron, this causes:
- a. Depolarization / **b. Hyperpolarization** / c. K⁺ replaced by Na⁺ / d. K⁺ replaced by Ca²⁺

**[Practice — Nervous System]** Which statement about the refractory period is TRUE?
- a. During absolute refractory period, a strong stimulus can trigger another AP
- b. Relative refractory period occurs when Na⁺ channels are open and inactivated
- **c. During the relative refractory period, K⁺ channels are open**
- d. The refractory period affects the entire neuron equally

**[Practice — Nervous System]** A neuron's threshold is −55 mV and it is in its absolute refractory period. How strong a stimulus is needed for a second AP?
- **d. None — no stimulus can trigger an AP during absolute refractory period**

**[Practice — Nervous System]** What is the "threshold" potential of a membrane?
- **d. The minimum depolarization needed to operate voltage-gated sodium and potassium channels**

**[Practice — Nervous System]** True/False: Action potentials happen in an all-or-nothing fashion. *(True)*

**[Practice — Nervous System]** True/False: In a neuron that depolarizes, Na⁺ flows into the cell. *(True)*

**[Practice — Nervous System]** The Goldman-Hodgkin-Katz equation is used to calculate what? *(Resting membrane potential)*

**[Practice — Nervous System]** What ion primarily drives the depolarization phase? *(Na⁺)*

**[Practice — Nervous System — FRQ]** Describe what happens to a neuron's membrane during each phase of an action potential (depolarization, repolarization, hyperpolarization).

**[Practice — Nervous System — FRQ]** Describe the all-or-nothing law.

**[Practice — Nervous System — FRQ]** Which class of neurotransmitters is acetylcholine most similar to structurally? What differentiates it? Why are its distinctive properties crucial to its usability in the brain?

**[Practice — Nervous System — FRQ]** If inhibitory neurotransmitters were dysfunctional, what would happen to neurons? Give an example using a specific neurotransmitter.

## SALTATORY CONDUCTION & MYELINATION

**[Practice — Nervous System]** In saltatory conduction, action potentials propagate along what part of myelinated axons?
- a. Along the entire axon / **b. Nodes of Ranvier** / c. Only through the myelin sheath / d. Dendrites only

**[Practice — Nervous System]** What process describes an action potential jumping from one node of Ranvier to the next? *(Saltatory conduction)*

**[Practice — Nervous System]** Which part of the neuron integrates incoming signals and initiates an action potential?
- a. Axon terminal / **b. Axon hillock** / c. Dendrite / d. Myelin sheath

**[Practice — Nervous System]** Action potentials move along axons:
- a. More slowly in large-diameter axons
- b. By direct action of acetylcholine on the axonal membrane
- c. By activating the sodium-potassium pump at each point
- **d. More rapidly in myelinated than in non-myelinated axons**

**[Practice — Nervous System]** Saltatory conduction refers to:
- a. Conduction across electrical synapses / b. AP skipping the axon hillock / c. Rapid reverberating movement / d. Jumping from one neuron to another / **e. Jumping from one node of Ranvier to the next**

**[Practice — Nervous System]** The progression of a nerve impulse along nodes of Ranvier is called:
- **a. Saltatory Conduction** / b. Relative Conduction / c. Action Potential / d. Resting Potential

**[Practice — Nervous System]** What are the gaps between myelinated axons called? *(Nodes of Ranvier)*

**[Practice — Nervous System]** True/False: The midbrain is the largest region of the brainstem. *(False — the pons is the largest)*

**[Practice — Nervous System]** True/False: Slightly decreasing axon diameter increases conduction velocity by reducing internal resistance. *(False — larger diameter increases velocity)*

**[Practice — Nervous System]** Which is characteristic of myelination exclusively in the CNS?
- **d. Newer layers of myelin are formed under preexisting layers**

**[Practice — Nervous System]** Which best describes Wallerian degeneration?
- **b. Degeneration of the axon distal to the site of injury, while the proximal segment remains connected to the neurosoma**

**[Practice — Nervous System — FRQ]** Explain the function of myelination, which cells perform it, and give an example of a related disorder.

**[Practice — Nervous System — FRQ]** Describe the regeneration of a damaged nerve.

## SYNAPTIC TRANSMISSION & NEUROTRANSMITTERS

**[Practice — Nervous System]** Neurotransmitters are released from axon terminals via:
- a. Osmosis / b. Active transport / c. Diffusion / d. Transcytosis / **e. Exocytosis**

**[Practice — Nervous System]** An IPSP occurs when the membrane becomes more permeable to:
- **a. Potassium ions** / b. Sodium ions / c. Calcium ions

**[Practice — Nervous System]** What property distinguishes temporal summation from spatial summation?
- **a. It involves inputs at the same synapse over time**

**[Practice — Nervous System]** When several EPSPs arrive at the axon hillock from different dendritic locations, this is an example of:
- **b. Spatial summation**

**[Practice — Nervous System]** What best distinguishes monosynaptic from polysynaptic reflexes?
- **a. Polysynaptic reflexes require interneurons while monosynaptic reflexes do not**

**[Practice — Nervous System]** Which structure detects muscle stretch to initiate the stretch reflex?
- **c. Muscle spindle**

**[Practice — Nervous System]** Where is acetylcholine synthesized and enclosed?
- **c. Presynaptic terminal and synaptic vesicles**

**[Practice — Nervous System]** True/False: Voltage-gated calcium channels at the axon terminal trigger neurotransmitter release. *(True)*

**[Practice — Nervous System]** What neurotransmitter is released at the neuromuscular junction? *(Acetylcholine)*

**[Practice — Nervous System]** What neurotransmitter do muscarinic receptors bind to? *(Acetylcholine)*

**[Practice — Nervous System]** Which of the following is NOT a catecholamine? **a. Serotonin**

**[Practice — Nervous System]** All catecholamines are synthesized from: **b. Tyrosine**

**[Practice — Nervous System]** Which neuromodulator is created via arginine metabolism in endothelial cells? **d. Nitric oxide**

**[Practice — Nervous System]** What is the role of an enkephalin? **b. To act as a natural painkiller by inhibiting pain signals**

**[Practice — Nervous System]** Which receptor decreases heart rate when activated? **b. M2**

**[Practice — Nervous System]** True/False: All biogenic amines are derived from modifications to threonine. *(False — from aromatic amino acids)*

**[Practice — Nervous System]** True/False: The effects of serotonin tend to have a slower onset than other neurotransmitters. *(True)*

**[Practice — Nervous System]** True/False: Stimulation of 5-HT2A receptors can cause hallucinogenic effects. *(True)*

| Neurotransmitter | Category |
|---|---|
| Epinephrine | Catecholamine |
| Norepinephrine | Catecholamine |
| Dopamine | Catecholamine |
| Serotonin | Indolamine |
| Histamine | Imidazolamine |

**[Practice — Nervous System]** What is the neurotransmitter used in the sympathetic nervous system? **a. Epinephrine**

**[Practice — Nervous System]** What is the brain's main excitatory neurotransmitter? **b. Glutamate**

**[Practice — Nervous System]** What do muscarinic receptors bind to? **c. Acetylcholine**

**[Practice — Nervous System]** The primary effect of cocaine is that it blocks re-uptake of: **a. Monoamines**

**[Practice — Nervous System]** Botulinum toxin impairs neurotransmission by: **c. Preventing vesicular release of ACh**

**[Practice — Nervous System]** In a short sentence, how is botulinum toxin used in cosmetic medicine? *(Injected in small amounts to temporarily paralyze facial muscles, reducing wrinkles)*

**[Practice — Nervous System]** Which medication is commonly used to treat spasticity in multiple sclerosis? **b. Baclofen**

**[Practice — Nervous System]** True/False: Botulism destroys SNARES that activate skeletal muscle in excitatory neurons. *(True)*

**[Practice — Nervous System]** True/False: Monoamine oxidase inhibitors act by inhibiting the degradation of glutamate. *(False — they inhibit degradation of monoamines)*

**[Practice — Nervous System — FRQ]** When *Clostridium tetani* produces tetanospasmin, what disorder results and how does it cause muscle stiffness? *(Tetanus; disinhibition of motor neurons by a GABA blockade)*

**[Practice — Nervous System]** Which brain region converts short-term memory into long-term memory? *(Hippocampus)*

**[Practice — Nervous System]** What structure allows communication between the two cerebral hemispheres? *(Corpus callosum)*

## NEUROLOGICAL DISORDERS

**[Practice — Nervous System]** In which disease do sclerotic sheaths result in poor conduction of action potentials? **a. Multiple Sclerosis**

**[Practice — Nervous System]** What disease is caused by the loss of dopamine? **a. Parkinson's Disease**

**[Practice — Nervous System]** Which neurotransmitter system is primarily affected in Parkinson's disease? **b. Dopamine**

**[Practice — Nervous System]** A patient presents with optic neuritis and intermittent limb weakness. Which diagnosis is most likely? **d. Multiple sclerosis**

**[Practice — Nervous System]** In ALS, which neurons are lost? **b. Upper and lower motor neurons**

**[Practice — Nervous System]** Which best explains why hyperkalemia can lead to muscle weakness?
- **a. It increases the resting membrane potential, inactivating voltage-gated sodium channels**

**[Practice — Nervous System]** What is an early symptom of Alzheimer's disease? *(Memory loss / forgetting recent events)*

**[Practice — Nervous System]** True/False: Overproduction of cholinergic receptors is linked to Alzheimer's disease. *(False)*

**[Practice — Nervous System — Disorder Matching]**

| Disorder | Key Features |
|---|---|
| Epilepsy | Sudden bursts of electrochemical activity; seizures |
| Multiple Sclerosis | Autoimmune demyelination; worsens with heat |
| Alzheimer's Disease | APOE gene risk; cholinergic neuron loss |
| ALS | Upper and lower motor neuron loss; progressive paralysis |
| Tay-Sachs | Enzyme deficiency; cannot break down lipids in nerve cells |
| Parkinson's Disease | Dopamine loss in substantia nigra; tremors, rigidity |
| Cerebral Palsy | Brain damage before/during/shortly after birth |
| Myasthenia Gravis | ACh receptor blockade by antibodies; muscle weakness |
| Shingles (Herpes Zoster) | Painful stripe of blisters on one side |
| Stroke | Blood flow to brain interrupted |

**[Practice — Nervous System — FRQ]** FRQ (case): A 52-year-old woman has progressive difficulty walking, blurred vision, muscle weakness worsened by heat, nystagmus, demyelinating MRI lesions, and oligoclonal bands in CSF. Explain the diagnosis and pathophysiology. Address: role of myelin, how demyelination affects action potentials, and why heat worsens symptoms.

**[Practice — Nervous System — FRQ]** FRQ (case): Phillip, 67, misplaces items, repeats words, struggles to finish sentences, loses interest in crossword puzzles. a) Disease? b) Brain structures, cell type, and neurotransmitter primarily involved? c) Stage? d) Treatments and mechanism?

**[Practice — Nervous System — FRQ]** FRQ (case): Neural tube defects — fetus with craniorachischisis. Predict three specific brain regions and one spinal cord structure that would fail to develop properly and explain why.

**[Practice — Nervous System — FRQ]** FRQ: Compare anencephaly with meningoencephalocele. What key difference in tissue organization explains why one condition may retain partial brain structures?

**[Practice — Nervous System — FRQ]** FRQ (case): A 17-year-old with focal epilepsy. fMRI shows Wernicke's area overlapping the tumor, with developing activation in the contralateral hemisphere. Why was Wernicke's area targeted for inhibition, and how did this cause language activation on the contralateral hemisphere?

- B. Peptide hormones require intracellular transport proteins; steroid hormones are freely soluble
- **C. Peptide hormones typically activate second messenger systems; steroid hormones directly influence gene expression**
- D. Peptide hormones alter transcription directly; steroid hormones act only through enzymatic phosphorylation

- A. Catecholamines are lipid-soluble / **B. Thyroid hormones require plasma transport proteins** / C. Catecholamines bind nuclear receptors / D. Thyroid hormones act within the cytoplasm

**[Practice — Endocrine]** Why do peptide hormones act quickly but briefly? **A. They're stored in vesicles and cleared fast**

**[Practice — Endocrine]** Which limits the duration of peptide hormone signaling most?
- A. Receptor regulation of lysosomal digestion / **B. Conversion of cAMP to AMP by phosphodiesterase** / C. Desensitization of GPCRs / D. Competitive inhibition at receptor site

**[Practice — Endocrine]** Which hormone is both an amine and lipid-soluble, enabling nuclear receptor action? **B. Thyroxine (T4)**

**[Practice — Endocrine]** Which mechanism best describes the action of insulin?
- A. Binds to nuclear receptors / B. Inhibits secondary messengers / **C. Activates membrane receptors leading to glucose uptake** / D. Crosses membrane to alter transcription

**[Practice — Endocrine]** Which hormone has a mechanism that acts on nuclear receptors?
- A. Insulin / B. PTH / **C. Cortisol** / D. TSH

**[Practice — Endocrine]** Which hormone cannot be taken orally due to being denatured by digestive enzymes? **A. Insulin**

**[Practice — Endocrine]** Which hormone is most likely able to easily pass through a semi-permeable cell membrane? **D. Estrogen**

**[Practice — Endocrine]** Which best explains how steroid hormones induce gene transcription?
- A. Activation of membrane receptors / B. Secondary messenger cascades / **C. Direct DNA binding in the nucleus** / D. mRNA translation control

**[Practice — Endocrine]** What enzyme, inhibited by Viagra, breaks down cAMP and cGMP? **B. Phosphodiesterase**

**[Practice — Endocrine]** The eicosanoid family of paracrine regulators have backbones derived from: **B. Arachidonic acid**

**[Practice — Endocrine]** Uptake of iodine into thyroid cells is powered by:
- A. Primary active transport via ATP hydrolysis by apical NIS symporter
- **B. Secondary active transport via the Na⁺ gradient by the basolateral NIS symporter**
- C. Facilitated diffusion through iodide channels
- D. Secondary active transport via proton-coupled transport at the apical NIS symporter

**[Practice — Endocrine — FRQ]** Why can steroids diffuse through the plasma membrane, unlike amines/proteins? Describe in terms of structure and membrane permeability.

**[Practice — Endocrine — FRQ]** Describe how amine/protein hormones affect their target cell.

**[Practice — Endocrine — FRQ]** Describe how steroid hormones affect their target cell.

**[Practice — Endocrine — FRQ]** What does the cyclic AMP signaling mechanism involve? Trace the steps from hormone binding to cell response.

**[Practice — Endocrine — FRQ]** What is the pathway involving Phospholipase C (PLC) and Protein Kinase C (PKC), and what are their respective roles?

**[Practice — Endocrine — FRQ]** Describe the mechanism of action for steroid hormones from blood transport to target gene expression.

**[Practice — Endocrine — FRQ]** Suppose one molecule of epinephrine contacts a liver cell. Estimate how many molecules of glucose would be released and explain the GPCR signaling cascade. *(Answer in scientific notation)*

**[Practice — Endocrine — FRQ]** Explain what Pendrin is and, if blocked long-term, what the body would do to maintain homeostasis.
