## PUPILLARY REFLEX & OCULOMOTOR

**[UCR Div B]** What are the afferent and efferent nerve fibers for the pupillary reflex?
- Afferent: _______________ / Efferent: _______________

**[Archimedean]** True/False: The optomotor nerve is responsible for pupil constriction.

**[Archimedean]** Which statement correctly describes autonomic control of the ciliary muscle?
- A) Parasympathetic activation relaxes the ciliary muscle, flattening the lens for near vision
- B) Sympathetic activation contracts the ciliary muscle to increase lens curvature
- C) Parasympathetic activation contracts the ciliary muscle, allowing the lens to thicken for near vision
- D) Sympathetic activation has no effect on the ciliary muscle

**[Archimedean]** Which structure directly sends signals to the Edinger-Westphal nucleus during the pupillary light reflex?
- A. LGN / B. Optic tract / C. Oculomotor nucleus / D. Pretectal nucleus

**[Archimedean]** Which pairing correctly matches the autonomic pathway with its effect on the pupil?
- A. Parasympathetic activation causes constriction by stimulating the sphincter pupillae
- B. Parasympathetic activation causes dilation by stimulating the radial muscles
- C. Sympathetic activation causes constriction by stimulating the sphincter pupillae
- D. Sympathetic activation has no effect on pupil size

**[MVSO — FRQ]** Contrast sympathetic and parasympathetic control of the pupil and accommodation. Include neurotransmitters and receptors. Predict pharmacologic pupil responses to phenylephrine vs. pilocarpine in Adie pupil vs. CN III palsy.

**[HG]** Accommodation — which structure adjusts the shape of the lens for near vs. far vision?
- A) iris / B) ciliary muscles / C) cornea / D) sclera

**[HG]** Pupil dilation is primarily controlled by which system?
- A) parasympathetic / B) sympathetic / C) somatic / D) enteric

**[Seven Lakes]** Which statement correctly identifies the innervation of the eye related to the Edinger-Westphal nucleus?
- A) Ciliary ganglion lies caudal to EW and stimulated for accommodation
- B) Fibers from EW to ciliary ganglion control ciliary muscle and sphincter of iris
- C) The ciliary ganglion has no true synapses
- D) CN II is responsible for efferent stimulation of ciliary muscles more than EW/CN III

**[TAMU]** Jack has a negative direct and positive indirect pupillary response when light is shone into his left eye. Damage is to:
- a. Right Optic Tract / b. Left Optic Tract / c. Right Oculomotor Nerve / d. Left Oculomotor Nerve / e. Optic Chiasm

**[USC]** Light shone into right eye — right pupil doesn't constrict but left does. Which CN is impaired?
- a) II / b) III / c) V / d) VI / e) XI

**[UCR Div C]** What are the afferent and efferent nerve fibers for the pupillary reflex?

**[Practice — Sense Organs]** Which structure controls the amount of light entering the eye?
- a. Lens / b. Cornea / **c. Iris** / d. Ciliary bodies

**[Practice — Sense Organs — FRQ]** What is the purpose of pupil constriction?

- D) Bilateral abduction deficits

- A) optic / B) oculomotor / C) trochlear / D) abducens

## OLFACTORY SYSTEM

**[Troy]** What does it mean for an odorant to be volatile, and why does this matter for smell?

- a. Contains discarded cell bodies of dead olfactory receptors
- b. Traps and dissolves odorants to allow receptor binding
- c. Contains odorant-binding proteins (OBPs)
- d. Travels to the lacrimal gland to produce tears
- e. Lubricates the nasal cavity

**[HPDI]** The olfactory epithelium is located in which structure?
- A. Oral cavity / B. Pharynx / C. Superior portion of the nasal cavity / D. Larynx

**[HPDI]** An individual with anosmia would have difficulty with:
- A. Distinguishing sweet and sour / B. Detecting the scent of a flower / C. Hearing whispered speech / D. Maintaining balance

**[HPDI]** A patient tastes sweet, sour, salty, and bitter but reports food "has no flavor." Most likely explanation?
- A. Damage to gustatory cortex / B. Damage to CN VII / C. Loss of olfactory function (anosmia) / D. Destruction of taste receptor cells

**[HPDI]** Which second messenger system is primarily involved in olfactory signal transduction?
- A. IP3 pathway / B. cAMP pathway involving Golf protein / C. cGMP pathway / D. JAK-STAT pathway

- A. Temporal Lobe / B. Insula / C. Frontal Lobe / D. Occipital Lobe

- a. Pseudostratified columnar / b. Stratified cuboidal / c. Stratified squamous / d. Simple squamous

**[Archimedean — FRQ]** Briefly describe how odor molecules go from the air to being recognized by olfactory cells.

- A. Smell / B. Vision / C. Taste / D. Hearing

**[MVSO]** Which statement about the olfactory pathway is unique/correct?
- A) Projects first to thalamus / B) Regenerating receptor neurons from basal cells / C) Uses primarily glycine / D) Travels exclusively in CN V

**[Wolfpack]** Which kind of cellular receptors do olfactory receptors use to trigger an action potential?

**[Wolfpack]** What is the first structure that the olfactory nerves send their signal to?

- A) through the cerebellum first / B) directly to the cortex without thalamic relay / C) via the vagus nerve / D) through the optic nerve

- A) Olfactory fibers only go from receptor to brain
- B) Olfactory cells from brain terminate on inhibitory granule cells
- C) Feedback inhibition suppresses smell signals in olfactory bulbs
- D) Feedback inhibition happens mainly outside the olfactory bulb

- A) Saliva production / B) Gastric acid secretion / C) Instinctually avoiding foul odors / D) Conscious analysis of odor / E) Vomiting from a rotten odor

- a. Sniffing / b. Increasing airflow / c. Having a moist surface / d. Decreasing mucus production / e. More odor molecules binding

- a) Ability to taste bitter foods / b) Ability to detect odorants / c) Reflexive pupil constriction / d) Detection of rotational acceleration

- a) Taste / b) Smell / c) Sight / d) Hearing

**[USC]** What is the correct order of the pathway in which you perceive a scent?
- a) Nasal cavity → olfactory nerve → olfactory tract → olfactory bulb → ethmoid bone → olfactory cortex
- b) Cribriform plate → nasal cavity → olfactory bulb → olfactory tract → olfactory cortex
- c) Nasal cavity → olfactory nerve → olfactory bulb → olfactory tract → cribriform plate → thalamus → olfactory cortex
- d) Nasal cavity → olfactory bulb → cribriform plate → olfactory tract → thalamus → olfactory cortex
- e) Nasal cavity → olfactory nerve → cribriform plate → olfactory bulb → olfactory tract → olfactory cortex

**[UCR Div C]** Which special sense doesn't relay through the thalamus?
- a. Vision / b. Olfaction / c. Auditory / d. Gustatory / e. Vestibular

**[WashU]** The olfactory nerve is the only cranial nerve that can regenerate. Which best describes why?
- 1. High amounts of Dysferlin and MG53
- 2. Olfactory epithelium contains horizontal and globose basal cells that differentiate into olfactory receptor neurons
- 3. Neurogenesis at damaged nerve ends
- 4. Wallerian degeneration and regeneration
- 5. Actually the optic nerve regenerates

**[WashU]** Which type of receptor is most prominent in the nose?
- 1. Mechanoreceptors / 2. Thermoreceptors / 3. Chemoreceptors / 4. Photoreceptors / 5. Odoroceptors

**[Practice — Sense Organs]** Which part of the olfactory system contains the sensory neurons that detect odor molecules? **c. Olfactory epithelium**

**[Practice — Sense Organs]** The first relay station for olfactory information is the: **b. Olfactory bulb**

**[Practice — Sense Organs]** Olfactory receptor neurons are unusual because they: **c. Are replaced every ~30–60 days**

**[Practice — Sense Organs]** The vomeronasal organ's main function is to: **e. Perceive pheromones related to social behaviors**

## GUSTATORY SYSTEM

**[UMSO]** Which cranial nerve carries taste from the anterior two-thirds of the tongue?
- a. CN IX / b. CN VII / c. CN X / d. CN V3

**[UMSO]** Which cranial nerve carries general sensation from the posterior one-third of the tongue?
- a. CN IX / b. CN V3 / c. CN VI / d. CN VII

**[Archimedean]** Which are tastes of the gustatory system? Select all that apply.
- A) Sweet / B) Sour / C) Spicy / D) Salty / E) Bitter / F) Umami

**[Archimedean]** Which taste papillae does NOT carry sensory information?
- A) Fungiform / B) Foliate / C) Circumvallate / D) Filiform

**[Archimedean — FRQ]** What is the purpose of the papillae that does not carry sensory information?

**[Archimedean]** Which cranial nerve carries taste from the anterior two thirds of the tongue?
- A) CN I / B) CN II / C) CN V / D) CN VII

**[HG]** Which papillae are primarily responsible for detecting bitter taste?
- A) fungiform / B) filiform / C) vallate / D) foliate

**[HG]** What cranial nerves are involved with the gustatory system?
- A) VII, IX, X / B) IV, V, VII / C) IX, X, XII / D) III, IV, VII

**[Seven Lakes]** The taste pathway terminates at which cortical area after VPM thalamus and lower post-central gyrus?
- A) Calcarine sulcus / B) Heschl's gyrus / C) Piriform cortex / D) Posterior insula / E) Opercular insular area

**[Seven Lakes]** From the tractus solitarius and brainstem, where are taste signals projected for the salivary reflex?
- A) Raphe nuclei / B) Substantia nigra and VTA / C) Superior and inferior salivatory nuclei / D) Nucleus ambiguus / E) Solitary nucleus

**[TAMU]** Which taste sensation is triggered by hydrogen ions?
- a. Salty / b. Bitter / c. Sour / d. Umami / e. Sweet

**[TAMU]** Which taste sensation is triggered by glutamate?
- a. Sweet / b. Salty / c. Bitter / d. Umami / e. Sour

**[TAMU]** A student struggles to distinguish between sweet and salty foods. Which structure is most likely malfunctioning?
- a. Olfactory bulb / b. Taste receptor cells / c. Parietal lobe / d. Semicircular canals / e. Tympanic membrane

**[TAMU]** Taste receptor cells are located primarily within structures called __________ __________.

**[USC]** Which carry taste sensations in some capacity? Select all.
- a) Facial Nerve / b) Trigeminal Nerve / c) Glossopharyngeal Nerve / d) Vagus Nerve

**[USC]** Damage to the chorda tympani could lead to:
- a) Anosmia / b) Dysgeusia / c) Hyperopia / d) Vertigo

**[Practice — Nervous System]** Which cranial nerve carries taste sensations from the posterior one-third of the tongue?
- a. Facial nerve / b. Trigeminal nerve / **c. Glossopharyngeal nerve** / d. Vagus nerve

**[Practice — Sense Organs]** How many specialized receptor cells are in each taste bud?
- a. 1000 / b. 100 / **c. 20** / d. 5

**[Practice — Sense Organs]** Gustatory sense refers to what? *(The sense of taste)*

**[Practice — Sense Organs]** Which gustatory papillae lack taste buds and are primarily implicated in texture sensation? **a. Filiform**

**[Practice — Sense Organs]** Which papillae contain the highest density of taste buds? **c. Vallate (circumvallate)**

**[Practice — Sense Organs]** Taste and smell are what type of receptors? *(Chemoreceptors)*

**[Practice — Sense Organs]** Which of the following is NOT a basic taste? **b. Spicy**

**[Practice — Sense Organs]** Which taste sensation is mediated by ion channels rather than GPCRs? **d. Salty** *(and sour)*

- B) Toward tallest cilium → K⁺ influx from endolymph

- D) Toward shortest cilium → Ca²⁺ influx from endolymph

- E) Long-term MOC-dependent plasticity occurs after chronic afferent manipulation

**[Practice — Cranial Nerve Reference]**

| Test / Function | Cranial Nerve |
|---|---|
| Tongue symmetry | CN XII (Hypoglossal) |
| Strength/evenness of shrug | CN XI (Accessory) |
| Biting pencil, skin sensation, corneal reflex | CN V (Trigeminal) |
| Smelling aromatic oils | CN I (Olfactory) |
| Rinne/Weber tuning fork, balance | CN VIII (Vestibulocochlear) |
| "Ah!", uvula position, gag reflex | CN IX/X (Glossopharyngeal/Vagus) |
| Taste posterior 1/3 tongue | CN IX (Glossopharyngeal) |
| Motor to facial muscles | CN VII (Facial) |
| Eye abduction | CN VI (Abducens) |
| Eye movement (most), pupillary constriction | CN III (Oculomotor) |

How many pairs of cranial nerves? **12 pairs**
How many pairs of spinal nerves? **31 pairs**

## NEUROPHYSIOLOGY — ACTION POTENTIALS & MEMBRANE POTENTIALS

**[Practice — Nervous System]** What primarily causes the neuron's resting membrane potential to be negative?
- a. The Na⁺/K⁺ pump pumps out 3 Na⁺ for every 2 K⁺
- **b. Open K⁺ leak channels allow more K⁺ to leave than Na⁺ can enter**
- c. Intracellular proteins carry negative charge
- d. Voltage-gated Na⁺ channels are closed at rest

**[Practice — Nervous System]** What is the approximate resting potential of a neuron?
- a. −90 mV / b. −82 mV / **c. −70 mV** / d. +20 mV

**[Practice — Nervous System]** At resting membrane potential, potassium wants to move ___ the cell, and sodium wants to move ___ the cell? *(K⁺: out; Na⁺: in)*

**[Practice — Nervous System]** Initial depolarization of an action potential is caused by:
- a. K⁺ entering / b. K⁺ exiting / **c. Na⁺ entering** / d. Na⁺ exiting

**[Practice — Nervous System]** Which is a factor in generating the resting membrane potential?
- a. Lower concentration of K⁺ immediately inside the cell membrane
- **b. Higher concentration of Na⁺ immediately outside the cell membrane**
- c. Greater permeability to Na⁺ than K⁺
- d. All of the above

**[Practice — Nervous System]** Which correctly describes the function of the sodium-potassium pump?
- a. Allows passive movement of ions / b. Hyperpolarizes by allowing Cl⁻ in
- **c. Actively exports 3 Na⁺ and imports 2 K⁺ using ATP**
- d. Actively transports 3 K⁺ in and 2 Na⁺ out

**[Practice — Nervous System]** Which ion's relative permeability influences membrane potential the most (GHK equation)?
- a. Na⁺ / b. Cl⁻ / **c. K⁺** / d. Ca²⁺

**[Practice — Nervous System]** For an increase in K⁺ movement out of the neuron, this causes:
- a. Depolarization / **b. Hyperpolarization** / c. K⁺ replaced by Na⁺ / d. K⁺ replaced by Ca²⁺

**[Practice — Nervous System]** Which statement about the refractory period is TRUE?
- a. During absolute refractory period, a strong stimulus can trigger another AP
- b. Relative refractory period occurs when Na⁺ channels are open and inactivated
- **c. During the relative refractory period, K⁺ channels are open**
- d. The refractory period affects the entire neuron equally

**[Practice — Nervous System]** A neuron's threshold is −55 mV and it is in its absolute refractory period. How strong a stimulus is needed for a second AP?
- **d. None — no stimulus can trigger an AP during absolute refractory period**

**[Practice — Nervous System]** What is the "threshold" potential of a membrane?
- **d. The minimum depolarization needed to operate voltage-gated sodium and potassium channels**

**[Practice — Nervous System]** True/False: Action potentials happen in an all-or-nothing fashion. *(True)*

**[Practice — Nervous System]** True/False: In a neuron that depolarizes, Na⁺ flows into the cell. *(True)*

**[Practice — Nervous System]** The Goldman-Hodgkin-Katz equation is used to calculate what? *(Resting membrane potential)*

**[Practice — Nervous System]** What ion primarily drives the depolarization phase? *(Na⁺)*

**[Practice — Nervous System — FRQ]** Describe what happens to a neuron's membrane during each phase of an action potential (depolarization, repolarization, hyperpolarization).

**[Practice — Nervous System — FRQ]** Describe the all-or-nothing law.

**[Practice — Nervous System — FRQ]** Which class of neurotransmitters is acetylcholine most similar to structurally? What differentiates it? Why are its distinctive properties crucial to its usability in the brain?

**[Practice — Nervous System — FRQ]** If inhibitory neurotransmitters were dysfunctional, what would happen to neurons? Give an example using a specific neurotransmitter.

## SALTATORY CONDUCTION & MYELINATION

**[Practice — Nervous System]** In saltatory conduction, action potentials propagate along what part of myelinated axons?
- a. Along the entire axon / **b. Nodes of Ranvier** / c. Only through the myelin sheath / d. Dendrites only

**[Practice — Nervous System]** What process describes an action potential jumping from one node of Ranvier to the next? *(Saltatory conduction)*

**[Practice — Nervous System]** Which part of the neuron integrates incoming signals and initiates an action potential?
- a. Axon terminal / **b. Axon hillock** / c. Dendrite / d. Myelin sheath

**[Practice — Nervous System]** Action potentials move along axons:
- a. More slowly in large-diameter axons
- b. By direct action of acetylcholine on the axonal membrane
- c. By activating the sodium-potassium pump at each point
- **d. More rapidly in myelinated than in non-myelinated axons**

**[Practice — Nervous System]** Saltatory conduction refers to:
- a. Conduction across electrical synapses / b. AP skipping the axon hillock / c. Rapid reverberating movement / d. Jumping from one neuron to another / **e. Jumping from one node of Ranvier to the next**

**[Practice — Nervous System]** The progression of a nerve impulse along nodes of Ranvier is called:
- **a. Saltatory Conduction** / b. Relative Conduction / c. Action Potential / d. Resting Potential

**[Practice — Nervous System]** What are the gaps between myelinated axons called? *(Nodes of Ranvier)*

**[Practice — Nervous System]** True/False: The midbrain is the largest region of the brainstem. *(False — the pons is the largest)*

**[Practice — Nervous System]** True/False: Slightly decreasing axon diameter increases conduction velocity by reducing internal resistance. *(False — larger diameter increases velocity)*

**[Practice — Nervous System]** Which is characteristic of myelination exclusively in the CNS?
- **d. Newer layers of myelin are formed under preexisting layers**

**[Practice — Nervous System]** Which best describes Wallerian degeneration?
- **b. Degeneration of the axon distal to the site of injury, while the proximal segment remains connected to the neurosoma**

**[Practice — Nervous System — FRQ]** Explain the function of myelination, which cells perform it, and give an example of a related disorder.

**[Practice — Nervous System — FRQ]** Describe the regeneration of a damaged nerve.

## SYNAPTIC TRANSMISSION & NEUROTRANSMITTERS

**[Practice — Nervous System]** Neurotransmitters are released from axon terminals via:
- a. Osmosis / b. Active transport / c. Diffusion / d. Transcytosis / **e. Exocytosis**

**[Practice — Nervous System]** An IPSP occurs when the membrane becomes more permeable to:
- **a. Potassium ions** / b. Sodium ions / c. Calcium ions

**[Practice — Nervous System]** What property distinguishes temporal summation from spatial summation?
- **a. It involves inputs at the same synapse over time**

**[Practice — Nervous System]** When several EPSPs arrive at the axon hillock from different dendritic locations, this is an example of:
- **b. Spatial summation**

**[Practice — Nervous System]** What best distinguishes monosynaptic from polysynaptic reflexes?
- **a. Polysynaptic reflexes require interneurons while monosynaptic reflexes do not**

**[Practice — Nervous System]** Which structure detects muscle stretch to initiate the stretch reflex?
- **c. Muscle spindle**

**[Practice — Nervous System]** Where is acetylcholine synthesized and enclosed?
- **c. Presynaptic terminal and synaptic vesicles**

**[Practice — Nervous System]** True/False: Voltage-gated calcium channels at the axon terminal trigger neurotransmitter release. *(True)*

**[Practice — Nervous System]** What neurotransmitter is released at the neuromuscular junction? *(Acetylcholine)*

**[Practice — Nervous System]** What neurotransmitter do muscarinic receptors bind to? *(Acetylcholine)*

**[Practice — Nervous System]** Which of the following is NOT a catecholamine? **a. Serotonin**

**[Practice — Nervous System]** All catecholamines are synthesized from: **b. Tyrosine**

**[Practice — Nervous System]** Which neuromodulator is created via arginine metabolism in endothelial cells? **d. Nitric oxide**

**[Practice — Nervous System]** What is the role of an enkephalin? **b. To act as a natural painkiller by inhibiting pain signals**

**[Practice — Nervous System]** Which receptor decreases heart rate when activated? **b. M2**

**[Practice — Nervous System]** True/False: All biogenic amines are derived from modifications to threonine. *(False — from aromatic amino acids)*

**[Practice — Nervous System]** True/False: The effects of serotonin tend to have a slower onset than other neurotransmitters. *(True)*

**[Practice — Nervous System]** True/False: Stimulation of 5-HT2A receptors can cause hallucinogenic effects. *(True)*

**[Practice — Nervous System — Neurotransmitter Categorization]**

| Neurotransmitter | Category |
|---|---|
| Epinephrine | Catecholamine |
| Norepinephrine | Catecholamine |
| Dopamine | Catecholamine |
| Serotonin | Indolamine |
| Histamine | Imidazolamine |

**[Practice — Nervous System]** What is the neurotransmitter used in the sympathetic nervous system? **a. Epinephrine**

**[Practice — Nervous System]** What is the brain's main excitatory neurotransmitter? **b. Glutamate**

**[Practice — Nervous System]** What do muscarinic receptors bind to? **c. Acetylcholine**

**[Practice — Nervous System — FRQ]** Describe the 5 neurotransmitter categories and give one example of each.

## PHARMACOLOGY OF THE NERVOUS SYSTEM

**[Practice — Nervous System]** How does caffeine enhance alertness? **d. Blocking adenosine receptors**

**[Practice — Nervous System]** How does caffeine affect coffee drinkers?
- a. Prevents reuptake of dopamine / b. Causes dopamine to bind to receptors / **c. Binds adenosine receptors causing epinephrine release** / d. Acts on hypothalamus to increase heart rate

**[Practice — Nervous System]** The primary effect of cocaine is that it blocks re-uptake of: **a. Monoamines**

**[Practice — Nervous System]** Botulinum toxin impairs neurotransmission by: **c. Preventing vesicular release of ACh**

**[Practice — Nervous System]** In a short sentence, how is botulinum toxin used in cosmetic medicine? *(Injected in small amounts to temporarily paralyze facial muscles, reducing wrinkles)*

**[Practice — Nervous System]** Which medication is commonly used to treat spasticity in multiple sclerosis? **b. Baclofen**

**[Practice — Nervous System]** True/False: Botulism destroys SNARES that activate skeletal muscle in excitatory neurons. *(True)*

**[Practice — Nervous System]** True/False: Monoamine oxidase inhibitors act by inhibiting the degradation of glutamate. *(False — they inhibit degradation of monoamines)*

**[Practice — Nervous System — FRQ]** Explain the effects of the following drugs on the nervous system: a) Alcohol, b) Caffeine, c) Nicotine.

**[Practice — Nervous System — FRQ]** Describe the paralytic effects of nerve gas. Mention the enzyme affected and what is inhibited.

**[Practice — Nervous System — FRQ]** When *Clostridium tetani* produces tetanospasmin, what disorder results and how does it cause muscle stiffness? *(Tetanus; disinhibition of motor neurons by a GABA blockade)*

**[Practice — Nervous System — FRQ]** Outline the behavioral effects of cannabis and alcohol.

## GLIAL CELLS

**[Practice — Nervous System]** Which type of cells line the ventricles and spinal cord?
- a. Astrocytes / b. Schwann Cells / **c. Ependymal Cells** / d. Oligodendrocytes

**[Practice — Nervous System]** Which type of glial cell is most abundant in the CNS? **a. Astrocytes**

**[Practice — Nervous System]** Which glial cell type is primarily responsible for myelinating axons in the CNS?
- a. Schwann cells / b. Astrocytes / **c. Oligodendrocytes** / d. Ependymal cells

**[Practice — Nervous System]** Which glial cell has mesodermal rather than ectodermal origin?
- a. Oligodendrocytes / b. Ependymal cells / **c. Microglia** / d. Astrocytes

**[Practice — Nervous System]** Which glial cell type lines the ventricles and helps produce CSF? **d. Ependymal cells**

**[Practice — Nervous System]** Which best describes the astrocyte-neuron lactate shuttle hypothesis?
- **b. Astrocytes convert glucose to lactate then shuttle it to neurons**

**[Practice — Nervous System]** True/False: Neurons outnumber neuroglia 3 to 1. *(False — neuroglia outnumber neurons)*

## NEUROLOGICAL DISORDERS

**[Practice — Nervous System]** In which disease do sclerotic sheaths result in poor conduction of action potentials? **a. Multiple Sclerosis**

**[Practice — Nervous System]** What disease is caused by the loss of dopamine? **a. Parkinson's Disease**

**[Practice — Nervous System]** Which neurotransmitter system is primarily affected in Parkinson's disease? **b. Dopamine**

**[Practice — Nervous System]** A patient presents with optic neuritis and intermittent limb weakness. Which diagnosis is most likely? **d. Multiple sclerosis**

**[Practice — Nervous System]** In ALS, which neurons are lost? **b. Upper and lower motor neurons**

**[Practice — Nervous System]** Which best explains why hyperkalemia can lead to muscle weakness?
- **a. It increases the resting membrane potential, inactivating voltage-gated sodium channels**

**[Practice — Nervous System]** What is an early symptom of Alzheimer's disease? *(Memory loss / forgetting recent events)*

**[Practice — Nervous System]** True/False: Overproduction of cholinergic receptors is linked to Alzheimer's disease. *(False)*

**[Practice — Nervous System — Disorder Matching]**

| Disorder | Key Features |
|---|---|
| Epilepsy | Sudden bursts of electrochemical activity; seizures |
| Multiple Sclerosis | Autoimmune demyelination; worsens with heat |
| Alzheimer's Disease | APOE gene risk; cholinergic neuron loss |
| ALS | Upper and lower motor neuron loss; progressive paralysis |
| Tay-Sachs | Enzyme deficiency; cannot break down lipids in nerve cells |
| Parkinson's Disease | Dopamine loss in substantia nigra; tremors, rigidity |
| Cerebral Palsy | Brain damage before/during/shortly after birth |
| Myasthenia Gravis | ACh receptor blockade by antibodies; muscle weakness |
| Shingles (Herpes Zoster) | Painful stripe of blisters on one side |
| Stroke | Blood flow to brain interrupted |

**[Practice — Nervous System — FRQ]** FRQ (case): A 52-year-old woman has progressive difficulty walking, blurred vision, muscle weakness worsened by heat, nystagmus, demyelinating MRI lesions, and oligoclonal bands in CSF. Explain the diagnosis and pathophysiology. Address: role of myelin, how demyelination affects action potentials, and why heat worsens symptoms.

**[Practice — Nervous System — FRQ]** FRQ (case): Phillip, 67, misplaces items, repeats words, struggles to finish sentences, loses interest in crossword puzzles. a) Disease? b) Brain structures, cell type, and neurotransmitter primarily involved? c) Stage? d) Treatments and mechanism?

**[Practice — Nervous System — FRQ]** FRQ (case): Neural tube defects — fetus with craniorachischisis. Predict three specific brain regions and one spinal cord structure that would fail to develop properly and explain why.

**[Practice — Nervous System — FRQ]** FRQ: Compare anencephaly with meningoencephalocele. What key difference in tissue organization explains why one condition may retain partial brain structures?

**[Practice — Nervous System — FRQ]** FRQ (case): A 17-year-old with focal epilepsy. fMRI shows Wernicke's area overlapping the tumor, with developing activation in the contralateral hemisphere. Why was Wernicke's area targeted for inhibition, and how did this cause language activation on the contralateral hemisphere?

- B. Peptide hormones require intracellular transport proteins; steroid hormones are freely soluble
- **C. Peptide hormones typically activate second messenger systems; steroid hormones directly influence gene expression**
- D. Peptide hormones alter transcription directly; steroid hormones act only through enzymatic phosphorylation

- A. Catecholamines are lipid-soluble / **B. Thyroid hormones require plasma transport proteins** / C. Catecholamines bind nuclear receptors / D. Thyroid hormones act within the cytoplasm

- A. Receptor regulation of lysosomal digestion / **B. Conversion of cAMP to AMP by phosphodiesterase** / C. Desensitization of GPCRs / D. Competitive inhibition at receptor site

- A. Activation of membrane receptors / B. Secondary messenger cascades / **C. Direct DNA binding in the nucleus** / D. mRNA translation control

**[Practice — Endocrine]** What enzyme, inhibited by Viagra, breaks down cAMP and cGMP? **B. Phosphodiesterase**

## ENDOCRINE SYSTEM — GLANDS, HORMONES & FEEDBACK

**[Practice — Endocrine]** What gland controls other glands? **B. Pituitary**

**[Practice — Endocrine]** What is another name for the posterior pituitary gland? **A. Neurohypophysis**

**[Practice — Endocrine]** What is another name for the anterior pituitary gland? **B. Adenohypophysis**

**[Practice — Endocrine]** What type of hormones are produced by the anterior pituitary and target other endocrine glands? **A. Tropic**

**[Practice — Endocrine]** Which portion of the pituitary gland is composed of nervous tissue? **C. Posterior pituitary**

**[Practice — Endocrine]** A researcher blocks all hypothalamic releasing hormones. Which hormone would still be secreted normally? **D. Oxytocin**

**[Practice — Endocrine]** Which is a unique feature of the supraoptic nucleus compared to other hypothalamic nuclei?
- A. Projects to the anterior pituitary via releasing factors
- **B. Contains osmoreceptors and sends long axons directly into the posterior pituitary**
- C. Regulates circadian rhythm by responding to retinal light signals
- D. Secretes dopamine to inhibit prolactin

- A. They synapse on anterior pituitary cells / B. Use axonal transport to reach median eminence capillaries / **C. They release hormones into systemic circulation** / D. They release both releasing and inhibiting factors

**[Practice — Endocrine]** Which cell type plays a bigger role in estrogen production in prepubertal males? **B. Sertoli cells**

**[Practice — Endocrine]** The release of most hormones is regulated by the: **B. Negative feedback loop**

**[Practice — Endocrine]** Which represents negative feedback? **D. X stimulates Y, then Y inhibits X**

**[Practice — Endocrine]** Which represents positive feedback? **E. X stimulates Y, then Y stimulates X**

**[Practice — Endocrine]** How does prolactin inhibit the reproductive axis? **A. Suppresses GnRH release**

**[Practice — Endocrine]** Which hormone is secreted in a pulsatile manner to prevent receptor desensitization? **C. GnRH**

**[Practice — Endocrine]** What cells secrete thyroid hormone (TH)? **A. Follicular cells**

**[Practice — Endocrine]** Which layer of the suprarenal gland secretes mineralocorticoids? **A. Zona glomerulosa**

**[Practice — Endocrine]** Which adrenal cortex zone produces glucocorticoids? **B. Zona fasciculata**

**[Practice — Endocrine]** What are the main hormones released from the adrenal medulla? *(Epinephrine ~80% and norepinephrine ~20%)*

**[Practice — Endocrine]** Which cells in the ovaries secrete estrogen? **A. Granulosa cells**

**[Practice — Endocrine]** Which enzyme is critical in the conversion of testosterone to estrogen in adipose tissue? **A. Aromatase**

**[Practice — Endocrine]** Which enzyme catalyzes the rate-limiting step of steroid hormone synthesis from cholesterol? **C. Cholesterol desmolase**

**[Practice — Endocrine]** Which hormone primarily regulates serum potassium levels? **B. Aldosterone**

**[Practice — Endocrine]** Which hormone increases renal calcium reabsorption and decreases phosphate reabsorption? **C. PTH**

**[Practice — Endocrine]** Which correctly traces how PTH increases intestinal calcium uptake?
- A. PTH → thyroid → calcitonin / B. PTH → liver → vitamin D3
- **C. PTH → kidney → activation of vitamin D → calcitriol → increased Ca absorption**
- D. PTH → adrenal cortex → aldosterone → sodium retention

**[Practice — Endocrine]** Which best describes how PTH stimulates calcium release from bone at the cellular level?
- A. PTH binds to osteoclast receptors, directly stimulating bone resorption
- B. PTH promotes osteoblast activity, which inhibits osteoclasts
- **C. PTH stimulates osteoblasts to express RANKL, which activates osteoclast precursors**
- D. PTH activates vitamin D in bone tissue

**[Practice — Endocrine]** Calcitonin is produced in what part of the thyroid gland? **B. Parafollicular cells**

**[Practice — Endocrine]** Which hormone decreases the rate of bone resorption? **A. Calcitonin**

**[Practice — Endocrine]** Which is promoted by the direct secretory product of alpha cells of the pancreas? **A. Gluconeogenesis / Cori cycle** *(glucagon)*

**[Practice — Endocrine]** Insulin is secreted by which specific cell? **A. Beta cells of the Islet of Langerhans**

**[Practice — Endocrine]** Which hormones are secreted from the beta cells of the pancreas? **A. Insulin and Amylin**

**[Practice — Endocrine]** Which hormone is known as the "satiety hormone"? **D. Leptin**

**[Practice — Endocrine]** Which hormone causes blood sugar to rise? **B. Glucagon**

**[Practice — Endocrine]** What hormone is measured during a dexamethasone suppression test? **A. Cortisol** *(screening for Cushing's syndrome)*

**[Practice — Endocrine]** Which hormone is primarily involved in regulating the body's response to stress? **B. Cortisol**

**[Practice — Endocrine]** In evolution, what is the primary survival benefit of secreting cortisol? **C. Mobilizes energy during a threat**

**[Practice — Endocrine]** Which hormone is photosensitive? *(Melatonin)*

**[Practice — Endocrine]** The thymus secretes hormones that help mature which type of immune cell? *(T cells)*

**[Practice — Endocrine]** Which hormone increases Na reabsorption in the kidneys, affecting blood pressure? *(Aldosterone)*

**[Practice — Endocrine]** What is the primary function of prolactin? *(Milk production/lactation)*

**[Practice — Endocrine]** Which gland is connected to both the nervous and endocrine systems? *(Hypothalamus)*

**[Practice — Endocrine]** What is it called when a hormone acts on another endocrine gland, stimulating it to release its own hormones? *(Tropic effect)*

**[Practice — Endocrine]** Which endocrine organ stores hormone-containing colloid extracellularly before secretion? *(Thyroid gland)*

**[Practice — Endocrine]** Which hormone is responsible for upregulating aquaporin-2 in the collecting ducts? *(ADH/Vasopressin)*

**[Practice — Endocrine]** What is the primary site of peripheral T4-to-T3 conversion? *(Liver)*

**[Practice — Endocrine]** Which hormone is secreted in pulses primarily during sleep? *(Growth hormone)*

**[Practice — Endocrine]** What hormone is secreted by Leydig cells? *(Testosterone)*

**[Practice — Endocrine]** High levels of what hormone directly induces ovulation? *(LH)*

**[Practice — Endocrine]** Which hormone has both endocrine and neurohormonal functions and is involved in circadian rhythm regulation? **B. Melatonin**

**[Practice — Endocrine]** Which hormones will be secreted in response to vasodilatory shock? **E. All of the above** *(Epinephrine, Cortisol, ADH/Vasopressin, Aldosterone)*

**[Practice — Endocrine]** Normal fasting glucose in mg/dL is closest to: **B. 60–100**

**[Practice — Endocrine]** Which endocrine disruptor binds to both ERα and ERβ? **C. BPA**

**[Practice — Endocrine — True/False]**
- Aldosterone is a glucocorticoid. *(False — mineralocorticoid)*
- In the Zona Fasciculata you'd find enzymes that synthesize aldosterone. *(False — zona glomerulosa)*
- Phenylalanine N-Methyltransferase catalyzes conversion of Vitamin D to calcitriol. *(False)*
- Hyperalgesia is linked to an increase in β-endorphins. *(False — β-endorphins decrease pain)*
- Administering epinephrine to adipocytes leads to significant fatty acid release. *(True)*
- A mutation rendering chondrocytes insensitive to peptide GHs would make an individual taller. *(False — shorter)*
- EPO release is normally triggered by high oxygen in the blood. *(False — low oxygen triggers EPO)*
- An increase in PTHrp and decrease in PTH is likely due to a cytotoxic tumor. *(True)*
- Treatment with 1,25-dihydroxyvitamin D is a common solution to hypocalcemia. *(True)*
- Steroids are water-soluble. *(False — lipid-soluble)*
- Amine hormones are synthesized from tryptophan or threonine. *(False — tryptophan or tyrosine)*
- Type 2 diabetes mellitus occurs when too little insulin is secreted by the pancreas. *(False — primarily insulin resistance)*

**[Practice — Endocrine — Fill in the Blank — HPT Axis]**
To maintain stable metabolism: (1) hypothalamus detects low TH → releases TRH → travels through (2) hypophyseal portal system → anterior pituitary releases TSH → stimulates (3) thyroid gland to produce (4) T3 (triiodothyronine) and (5) T4 (thyroxine) → sufficient T3+T4 inhibits TRH and TSH through (6) negative feedback.

**[Practice — Endocrine — Gland Matching]**

| Gland | Key Secretions / Notes |
|---|---|
| Anterior Pituitary | GH, TSH, ACTH, FSH, LH, Prolactin, MSH; tropic hormones targeting other endocrine glands |
| Posterior Pituitary | Oxytocin and ADH (synthesized by hypothalamus, stored/released here) |
| Thyroid | T3, T4 from follicular cells; calcitonin from parafollicular cells |
| Parathyroid | PTH; increases blood calcium |
| Adrenal Medulla | Epinephrine (~80%), Norepinephrine (~20%) |
| Adrenal Cortex — Zona Glomerulosa | Mineralocorticoids (aldosterone) |
| Adrenal Cortex — Zona Fasciculata | Glucocorticoids (cortisol) |
| Adrenal Cortex — Zona Reticularis | Androgens (DHEA) |
| Thymus | Thymosin; promotes T-cell maturation |
| Pineal Gland | Melatonin; circadian rhythm regulation |
| Pancreas — Beta cells | Insulin, amylin |
| Pancreas — Alpha cells | Glucagon |
| Ovaries — Granulosa cells | Estrogen |
| Testes — Leydig cells | Testosterone |
| Testes — Sertoli cells | Inhibin B; estrogen in prepubertal males |

**[Practice — Endocrine — FRQ]** Define hypersecretion and hyposecretion. Give one disorder resulting from each.

**[Practice — Endocrine — FRQ]** Explain the relation between oxytocin and prolactin.

**[Practice — Endocrine — FRQ]** What does MSH stand for, what does it do, and where is it released from?

**[Practice — Endocrine — FRQ]** Describe the difference between the posterior and anterior pituitary glands and their association with the hypothalamus. List and describe 2 hormones for each.

**[Practice — Endocrine — FRQ]** What does ADH stand for, what does it do, and where is it released from?

**[Practice — Endocrine — FRQ]** Describe the mechanism of cortisol release AND the general mechanism of cortisol activity.

**[Practice — Endocrine — FRQ]** What is the thyroid's main secretion and what effect does the hormone have?

**[Practice — Endocrine — FRQ]** List the 3 classes of corticosteroids and their respective functions.

**[Practice — Endocrine — FRQ]** Describe the three chemical classes of hormones.

**[Practice — Endocrine — FRQ]** What are the two products that proinsulin is converted to? *(Insulin and C-peptide)*

**[Practice — Endocrine — FRQ]** Describe what happens to a person's insulin levels when afflicted with Type 1 Diabetes.

**[Practice — Endocrine — FRQ]** What are 3 factors that can increase the likelihood of developing hypoglycemia?

**[Practice — Endocrine — FRQ]** Name the two products that proinsulin (from β-islet cells) is converted to.

**[Practice — Endocrine — FRQ]** Compare and contrast primary vs. secondary adrenal insufficiency (endocrine profiles and lab findings).

**[Practice — Endocrine — FRQ]** Explain the role of the hypothalamus in integrating nervous and endocrine responses (cite at least two hormone pathways).

**[Practice — Endocrine — FRQ]** Discuss the feedback regulation of the thyroid axis and predict lab results for primary hyperthyroidism.

**[Practice — Endocrine — FRQ]** Analyze the hormonal disruptions seen in polycystic ovarian syndrome and their clinical consequences.

**[Practice — Endocrine — FRQ]** Construct a detailed flowchart tracing the hormonal response to acute stress starting from the hypothalamus.

**[Practice — Endocrine — FRQ]** Explain why the gonads do not secrete aldosterone or cortisol, and which intermediates of the steroid hormone synthesis pathway gonads cannot convert.

**[Practice — Endocrine — FRQ]** If the hypothalamus fails to produce GnRH, what will happen to LH and FSH levels?

**[Practice — Endocrine — FRQ]** A patient has low ACTH and low cortisol. Which gland is malfunctioning? *(Pituitary)*

**[Practice — Endocrine — FRQ]** A forensic scientist finds a shrunken gland below the thyroid between the lungs. Is the victim young or old? What gland and why?

**[Practice — Endocrine — FRQ]** Hypersecretion by an endocrine gland is most often caused by what? If untreated in childhood, what conditions can result?

**[Practice — Endocrine — FRQ]** In addition to oxytocin, the pituitary gland produces 3 other hormones. Name them and their functions.

---

## ENDOCRINE SYSTEM — DISORDERS

**[Practice — Endocrine]** An individual presents with fatigue, weight gain, and bradycardia. Labs: elevated TSH, low T3. Which best explains this?
- **A. Primary hypothyroidism from thyroid gland failure** / B. Secondary hypothyroidism / C. Hypersecretion of TRH / D. Hyposensitivity to thyroid hormone

**[Practice — Endocrine]** What would be the expected hormonal profile in secondary hypothyroidism?
- A. High TSH, Low T3/T4 / **B. Low TSH, Low T3/T4** / C. High TSH, High T3/T4 / D. Normal TSH, High T3/T4

**[Practice — Endocrine]** Which disorder is caused by TSH receptor autoantibodies? **A. Graves' disease**

**[Practice — Endocrine]** Which is NOT an effect of Graves' Disease?
- A. Enlargement of extrinsic eye muscles / B. Louder heart sounds / **C. Decreased bone reabsorption** / D. Muscle atrophy and weakness

**[Practice — Endocrine]** Which symptom is most specific to myxedema?
- A. Hypertension and weight loss / **B. Puffy face with dry skin** / C. Sudden drop in blood calcium / D. Flushed skin with tremors

**[Practice — Endocrine]** Melissa: thickened skin on shins and bulging eyes. What does her doctor suspect? *(Graves' disease / hyperthyroidism)*

**[Practice — Endocrine]** Billy: intense swelling in neck, cough, hoarseness. What condition and treatments? *(Goiter; iodine supplementation, thyroid hormone therapy, or surgery)*

**[Practice — Endocrine]** Caitlyn: severe mental deficiency due to lack of iodine, causing motor spasticity, deaf mutism, and squint. What condition? *(Congenital Iodine Deficiency Syndrome / Cretinism)*

**[Practice — Endocrine]** Which lab results hint at Addison's disease?
- A. High cortisol, high ACTH / B. Low cortisol, low ACTH / **C. Low cortisol, high ACTH** / D. High aldosterone, low renin

**[Practice — Endocrine]** Which condition presents with high ACTH but low cortisol? **B. Addison's disease**

**[Practice — Endocrine]** In Cushing's disease, what is the most common underlying pathophysiology?
- A. Adrenal tumor / B. Ectopic ACTH secretion / **C. Pituitary adenoma overproducing ACTH** / D. Iatrogenic steroid use

**[Practice — Endocrine]** Pituitary dwarfism is caused by: **C. GH hyposecretion**

**[Practice — Endocrine]** A condition caused by hypersecretion of somatotrophin in adulthood, leading to enlargement of hands, feet, and facial features: **B. Acromegaly**

**[Practice — Endocrine]** What hormone causes gigantism? *(Growth hormone; anterior pituitary)*

**[Practice — Endocrine]** In primary hyperaldosteronism (Conn's syndrome), which laboratory finding is most characteristic?
- A. Hyperkalemia and metabolic acidosis / **B. Hypokalemia and metabolic alkalosis** / C. Hypernatremia and hyperkalemia / D. Hyponatremia and metabolic acidosis

**[Practice — Endocrine]** A tumor in the adrenal medulla causes excessive catecholamine release. What symptoms?
- A. Bradycardia, hypotension, fatigue / **B. Tachycardia, hypertension, anxiety** / C. Weight gain, heat intolerance / D. Hypoglycemia, sweating, coma

**[Practice — Endocrine]** Pheochromocytomas are a tumor of the: **D. Adrenal Medulla**

**[Practice — Endocrine]** Type 1 Diabetes Mellitus results from: **B. Immune system destruction of insulin-producing beta cells**

**[Practice — Endocrine]** Type 2 Diabetes accounts for about ___ of all diagnosed cases, while Type 1 accounts for ___: **C. 90%, 10%**

**[Practice — Endocrine]** A patient with low serum calcium and high PTH likely has: **B. Secondary hyperparathyroidism**

**[Practice — Endocrine]** Which is the primary symptom of Congenital Iodine Deficiency Syndrome? **C. Cognitive developmental delays**

**[Practice — Endocrine]** Tumors in which gland can cause changes in vision due to proximity to the optic nerve? **A. Pituitary gland**

**[Practice — Endocrine]** The hypothalamus-pituitary-adrenal axis is suppressed in: **B. Long-term corticosteroid therapy**

**[Practice — Endocrine]** In SIADH, what is the most likely combination of serum sodium and urine osmolality?
- A. Low serum sodium, low urine osmolality / B. High serum sodium, high urine osmolality / **C. Low serum sodium, high urine osmolality** / D. High serum sodium, low urine osmolality

**[Practice — Endocrine]** A patient presents with hypernatremia and low ADH. What condition is most likely?
- A. SIADH / B. Diabetes mellitus / **C. Diabetes insipidus** / D. Addison's disease

**[Practice — Endocrine]** Diabetes insipidus is due to a deficiency of: **C. ADH**

**[Practice — Endocrine]** Which patient is most likely to experience a temporary form of diabetes insipidus?
- A. Patient with tumor directly damaging hypothalamus / B. Patient whose kidneys don't respond to ADH / **C. Patient who recently had pituitary stalk surgery** / D. Patient with lung cancer producing excess ADH

**[Practice — Endocrine]** What is the name of the autoimmune disorder that targets the thyroid and causes hypothyroidism? *(Hashimoto's thyroiditis)*

**[Practice — Endocrine]** Endemic goiter results from a lack of _______ in the diet: **B. Iodine**

**[Practice — Endocrine]** Giantism is abnormal growth in young people because of the __________ of growth hormone by the pituitary gland.

**[Practice — Endocrine — FRQ]** Name a disorder caused by hyposecretion that results in pancreatic cell destruction.

**[Practice — Endocrine — Case: Lisa (Type 1 Diabetes)]**
Lisa (7 yr): thirsty, polyuria, polyphagia, weight loss; glucose 545 mg/dL; pH 7.23; urine + glucose and ketones.
1. Diagnosis? 2. Why elevated glucose? 3. Why fruity breath? 4. Describe mechanism of glucagon action.

**[Practice — Endocrine — Case: Harry Sullivan (Addison's Disease)]**
Harry (21 yr): weakness, fatigue, dizziness, salt craving, persistent tan, weight loss.
1. Disease? 2. Due to hyposecretion of cortisol? 3. Best treatment? 4. Hyperpigmentation due to hypersecretion of ACTH?

---

## MASTER DISORDER REFERENCE

| Disorder | System | Key Features |
|---|---|---|
| Epilepsy | Nervous | Sudden bursts of electrochemical activity; seizures |
| Multiple Sclerosis | Nervous | Autoimmune demyelination; optic neuritis, limb weakness, worsens with heat |
| Alzheimer's Disease | Nervous | APOE gene risk; cholinergic neuron loss; memory deterioration |
| ALS | Nervous | Upper and lower motor neuron loss; progressive paralysis |
| Tay-Sachs | Nervous | Enzyme deficiency; cannot break down lipids in nerve cells |
| Parkinson's Disease | Nervous | Dopamine loss in substantia nigra; tremors, rigidity |
| Cerebral Palsy | Nervous | Brain damage before/during/shortly after birth; movement disorders |
| Myasthenia Gravis | Nervous | ACh receptor blockade by antibodies; muscle weakness |
| Graves' Disease | Endocrine | TSH receptor autoantibodies; hyperthyroidism, exophthalmos |
| Hashimoto's Disease | Endocrine | Autoimmune thyroid destruction; hypothyroidism |
| Addison's Disease | Endocrine | Low cortisol, high ACTH; hyperpigmentation, fatigue |
| Cushing's Syndrome | Endocrine | High cortisol; moon face, central obesity |
| Acromegaly | Endocrine | GH hypersecretion in adulthood; enlarged extremities |
| Pheochromocytoma | Endocrine | Adrenal medulla tumor; excess catecholamines |
| Diabetes Insipidus | Endocrine | ADH deficiency; excessive dilute urine |
| Glaucoma | Sensory | Increased intraocular pressure; optic nerve degeneration |
| Cataracts | Sensory | Lens opacity; cloudy vision |
| Macular Degeneration | Sensory | Breakdown of cells near center of retina; central vision loss |
| Conjunctivitis | Sensory | Inflammation of conjunctiva; pink eye |
| Otitis Media | Sensory | Middle ear infection; common in children |
| Presbycusis | Sensory | Age-related cochlear hair cell loss |
| Otosclerosis | Sensory | Stapes fixation; inherited conductive hearing loss |
| Ménière's Disease | Sensory | Endolymphatic hydrops; episodic vertigo, tinnitus, hearing loss |
| Myopia | Sensory | Nearsightedness; eyeball too long |
| Hyperopia | Sensory | Farsightedness; eyeball too short |
| Presbyopia | Sensory | Age-related loss of near vision; decreased lens flexibility |
| Nyctalopia | Sensory | Night blindness; vitamin A deficiency / rhodopsin deficiency |
| Astigmatism | Sensory | Uneven focus; irregular curvature of cornea or lens |
| Anosmia | Sensory | Loss of the ability to smell |
| Dysgeusia | Sensory | Distorted or altered sense of taste |

# Anatomy & Physiology — Master Question Bank
## Nervous System, Endocrine System & Sense Organs
*Compiled from multiple Science Olympiad practice and invitational exams:*
*2010–2013 practice exams, 2016–2017 practice exams, 2021 Fall Invitational, Mesa-Wilson Invitational 2017, Eastside Invitational 2013, Holly Springs HS CSE 2025-2026, Dulles HS CSE 2025, FCS Innovation Academy CSE 2025, Bergen County Academies, Columbia University Invitational Jan 2026*

---
> **HOW TO USE THIS BANK:**
> - Questions are organized by system, then by question type
> - Correct answers are **bolded** where known
> - FRQs are open-ended; use as written practice or discussion prompts
> - Questions from older exams tend toward Division B depth; newer CSE questions trend toward Division C/college depth

---

## ENDOCRINE SYSTEM

### Multiple Choice — Hormone Mechanisms & Signaling

1. Which of the following most accurately describes the difference in mechanism of action between peptide and steroid hormones?
   - A. Peptide hormones bind to nuclear receptors; steroid hormones bind to membrane-bound receptors.
   - B. Peptide hormones require intracellular transport proteins; steroid hormones are freely soluble in plasma.
   - **C. Peptide hormones typically activate second messenger systems; steroid hormones directly influence gene expression.**
   - D. Peptide hormones alter transcription directly; steroid hormones act only through enzymatic phosphorylation.

2. What is a major structural difference between catecholamines and thyroid hormones?
   - A. Catecholamines are lipid-soluble
   - **B. Thyroid hormones require plasma transport proteins**
   - C. Catecholamines bind nuclear receptors
   - D. Thyroid hormones act within the cytoplasm

3. Why do peptide hormones act quickly but briefly?
   - **A. They're stored in vesicles and cleared fast**
   - B. They bind intracellularly
   - C. They're made from cholesterol
   - D. They travel with hormone chaperones

4. Which of the following limits the duration of peptide hormone signaling inside the cell the most?
   - A. Receptor regulation of lysosomal digestion
   - **B. Conversion of cAMP to AMP by phosphodiesterase**
   - C. Desensitization of G-protein-coupled receptors
   - D. Competitive inhibition at the hormone receptor site

5. Which hormone is both an amine and lipid-soluble, enabling nuclear receptor action?
   - A. Dopamine
   - **B. Thyroxine (T4)**
   - C. Norepinephrine
   - D. Epinephrine

6. Which mechanism best describes the action of insulin?
   - A. Binds to nuclear receptors to increase synthesis
   - B. Inhibits secondary messengers in tissue
   - **C. Activates membrane receptors leading to glucose uptake**
   - D. Crosses the membrane to alter transcription

7. Which hormone has a mechanism that acts on nuclear receptors?
   - A. Insulin
   - B. Parathyroid Hormone
   - **C. Cortisol**
   - D. Thyroid Stimulating Hormone

8. Why can steroids diffuse through the plasma membrane, unlike amines/proteins? *(Describe in terms of structure and membrane permeability.)*

9. Describe how amine/protein hormones affect their target cell.

10. Describe how steroid hormones affect their target cell.

11. What does the cyclic AMP signaling mechanism involve? Trace the steps from hormone binding to cell response.

12. What enzyme, inhibited by Viagra, breaks down cAMP and cGMP?
    - A. cAMPase
    - **B. Phosphodiesterase**
    - C. Monoamine oxidase
    - D. Adenosinase

13. The eicosanoid family of paracrine regulators have backbones derived from what compound?
    - A. Linoleic acid
    - **B. Arachidonic acid**
    - C. Omega 3 fatty acids
    - D. Cholesterol

14. What is the pathway involving Phospholipase C (PLC) and Protein Kinase C (PKC), and what are their respective roles?

15. Which of the following best explains how steroid hormones induce gene transcription?
    - A. Activation of membrane receptors
    - B. Secondary messenger cascades
    - **C. Direct DNA binding in the nucleus**
    - D. mRNA translation control

16. Which hormone cannot be taken orally due to being denatured by digestive enzymes?
    - **A. Insulin**
    - B. Epinephrine
    - C. Progesterone
    - D. Cortisol

17. Which hormone is most likely able to easily pass through a semi-permeable cell membrane to bind to intracellular receptors?
    - A. Insulin
    - B. Epinephrine
    - C. TSH
    - **D. Estrogen**

18. Uptake of iodine into thyroid cells is powered by:
    - A. Primary active transport via ATP hydrolysis by the apical NIS symporter
    - **B. Secondary active transport via the Na⁺ gradient by the basolateral NIS symporter**
    - C. Facilitated diffusion through iodide channels
    - D. Secondary active transport via proton-coupled transport at the apical NIS symporter

### Multiple Choice — Glands, Hormones & Feedback

19. What gland controls other glands?
    - A. Hypothalamus
    - **B. Pituitary**
    - C. Thyroid
    - D. Thymus

20. What is another name for the posterior pituitary gland?
    - **A. Neurohypophysis**
    - B. Postneurotuitary
    - C. Adenohypophysis
    - D. Hypothalamus

21. What is another name for the anterior pituitary gland?
    - A. Neurohypophysis
    - **B. Adenohypophysis**
    - C. Anteneuropituitary
    - D. Anterior Hypothalamus

22. What type of hormones are produced by the anterior pituitary and target other endocrine glands?
    - **A. Tropic**
    - B. Polyurethane
    - C. Julimonis
    - D. Testicles

23. Which portion of the pituitary gland is composed of nervous tissue?
    - A. Adenohypophysis
    - B. Sella turcica
    - **C. Posterior pituitary**
    - D. Stalk

24. A researcher blocks all hypothalamic releasing hormones. Which hormone would still be secreted normally?
    - A. ACTH
    - B. TSH
    - C. LH
    - **D. Oxytocin**

25. Which of the following is a unique feature of the supraoptic nucleus compared to other hypothalamic nuclei?
    - A. It projects to the anterior pituitary to control hormone synthesis via releasing factors.
    - **B. It contains osmoreceptors and sends long axons directly into the posterior pituitary.**
    - C. It regulates circadian rhythm by responding to retinal light signals.
    - D. It secretes dopamine to inhibit prolactin from the anterior pituitary.

26. Which feature most distinguishes magnocellular hypothalamic neurons from parvocellular ones?
    - A. They synapse on anterior pituitary cells.
    - B. They use axonal transport to reach median eminence capillaries.
    - **C. They release hormones into systemic circulation.**
    - D. They release both releasing and inhibiting factors.

27. Which cell type plays a bigger role in estrogen production in prepubertal males compared to adults?
    - A. Leydig cells
    - **B. Sertoli cells**
    - C. Germ cells
    - D. Adrenal medullary chromaffin cells

28. The release of most hormones is regulated by the:
    - A. Positive feedback loop
    - **B. Negative feedback loop**
    - C. Hilton's Law
    - D. Starling's Equilibrium

29. Which of the following statements is correct regarding hormones?
    - A. Hormones are always secreted into the blood.
    - B. Hormones are secreted at a constant rate to maintain homeostasis.
    - C. Hormones always exert tropic effects.
    - D. Hormones are secreted through ducts into the blood.
    - **E. None of the above** *(hormones are sometimes released by exocytosis; not always into blood)*

30. Which of the following represents negative feedback?
    - A. X stimulates Y, then inhibits Z
    - B. X inhibits Y, then Y inhibits Z
    - C. X inhibits Y, then Y inhibits X
    - **D. X stimulates Y, then Y inhibits X**
    - E. X stimulates Y, then Y stimulates X

31. Which of the following represents positive feedback?
    - A. X stimulates Y, then inhibits Z
    - B. X inhibits Y, then Y inhibits Z
    - C. X inhibits Y, then Y inhibits X
    - D. X stimulates Y, then Y inhibits X
    - **E. X stimulates Y, then Y stimulates X**

32. How does prolactin inhibit the reproductive axis?
    - **A. Suppresses GnRH release**
    - B. Enhances FSH secretion
    - C. Activates LH surge
    - D. Promotes estrogen synthesis

33. Which hormone is secreted in a pulsatile manner to prevent receptor desensitization?
    - A. LH
    - B. FSH
    - **C. GnRH**
    - D. Estrogen

34. What cells secrete thyroid hormone (TH)?
    - **A. Follicular cells**
    - B. Parafollicular cells
    - C. TG cells
    - D. It depends whether T3 or T4 is being synthesized

35. Which layer of the suprarenal gland secretes mineralocorticoids?
    - **A. Zona glomerulosa**
    - B. Zona fasciculata
    - C. Zona reticularis
    - D. Medulla

36. Which adrenal cortex zone is primarily responsible for producing glucocorticoids?
    - A. Zona glomerulosa
    - **B. Zona fasciculata**
    - C. Zona reticularis
    - D. Medulla

37. What are the main hormones released from the adrenal medulla?
    *(Epinephrine ~80% and norepinephrine ~20%)*

38. Which cells in the ovaries secrete estrogen?
    - **A. Granulosa cells**
    - B. Thecal cells
    - C. Nurse cells
    - D. Germ cells

39. Which enzyme is critical in the conversion of testosterone to estrogen in adipose tissue?
    - **A. Aromatase**
    - B. 5-alpha reductase
    - C. Tyrosine hydroxylase
    - D. MAO

40. Which enzyme catalyzes the rate-limiting step of steroid hormone synthesis from cholesterol?
    - A. Aromatase
    - B. 21-hydroxylase
    - **C. Cholesterol desmolase**
    - D. 17β-hydroxysteroid dehydrogenase

41. Which hormone primarily regulates serum potassium levels?
    - A. Insulin
    - **B. Aldosterone**
    - C. PTH
    - D. ADH

42. Which hormone increases renal calcium reabsorption and decreases phosphate reabsorption?
    - A. Calcitonin
    - B. Vitamin D
    - **C. PTH**
    - D. TSH

43. Which of the following correctly traces how PTH increases intestinal calcium uptake?
    - A. PTH → thyroid → calcitonin → increased Ca absorption
    - B. PTH → liver → vitamin D3 → increased Ca uptake
    - **C. PTH → kidney → activation of vitamin D → calcitriol → increased Ca absorption**
    - D. PTH → adrenal cortex → aldosterone → sodium retention → increased Ca absorption

44. Which best describes how PTH stimulates calcium release from bone at the cellular level?
    - A. PTH binds to osteoclast receptors, directly stimulating bone resorption
    - B. PTH promotes osteoblast activity, which inhibits osteoclasts
    - **C. PTH stimulates osteoblasts to express RANKL, which activates osteoclast precursors and promotes bone resorption**
    - D. PTH activates vitamin D in bone tissue, leading to localized calcium mobilization

45. Calcitonin is produced in what part of the thyroid gland?
    - A. Colloid
    - **B. Parafollicular cells**
    - C. Follicle cells
    - D. Parathyroid cells

46. Which hormone decreases the rate of bone resorption?
    - **A. Calcitonin** *(from parafollicular/C-cells of thyroid)*
    - B. PTH
    - C. Aldosterone
    - D. Cortisol

47. Which of the following is promoted by the direct secretory product of alpha cells of the pancreas?
    - **A. Gluconeogenesis / Cori cycle** *(glucagon promotes this)*

48. Insulin is secreted by which specific cell of the pancreas?
    - **A. Beta cells of the Islet of Langerhans**
    - B. Delta cells
    - C. Theta cells
    - D. Alpha cells

49. Which hormone(s) are secreted from the beta cells of the pancreas?
    - **A. Insulin and Amylin**
    - B. Insulin and Glucagon
    - C. Amylin only
    - D. Insulin only

50. What hormone is measured during a dexamethasone suppression test?
    - **A. Cortisol**
    - B. ACTH
    - C. Aldosterone
    - D. CRH

51. What disease is a dexamethasone suppression test trying to screen for? *(Cushing's syndrome/disease)*

52. Which hormone is primarily involved in regulating the body's response to stress?
    - A. Growth Hormone
    - **B. Cortisol**
    - C. Thyroxine
    - D. Oxytocin

53. In evolution, what is the primary survival benefit of secreting cortisol?
    - A. Reduces appetite
    - B. Suppresses immune responses long-term
    - **C. Mobilizes energy during a threat**
    - D. Stimulates oxytocin release

54. Which hormone is photosensitive? *(Melatonin)*

55. The thymus secretes hormones that help mature which type of immune cell? *(T cells)*

56. Which hormone increases Na reabsorption in the kidneys, affecting blood pressure? *(Aldosterone)*

57. What is the primary function of prolactin? *(Milk production/lactation)*

58. Which gland is connected to both the nervous and endocrine systems? *(Hypothalamus)*

59. What is it called when a hormone acts on another endocrine gland, stimulating it to release its own hormones? *(Tropic effect)*

60. Which endocrine organ stores hormone-containing colloid extracellularly before secretion? *(Thyroid gland)*

61. Which hormone is responsible for upregulating aquaporin-2 in the collecting ducts of the kidney? *(ADH/Vasopressin)*

62. What is the primary site of peripheral T4-to-T3 conversion in the body? *(Liver)*

63. Which hormone is secreted in pulses primarily during sleep? *(Growth hormone)*

64. What hormone is secreted by Leydig cells? *(Testosterone)*

65. High levels of what hormone directly induces ovulation? *(LH)*

66. Which hormone has both endocrine and neurohormonal functions and is involved in circadian rhythm regulation?
    - A. ACTH
    - **B. Melatonin**
    - C. Cortisol
    - D. Oxytocin

67. Which hormones will be secreted in response to vasodilatory shock?
    - **E. All of the above** *(Epinephrine, Cortisol, ADH/Vasopressin, Aldosterone)*

68. In SIADH, what is the most likely combination of serum sodium and urine osmolality?
    - A. Low serum sodium, low urine osmolality
    - B. High serum sodium, high urine osmolality
    - **C. Low serum sodium, high urine osmolality**
    - D. High serum sodium, low urine osmolality

69. A patient presents with hypernatremia and low ADH. What condition is most likely?
    - A. SIADH
    - B. Diabetes mellitus
    - **C. Diabetes insipidus**
    - D. Addison's disease

70. Diabetes insipidus is due to a deficiency of:
    - A. Insulin
    - B. Glucose
    - **C. ADH**
    - D. Glucagon

71. Which patient is most likely to experience a temporary form of diabetes insipidus that could resolve without lifelong treatment?
    - A. A patient with a tumor that directly damages the hypothalamus.
    - B. A patient whose kidneys don't respond to ADH.
    - **C. A patient who recently had pituitary stalk surgery.**
    - D. A patient with lung cancer producing excess ADH.

72. Normal fasting glucose in mg/dL is closest to:
    - A. 100–140
    - **B. 60–100**
    - C. 140–180
    - D. 40–60

### Multiple Choice — Endocrine Disorders

73. An individual presents with fatigue, weight gain, and bradycardia. Labs reveal elevated TSH and low T3. Which best explains this?
    - **A. Primary hypothyroidism from thyroid gland failure**
    - B. Secondary hypothyroidism due to pituitary underactivity
    - C. Hypersecretion of TRH from the hypothalamus
    - D. Hyposensitivity to thyroid hormone at the cellular level

74. What would be the expected hormonal profile in secondary hypothyroidism?
    - A. High TSH, Low T3/T4
    - **B. Low TSH, Low T3/T4**
    - C. High TSH, High T3/T4
    - D. Normal TSH, High T3/T4

75. Which disorder is caused by TSH receptor autoantibodies?
    - **A. Graves' disease**
    - B. Hashimoto's disease
    - C. Cushing's syndrome
    - D. Myxedema

76. Which of the following is NOT an effect of Graves' Disease?
    - A. Enlargement of the extrinsic eye muscles
    - B. Heart sounds that are louder than normal
    - **C. Decreased bone reabsorption**
    - D. Muscle atrophy and weakness

77. Which symptom is most specific to myxedema?
    - A. Hypertension and weight loss
    - **B. Puffy face with dry skin**
    - C. Sudden drop in blood calcium
    - D. Flushed skin with tremors

78. Melissa goes to the doctor for diarrhea. Her doctor notices thickened skin on her shins and bulging eyes. What does her doctor suspect?
    - *(Graves' disease / hyperthyroidism)*

79. Billy is experiencing intense swelling in his neck, cough, and hoarseness. What condition is he experiencing? What treatments are available?
    - *(Goiter; iodine supplementation, thyroid hormone therapy, or surgery depending on cause)*

80. Caitlyn has severe mental deficiency due to lack of iodine, causing motor spasticity, deaf mutism, and squint. What condition is this?
    - *(Congenital Iodine Deficiency Syndrome / Cretinism)*

81. Which lab results hint at Addison's disease?
    - A. High cortisol, high ACTH
    - B. Low cortisol, low ACTH
    - **C. Low cortisol, high ACTH**
    - D. High aldosterone, low renin

82. Which condition would present with high ACTH but low cortisol?
    - A. Cushing's disease
    - **B. Addison's disease**
    - C. Ectopic ACTH syndrome
    - D. Cortisol-secreting adrenal adenoma

83. In Cushing's disease, what is the most common underlying pathophysiology?
    - A. Adrenal tumor
    - B. Ectopic ACTH secretion
    - **C. Pituitary adenoma overproducing ACTH**
    - D. Iatrogenic steroid use

84. Pituitary dwarfism is caused by:
    - A. GH receptor mutation
    - B. IGF overproduction
    - **C. GH hyposecretion**
    - D. Thyroid hormone excess

85. A condition caused by hypersecretion of somatotrophin in adulthood, leading to enlargement of hands, feet, and facial features:
    - A. Gigantism
    - **B. Acromegaly**
    - C. Dwarfism
    - D. Diabetes insipidus

86. What hormone causes gigantism? What gland secretes it?
    - *(Growth hormone (GH); anterior pituitary)*

87. Which of the following disorders results from hypersecretion of hormones?
    - *(Cushing's syndrome, Graves' disease, Acromegaly, Gigantism)*

88. Which of the following disorders results from hyposecretion of hormones?
    - *(Addison's disease, Cretinism, Dwarfism, Diabetes insipidus)*

89. Which of the following disorders results from hypersensitivity to target hormones?
    - **A. Graves' Disease**

90. In primary hyperaldosteronism (Conn's syndrome), which laboratory finding is most characteristic?
    - A. Hyperkalemia and metabolic acidosis
    - **B. Hypokalemia and metabolic alkalosis**
    - C. Hypernatremia and hyperkalemia
    - D. Hyponatremia and metabolic acidosis

91. A tumor in the adrenal medulla causes excessive catecholamine release. What symptoms would you expect?
    - A. Bradycardia, hypotension, fatigue
    - **B. Tachycardia, hypertension, anxiety**
    - C. Weight gain, heat intolerance
    - D. Hypoglycemia, sweating, coma

92. Pheochromocytomas are a tumor of the:
    - A. Parathyroid Gland
    - B. Pineal Gland
    - C. Pituitary Gland
    - **D. Adrenal Medulla**

93. Which of the following is a hyposecretion disorder?
    - A. Pheochromocytoma
    - **B. Addison's disease**
    - C. Graves disease
    - D. Cushing's disease

94. Type 1 Diabetes Mellitus is a result of:
    - A. Insulin insensitivity
    - **B. Immune system destruction of insulin-producing beta cells**
    - C. Defective GLUT 4 receptors
    - D. Weight gain and excessive sugar intake

95. Type 2 Diabetes accounts for about \_\_\_ of all diagnosed cases, while Type 1 accounts for \_\_\_.
    - **C. 90%, 10%**

96. A patient with low serum calcium and high PTH likely has:
    - A. Primary hypoparathyroidism
    - **B. Secondary hyperparathyroidism**
    - C. Addison's disease
    - D. Hyperthyroidism

97. Which of the following is the primary symptom of Congenital Iodine Deficiency Syndrome?
    - A. Excessive growth
    - B. Precocious puberty
    - **C. Cognitive developmental delays**

98. Tumors in which gland can cause changes in vision due to proximity to the optic nerve?
    - **A. Pituitary gland**

99. The hypothalamus-pituitary-adrenal axis is suppressed in:
    - A. Untreated Addison's disease
    - **B. Long-term corticosteroid therapy**
    - C. Primary hyperaldosteronism
    - D. Acute stress

100. Which endocrine disruptor binds to both ERα and ERβ?
     - A. DDT
     - B. PFAS
     - **C. BPA**

### True / False — Endocrine

1. Aldosterone is a glucocorticoid. **(False — mineralocorticoid)**
2. In the Zona Fasciculata you'd find enzymes that synthesize aldosterone. **(False — zona glomerulosa)**
3. Phenylalanine N-Methyltransferase catalyzes the conversion of Vitamin D to calcitriol. **(False — CYP27B1/1α-hydroxylase)**
4. Hyperalgesia is linked to an increase in β-endorphins. **(False — β-endorphins decrease pain)**
5. Administering epinephrine to adipocytes leads to significant fatty acid release. **(True)**
6. Neutrophil overaction is responsible for overproduction of chemicals causing hyperthyroidism. **(False — autoantibodies)**
7. A mutation rendering chondrocytes insensitive to peptide GHs would make an individual taller. **(False — shorter)**
8. EPO release is normally triggered by high oxygen in the blood. **(False — low oxygen triggers EPO)**
9. An increase in PTHrp and decrease in PTH is likely due to a cytotoxic tumor. **(True)**
10. Treatment with 1,25-dihydroxyvitamin D is a common solution to hypocalcemia. **(True)**
11. Steroids are water-soluble. **(False — lipid-soluble)**
12. Amine hormones are synthesized from the amino acids tryptophan or threonine. **(False — tryptophan or tyrosine)**
13. Type 2 diabetes mellitus occurs when too little insulin is secreted by the pancreas. **(False — primarily insulin resistance)**

---

### Fill in the Blank — HPT Axis

To maintain stable metabolism, the body uses the HPT axis. It begins when the **(1) hypothalamus** detects low circulating thyroid hormone and releases TRH. TRH travels through the **(2) hypophyseal portal** system to reach the anterior pituitary, which secretes TSH. TSH stimulates the **(3) thyroid** gland to produce T3 **(4) triiodothyronine** and T4 **(5) thyroxine**. These hormones increase cellular metabolism. Sufficient T3 + T4 inhibits TRH and TSH through **(6) negative** feedback.

---

### Endocrine Gland Matching

| Gland | Key Secretions / Notes |
|---|---|
| Anterior Pituitary | GH, TSH, ACTH, FSH, LH, Prolactin, MSH; tropic hormones targeting other endocrine glands |
| Posterior Pituitary | Oxytocin and ADH (synthesized by hypothalamus, stored/released here) |
| Thyroid | T3 (triiodothyronine), T4 (thyroxine) from follicular cells; calcitonin from parafollicular cells |
| Parathyroid | PTH; increases blood calcium |
| Adrenal Medulla | Epinephrine (~80%), Norepinephrine (~20%) |
| Adrenal Cortex — Zona Glomerulosa | Mineralocorticoids (aldosterone) |
| Adrenal Cortex — Zona Fasciculata | Glucocorticoids (cortisol) |
| Adrenal Cortex — Zona Reticularis | Androgens (DHEA) |
| Thymus | Thymosin; promotes T-cell maturation |
| Pineal Gland | Melatonin; circadian rhythm regulation |
| Pancreas — Beta cells | Insulin, amylin |
| Pancreas — Alpha cells | Glucagon |
| Ovaries — Granulosa cells | Estrogen |
| Testes — Leydig cells | Testosterone |
| Testes — Sertoli cells | Inhibin B; estrogen in prepubertal males |

### Endocrine FRQs

1. Which pair of hormones shows antagonism in calcium homeostasis, and what are their primary sources? *(PTH from parathyroid glands vs. calcitonin from thyroid parafollicular cells)*
2. In a sentence, explain why the anterior pituitary is considered an endocrine gland while the posterior pituitary is not.
3. What is the difference between endocrine and exocrine glands?
4. Explain the relation between oxytocin and prolactin.
5. What does MSH stand for, what does it do, and where is it released from?
6. Describe the difference between the posterior and anterior pituitary glands and their association with the hypothalamus. List and describe 2 hormones for each.
7. What does ADH stand for, what does it do, and where is it released from?
8. Describe the mechanism of cortisol release AND the general mechanism of cortisol activity.
9. What is the thyroid's main secretion and what effect does the hormone have?
10. List the 3 classes of corticosteroids and their respective functions.
11. Describe the three chemical classes of hormones.
12. Describe what happens to a person's insulin levels when afflicted with Type 1 Diabetes.
13. What are 3 factors that can increase the likelihood of developing hypoglycemia?
14. Name the two products that proinsulin (from β-islet cells) is converted to. *(Insulin and C-peptide)*
15. Describe the mechanism of action for steroid hormones from blood transport to target gene expression.
16. Compare and contrast primary vs. secondary adrenal insufficiency (endocrine profiles and lab findings).
17. Explain the role of the hypothalamus in integrating nervous and endocrine responses (cite at least two hormone pathways).
18. Discuss the feedback regulation of the thyroid axis and predict lab results for primary hyperthyroidism.
19. Analyze the hormonal disruptions seen in polycystic ovarian syndrome and their clinical consequences.
20. Construct a detailed flowchart tracing the hormonal response to acute stress starting from the hypothalamus.
21. Explain what Pendrin is and, if blocked long-term, what the body would do to maintain homeostasis.
22. Explain why the gonads do not secrete aldosterone or cortisol, and which intermediates of the steroid hormone synthesis pathway gonads cannot convert.
23. Suppose one molecule of epinephrine contacts a liver cell. Estimate how many molecules of glucose would be released and explain the GPCR signaling cascade. *(Answer in scientific notation.)*
24. If the hypothalamus fails to produce GnRH, what will happen to LH and FSH levels? *(Decrease)*
25. A patient has low ACTH and low cortisol. Which gland is malfunctioning? *(Pituitary)*

### Endocrine Case Studies

**Case — Lisa (7-year-old, Type 1 Diabetes):**
Lisa has increased thirst, frequent urination, increased appetite, weight loss. Labs: Blood glucose 545 mg/dL; Blood pH 7.23; Urine positive for glucose and ketone bodies.
1. Which condition does Lisa most likely have? *(Diabetes mellitus Type 1)*
2. Why are Lisa's blood-glucose levels elevated? *(Pancreas not making enough insulin)*
3. Lisa has a fruity odor to her breath. Why? *(Acetone from ketone bodies)*
4. Describe the mechanism of action of glucagon from its gland to its target.

**Case — Harry Sullivan (21-year-old, Addison's Disease):**
Harry reports weakness, fatigue, knee pain, dizziness, nausea, weight loss, craving for salty foods, persistent tan despite winter, and irritability.
1. What disease does Harry have? *(Addison's disease)*
2. This disease is due to: **D. Hyposecretion of cortisol**
3. Which treatment is most useful? **A. Dual Release Hydrocortisone**
4. The hyperpigmentation is due to: **C. Hypersecretion of ACTH**
