## NERVOUS SYSTEM

### Multiple Choice — Neurons & Physiology

1. What is the brain region best known as the sensory routing system? If cortical nerves from sensory organs bypass this region, the cortex won't perceive those sensations.
   - a. Hypothalamus
   - b. Post central gyrus
   - c. Cerebrum
   - **d. Thalamus**

2. What primarily causes the neuron's resting membrane potential to be negative?
   - a. The Na⁺/K⁺ pump pumps out three Na⁺ ions for every two K⁺ ions brought in
   - **b. Open K⁺ leak channels allow more K⁺ to leave than Na⁺ can enter**
   - c. Intracellular proteins carry negative charge, directly setting the membrane potential
   - d. Voltage-gated Na⁺ channels are closed at rest

3. What is the approximate resting potential of a neuron?
   - a. −90 mV
   - b. −82 mV
   - **c. −70 mV**
   - d. +20 mV

4. At resting membrane potential, potassium wants to move \_\_\_\_ the cell, and sodium wants to move \_\_\_\_ the cell?
   - *(K⁺: out; Na⁺: in)*

5. Initial depolarization of an action potential is caused by:
   - a. Potassium ions entering the cell
   - b. Potassium ions exiting the cell
   - **c. Sodium ions entering the cell**
   - d. Sodium ions exiting the cell

6. Which of the following is a factor in generating the resting membrane potential?
   - a. Lower concentration of K⁺ immediately inside the cell membrane
   - **b. Higher concentration of Na⁺ immediately outside the cell membrane**
   - c. Greater permeability of the cell membrane to Na⁺ than K⁺
   - d. All of the above

7. Which correctly describes the function of the sodium-potassium pump?
   - a. Allows passive movement of ions across the membrane
   - b. Hyperpolarizes the membrane by allowing Cl⁻ to enter
   - **c. Actively exports 3 Na⁺ and imports 2 K⁺ using ATP**
   - d. Actively transports 3 K⁺ in and 2 Na⁺ out per ATP cycle

8. Which ion's relative permeability influences membrane potential the most (GHK equation)?
   - a. Na⁺
   - b. Cl⁻
   - **c. K⁺**
   - d. Ca²⁺

9. For an increase in the movement of potassium ions out of a neuron's cytoplasm:
   - a. Depolarization of the neuron
   - **b. Hyperpolarization of the neuron**
   - c. Replacement of potassium ions with sodium ions
   - d. Replacement of potassium ions with calcium ions

10. Which statement about the refractory period is TRUE?
    - a. During the absolute refractory period, a strong stimulus can trigger another action potential
    - b. The relative refractory period occurs when Na⁺ channels are open and inactivated
    - **c. During the relative refractory period, K⁺ channels are open**
    - d. The refractory period affects the entire neuron equally

11. A neuron's threshold potential is −55 mV, and it is currently in its absolute refractory period at +30 mV. How strong would a depolarizing stimulus need to be to trigger a second action potential?
    - **d. None of the above** *(no stimulus can trigger an AP during absolute refractory period)*

12. What is the "threshold" potential of a membrane?
    - **d. The minimum depolarization needed to operate the voltage-gated sodium and potassium channels**

13. In saltatory conduction, action potentials propagate along what part of myelinated axons?
    - a. Along the entire axon
    - **b. Nodes of Ranvier**
    - c. Only through the myelin sheath
    - d. Dendrites only

14. What process is described as an action potential jumping from one node of Ranvier to the next?
    - **Saltatory conduction**

15. Which part of the neuron integrates incoming signals and initiates an action potential?
    - a. Axon terminal
    - **b. Axon hillock**
    - c. Dendrite
    - d. Myelin sheath

16. Which type of neuron is most abundant?
    - a. Sensory Neuron
    - **b. Interneuron**
    - c. Motor Neuron
    - d. Central Neuron

17. What type of neurons carry signals from sensory receptors to the CNS?
    - **c. Afferent**

18. What type of neurons carry signals from the brain/CNS to muscles and glands?
    - **d. Efferent**

19. In an excitatory synapse, activity from one neuron increases the likelihood of an action potential in an \_\_\_\_\_ neuron.
    - **b. Adjacent**

20. Where is acetylcholine synthesized and enclosed?
    - **c. Presynaptic terminal and synaptic vesicles**

21. Neurotransmitters are released from axon terminals via:
    - **e. Exocytosis**

22. An inhibitory postsynaptic potential (IPSP) occurs when the membrane becomes more permeable to:
    - **a. Potassium ions**

23. What property distinguishes temporal summation from spatial summation?
    - **a. It involves inputs at the same synapse over time**

24. When several EPSPs arrive at the axon hillock from different dendritic locations, this is an example of:
    - **b. Spatial summation**

25. What best distinguishes monosynaptic from polysynaptic reflexes?
    - **a. Polysynaptic reflexes require interneurons while monosynaptic reflexes do not**

26. Which structure detects muscle stretch to initiate the stretch reflex?
    - **c. Muscle spindle**

27. What are Nissl bodies and how are they useful?

28. How do viruses get to the neurosoma?

29. Which glial cell type is primarily responsible for myelinating axons in the CNS?
    - a. Schwann cells
    - b. Astrocytes
    - **c. Oligodendrocytes**
    - d. Ependymal cells

30. Which glial cell has mesodermal rather than ectodermal origin?
    - a. Oligodendrocytes
    - b. Ependymal cells
    - **c. Microglia**
    - d. Astrocytes

31. Which glial cell type acts as an immune cell in the CNS?
    - **a. Microglia**

32. Which glial cell type lines the ventricles and helps produce CSF?
    - **d. Ependymal cells**

33. Which best describes the astrocyte-neuron lactate shuttle hypothesis?
    - **b. Astrocytes convert glucose to lactate then shuttle it to neurons**

34. Which is characteristic of myelination exclusively in the CNS?
    - **d. Newer layers of myelin are formed under preexisting layers**

35. Which best describes Wallerian degeneration?
    - **b. Degeneration of the axon distal to the site of injury, while the proximal segment remains connected to the neurosoma**

36. Which of the following is NOT a catecholamine?
    - **a. Serotonin**

37. All catecholamines are synthesized from:
    - **b. Tyrosine**

38. Which neuromodulator is created via arginine metabolism in endothelial cells?
    - **d. Nitric oxide**

39. Which neurotransmitter and receptor pairing is INCORRECT?
    - **d. Dopamine — AMPA receptors in motor control pathways**

40. What is the role of an enkephalin?
    - **b. To act as a natural painkiller by inhibiting pain signals**

41. Which of the following is characteristic of the sympathetic nervous system?
    - a. Inhibits the digestive tract
    - b. Dilates the bronchi
    - c. Accelerates the heartbeat
    - **d. All of the above**

42. Which is NOT associated with activation of the sympathetic division of the ANS?
    - **b. Bronchial constriction** *(sympathetic dilates bronchi)*

43. A normal response of the body under parasympathetic stimulation:
    - **c. Constriction of pupils**

44. Which receptor decreases heart rate when activated?
    - **b. M2**

45. During a sudden fight-or-flight response, the adrenal medulla releases epinephrine and norepinephrine in which ratio?
    - **c. 80% epinephrine, 20% norepinephrine**

46. Which of the following is NOT true of the enteric nervous system?
    - **c. It cannot function without input from the central nervous system**

47. How does caffeine enhance alertness?
    - **d. Blocking adenosine receptors**

48. Botulinum toxin impairs neurotransmission by:
    - **c. Preventing vesicular release of ACh**

49. In a short sentence, how is botulinum toxin used in cosmetic medicine procedures?
    - *(Injected in small amounts to temporarily paralyze facial muscles, reducing the appearance of wrinkles)*

50. Which best describes the function of the Circle of Willis?
    - **c. Provides collateral blood flow to the brain**

51. Which medication is commonly used to treat spasticity in multiple sclerosis?
    - **b. Baclofen**

52. Which is NOT associated with REM sleep?
    - **e. Body repair** *(occurs in N3 deep sleep)*

53. Sleep spindles and K-complexes are most characteristic of which sleep stage?
    - **b. N2**

54. Which EEG waveform is most prominent during deep sleep?
    - **d. Delta**

55. When using an EEG, which brain wave is detected when you are awake?
    - **b. Beta**

56. What is the frequency of a theta wave?
    - **b. 4–7 Hertz**

57. Which of the following statements about the blood-brain barrier is true?
    - **c. Maintained by tight junctions between endothelial cells of brain capillaries**

58. Which neurotransmitter and receptor pairing is incorrect for normal human physiology?
    - **d. Dopamine — AMPA receptors in motor control pathways**

59. Which best explains why hyperkalemia can lead to muscle weakness?
    - **a. It increases the resting membrane potential, inactivating voltage-gated sodium channels**

60. Interneurons in the PNS can be found in which two locations?
    - **d. Enteric nervous system; autonomic ganglia**

61. What pigment accumulates in older neurons and displaces the nucleus?
    - **b. Lipofuscin**

62. Which of the following accurately orders neuronal structures from proximal to distal?
    - **a. Hillock, initial segment, internodal segments, terminal arborization**

63. Which neuron type exhibits the most prominent terminal arborization?
    - **b. Purkinje cell**

64. Which statement about axonal transport is NOT true?
    - **c. Slow axonal transport travels nonstop unlike fast axonal transport**

65. Which region of the vertebral column contains more spinal nerves than vertebrae?
    - **d. Sacral**

66. Intercostal nerves are found in which region?
    - **b. Thoracic**

67. The pia mater continues beyond the medullary cone to form what structure?
    - **b. Terminal filum**

68. Which disorder is NOT caused by improper closure of the posterior neuropore?
    - **b. Encephalocele**

69. Which B vitamin is required for proper neural tube closure?
    - **d. Folate**

70. What part of the spinal cord primarily carries motor signals?
    - **b. Ventral horn**

71. Which tract carries fine touch and proprioceptive information to the brain?
    - **b. Dorsal column**

72. Which cranial nerve carries parasympathetic fibers to thoracic and abdominal viscera?
    - **d. CN X (Vagus nerve)**

73. A stroke in the MCA would most likely affect:
    - **c. Face and arm movement**

74. A patient demonstrates difficulty forming new memories but recalls old ones. Which region is most likely affected?
    - **c. Hippocampus**

75. Damage to the right posterior parietal cortex most likely causes:
    - **c. Lack of spatial awareness**

76. Which of the following lists the correct path of CSF?
    - **b. Lateral ventricles → third ventricle → fourth ventricle → subarachnoid space**

77. A split-brain patient is shown a picture of a key flashed in the left visual field. Which response is most likely?
    - **b. They are unable to say what it is but can select the key with the left hand**

78. A patient suffers a stroke affecting the internal capsule. Which is most likely to occur?
    - **a. Loss of signal transmission from cortex to spinal cord**

79. Which of the following statements about cerebrospinal fluid is true?
    - **a. It is produced primarily by ependymal cells lining the ventricles**

80. Which neurotransmitter system is primarily affected in Parkinson's disease?
    - **b. Dopamine**

81. A patient presents with optic neuritis and intermittent limb weakness. Which diagnosis is most likely?
    - **d. Multiple sclerosis**

82. In ALS, which neurons are lost?
    - **b. Upper and lower motor neurons**

83. When *Clostridium tetani* produces tetanospasmin, what disorder results and how does it cause muscle stiffness?
    - *(Tetanus; **a. Disinhibition of motor neurons by a GABA blockade**)*

84. Which brain region is most important for initiating voluntary motor commands?
    - **d. Primary motor cortex**

85. Which neurotransmitter is released at the neuromuscular junction? *(Acetylcholine)*

86. What ion primarily drives the depolarization phase? *(Na⁺)*

87. Which brain region coordinates voluntary movement? *(Cerebellum)*

88. What are the gaps between myelinated axons called? *(Nodes of Ranvier)*

89. The Goldman-Hodgkin-Katz equation is used to calculate what? *(Resting membrane potential)*

90. What is an early symptom of Alzheimer's disease? *(Memory loss / forgetting recent events)*

91. Which brain region converts short-term memory into long-term memory? *(Hippocampus)*

92. What structure allows communication between the two cerebral hemispheres? *(Corpus callosum)*

93. Which specific part of the brain is affected in Parkinson's disease? *(Substantia nigra)*

94. What neurotransmitter do muscarinic receptors bind to? *(Acetylcholine)*

95. Cranial nerve VII is called \_\_\_\_ and has \_\_\_\_ function:
    - **c. Facial nerve — motor to the facial muscles**

96. Which cranial nerve controls eye movement (abducens)?
    - **a. CN VI**

### Multiple Choice — Brain Regions & Tracts

97. Which brain region regulates vital functions like breathing and heartbeat?
    - *(Medulla oblongata)*

98. Which correctly matches a brainstem region with its function?
    - **c. Pons — autonomic breathing regulation**

99. What are the parts of the brainstem?
    - *(Medulla, Pons, Midbrain)*

100. What is the function of the cerebellum?
     - *(Muscle coordination, balance, muscle tone)*

101. What is the function of the thalamus?
     - *(Brain's switchboard / sensory relay station)*

102. Which lobe of the brain houses the visual areas?
     - **d. Occipital Lobe**

103. Auditory information is processed in which lobe?
     - *(Temporal lobe)*

104. Which brain region is responsible for proper balance and posture?
     - **c. Cerebellum**

105. Which tract carries fine touch and proprioception to the brain?
     - **b. Dorsal column (dorsal column-medial lemniscal pathway)**

106. Which pathway is related to movement coordination?
     - **a. Rubrospinal**

107. People who suffer a stroke in Wernicke's area can speak fluently but with no meaning. What is that region called?
     - *(Wernicke's area — located in the temporal lobe)*

108. What are the functions of Broca's and Wernicke's areas? Where are they located?

109. Which region of the brain is most associated with coordination of voluntary movement?
     - *(Cerebellum)*

110. What structure allows communication between the two cerebral hemispheres?
     - *(Corpus callosum)*

### True / False — Nervous System

1. In a neuron that depolarizes, Na⁺ flows into the cell. **(True)**
2. Botulism destroys SNARES that activate skeletal muscle in excitatory neurons. **(True)**
3. All biogenic amines are derived from modifications to threonine. **(False — from aromatic amino acids like tyrosine and tryptophan)**
4. A Clitocybe mushroom could have toxins that hamper acetylcholine receptors, but likely not those in learning and behavioral pathways. **(True — muscarinic, not nicotinic)**
5. Overproduction of cholinergic receptors is linked to Alzheimer's disease. **(False — loss/reduction of cholinergic neurons)**
6. Monoamine oxidase inhibitors act by inhibiting the degradation of glutamate, treating depression. **(False — inhibit degradation of monoamines)**
7. The effects of serotonin tend to have a slower onset than other neurotransmitters. **(True)**
8. Stimulation of 5-HT2A receptors can cause hallucinogenic effects. **(True)**
9. The forebrain develops into the cerebrum and diencephalon. **(True)**
10. Pyramidal neurons tend to give outputs to other parts of the CNS and cortex. **(True)**
11. Slightly decreasing axon diameter can increase conduction velocity by reducing internal resistance. **(False — larger diameter increases velocity)**
12. Sensory information about fine touch and proprioception ascends via the dorsal column-medial lemniscal pathway. **(True)**
13. The myelin sheath in the CNS is produced by Schwann cells. **(False — oligodendrocytes)**
14. The dorsal root contains motor fibers. **(False — sensory/afferent; motor fibers in ventral root)**
15. Voltage-gated calcium channels at the axon terminal trigger neurotransmitter release. **(True)**
16. Neurons outnumber neuroglia 3 to 1. **(False — neuroglia outnumber neurons)**
17. Action potentials happen in an all-or-nothing fashion. **(True)**
18. The midbrain is the largest region of the brainstem. **(False — the pons is the largest)**
19. The spinothalamic tract transmits information about body position to the cerebellum. **(False — spinothalamic carries pain and temperature; proprioception goes via dorsal columns)**

### Neurotransmitter Categorization

Categorize each as **catecholamine** or **indolamine**:

| Neurotransmitter | Category |
|---|---|
| Epinephrine | Catecholamine |
| Norepinephrine | Catecholamine |
| Dopamine | Catecholamine |
| Serotonin | Indolamine |
| Histamine | Neither (imidazolamine) |

### Cranial Nerve Reference

| Test / Function | Cranial Nerve |
|---|---|
| Sticking out the tongue — symmetry | CN XII (Hypoglossal) |
| Strength and evenness of a shrug | CN XI (Accessory) |
| Biting on a pencil, skin sensation, corneal reflex | CN V (Trigeminal) |
| Smelling aromatic oils or salts | CN I (Olfactory) |
| Rinne or Weber tuning fork test, balance | CN VIII (Vestibulocochlear) |
| Saying "Ah!", uvula position, gag reflex | CN IX/X (Glossopharyngeal/Vagus) |
| Taste posterior 1/3 of tongue | CN IX (Glossopharyngeal) |
| Motor to facial muscles | CN VII (Facial) |
| Eye abduction | CN VI (Abducens) |
| Eye movement (most), pupillary constriction | CN III (Oculomotor) |
| Parasympathetic to thoracic and abdominal viscera | CN X (Vagus) |

How many pairs of cranial nerves? **12 pairs**
How many pairs of spinal nerves? **31 pairs**

## SENSE ORGANS

### Multiple Choice — The Eye

1. What is the point in the retina where the optic nerve exits and there are no photoreceptors (blind spot)?
   - *(Optic disc / blind spot)*

2. Which structure controls the amount of light entering the eye?
   - **c. Iris**

3. The fovea is specialized for:
   - **c. Highest cone density and sharpest vision**

4. Damage to the macula lutea affects which function most directly?
   - **b. Central sharp vision**

5. Rods differ from cones in that they:
   - **c. Have higher sensitivity to low light**

6. Which cells in the retina contain melanopsin?
   - **d. Ganglion cells (ipRGCs)**

7. Which cells in the retina contain photopsin?
   - **b. Cones**

8. Night blindness is due to inability to form the vitamin A-derived component of what visual pigment?
   - **b. Rhodopsin**

9. Which retinal cells perform lateral inhibition to enhance contrast detection?
   - **d. Horizontal cells**

10. Which retinal cells directly modulate bipolar cells via graded potentials?
    - **b. Horizontal cells**

11. Which photoreceptor cells contribute to circadian rhythm regulation rather than image formation?
    - **c. Intrinsically photosensitive retinal ganglion cells (ipRGCs)**

12. What neurotransmitter is released by photoreceptors in the dark?
    - **a. Glutamate**

13. Which structure adapts the eye for near vision by changing lens shape?
    - **c. Ciliary body**

14. What are the names of the muscles that allow focusing of the eye by changing the shape of the lens?
    - *(Ciliary muscles)*

15. Which structure in the eye serves to control light entering the eye by dilating and constricting the pupil?
    - **c. Iris**

16. The ganglionic cells have axons that become the \_\_\_\_ nerve.
    - **d. Optic**

17. The function of the choroid is to:
    - **c. Absorb stray light**

18. Which structure posterior to the retina provides nourishment and removes waste?
    - **b. Choroid**

19. What disorder is characterized by an imbalance in secretion and reabsorption of aqueous humor?
    - **a. Glaucoma**

20. Glaucoma is caused by:
    - **c. Increased intraocular pressure damaging the optic nerve**

21. Which disorder involves a distorted or altered sense of taste?
    - **a. Dysgeusia**

22. Diabetic retinopathy initially results from:
    - **e. Microaneurysms from damaged retinal vessels**

23. Which disorder is characterized by degeneration of the optic nerve head?
    - **a. Glaucoma**

24. Mark goes to the eye doctor reporting cloudy spots, difficulty seeing at night, and light sensitivity. What is the likely diagnosis?
    - **b. Cataracts; lens**

25. Myopia results from:
    - **b. Too long an eyeball or excessively curved cornea**

26. Damage to the right optic tract results in:
    - **b. Left homonymous hemianopia**

27. Which pathway correctly describes how visual field information is mapped?
    - **c. Left visual field → right retina → right cortex**

28. When following a car moving left to right, the extrinsic muscles and cranial nerves used are:
    - **b. Left medial rectus (CN III) and right lateral rectus (CN VI)**

29. Color blindness is caused primarily by:
    - **c. Defective or missing cone opsins**

30. Which structure (in eye anatomy) is most affected by each condition?
    - Conjunctivitis → conjunctiva
    - Glaucoma → trabecular meshwork / optic nerve
    - Cataracts → lens
    - Iritis → iris
    - Retinopathy → retina

31. Which disorder stems from declining flexibility of the lens, correlated to age?
    - **d. Presbyopia**

32. What type of eye disorder is caused by the eyeball being too long? *(Myopia / nearsightedness)*

33. Which condition is caused by an uneven curvature of the cornea or lens?
    - **b. Astigmatism**

---

### Multiple Choice — The Ear

34. The cochlea is part of the \_\_\_\_ ear. *(Inner)*
35. The incus is part of the \_\_\_\_ ear. *(Middle)*
36. The auricle is part of the \_\_\_\_ ear. *(Outer)*
37. Motion sickness is due to disturbances within the \_\_\_\_ ear. *(Inner)*

38. What is the function of the pinna?
    - **c. Collects sound waves and funnels them into the ear canal.**

39. What are the tiny bones in the middle ear responsible for?
    - **c. Conducting sound waves**

40. Name the three bones that make up the middle ear.
    - *(Malleus, Incus, Stapes)*

41. Which structure in the vestibular apparatus detects angular acceleration in the horizontal plane?
    - **c. Ampulla of the lateral semicircular canal**

42. Which structure in the vestibular apparatus is responsible for detection of "side-to-side" movements?
    - **b. Utricle**

43. What fluid is located within the membranous labyrinth? *(Endolymph)*
44. What fluid is located in the scala vestibuli and scala tympani? *(Perilymph)*

45. Which ion causes depolarization of hair cells when stereocilia are deflected?
    - **b. Potassium (K⁺)**

46. Which structure detects angular acceleration in the semicircular canals?
    - **c. Ampulla of the lateral semicircular canal**

47. The tympanic reflex protects the inner ear from loud sounds by:
    - **b. Contracting the tensor tympani and stapedius**

48. Which structure equalizes pressure between the middle ear and throat?
    - **a. Eustachian tube**

49. Children are more likely to get ear infections because their Eustachian tubes are:
    - **a. Shorter and more horizontal**

50. The cochlea coils around what screwlike bony structure?
    - **a. Modiolus**

51. Which cranial nerve is formed via convergence of the vestibular and cochlear nerves?
    - **c. CN VIII (Vestibulocochlear)**

52. High-frequency sounds are detected where in the cochlea?
    - **b. Near the base of the cochlea**

53. The function of inner hair cells is to:
    - **b. Provide afferent auditory input**

54. Which type of deafness occurs when there is damage to the cochlea or auditory nerve?
    - **c. Sensorineural deafness**

55. Which of the following is a cause of conductive hearing loss?
    - **c. Blockage from earwax**

56. Presbycusis primarily results from degeneration of:
    - **c. Hair cells in the cochlea**

57. In the auditory pathway, where does the first binaural processing occur?
    - **b. Superior olivary complex**

58. The semicircular canals detect:
    - **e. Angular acceleration**

59. What structure vibrates first when sound waves enter the ear?
    - **b. Eardrum (tympanic membrane)**

60. Name the blood pressure cuff and meter used in BP measurement. *(Sphygmomanometer)*
61. What sounds are heard by stethoscope during BP measurement? *(Sounds of Korotkoff)*

---

### Multiple Choice — Olfaction, Gustation & Touch

62. Which part of the olfactory system contains the sensory neurons that detect odor molecules?
    - **c. Olfactory epithelium**

63. The first relay station for olfactory information is the:
    - **b. Olfactory bulb**

64. Olfactory receptor neurons are unusual because they:
    - **c. Are replaced every ~30–60 days**

65. The vomeronasal organ's main function is to:
    - **e. Perceive pheromones related to social behaviors**

66. Why is smell so strongly attached to emotion and memory?
    - *(The olfactory bulb connects directly to the limbic system — emotion and memory centers)*

67. Which gustatory papillae lack taste buds and are primarily implicated in texture sensation?
    - **a. Filiform**

68. Which papillae contain the highest density of taste buds?
    - **c. Vallate (circumvallate)**

69. Taste and smell are what type of receptors?
    - *(Chemoreceptors)*

70. Which of the following is NOT a basic taste?
    - **b. Spicy**

71. Which taste sensation is mediated by ion channels rather than GPCRs?
    - **d. Salty** *(and sour)*

72. Sensory receptors sensitive to deep pressure are called:
    - **c. Pacinian corpuscles**

73. Which touch receptor senses pressure and vibration changes deep in the skin?
    - **a. Pacinian corpuscles**

74. Which of the following nerve endings are NOT wrapped in glial cells or connective tissue?
    - **a. Tactile discs (Merkel discs)**

75. What are proprioceptors?
    - **c. Receptors that detect stretch and stimulate a reflex contraction**

76. Stimuli for pain is detected by:
    - **d. Free nerve endings (nociceptors)**

77. Which sensory system is responsible for detecting limb position? *(Proprioception)*

78. What cranial structure receives smell signals first? *(Olfactory bulb)*

79. What nerve carries visual signals to the brain? *(Optic nerve / CN II)*

80. What photoreceptors function best in dim light? *(Rods)*

81. What is the loss of the sense of smell called? *(Anosmia)*

82. What condition results from inflammation of the outer ear canal? *(Otitis externa)*

83. Which condition results from vestibular dysfunction? *(Vertigo)*

### Sense Organ Disorder Matching

| Term | Definition |
|---|---|
| Myopia | Nearsightedness; distant objects appear blurry |
| Hyperopia | Farsightedness; cannot focus on close objects |
| Presbyopia | Age-related loss of near vision; decreased lens flexibility |
| Nyctalopia | Difficulty seeing in low light or darkness (night blindness) |
| Astigmatism | Uneven focus due to irregular curvature of cornea or lens |
| Conjunctivitis | Eye infection causing redness, irritation, discharge (pink eye) |
| Color Blindness | Inherited or acquired inability to distinguish certain colors |
| Otitis Media | Inflammation of the middle ear, often caused by infection |
| Sensorineural Deafness | Damage to inner ear structures or auditory nerve |
| Conductive Deafness | Blockage or damage in outer/middle ear |
| Anosmia | Loss of the ability to smell |
| Dysosmia | Blurred or distorted sense of smell |
| Dysgeusia | Distorted or altered sense of taste |
| Presbycusis | Age-related degeneration of cochlear hair cells |

### Sense Organ FRQs

1. Explain the process of phototransduction, starting from photon absorption to action potential generation in the optic nerve.
2. Compare the auditory pathways for detecting pitch versus loudness in the cochlea and auditory cortex.
3. Analyze how a lesion in the right optic tract would affect a patient's vision.
4. Describe the neuroanatomical pathway of olfactory signal transduction and its connection to emotion and memory.
5. Discuss the pathophysiology, risk factors, and treatment of age-related macular degeneration.
6. Draw and label a cross-section of the cochlea, indicating where different frequencies are perceived.
7. What is the difference between general senses and special senses?
8. Explain why damage to the optic chiasm produces bitemporal hemianopia. Which retinal fibers cross and what visual field do they represent?
9. Compare and contrast rods and cones in terms of structure, distribution, and specific functions.
10. Explain how a 1000 Hz sound wave moves from a rock concert amplifier to the listener's brain:
    - a. Function of outer, middle, and inner ear (include specific structures)
    - b. How the basilar membrane responds to 1000 Hz and how this leads to action potentials
    - c. One condition or abnormality that impairs hearing and how it affects transmission
11. Explain the place theory of audition and what the tonotopic organization of hair cells refers to.
12. Explain the purpose of the round window.
13. Describe the basic aspects of static equilibrium and dynamic equilibrium in the ear. In which process are the maculae utilized?
14. List the 5 types of sensory receptors and provide an example or function of each.
15. Contrast the functions of primary and secondary afferent muscle spindle fibers.
16. Why do heart attack patients feel pain in both the skin and the heart? *(Referred pain)*
17. Differentiate outer and inner hair cells. Describe how inner hair cells function.
18. Establish the similarities and differences between rods and cones.
19. Why is it sometimes difficult to distinguish very cold from very warm objects touching the skin?
20. A woman used eye drops and could smell and "taste" them afterward. Explain in detail how this could occur.
21. What causes the irises of albino people to appear red or pink?
22. Bobby fell asleep in a dark movie theater. When he walked into bright sunlight, he was momentarily blinded. Explain why, using: sensitivity, photopigment, rods, cones.
23. FRQ: Rohan was swimming and got bonked on the head. He rubs the area to reduce pain. Explain why rubbing reduces pain. *(Gate control theory)*
24. FRQ: Rohan gets bonked on his left arm. Where will the second synapse occur when transmitting this information to the brain, and which somatosensory neural pathway is used?
25. FRQ: Rohan cannot see the colors of the sky. Which type of cone is he lacking and at what chromosome does he likely have a mutation?
26. FRQ: Vascular tissue near Rohan's ciliary muscle has a thrombus. Which disease could result, and how would it be treated? *(Glaucoma; eye drops, laser surgery, or drainage surgery)*
27. FRQ: A patient's eyes are swinging back and forth (nystagmus) due to unusual vestibular input. Propose a mechanism for why this occurs.
28. FRQ: Describe how glaucoma leads to blindness.
29. FRQ: What is the purpose of pupil constriction?

---

### Sense Organ Case Studies

**Case — Tina (Myopia):**
Tina notices blurry street signs and difficulty reading. She has headaches after prolonged screen use.
1. What is this condition? *(Myopia)*
2. What type of lenses would her glasses have? *(Concave)*
3. What will the glasses do? *(Cause the image to focus on the retina)*

**Case — Beethoven (Otosclerosis):**
Beethoven showed symptoms at age 26 (buzzing sounds), lost 60% of hearing by 31, completely deaf by 46. The inherited disorder caused hearing loss due to inability to amplify sound and is potentially lifelong.
1. What disorder is this? *(Otosclerosis)*
2. What causes hearing loss in this disorder? *(The stapes becomes stuck in place and unable to vibrate)*
3. List two possible treatments. *(Hearing aids; surgical stapedectomy)*

---

## MIXED DISORDERS — Master Matching Reference

| Disorder | System | Key Features |
|---|---|---|
| Epilepsy | Nervous | Sudden bursts of electrochemical activity; seizures |
| Multiple Sclerosis | Nervous | Autoimmune demyelination; optic neuritis, limb weakness, worsens with heat |
| Alzheimer's Disease | Nervous | APOE gene risk; cholinergic neuron loss; memory deterioration |
| ALS | Nervous | Upper and lower motor neuron loss; progressive paralysis |
| Tay-Sachs | Nervous | Enzyme deficiency; cannot break down lipids in nerve cells |
| Parkinson's Disease | Nervous | Dopamine loss in substantia nigra; tremors, rigidity |
| Cerebral Palsy | Nervous | Brain damage before/during/shortly after birth; movement disorders |
| Myasthenia Gravis | Nervous | ACh receptor blockade by antibodies; muscle weakness |
| Graves' Disease | Endocrine | TSH receptor autoantibodies; hyperthyroidism, exophthalmos |
| Hashimoto's Disease | Endocrine | Autoimmune thyroid destruction; hypothyroidism |
| Addison's Disease | Endocrine | Low cortisol, high ACTH; hyperpigmentation, fatigue |
| Cushing's Syndrome | Endocrine | High cortisol; moon face, central obesity |
| Glaucoma | Sensory | Increased intraocular pressure; optic nerve degeneration |
| Cataracts | Sensory | Lens opacity; cloudy vision |
| Macular Degeneration | Sensory | Breakdown of cells near center of retina; central vision loss |
| Conjunctivitis | Sensory | Inflammation of conjunctiva; pink eye; contagious |
| Otitis Media | Sensory | Middle ear infection; common in children |
| Presbycusis | Sensory | Age-related cochlear hair cell loss |
| Otosclerosis | Sensory | Stapes fixation; inherited conductive hearing loss |

### Nervous System Disorders — Matching

Match each disorder to its description:

| Disorder | Description |
|---|---|
| Epilepsy | Marked by sudden bursts of electrochemical activity that scramble the brain's messages |
| Multiple Sclerosis | Caused by autoimmunity; demyelination of nerves |
| Alzheimer's Disease | Increased risk linked to inheritance of apolipoprotein E (APOE) gene |
| ALS (Lou Gehrig's Disease) | Amyotrophic Lateral Sclerosis |
| Tay-Sachs Disease | Results from enzyme deficiency leading to inability of nerve cells to break down lipids |
| Parkinson's Disease | Loss of midbrain dopaminergic neurons |
| Cerebral Palsy | Disrupts motor messages from brain to muscle; usually caused by brain damage during/before birth |

### Matching — Endocrine Glands

Match each gland to the statements:

| Gland | Statement |
|---|---|
| Anterior Pituitary | Releases tropic hormones targeting other endocrine glands; releases MSH, prolactin |
| Posterior Pituitary | Releases oxytocin and ADH (synthesized by hypothalamus) |
| Thyroid | Releases triiodothyronine (T3) and thyroxine (T4); calcitonin from parafollicular cells |
| Parathyroid | Releases PTH; increases blood calcium |
| Adrenal Medulla | Releases epinephrine and norepinephrine |
| Adrenal Cortex | Releases glucocorticoids, mineralocorticoids, androgens |
| Thymus | Promotes immune system development and function |
| Pineal Gland | Secretes melatonin |
| Pancreas | Beta cells: insulin and amylin; Alpha cells: glucagon |

---

### Endocrine Case Study

**Case — Lisa (7-year-old girl):**
Lisa has been increasingly thirsty, urinating frequently at night, eating twice as much, yet losing weight. Lab results: Blood glucose 545 mg/dL (normal 50–170); Blood pH 7.23 (normal 7.35–7.45); Urine positive for glucose and ketone bodies.

1. Which condition does Lisa most likely have?
2. Why are Lisa's blood-glucose levels elevated?
3. Lisa has a fruity odor to her breath. Why?
4. Describe the mechanism of action of glucagon from its gland to its target.

---

**Case — Harry Sullivan (21-year-old male):**
Harry reports weakness, fatigue, knee pain, dizziness, nausea, weight loss, craving for salty foods, persistent tan despite winter, and irritability.

1. What disease does Harry have?
2. This disease is due to:
   - a. Hypersecretion of insulin
   - b. Hyposecretion of aldosterone
   - c. Hypersecretion of aldosterone
   - **d. Hyposecretion of cortisol**
3. Which treatment is most useful?
4. The hyperpigmentation is due to:
   - a. Hyposecretion of ACTH
   - b. Hyposecretion of aldosterone
   - **c. Hypersecretion of ACTH**
   - d. Hypersecretion of MSH

## SENSE ORGANS

### Multiple Choice

1. What is the point in the retina where the optic nerves exit and there are no photoreceptors (blind spot)?

2. Which structure controls the amount of light entering the eye by dilating and constricting the pupil?
   - a. Lens
   - b. Cornea
   - **c. Iris**
   - d. Ciliary bodies

3. Mark goes to the eye doctor reporting cloudy spots, difficulty seeing at night, and light sensitivity. What is the likely diagnosis and affected structure?
   - a. Cataracts; retina
   - **b. Cataracts; lens**
   - c. Macular degeneration; retina
   - d. Macular degeneration; lens

4. What are the names of the muscles that allow focusing of the eye by changing the shape of the lens?

5. Name the three bones that make up the middle ear.

6. The cochlea is part of the \_\_\_\_\_ (outer/middle/inner) ear.

7. The incus is part of the \_\_\_\_\_ (outer/middle/inner) ear.

8. The auricle is part of the \_\_\_\_\_ (outer/middle/inner) ear.

9. Motion sickness is due to disturbances within the \_\_\_\_\_ (outer/middle/inner) ear.

10. Gustatory sense refers to what?

11. What is the function of the vestibule located in the inner ear?

12. Which touch receptor senses pressure and vibration changes deep in the skin?
    - **a. Pacinian corpuscles**
    - b. Meissner's corpuscles
    - c. Merkel disks

13. Visceral nociceptors respond to lack of \_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_.

14. What type of eye disorder is caused by the eyeball being too long?

15. Which structure (in the eye) is most affected by:
    - Conjunctivitis?
    - Glaucoma?
    - Cataracts?
    - Iritis?
    - Retinopathy?

16. Which of the following statements about rods is NOT true?
    - **a. Rods are about 10 times more common than cones** *(this is actually true — rods outnumber cones ~20:1; the false statement varies by source)*
    - b. Rods contain a photosensitive pigment called rhodopsin
    - c. Rods function better than cones in dim light
    - d. Rods do not provide color vision

17. What type of eye disorder is nearsightedness?
    - **a. Myopia**
    - b. Hyperopia
    - c. Cataracts

18. What are proprioceptors?
    - a. Receptors that help maintain internal homeostasis
    - b. Receptors that sense pressure
    - **c. Receptors that detect stretch and stimulate a reflex contraction**

19. How many specialized receptor cells are in each taste bud?
    - a. 1000
    - b. 100
    - **c. 20**
    - d. 5

20. The function of the choroid is to:
    - a. Make color vision possible
    - b. Refract light rays
    - **c. Absorb stray light**
    - d. Regulate light entrance

21. The ganglionic cells have axons that become the \_\_\_\_\_ nerve.
    - a. Auditory
    - b. Olfactory
    - c. Facial
    - **d. Optic**

22. The function of the Eustachian tube is to:
    - **a. Drain fluid from the inner ear into the throat / equalize pressure on both sides of the eardrum**

23. Which of the following vision disorders is associated with Vitamin A deficiency?
    - a. Myopia
    - b. Hyperopia
    - c. Presbyopia
    - **d. Nyctalopia**

24. Which disorder results from a cornea that is too steep?
    - **a. Myopia or Astigmatism** (steep cornea → astigmatism; too much refractive power → myopia)

25. Caused by reduced flexibility in the lens:
    - **Presbyopia**

26. This part focuses light, changing shape as it takes in reflected light from objects near and far:
    - **a. Lens**

### Sense Organ Case Studies

**Case — Tina (myopia):**
Tina notices blurry street signs and difficulty reading. She has headaches after prolonged screen use.
1. What is this condition called? (Myopia / Hyperopia / Nyctalopia / Presbyopia)
2. What type of lenses would her glasses have? (Concave / Convex)
3. What will the glasses do to help her see better?

**Case — Beethoven (hearing loss):**
Beethoven showed symptoms at age 26 (buzzing sounds), lost 60% of hearing by 31, completely deaf by 46. The inherited disorder caused hearing loss due to the ear's inability to amplify sound and is potentially lifelong.
1. What disorder is this? (Otitis Media / Presbycusis / Ménière's Disease / Otosclerosis)
2. What causes hearing loss in this disorder?
3. List two possible treatments.

## MIXED NERVOUS / ENDOCRINE / SENSE ORGAN — Matching

Match the following disorders with their descriptions:

| Disorder | Description |
|---|---|
| Glaucoma | Abnormally high pressure in the eye |
| Conjunctivitis | Inflammation of the outermost layer of the eye and inner surface of the eyelids |
| Alzheimer's Disease | Abnormal changes in the brain mainly affecting memory and mental abilities |
| Multiple Sclerosis | Chronic disease affecting myelin around nerves in the brain and spinal cord |
| Epilepsy | Episodes of disturbed brain activity causing changes in attention or behavior |
| Cerebral Palsy | Brain damage occurring before or during birth or within the first 3–5 years of life |
| Myasthenia Gravis | Autoimmune; circulating antibodies block acetylcholine receptors at the neuromuscular junction |
| Graves' Disease | Autoimmune; thyroid overactive, producing excess thyroid hormones |

Part A — Multiple Choice (22 questions)
1. Which of the following is true of vision and phototransduction? [select all that apply]
•	a. Damage to the optic chiasm will cause bitemporal hemianopia
•	b. Guanosine monophosphate (GMP) is converted to cGMP by phosphodiesterase
•	c. Transducin is a G-protein
•	d. Na+ channels open when rhodopsin's retinal is converted to its all-trans form
•	e. Vitamin A is converted into retinal for use in rhodopsins
2. She cuts the fibers of Larry's left nasal optic nerve before the optic chiasm. What visual field defect will this result in?
•	a. Loss of vision in left visual field in both eyes
•	b. Loss of vision in right visual field in both eyes
•	c. Loss of vision in left visual field in left eye
•	d. Loss of vision in right visual field in left eye
•	e. Loss of temporal vision in both eyes
3. Which of the following is NOT a function of mucus in the nasal cavity?
•	a. Mucus contains the discarded cell bodies of dead olfactory receptors
•	b. Mucus traps and dissolves odorants to allow them to bind to receptors
•	c. Mucus contains odorant-binding proteins (OBPs) to aid in transport of odorants to receptors
•	d. Mucus travels from the nasal cavity to the lacrimal gland above the eyes in order to produce tears
•	e. Mucus lubricates the nasal cavity to prevent epithelium from drying out
4. Moebius Syndrome is a congenital neurological disorder with significant effects on the body's sense organs, particularly in the face. It is caused by underdeveloped cranial nerves 6 and 7. Based on your knowledge of cranial nerves, which of the following would likely NOT be affected in Moebius Syndrome?
•	a. Facial paralysis
•	b. Movement of eyes
•	c. Sensation of the eyelid
•	d. All of the above
5. Which of the following nuclei located in the medulla receive visceral sensory input from cranial nerves VII, IX, and X?
•	a. Nucleus ambiguus
•	b. Dorsal motor nucleus
•	c. Inferior olivary nucleus
•	d. Solitary nucleus
•	e. Sensorinucleus
6. The olfactory epithelium, which contains olfactory receptor cells, is located in which structure?
•	A. Oral cavity
•	B. Pharynx
•	C. Superior portion of the nasal cavity
•	D. Larynx
7. Which type of hearing loss results from damage to the hair cells of the cochlea or the auditory nerve?
•	A. Conductive hearing loss
•	B. Sensorineural hearing loss
•	C. Mixed hearing loss
•	D. Central hearing loss
8. An individual with anosmia would have difficulty with which of the following?
•	A. Distinguishing between sweet and sour tastes
•	B. Detecting the scent of a flower
•	C. Hearing whispered speech
•	D. Maintaining balance when standing on one foot
9. A clinical test reveals that a patient can taste sweet, sour, salty, and bitter substances but reports that food "has no flavor." Which of the following is the most likely explanation?
•	A. Damage to the gustatory cortex
•	B. Damage to the facial nerve (CN VII)
•	C. Loss of olfactory function (anosmia)
•	D. Destruction of taste receptor cells on the tongue
10. A neurologist examines a patient with astigmatism. Which of the following best describes the underlying optical defect in this condition?
•	A. The lens loses its elasticity and cannot accommodate for near vision
•	B. The cornea or lens has an irregular curvature, causing light to focus at multiple points
•	C. The eyeball is too short, causing light to focus behind the retina
•	D. Increased intraocular pressure damages the optic nerve
11. A researcher is studying how odor molecules are detected. The binding of an odorant molecule to an olfactory receptor triggers a signaling cascade that ultimately opens ion channels. Which second messenger system is primarily involved in olfactory signal transduction?
•	A. The inositol triphosphate (IP3) pathway
•	B. The cyclic AMP (cAMP) pathway involving Golf protein
•	C. The cyclic GMP (cGMP) pathway
•	D. The JAK-STAT signaling pathway
12. Which cranial nerve carries taste information specifically from the anterior two-thirds of the tongue?
•	a. Glossopharyngeal nerve (CN IX)
•	b. Facial nerve (CN VII)
•	c. Vagus nerve (CN X)
•	d. Trigeminal nerve (CN V3)
13. Which cranial nerve carries visual information from the retina to the brain?
•	a. Trochlear nerve (CN IV)
•	b. Optic nerve (CN II)
•	c. Oculomotor nerve (CN III)
•	d. Abducens nerve (CN VI)
14. Which cranial nerve carries general sensation information specifically from the posterior one-third of the tongue?
•	a. Glossopharyngeal nerve (CN IX)
•	b. Trigeminal nerve (CN V3)
•	c. Abducens nerve (CN VI)
•	d. Facial nerve (CN VII)
15. The prevertebral ganglia are innervated by which nerve?
•	a. Greater splanchnic nerve
•	b. Vagus nerve
•	c. Sympathetic chain
•	d. Phrenic nerve
16. Match the special sense to the area of the brain where it is processed: Smell → ?
•	A. Temporal Lobe
•	B. Insula
•	C. Frontal Lobe
•	D. Occipital Lobe
17. What type of epithelium is the olfactory epithelium composed of?
•	a. Pseudostratified columnar epithelium
•	b. Stratified cuboidal
•	c. Stratified squamous
•	d. Simple squamous epithelium
18. How does the trigeminal (V) nerve compare in size with the other cranial nerves?
•	a. It is the smallest cranial nerve
•	b. It is the largest cranial nerve
•	c. It is medium-sized compared to other cranial nerves
•	d. It is the same size as the facial nerve
19. Which cranial nerve is the only one that originates from both the brain and spinal cord?
•	a. Vagus (X)
•	b. Glossopharyngeal (IX)
•	c. Accessory (XI)
•	d. Hypoglossal (XII)
20. Where do motor axons of the facial (VII) nerve originate?
•	a. Medulla oblongata
•	b. Midbrain
•	c. Pons
•	d. Cerebral cortex
21. Through which foramen does the glossopharyngeal (IX) nerve exit the skull?
•	a. Foramen ovale
•	b. Foramen rotundum
•	c. Jugular foramen
•	d. Hypoglossal canal
22. Which cranial nerve is the smallest?
•	a. Olfactory (I)
•	b. Trochlear (IV)
•	c. Abducens (VI)
•	d. Accessory (XI)

Nervous System MCQ Set
Part 1: Disorder Identification

1. Which disorder is characterized by tremors, muscle rigidity, and loss of dopamine-producing cells?
A. Alzheimer's Disease
B. Parkinson's Disease
C. ALS
D. Multiple Sclerosis
Answer: B

2. Which condition involves recurrent seizures due to abnormal brain electrical activity?
A. Stroke
B. Epilepsy
C. Tay-Sachs
D. Myasthenia Gravis
Answer: B

3. Which disorder involves autoimmune destruction of myelin?
A. ALS
B. Parkinson's Disease
C. Multiple Sclerosis
D. Cerebral Palsy
Answer: C

4. Which disorder affects movement and posture due to early brain damage?
A. Cerebral Palsy
B. Stroke
C. Alzheimer's Disease
D. Epilepsy
Answer: A

5. Which disease primarily affects memory and cognition due to cholinergic neuron degeneration?
A. ALS
B. Alzheimer's Disease
C. Parkinson's Disease
D. Stroke
Answer: B

6. Which disease affects both upper and lower motor neurons leading to paralysis?
A. ALS
B. MS
C. Epilepsy
D. Tay-Sachs
Answer: A

7. Which disorder involves antibodies blocking acetylcholine receptors?
A. Myasthenia Gravis
B. Parkinson's Disease
C. Stroke
D. ALS
Answer: A

8. Which condition presents as a painful stripe of blisters on one side of the body?
A. Stroke
B. Shingles
C. Epilepsy
D. MS
Answer: B

9. What condition results from interrupted blood flow to the brain?
A. Stroke
B. ALS
C. Alzheimer's Disease
D. Myasthenia Gravis
Answer: A

10. Which disease involves an enzyme deficiency leading to lipid buildup in nerve cells?
A. Tay-Sachs
B. Parkinson's Disease
C. MS
D. Epilepsy
Answer: A

Part 2: Conceptual MCQs

11. Why is white matter called "white"?
A. It contains more blood vessels
B. It contains myelin sheaths
C. It has more neurons
D. It lacks glial cells
Answer: B

12. Which meningeal layer resembles a spider web?
A. Dura mater
B. Pia mater
C. Arachnoid mater
D. Endoneurium
Answer: C

13. What is the correct order of meninges (deep → superficial)?
A. Dura → Arachnoid → Pia
B. Pia → Arachnoid → Dura
C. Arachnoid → Pia → Dura
D. Pia → Dura → Arachnoid
Answer: B

14. What fluid is collected during a lumbar puncture?
A. Blood plasma
B. Interstitial fluid
C. Cerebrospinal fluid
D. Lymph
Answer: C

15. Which cells are primarily responsible for myelination in the CNS?
A. Astrocytes
B. Microglia
C. Oligodendrocytes
D. Schwann cells
Answer: C

16. What is the main function of microglial cells?
A. Signal transmission
B. Myelination
C. Immune defense and debris removal
D. Hormone production
Answer: C

17. The all-or-nothing law means:
A. Neurons fire at variable strengths
B. Neurons either fire completely or not at all
C. Only some neurons fire
D. Signals weaken over time
Answer: B

18. What happens if inhibitory neurotransmitters fail?
A. Reduced neuron firing
B. Excessive neuron firing
C. No effect
D. Slower reflexes only
Answer: B

19. Which brain region is part of the hindbrain?
A. Cerebrum
B. Thalamus
C. Medulla
D. Hypothalamus
Answer: C

20. Heat worsens symptoms in multiple sclerosis because:
A. It increases blood pressure
B. It slows nerve conduction further in demyelinated neurons
C. It increases neurotransmitter release
D. It damages neurons directly
Answer: B

Part 3: Case-Based MCQs

21. A patient with demyelination, blurred vision, and muscle weakness likely has:
A. ALS
B. Alzheimer's Disease
C. Multiple Sclerosis
D. Parkinson's Disease
Answer: C

22. A patient with memory loss, repetition, and language difficulty likely has:
A. Stroke
B. Alzheimer's Disease
C. Epilepsy
D. MS
Answer: B

23. Which neurotransmitter is most affected in Alzheimer's disease?
A. Dopamine
B. Serotonin
C. Acetylcholine
D. GABA
Answer: C

24. What causes language function to shift to the opposite hemisphere in brain tumors?
A. Neuron death
B. Brain plasticity
C. Increased blood flow
D. Hormonal changes
Answer: B

Part 4: Action Potential MCQs

25. During depolarization, what occurs?
A. K⁺ enters the cell
B. Na⁺ enters the cell
C. Cl⁻ exits the cell
D. Ca²⁺ leaves the cell
Answer: B

26. During repolarization, what occurs?
A. Na⁺ enters
B. K⁺ exits
C. Ca²⁺ enters
D. Cl⁻ enters
Answer: B

27. Hyperpolarization occurs because:
A. Too much Na⁺ enters
B. Too much K⁺ leaves
C. Cl⁻ leaves
D. Ca²⁺ accumulates
Answer: B

Part 5: Higher-Level Thinking

28. Why is myelin important?
A. It produces neurotransmitters
B. It speeds up action potential conduction
C. It protects blood vessels
D. It generates impulses
Answer: B

29. Which structure is most affected in Parkinson’s disease?
A. Hippocampus
B. Substantia nigra
C. Cerebellum
D. Medulla
Answer: B

30. Which disorder involves neuromuscular junction dysfunction?
A. ALS
B. Myasthenia Gravis
C. Stroke
D. MS
Answer: B

Cranial Nerves & Sensory System MCQ Set
Part B: Short Answer → MCQs

1. What is the correct order of structures that light passes through?
A. Cornea → Pupil → Lens → Retina → Optic nerve
B. Lens → Cornea → Pupil → Retina → Optic nerve
C. Cornea → Lens → Pupil → Retina → Optic nerve
D. Pupil → Cornea → Lens → Retina → Optic nerve
Answer: A

2. What does it mean for an odorant to be volatile?
A. It dissolves in water easily
B. It can evaporate and enter the air
C. It binds strongly to receptors
D. It is electrically charged
Answer: B

3. What are the afferent and efferent nerves of the pupillary reflex?
A. Afferent: CN III, Efferent: CN II
B. Afferent: CN II, Efferent: CN III
C. Afferent: CN V, Efferent: CN VII
D. Afferent: CN VIII, Efferent: CN VI
Answer: B

4. The oculomotor nerve (CN III) innervates which of the following muscles?
A. Lateral rectus
B. Superior rectus
C. Superior oblique
D. Medial rectus only
Answer: B

5. Which extraocular muscles are NOT innervated by CN III?
A. Superior rectus and inferior rectus
B. Lateral rectus and superior oblique
C. Medial rectus and inferior oblique
D. Levator palpebrae and inferior rectus
Answer: B

6. Which cranial nerve is affected if a patient has facial paralysis, loss of taste, dry mouth, and inability to close one eye?
A. CN V
B. CN VII
C. CN IX
D. CN X
Answer: B

7. A patient with nystagmus, gaze palsy, dizziness, and speech coordination issues most likely has damage in which brain region?
A. Cerebrum
B. Cerebellum/Brainstem
C. Thalamus
D. Hypothalamus
Answer: B

8. Based on Question 7, why did the patient most likely fall?
A. Loss of vision
B. Loss of balance and coordination
C. Muscle atrophy
D. Cognitive decline
Answer: B

9. Where is the vagus nerve (CN X) located in the neck?
A. Inside the spinal cord
B. Within the carotid sheath
C. Behind the trachea
D. Inside the esophagus
Answer: B

10. What are the two major motor functions of the hypoglossal nerve (CN XII)?
A. Eye movement and blinking
B. Tongue movement and speech articulation
C. Swallowing and breathing
D. Facial expression and chewing
Answer: B

11. Where do axons in the olfactory tract terminate?
A. Thalamus
B. Occipital lobe
C. Olfactory cortex
D. Cerebellum
Answer: C

12. Where do most optic tract axons terminate?
A. Olfactory bulb
B. Lateral geniculate nucleus (thalamus)
C. Cerebellum
D. Brainstem
Answer: B

13. Which branch of CN III innervates the superior rectus muscle?
A. Inferior division
B. Superior division
C. Medial branch
D. Lateral branch
Answer: B

14. What structures are found in the vestibular and spiral ganglia?
A. Motor neurons
B. Sensory neuron cell bodies
C. Interneurons
D. Glial cells only
Answer: B

15. Which cranial nerves are classified as special sensory?
A. CN I, II, VIII
B. CN III, IV, VI
C. CN V, VII, IX
D. CN X, XI, XII
Answer: A

16. What are the three branches of the trigeminal nerve (CN V)?
A. Frontal, parietal, occipital
B. Ophthalmic, maxillary, mandibular
C. Superior, middle, inferior
D. Anterior, posterior, lateral
Answer: B

Which branch is the largest?
A. Ophthalmic
B. Maxillary
C. Mandibular
D. All equal
Answer: C

17. On what basis is the vagus nerve named?
A. Its function
B. Its location
C. Its wandering course through the body
D. Its size
Answer: C

Part C: Review Questions → MCQs

18. How are cranial nerves named and numbered?
A. By size only
B. By function only
C. By order of emergence and function
D. Randomly
Answer: C

19. What distinguishes special sensory, motor, and mixed cranial nerves?
A. Location only
B. Function: sensory, motor, or both
C. Size
D. Shape
Answer: B

20. CN III, IV, and VI are functionally related because they:
A. Control facial expression
B. Control eye movements
C. Control hearing
D. Control swallowing
Answer: B

21. Why is the facial nerve (CN VII) considered the major motor nerve of the head?
A. It controls chewing
B. It controls most facial expressions
C. It controls vision
D. It controls balance
Answer: B

22. What are the functions of the two branches of the vestibulocochlear nerve (CN VIII)?
A. Vision and smell
B. Hearing and balance
C. Taste and touch
D. Movement and reflex
Answer: B




*End of compiled question set.*
